HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
American Express's Net Jumps 72.2%

BYLINE: By KURT EICHENWALD

 BODY:
Coming off a year of financial turmoil in its main lines of business, the American Express Company said yesterday that its earnings rose 72.2 percent last quarter, bolstered by record results in its consumer financial and information services business. But analysts viewed the overall results as poor.
The financial services company said its quarterly profits rose to $237.4 million, or 47 cents a share, compared with $137.9 million, or 28 cents a share, in the comparable period of 1990. 
 For all of 1991, earnings more than quadrupled, to $788.7 million, or $1.59 a share, from $181.5 million, or 34 cents a share, the year before.
 
 
Effect of Shearson Revamping
 But the apparent turnaround in the yearly results is not as strong as it appears. The 1990 results were weighed down by a charge of $837 million to pay for a reorganization of Shearson Lehman Brothers, the brokerage subsidiary of American Express. In 1989, without such large special charges, the company earned $1.16 billion.
Indeed, analysts said that despite the increase in earnings over the last year, the company's results were poor over all. "This is a lousy end to a lousy year," said John Keefe, an analyst with Lipper Analytical Services. "Many of its businesses were really weak."
American Express also bolstered its earnings last year by booking about $200 million worth of tax benefits earned in previous years. Many of those tax benefits stemmed from losses at Shearson.
In trading on the New York Stock Exchange, shares of American Express closed yesterday at $22.50, down 12.5 cents each.
 
 
Decline in Travel Division
 In a breakdown of the company's yearly earnings, American Express's largest business, the Travel Related Services division, which oversees the green, gold and platinum American Express charge cards, provided the biggest contribution for 1991, but was far from the money machine it has been in recent years. Earnings for the year in the division totaled $396 million, a 59 percent decline from the $956 million earned in 1990.
The division, the financial heart of American Express, had its first loss in decades in the third quarter of 1991 as defaults jumped among holders of its Optima credit card. For the year, the travel division increased provisions for credit losses to $697 million before taxes, with $123 million taken in the fourth quarter.
Operating expenses climbed by $323 million before taxes. The company also had a one-time charge in the year of $110 million to pay for a reorganization of the division.
Despite the recession, the travel division reported higher revenue for the year, at $9.9 billion, up from $9.7 billion in 1990.
 
 
Less Growth in Cards
 The division also reported some results that could present trouble in the future, analysts said. After years of explosive growth, the number of charge cards in force remained relatively flat. American Express reported 36.6 million active cards, with no domestic growth and slim overseas growth of 1.2 percent. The smaller IDS Financial Services division, the company's consumer finance unit, turned in the best performance in its history. The division's earnings reached $248 million for the year, a 20 percent increase from the $207 million reported in 1990.
Shearson reported net income of $207 million in 1991, compared with a loss of $966 million in the previous year. Analysts were surprised by the relatively poor performance, coming off of revenues of $11.2 billion, compared with $10.2 billion in 1990.
The Shearson results were driven down by several charges totaling $171 million to account for losses.

HEADLINE: Florio Says He May Back Sales-Tax Cut

BYLINE: By JERRY GRAY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TRENTON, Jan. 28

 BODY:
Having angered Republicans on Monday by announcing details of a budget that seemed to ignore their plan to cut the sales tax, Gov. Jim Florio went before the Legislature today to say that he would be happy to sign a tax-cut bill if his budget goals could be met.
"I know that many of you would like to reduce our sales-tax revenues," he said during his annual budget speech to the Republican-controlled Legislature. "I've never been fond of the sales tax, and I'm not here to mount a defense of it. 
 "But also understand that budgets are not made without a spirit of compromise. I would be very happy to achieve our goals and still reduce the sales tax by a penny. I'm willing and anxious to listen to any plan that will meet our state's priorities."
Taking the same conciliatory tack a few hours earlier, state education officials released revised school-aid figures that provide $341 million in additional money mostly for middle-income school districts. The new aid was generated through a proposal that would cut the government's contribution to state employee pension funds.
 
School Aid a Volatile Issue
 Middle-income taxpayers have severely criticized the Florio administration, and the allocation of school aid has become one of New Jersey's most volatile political issues recently.
Together with his conciliatory State of the State message on Jan. 14, Mr. Florio's measured comments for Republican tax foes today and his Education Department's allocation of more aid for middle-income districts suggested that the traditionally combative Governor hoped to deny the Republican opposition a clear target.
He also seemed to be reaching out to the middle class, which turned against the Democrats in November, helping elect Republican majorities in both houses of the Legislature for the first time in 20 years.
Mr. Florio said his major goals were to cut the size and cost of state government and to funnel more money back to taxpayers. And he vowed that New Jersey would "take care of our own" during the current recession by creating thousands of new jobs.
He repeatedly told New Jerseyans that they are better off than their neighbors in New York and most of the rest of the country. "While other states are cutting back and hurting people, we're giving back and helping people," he said.
His budget called for no new taxes for the second year in a row and no new raises for the state's nearly 66,000 employees. It would increase aid to secondary schools, thereby holding down local property tax increases, and expand college tuition-assistance programs.
Mr. Florio relied on a turnaround in the economy, a drastic reduction in state payments for pensions and an anticipated Federal Medicaid payment to balance the budget. He also relied on revenues from a 7 percent sales tax, though the Republicans have vowed to cut it to 6 percent.
"The Governor's failure to propose a tax-cut budget means that the Republican Legislature will have to develop a state spending plan which cuts taxes," said Donald T. DiFrancesco, the new Senate President.
He conceded that the change in the pension system, which is based on a revaluation of the funds' assets, and the anticipated revenue from Medicaid payments might produce a "windfall," which Mr. Florio's administration has projected at $1.1 billion.
 
G.O.P. Vows Tax Cut
 "But if that does happen," he said, "perhaps we should look for ways to return this money to the people. We shouldn't be looking for ways to spend it.
"If the Governor won't cut taxes," Mr. DiFrancesco added, "we will."
An increase in the sales tax to 7 percent from 6 percent was part of the record $2.8 billion tax increase the Legislature, then under Democratic control, passed in 1990.
"The extra penny was needed to balance the budget in 1990," Mr. Florio said today. "We had to pay our bills. But all of us know that making a budget is about making choices and setting priorities."
Mr. Florio used his budget speech today to briefly raise the issue of abortion rights and to obliquely criticize the Federal Government's decision to withhold money to abortion clinics and counseling services.
 
'We Do Not Gag Women'
 "I have included funding in this budget to give women in New Jersey the information they need to make their own reproductive choices," he said. "In New Jersey, we do not gag women or their doctors."
The Governor attributed many of the state's economic troubles to the national recession. But he said New Jersey was better off than any of its neighbors and most of the country.
"In New Jersey today, we don't have to make the education cuts we're seeing in other states in the Northeast and across the nation," he said. "We're not faced with insurmountable deficits. We will finish the current year with a balanced budget."
Mr. Florio also said his budget would hold down college tuition. "Across the Hudson in New York," he added, "tuition increases at the state university system are planned to double."

HEADLINE: McDonnell Posts Profit

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

 BODY:
A year after a financial crisis that left its viability in doubt, the McDonnell Douglas Corporation posted strong fourth-quarter earnings yesterday, continuing a steady comeback despite cuts in the Pentagon budget and a weak market for commercial jetliners.
The nation's largest military contractor and second-largest builder of commercial aircraft said it earned $211 million, or $5.50 a share, in the fourth quarter, compared with a loss of $1 million in the corresponding period a year earlier. Revenues for the period were $4.8 billion, an increase of 18 percent. 
 For the full year, the company earned $423 million, or $11.03 a share, an increase of 38.2 percent over 1990. Revenues increased 15.7 percent, to $18.4 billion.
McDonnell Douglas was so pressed for cash early last year that it sought financial help from the Pentagon. The request was later withdrawn, but the company was plagued by rumors last winter that it might even need to file for bankruptcy.
Although McDonnell Douglas still faces further Pentagon spending cuts and weakness among its airline customers, analysts said the St. Louis-based company had come a long way. They point out that McDonnell Douglas's debt was cut to $2.39 billion at the end of the fourth quarter from a peak of $3.3 billion in March.

HEADLINE: Bank Yields Drop Slightly

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH M. FOWLER

 BODY:
Yields on bank money market accounts and certificates of deposit dropped slightly for the week ended yesterday, according to figures released by The Bank Rate Monitor, which watches bank rates.
Robert K. Heady, publisher of The Monitor, said interest rates over the next couple of weeks will be influenced by the reaction to President Bush's State of the Union address and the possibility that the Federal Reserve will again reduce its key rates." 
 In the New York area, the average money market account yields at commercial banks was 3.69 percent, down a basis point, or one-hundredth of 1 percent. The average yield on money market accounts at savings banks was 4.23 percent, off 6 points.
For money market accounts, the steepest cut among area banks was made by Hudson City Savings Bank, Paramus, N.J., which lowered its rate by 40 basis points. Anchor Savings led the way with a drop of 20 basis points for its three-month certficate of deposit, and Crossland Federal Savings Bank made the largest cut in six-month certificates of deposit, dropping them 20 basis points.
The Bank Rate Monitor said the average yields at 20 large banks outside the New York area fell slightly, with the average yield on money market accounts down a point, to 3.70 percent, and on six-month certificates down 2 basis points, to 3.86 percent.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Stock Prices As Measured By Inflation

BYLINE: By Floyd Norris

 BODY:
JUST how high are stock prices? By one measure, they are nearing, but have not yet reached, the level set more than 23 years ago, back when Richard M. Nixon was winning the Presidency and a subway token was 20 cents.
That measure of stock prices is adjusted for inflation, comparing the prices of stocks with the Consumer Price Index. Rather than measure stock prices in dollars, as normal indexes do, that calculation measures them in the context of what other things cost. The Consumer Price Index reflects the cost of a basket of items purchased by consumers; this measurement effectively shows how many such baskets an investor would need to give up to buy a representative sample of American stocks. 
 Some bulls may take comfort from the fact that stock prices by that measure are no higher than they were in 1968, despite substantial economic growth. Over that period, it should be noted, stocks have outperformed inflation after dividends are included.
But there is also a bearish way to look at the figures. Prices now are at levels that three times in the past have proved to be market tops. By contrast, when the ratio was low, stocks have often been an excellent investment.
In the summer of 1982, the Standard & Poor's 500-stock average was barely higher than the Consumer Price Index. The ratio was just 1.1 to 1. One way to look at that is that to buy a certain amount of stocks one had to give up just more than one basket of consumer products.
That came at the time that most Americans believed that inflation was all but certain to continue, and that stocks were unlikely to be good values. In fact, inflation was dying and the stock market was about to begin a decade of explosive growth.
At the end of 1991, the ratio was 3.03 to 1. That means that to buy the same group of stocks, one would have to forgo more than three baskets of consumer goods. In January, the S.& P. 500 is about unchanged, having retreated from rises early in the month. So the ratio for this month seems likely to be about the same.
The first time the ratio got so high was in November 1968, when it peaked at 3.06. By the summer of 1970, the S.& P. 500 had fallen 33 percent.
There are other comparisons between 1992 and the late 1960's. Estimates of stock holdings by individuals, as a percentage of total investments by those individuals, indicate that they have risen to the highest levels since they began declining in the early 1970's. That rush to stocks, which has taken the form of both direct holdings and purchases of stock mutual funds, has followed the sharp decline of interest rates, which made many investors more willing to take risks in hopes of gaining higher rewards.
The second time that the ratio of stock prices to consumer prices reached a high level was in late 1972. The ratio, using month-end figures, peaked at the end of December of that year, at 2.78. That was just before the brutal 1973-74 bear market began. Two years later, the S.& P. 500 had dropped 42 percent.
In the summer of 1987, the ratio climbed back to 2.88 by the end of August. Three months later, after the October crash, the S.& P. 500 was down 30 percent.
It may be interesting to note that the economy was not in a recession during the three previous times that the ratio reached current levels. In that sense, the current run-up is unprecedented. The fact that prices have risen so far during the recession -- the ratio was at 2.28 at the end of October 1990, the recent low for stock prices -- is another indicator of the extent to which the stock market seems to have discounted a substantial economic recovery.
Such calculations do not prove that stock prices are about to collapse. But they do provide another reminder that whatever else the market is it is not very cheap by historical standards. Low inflation is beloved by the bulls, in part because it makes lower interest rates possible. But it also makes it harder for companies to raise prices, and thereby to push up profits. And current share prices may be assuming a sharp rise in corporate profits within a year or two.

GRAPHIC: Graph: "By One Measure, Back to the Peak" shows the Standard & Poor's 500, month by month from 1962 through 1991, divided by the consumer price index in order to account for inflation. (Sources: Datastream; Standard & Poor's)

HEADLINE: Weather Looking Good for Round-the-World Balloon Flight

BYLINE: By MALCOLM W. BROWNE

 BODY:
As helium gas screamed from a tank truck into a huge plastic gas bag yesterday, final preparations began for the launching of Earthwinds, a balloon whose three-member crew hopes to be the first to circumnavigate the world in a single balloon flight.
Larry Newman of Scottsdale, Ariz., captain of the Earthwinds, said there was a good chance that weather conditions in Akron, Ohio, would permit the launching of the balloon this weekend to begin its voyage of some 22,000 miles at an altitude of about 35,000 feet. 
 Although manned balloons have crossed both the Atlantic and Pacific oceans, none has successfully circled the earth, the last great goal of record-seeking aeronauts. Mr. Newman was a crew member of both the trans-Atlantic balloon flight in 1978 and the trans-Pacific flight in 1981.
With Mr. Newman on the Earthwinds flight will be Maj. Gen. Vladimir Dzhanibekov, chief of astronaut training in the Russian air force, and Don Moses, an American builder and expert mechanic. Besides the three men, the balloon, which is higher than the Statue of Liberty, will carry scientific experiments built in the former Soviet Union and by the National Aeronautics and Space Administration.
 
Two Balloons in One
 Earthwinds is radically different in design from any previous long-duration balloon. Shaped like an hourglass, it is actually two balloons -- an upper lifting balloon containing some 1.1 million cubic feet of helium, and an "anchor" balloon suspended beneath the crew capsule and containing compressed air.
Ordinary gas balloons carry ballast in the form of sand or water, and when a balloonist exhausts his supply of ballast, the balloon is forced to land. But Earthwinds makes its own ballast and will be able to stay aloft for more than a month.
The duration of conventional gas balloon flights is limited by the warming and cooling effects of daytime and nighttime flight. During the day, balloon gas warms, expands and provides so much lift that the balloon ascends to such heights that it must release some of its helium to prevent the gas from ripping open the balloon. But at night, the cooled helium contracts, and its reduced lift causes the balloon to descend too far, forcing the pilot to drop ballast. This cycle can continue for only a few days before ballast is exhausted and the balloon is forced to land.
But on Earthwinds, nearly one ton of extra air can be pumped from the atmosphere into the anchor balloon, thereby making the balloon system heavier and preventing it from rising during the warmth of the day.
In the cool of the night, compressed air can be released from the anchor balloon -- the equivalent of dropping sand ballast -- to prevent the balloon from descending. This unique system for replenishing ballast, its designers say, is expected to give the balloon a duration of at least 40 days, more than double the time expected to complete the round-the-world flight.
The pressurized crew capsule with its myriad of flight instruments, controls, compressor engines, generator, survival clothing, food and other equipment was moved last week from its stand in the Loral Defense Systems Air Dock to a flat-bed trailer from which it will be launched. The anchor balloon, resembling a 100-foot-diameter golf ball, was inflated and pressurized two weeks ago. Yesterday, the big plywood crate containing the folded plastic lift balloon was towed to the inflation site 100 yards from the hangar, and technicians began filling the balloon with helium from two tank trucks.
During the early stage of inflation, the height of the balloon is expected to greatly exceed the 200-foot height of the roof of the hangar, so inflation is being conducted outdoors. With a full load of helium, the balloon was expected to become round and low enough to squeeze back into the immense hangar for final rigging. In the last stage of preparations the lift balloon will be attached to the capsule and the anchor balloon, and this entire "flight train" will be moved to the nearby launching site.
 
A 'Different' Experience
 General Dzhanibekov said he gave the flight only a 50 percent chance of success but added that its potential scientific and technical rewards would make the risk worthwhile. "Also, the experience is different," he said. As a Soviet astronaut he made five space flights. In one he commanded the rescue operation that saved the Salyut 7 spacecraft.
"This flight will leave us broke and badly in debt," Mr. Newman said, "but it's something I've always dreamed of." Mr. Newman said much of the credit for the flight belonged to his partner, John Wilcox, who not only arranged for Soviet participation but obtained seed money for the venture from ESPN, the cable-television sports channel.
Besides ESPN and Mr. Newman himself, major sponsors include the Hilton Hotels Corporation, Virgin Atlantic Airways and the Canon Corporation.
Meteorologists say the balloon will probably travel northeast along the coast of the United States and cross Europe in the vicinity of Scandinavia. Beyond Europe, it may swing south over the Persian Gulf and then head northward over Russia and Siberia. Survival equipment aboard the balloon will include parachutes, flotation suits, Arctic clothing and a rifle.

HEADLINE: Dinkins Aides Fear Cuts In Construction Spending

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM

 BODY:
Mayor David N. Dinkins's latest proposals for cutting New York City's capital budget for construction took his own city agency heads by surprise when City Hall announced them on Monday, and yesterday many commissioners were still scrambling to figure out what the cuts would mean.
The cuts in capital spending are part of the Mayor's plan for closing at least $8 billion in gaps between projected city revenues and spending over the next four years, and were decided by senior city officials over the weekend, after they reached the limits of their willingess to cut other spending for city services. 
 Some other city officials and outside fiscal monitors expressed concern that the cutbacks in spending for construction risked damaging the city's aging infrastructure even further, while construction groups worried that they would hurt the economy. But the administration said that most of the cuts would not begin taking effect for 18 months, and that they had made special efforts to leave untouched vital areas like bridge reconstruction.
Struggling to fill a $1.8 billion gap in the $29 billion budget for the fiscal year that starts on July 1, Mr. Dinkins is to present his overall plan tomorrow. It calls for trimming thousands of jobs from the city work force, raising $115 million through new taxes on gasoline, automobiles and the income of commuters the first year, and freezing property-tax rates for four years.
 
Holding Payroll Costs Steady
 Officials said Mr. Dinkins would also propose, as he did last November, to grant no wage increases to municipal workers over the next two years, except in exchange for money-saving improvements in productivity, and to resort to layoffs only as a last resort, opting to shrink the work force by 20,000 jobs over the four years through attrition.
But Mr. Dinkins, who will have to run for re-election in large measure on the budget he is now proposing, has resisted deeper service cuts in other areas. Last night, in an apparent effort to demonstrate the Mayor's sensitivity, his office released a list of cuts he had refused to consider even as contingencies. Those included closing drop-in centers for the homeless, eliminating infant mortality programs, closing half the city's beaches and pools and eliminating all-night Staten Island Ferry service.
The $3.7 billion in proposed cuts would trim the capital budget to $8.5 billion over four years, about half what it was two years ago. But the effects are not yet clear. Officials at the School Construction Authority, speaking on condition of anonymity, said the cuts would "massacre" the five-year building plan, delaying the construction of a dozen new schools and the rehabilitation of scores of others, but the Parks Department said most of its cuts appeared scheduled to take effect several years from now. The Transportation Department would cut road reconstruction, but not resurfacing.
The capital budget is always the part of the city's spending plans most subject to change, because it stretches over such a long time, and even some projects included in it are never built.
But one agency head, who spoke on condition of anonymity, said, "If you take it out of there, it's out of there."
The capital budget is financed by the sale of long-term bonds, whose interest and principal must be repaid from operating revenues each year, and such payments have come to be one of the fastest-growing parts of the budget, projected to take one dollar of every five in tax revenues by 1996. Bond-rating agencies and fiscal monitors had strongly pressured the city to cut this expense, and the cuts would save $250 million a year in debt service by 1996.
But some questioned the wisdom of trimming a program that the city had painfully rebuilt after the fiscal crisis of the mid-70's, and that it has already trimmed by $2.9 billion in the last year.
"As in the 1970's, again there is the threat of further deterioration of the city's capital assets at a great cost to the future health of the local economy," State Comptroller Edward V. Regan said.

HEADLINE: Dow Corning's Failure In Public Opinion Test

BYLINE: By BARNABY J. FEDER

 BODY:
Regardless of whether Dow Corning Inc. ever convinces regulators that its silicone-gel breast implants are safe, the company seems likely to be branded as one of several that have bungled their handling of an accident or product defect, according to public relations and crisis management experts.
Dow Corning's handling of the growing controversy that led to the Food and Drug Administration on Jan. 6 to call for a moratorium on breast implants is increasingly being compared to the Exxon Corporation's response to the Exxon Valdez oil spill, the Johns Manville Corporation's treatment of its asbestos woes, and A. H. Robins's debacle with its Dalkon Shield birth control device. Critics say that Dow Corning's mistakes include a lack of public action by Lawrence A. Reed, the company's chief executive; too little sympathy and support for women who say they were harmed, and the failure to get the worst news out quickly. 
 "It's a textbook case of crisis mismanagement," said Gerald C. Meyers, a consultant on such matters who is based in Bloomfield Hills, Mich. "It looks like the lawyers are in charge, trying to limit their liability. But the damage is much worse to the corporation if they lose in the court of public opinion than if they lose in the court of law."
Dow Corning officials decline to speak about their crisis strategy. "We are busy trying to move this on a positive and constructive course," said Robert Grupp, a spokesman for the company, which is a joint venture of the Dow Chemical Company and Corning Inc. and is based in Midland, Mich.
 
A Question of Compassion
 Crisis management experts say that Dow Corning has courted trouble by being far too slow and low key in expressing compassion for women who say that they have suffered a variety of ailments from the implants, including immune-system diseases and painful hardening of the breasts. Critics also say that the implants obscure mammograms used to detect breast cancer. By the company's reckoning, women with ailments make up only a small percentage of the one million to two million who have had breast implants from Dow Corning and a handful of smaller competitors.
Consultants concede that because Dow Corning argues that there is little or no scientific evidence supporting many of the injury claims, it is difficult for the company to act sympathetically without appearing to undermine its legal strategy. And the entire implant industry's position has been, in essence, that the greatest social imperative is to avoid needlessly alarming the majority who are not having problems and other women who want implants.
But such a position, consultants say, ignores several lessons about how consumers respond to health threats. First, even a small number of people who feel misled or severely injured can rally enough relatives, friends and allies to give their claims great impact. Companies are expected to try to soothe anyone who feels victimized.
"It just a standard tenet of crisis situations involving health or safety that you have to strongly demonstrate concern, regardless of the numbers," said James E. Arnold, a Manhattan-based consultant who frequently deals in crisis management.
Second, the numbers involved in defective or dangerous product cases frequently turn out to be larger than the projections by the company involved. In some cases, like the cranberry cancer scare of 1959 and fears in the late 80's about the use of Alar on apples, the public needlessly panicked about health threats. But in Dow Corning's case, the company may have deceived itself by relying too heavily on the rosy assessments of plastic surgeons, who have a far bigger economic stake in implants than the company does. As with the Dalkon shield and asbestos, the publicity may now bring forth many more complaints, both valid and invalid.
The third lesson is that consumers become especially upset when they feel deceived. Information that has dribbled out over several years of litigation and regulatory hearings now suggests that Dow Corning misled regulators, physicians and consumers about the extent of uncertainty within the company itself about the safety of the implants.
"Most people didn't walk into this feeling they were meaningfully at risk," said Stephen A. Greyser, a public relations expert at the Harvard Business School, referring to those who received implants. "Most will now say that if they knew then what they know now, they wouldn't have done it." The result, consultants say, is that a public accustomed to being cynical about business ethics is now distrustful of Dow Corning, and that even people who would otherwise give the company the benefit of the doubt are now uncertain.
Even if a company is completely in the right, Mr. Meyers said, failure to voluntarily and dramatically accept losses and potential liability in favor of reassuring consumers is a mistake.
He cited the huge loss of market share that Gerber suffered for several years in the baby food business when it refused to remove any products from the market in 1986 after reports that glass shards were appearing in jars of baby food. It turned out, as Gerber insisted, that there were only isolated cases behind the reports and that the likely cause was mishandling by retailers or consumers.
Regardless of Dow Corning's position, most consultants say, the company's top managers are making a mistake by not taking a more public role. Mr. Reed has delegated basic responsibility for managing the controversy to J. Kermit Campbell, a former public relations executive who is group vice president in charge of the company's United States operations. Mr. Campbell, in turn, has transferred the task of speaking for the company to Robert T. Rylee, vice president in charge of health care; Dan M. Hayes, chief executive of Dow Corning Wright, the subsidiary that makes the breast implants, and the corporate public relations staff.
That division of labor reflects the minor role of medical products within the Dow Corning's overall business and the company's negligible profits from breast implants. Dow Corning produces nearly 5,000 products based on silicone, which is used in applications as diverse as caulking for construction, adhesives for labels, suds control in brewing, and Silly Putty in toys.
Mr. Reed's actions in particular call to mind the aloofness of Exxon's chief executive, Lawrence Rawl, during the early stages of the Valdez spill -- a marked contrast to the public leadership demonstrated by Johnson & Johnson's chairman, James Burke, following the discovery of poison-laced Tylenol capsules in 1983. Public relations executives say there is no doubt that Mr. Burke's approach did more for his company's reputation among customers and the larger public.
The breast-implant controversy is sending shock waves through Dow Corning, according to Barbara Carmichael, a senior public relations executive with the company. "This is a company that has never been particularly visible, much less questioned about its ethics," she said.
 
An Ethical Reputation
 Indeed, the picture painted by attorneys for women unhappy with their implants of a careless if not heartless company is not an image that squares with Dow Corning's reputation among corporate ethics consultants, nor does it reflect past actions that received little attention. In 1985, for example, Dow Corning reversed a decision to stop making silicone contact lens after learning that they were used to treat infant blindness.
"They were losing money hand over fist but they didn't realize they were the only ones making a lens that these babies could tolerate," said Dr. John Simon, a pediatric ophthamologist in Albany
Dr. Simon had scarcely begun a local publicity campaign to put pressure on the company when it agreed to continue making the product until it sold the business. "They were amazingly ethical once they understood," Dr. Simon said.
But Barbara Ley Toffler, an ethics expert at Resources for Responsible Management, a Boston consulting firm, said last week that Dow Corning's current plight underscored the way many ethics programs had become rote exercises that were naive about the true pressures in corporate life. The codes are often too vague to provide an easy road map through situations as complicated as the breast-implant controversy, she said.
Many of the most damaging ethical lapses occur when well-intentioned employees do something wrong that they believe is for the good of the company or most of its customers. That dynamic may still be unfolding at Dow Corning.
Last summer, the company set up a hot line for women with questions about implants. Early this month, the hot line was temporarily shut down after the F.D.A. complained that at least some of the 40 women answering the lines were misleadingly saying that the agency had determined that the products were safe. The operators were also playing down the potential hazards.
"I am willing to bet that part of what they were told to do by their trainers was to sound reassuring," said Ms. Toffler, the ethics expert. "In these kinds of situations, everyone goes into a panic mode that makes it harder to think clearly."

Reed, chief executive officer of Dow Corning, who is being criticized for being too aloof in handling the growing controversy over silicone breast implants. (Dow Corning Corporation)

HEADLINE: Consumer Confidence Fell in January

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
Americans became more pessimistic about their financial and job security this month despite lower interest rates aimed at helping the weak economy, a widely followed survey on consumer confidence said yesterday
The Conference Board's Consumer Confidence Index, which had leveled off in December after sharp declines the previous two months, fell again in January, largely on anxiety over future employment and business conditions. The index is at its lowest level since the 1981-82 recession. 
 The report from the business research group was considered especially significant by economists because it showed Americans were not responding to some of the most affordable borrowing costs in nearly two decades.
 
Focusing on 'Deterioration'
 "I think individuals are focusing more on the further deterioration of the job market and their own families' financial situation," said Gary Ciminero, chief economist at the Fleet/Norstar Financial Group in Providence, R.I.
 "Job prospects, job concerns, continue to mount. That's what's driving things," he said. "It should be patently obvious that interest rate cuts haven't worked."
The Federal Reserve Board chairman, Alan Greenspan, who orchestrated significant interest rate reductions last year to encourage more borrowing, has said these moves should be sufficient to get the economy growing again.
The Conference Board index is based on responses to questions sent to 5,000 households nationwide, on subjects ranging from home-buying plans to job availability. It is considered by many economists to be a useful barometer of the American consumer mood. Consumer spending accounts for about two-thirds of all economic activity.
The Conference Board said the January Consumer Confidence Index showed a reading of 50.4, a loss of 2.1 points from December. The index is calculated on a 1985 base of 100.
It has slid significantly from its 81.1 level last March, when consumer confidence reflected a euphoric mood after the gulf war.
In October, the index took an especially sharp dive, falling to 60.1 from 72.9 the month before. It slid to 52.7 in November before stabilizing somewhat in December.
Only 6.4 percent of all respondents in January considered business conditions good, while 51 percent said they were bad, the survey showed. Those figures were weaker than the preceding month.
Moreover, the survey showed an increasingly large number of households are experiencing hardship. Close to one in four reported that someone in their household was unemployed over the past 12 months. Of those who are back on the job, nearly half are making less money.
In expectations for the future, the survey said less than 19 percent believe conditions would be better in the next six months, while 20 percent feared they would worsen. Those figures are worse than a month earlier.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: Advertising;
Campbell Shapes Up All Around

BYLINE: By Stuart Elliott

 BODY:
THE Campbell Soup Company is ambitiously expanding its lines of pantry- and freezer-food products through one of the most comprehensive advertising programs being undertaken by a large American grocery marketer. The company, which spends more than $250 million annually on marketing, is lifting the budget by 8 percent, up from a 5 percent increase announced last year.
The expansion involves everything from Campbell's traditional red-and-white cans of condensed soup, which are being dressed up in new labels featuring photographs of the ingredients inside, to new lines of lower-fat, lower-calorie foods under brand names like Mrs. Paul's Healthy Treasures and Healthy Request. 
 All that product news is being conveyed to consumers in a barrage of television commercials, print advertisements and even miniature billboards attached to supermarket shopping carts.
"We have made a conscious decision to spend more," Herbert M. Baum, the new president of the Campbell North and South America division, said in a recent telephone interview, to prevent "our brand franchises from starting to slip away from us."
He added: "We know we're good at, and can make lots of money at, dry groceries and frozen foods. Where we think we're getting results, we'll get much more aggressive. If we don't think we're getting all out of our advertising that we should be, we'll put the money somewhere else."
His remarks alluded to previous expansion efforts, like the failed Fresh Chef line of refrigerated salads, that took Campbell far afield of its core businesses and were discontinued.
Campbell's decision to bolster marketing spending is significant beyond the fate of the company's new condensed broccoli cheese soup. Along with stepped-up spending by other packaged-goods giants, like the Procter & Gamble Company and Unilever P.L.C. of Britain, it could mean a rebirth for brand-name products, which have been hard-hit during the recession as consumers cut back or switch to store brands.
For marketers like those, "there is a need for growth," said Edward F. Ogiba, the president of Group EFO Ltd., a marketing consulting company in Weston, Conn.
In a survey conducted last year by Group EFO, he added, 61 percent of the companies responding said 30 percent or more of their sales over the next five years must come from new products, compared with 48 percent in 1990.
"Products like our condensed soups would do very well in an economy like this," Mr. Baum said.
As a result, ad dollars are being spent for the first time in many years on an underpromoted line of condensed soups in family-sized 26-ounce cans.
"When you reconstitute two of those, they will fill your bathtub," Mr. Baum said. Since August, sales of those larger cans are 64 percent higher than a year ago, he added.
Part of Campbell's increased spending is being financed by money that had been spent on promotions aimed at consumers, like cents-off coupons, and at grocery retailers, like bonuses to entice them to stock Campbell products.
Another aspect of Campbell's activities is changes in the company's roster of advertising agencies. Earlier this month, an estimated $25 million in frozen-food brands were moved to BBDO Worldwide from Ogilvy & Mather, which retains only Campbell's Pepperidge Farm line of baked goods. BBDO added brands like LeMenu frozen foods to a list that included products like Campbell's Chunky Soup and Mrs. Paul's frozen fish line.
Campbell, based in Camden, N.J., employs six other agencies, including Backer Spielvogel Bates, W. B. Doner & Company, FCB/Leber Katz Partners and Margeotes Fertitta & Weiss.
Campbell's panoply of marketing moves reflects the involvement of David W. Johnson, who became president and chief executive in 1990, determined to rejuvenate a marketer perceived to have fallen behind its grocery rivals.
"This is a company that has gone through a major cleaning-up and restructuring," said Nomi Ghez, a food-industry analyst at Goldman, Sachs & Company in New York, referring to steps Mr. Johnson took in the last two years. "Now is the time to focus on rebuilding its core businesses.
"That is especially true for a company that has such strong brands as Campbell," she added. "You have to make sure they grow and expand."

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
USX Reports a Loss in Both Steel and Oil

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: PITTSBURGH, Jan. 28

 BODY:
The USX Corporation today reported a $394 million fourth-quarter loss, partly because of the falling price of oil and restructuring costs in its steel division. And the Allegheny Ludlum Corporation, a specialty steelmaker, said its fourth-quarter earnings increased 53 percent because of less costly raw materials, cost controls and increased efficiency.
Following are the details of the companies' results:
 
USX
In contrast to the fourth-quarter loss, USX earned $267 million in the last three months of 1990. Sales fell 22 percent, to $4.87 billion in the latest fourth quarter, from $6.22 billion. 
 For all of 1991, the nation's largest steelmaker lost $578 million, compared with income of $818 million in 1990. Sales fell 9 percent, to $18.82 billion from $20.65 billion.
The company does not report earnings per share as a whole because its stock is split into separate classes for energy and steel.
The company's Marathon Oil group lost $229 million in the latest quarter, compared with earnings of $216 million, or 84 cents a share, in the period a year ago. Sales fell 22 percent, to $3.6 billion from $4.6 billion.
The fourth-quarter loss was caused largely by a $260 million accounting charge against income to lower the value of inventory in light of falling oil prices. In the period a year ago, the value of inventory was increased $140 million.
For all of 1991, Marathon lost $71 million on sales of $14 billion. Earnings for all of 1990 totaled $508 million, or $1.94 a share, on sales of $14.6 billion.
Marathon's 1991 results were affected by lower worldwide hydrocarbon prices and reduced refined product margins, USX's chairman, Charles A. Corry, said. Crude prices fell as concerns over Persian Gulf supplies abated.
The U.S. Steel Group, which also includes operations such as mining and real estate, lost $165 million in the fourth quarter, compared with earnings of $51 million, or 99 cents a share, in the period a year ago.
Sales fell 19 percent, to $1.3 billion from $1.6 billion.
"While some economists remain hopeful for 1992, the near-term outlook for the U.S. Steel Group is clouded by continuing weakness in the domestic economy and oversupply in world steel markets," Mr. Corry said.
On the New York Stock Exchange, USX-Steel Group shares slipped 25 cents, to $26.75, while USX-Marathon gained 12.5 cents, to $23.625.
 
Allegheny Ludlum
Allegheny Ludlum said earnings totaled $13 million, or 40 cents a share, in the three months ended Dec. 29, vs. earnings of $6 million, or 19 cents a share, in the period a year ago.
Sales fell 3 percent, to $231.86 million from $238.72 million.
The company said earnings fell 40 percent in 1991 because of the high cost of raw materials, depressed prices and increased Pennsylvania taxes.
The company earned $41.1 million, or $1.25 a share, in 1991, compared with $68.9 million, or $2.07 a share, in 1990. Sales fell 7 percent, to $1 billion, from $1.08 billion in 1990.
Allegheny Ludlum's shares added $1.375, to $32.125, in Big Board trading.

HEADLINE: Buyers of New Volkswagens Get Hedge Against Job Loss

BYLINE: By ADAM BRYANT, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DETROIT, Jan. 28

 BODY:
To ease the worries of skittish consumers and woo them to its showrooms, Volkswagen of America said today that it would cover car insurance and loan payments for up to a year for new Volkswagen buyers who lose their jobs because of the soured economy.
Volkswagen's coverage is unusually generous, analysts said. "This program seems to be a bit more extensive" than what other auto makers have done in the past, said L. Raymond Windecker, who runs American Autodatum, an industry research firm in Livonia, Mich. 
 The program, known as Payment Protection Plus, pays up to $500 a month for car insurance and lease or loan payments. The coverage lasts up to 12 months, and consumers may use the insurance any time during the first three years of ownership. They are not required to reimburse Volkswagen.
 
March 31 Deadline
 It will be offered to consumers who buy or lease a new Volkswagen before March 31. Buyers of all new Volkswagens are automatically covered; no separate application or screening process is required, the company said. The protection is also available on loans and leases arranged through lending institutions other than VW Credit, Volkswagen's financing arm.
For their payments to be covered by the auto maker, Volkswagen owners must provide a letter from their employers attesting that economic conditions, not performance, resulted in the job loss.
Volkswagen is hoping the program will also help increase its flagging sales, which dropped by 29.1 percent in 1991. But Mr. Windecker, a former researcher and analyst for the Ford Motor Company, said its effect would be marginal. The program may draw people who are already shopping for a car, but is not likely to bring consumers off the fence, he said.
"Very, very few people that are worried about losing their jobs would even consider buying a car," Mr. Windecker said. "If current interest rates won't jump-start the situation, this won't either."
 
Small Risk Is Seen
 Volkswagen officials declined to predict how many buyers would need coverage, but Mr. Windecker said their ranks would be small. The program therefore represents a small risk for Volkswagen, he added.
Auto makers have offered similar coverage in the past, and many car dealers sell insurance to pay for car loans should job loss occur.
The Chrysler Corporation offered a similar program in the early 1980's, but only in the economically depressed Southwest and around the Rochester area. By contrast, Volkswagen's program will be offered by all the company's American dealers.
Such programs are not unique to the automobile industry. In New Jersey, several housing developers offer similar protection for buyers worried about making mortgage payments should they lose their jobs.

HEADLINE: Compaq Earnings Off 51%; Sun Microsystems Up 21%

BYLINE: By THOMAS C. HAYES

 BODY:
The Compaq Computer Corporation said yesterday that its net income in the fourth quarter was about half what it was in the 1990 period. But sales and earnings rose modestly from depressed levels in the second and third quarters of 1991, exceeding Wall Street estimates.
Sun Microsystems Inc., the leading supplier of computer work stations, continued to grow through the recession, chalking up 21 percent gains in net income and revenues for its second fiscal quarter. 
 Analysts said Compaq had begun to repair damage inflicted last year by lower-priced rivals and the slumping market for personal computers. Still, the company's stock lost 7.3 percent of its value yesterday, reflecting investor worries about slow 1992 sales and rising costs when Compaq introduces several computers in coming months.
Compaq reported net income of $66.6 million, or 77 cents a share, down 51 percent from $134.8 million, or $1.50 a share, in the quarter last year. Most analysts had projected earnings between 45 cents and 60 cents a share for the recent quarter. Sales fell 13 percent, to $873.4 million from $1 billion.
Eckhard Pfeiffer, president and chief executive, said increases in industry unit sales both in the United States and abroad would be less than 10 percent this year, with prices continuing to decline.
"We are not seeing any significant market pickup via the economy or PC industry," he said in an interview from Compaq's Houston headquarters. "We are prepared for a year that is probably going to be difficult."
Compaq's share price fell $2.375, to $32.75, in trading yesterday on the New York Stock Exchange.
The company lost $70.3 million in the third quarter after taking a $135 million charge related to 1,400 layoffs and other cost-cutting steps.
For the year, Compaq recorded its first decline in sales and profits since the company shipped its first products in 1983.
Net income fell 71.2 percent, to $130.9 million, or $1.49 a share, from $454.9 million, or $5.14 a share. Sales declined 9.2 percent, to $3.27 billion from $3.6 billion.
 
Sun Microsystems
Sun Microsystems, which is based in Mountain View, Calif., reported net income for the quarter, which ended Dec. 27, of $47.4 million, or 47 cents a share, compared with $39.1 million, or 39 cents a share, for the corresponding period a year earlier. Revenues rose to $909.1 million, from $753.5 million a year earlier.
Sun said that it shipped a record 49,000 computers in the quarter and that it had seen good acceptance for its new family of servers, which are powerful computers that provide central data storage for a group of less powerful work stations.
Sun's stock, traded over the counter, closed at $33.875, up 37.5 cents. The earnings were announced after the close of trading. Robert G. Herwick, an analyst with Hambrecht & Quist in San Francisco, said earnings were about in line with Wall Street expectations, but that revenues were a bit higher and profit margins a little lower than expected.

HEADLINE: Southeast Asia Nations Sign Free-Trade Accord

BYLINE: By PHILIP SHENON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SINGAPORE, Jan. 28

 BODY:
Leaders of the Association of Southeast Asian Nations agreed today to take their first step toward creating a common market, a response to economic alliances being formed in Europe and North America.
Under the agreement, the six-nation association will attempt to integrate its economies, which are some of the fastest growing in the world, by reducing or eliminating tariffs on nonagricultural goods within 15 years. 
 Though it has been criticized for its drawn-out timetable and for loopholes that will permit each government to exempt hundreds of products from tariff cuts, the agreement was nevertheless hailed by leaders of the association's member nations -- Brunei, Indonesia, Malaysia, the Philippines, Singapore and Thailand.
"It is a new chapter for Asean and a new era for Southeast Asia," Prime Minister Anand Panyarachun of Thailand said.
 
Loopholes Are Acknowledged
 Singapore's Trade Minister, Lee Hsien Loong, acknowledged loopholes in the free-trade agreement but said that the pact represented "a considerable change of mood" within the association, which in the past has moved slowly on proposals to expand regional economic cooperation.
"The sentiment now is, we would like to move faster," he said.
The creation of the so-called Asean Free Trade Area is the association's response to the integration of the economies of Western Europe and to the North American free-trade agreement covering the United States, Canada and Mexico.
At this week's meeting, leaders of some of the six nations suggested that Southeast Asia could be shut out of the largely self-sufficient mega-markets being created elsewhere. "Unless Asean can match the other regions in attractiveness both as a base for investments and as a market for their products, investments by multinational companies are likely to flow away from our part of the world," Prime Minister Goh Chok Tong of Singapore said.
But the free-trade agreement, he said, showed that the association was a "strong player in the new world order."
The agreement is not expected to have a significant effect on American exports, since many American-made products sold in the region, such as jetliners and electronics-manufacturing equipment, cannot be replaced by goods made here.
 
Scheduled for Cuts
 Under the accord, 15 large groups of products made in the region will immediately be selected for tariff cuts, including textiles, electronics, chemicals, plastics, pharmaceuticals, ceramics, wooden furniture and jewelry.
The association's six members pledged today to reduce tariffs on the goods covered by the agreement to no more than 20 percent within five years, and to no more than 5 percent by the year 2008. The accord takes effect on Jan. 1, 1993.
The goal of the agreement is to expand trade among the six, mostly booming nations, and to attract new foreign investment to the region by offering investors a single, simplified and increasingly affluent market of nearly 330 million people.
Joe Rogers, a former United Nations ambassador to the Asian Development Bank, said in a telephone interview from Washington that the free-trade accord could be an important enticement to foreign investors.
"It would open a region market," said Mr. Rogers, who is now a private consultant on Asian business. "For example, you'd be able to go and manufacture in Malaysia and still be able to ship your product to Thailand. That's a great benefit."
 
Plan Nearly Vetoed
 The free-trade area was originally proposed last year by Prime Minister Anand of Thailand, who suggested a 10-year timetable for tariff reductions in the region. Thailand has some of the highest tariff rates in Southeast Asia.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Mixed in Wary Day; Gold Recovers in New York

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar settled mixed on world currency markets yesterday in seesaw trading ahead of President Bush's State of the Union Message.
Gold prices rose in domestic trading after posting losses in Europe. On the New York Commodity Exchange, bullion for current delivery settled at $355.80 a troy ounce, up $1 from Monday. The Republic National Bank said gold gained 85 cents an ounce, to a late bid price of $355.65. 
 Analysts said currency trading was characterized by a wait-and-see attitude ahead of the President's address last night.
"The market is a little scared; there is some trepidation ahead of the speech," said Randolph Donney, research director at the Pegasus Econometric Group in Hoboken, N.J. "But there is no real strong trend."
 
Report Leads to Selloff
 A Conference Board report released early in the day showing that consumer confidence declined in January caused a dollar selloff. With the economy in recession, the Federal Reserve is more likely to cut interest rates to stimulate growth, a move that is bearish for the dollar because it reduces the yield on fixed-income investments.
The confidence report caused the dollar "to come off a bit," said John McCarthy, chief dealer at ABN-Amro Bank N.V., noting that traders "overshot it on the downside." The currency recovered from its lows but still closed off on the day.
The dollar, some analysts say, remains poised for further declines. "The market will perceive it as an opportunity to sell if what Bush says is less than spectacular," Mr. McCarthy said.
In Tokyo, the dollar rose eight-tenths of 1 yen, closing at 125.37 yen. Later in London, it was quoted at 125.65 yen. In New York, the dollar settled at 125.70 yen, up from 125.23 yen on Monday.
In London, the British pound rose to $1.7950, from $1.7770 late Monday. In New York, it cost $1.7925, more than Monday's $1.7825.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Monday's rates, included: 1.5994 German marks, down from 1.6105; 1.4225 Swiss francs, down from 1.4300; 5.4495 French francs, down from 5.4840; 1,201.50 Italian lire, down from 1,209.25, and 1.1745 Canadian dollars, up from 1,1733.

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
Breathable Air For Swap or Sale

BYLINE: By Peter Passell

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES

 BODY:
HOW in the world is the city where nobody walks going to cut air pollutants by 85 percent? Not easily or cheaply: A ballpark estimate by the David Harrison and Albert Nichols of National Economic Research Associates in Cambridge puts the cost of meeting Federal standards at an astonishing $1,200 a year for every resident of the Los Angeles sprawl.
But many economists are convinced that the bill could be reduced sharply if the job of deciding who cuts emissions, and how, were left to markets rather than regulators. And they are on the verge of convincing the California's South Coast Air Quality Management District to give it a try. 
 Thanks to tough controls on cars and big industries, the air of Los Angeles is far cleaner than it was in the 1970's. But it is not nearly clean enough to meet Federal requirements. The region is now under orders to pare emissions by 5 percent annually until the air is pure enough to spare the lungs of the most sensitive residents.
James Lents, executive director of the South Coast District, has apparently never been gung-ho over market-based solutions to environmental problems. In 1988 he wrote to the Environmental Protection Agency that the "disadvantage of an emissions trading program far outweighs the possible advantages." A year later the district proposed to meet the clean-air requirement by turning the regulatory screws ever tighter, effectively barring the use of everything from backyard barbecues to aerosol deodorants.
But hard times have a way of changing minds. California business, laid low by recession, is now desperate for an alternative to regulation-as-usual. And local officials, fearing they will be crushed between legal mandates and economic realities, have every incentive to cooperate: "They're up the creek without a paddle," concludes Robert Hahn, an economist at the American Enterprise Institute in Washington.
Enter marketable pollution permits. A 1990 study by Mr. Harrison and Mr. Nichols for the California Council for Environmental and Economic Balance, an industry-labor group, estimated that smog-control costs could be cut by a quarter or more by creating a free market in pollution rights. The two economists envision a system much like the one soon to be used nationally to control acid emissions from power plants. The right to dump smog-producing gases would be auctioned off by government, or simply assigned to existing polluters by some historical basis. Thereafter, the permits, denominated in tons or pounds, would be cut back proportionately each year to hit increasingly ambitious antipollution targets.
Any polluter who could not meet the tightening limits, or anyone wanting to operate a new source of pollution would have to buy rights from another polluter. A computerized market, much like the over-the-counter market for stocks and bonds, might facilitate exchanges.
Fans of marketable permits think it beats conventional "command and control" regulation in a number of ways. It would give polluters flexibility to find least-cost controls: A dry cleaner, for example, whose own control costs might be $30,000 a ton, could buy pollution rights from a nearby bakery whose control costs are just $2,000 a ton. And it would also give polluters incentives to clean up even if they were already doing a good job on the antipollution front: Unused rights could always be sold for cash.
For maximum savings, experts say, it would make sense to encourage trading between any two consenting polluters -- say, oil refineries and truckers. To keep enforcement simple, however, South Coast regulators are likely to limit trades to stationary sources, while perhaps giving businesses limited permission to buy rights from mobile polluters, paying to junk older, dirtier cars.
Some form of emissions trading is almost certainly in the cards for Los Angeles in 1993. But supporters are not cheering yet: Victor Weisser, the president of the industry-labor group, worries the regulators will add "bells and whistles" that make trading too cumbersome to be practical for all but the largest transactions.
Jack Broadbent, an economist at the South Coast District, hastens to reassure business that the regulators "recognize their concerns." But he concedes that the staff is leaning toward a system in which trades will have to be pre-approved, rather than spot-checked after the fact. For example, emissions-control equipment would have to be inspected before credits were granted.
For Southern Californians, the stakes are clear. Without an affordable solution to the smog problem, the region's economy may never regain its competitive edge. But the rest of the country has a stake here, too: If this high-profile experiment fails to achieve major savings, it will be hard to convince other regulators to take a chance on market-based cleanup strategies.

HEADLINE: Books of The Times;
The Masters of War and the Monster They Made

BYLINE: By HERBERT MITGANG

 BODY:

The Death Lobby
How the West Armed Iraq
By Kenneth R. Timmerman
Illustrated. 443 pages. Houghton Mifflin. $21.95.
Among the growing number of books about the Persian Gulf war and its aftermath, "The Death Lobby," by Kenneth R. Timmerman, is the most shocking. It's about that familiar enemy of peace, the international arms merchant, a subject that has been around in fact, fiction and drama at least since World War I. This strongly documented book is essential reading for anyone interested in understanding how the most sophisticated shooting irons were sold to Iraq and what the shooting was all about. 
 Like the managed television pictures and military briefings, the first round of instant books on the gulf war emphasized the generalship and the high-tech blast of war but without much political or economic savvy. In light of subsequent information, some of these yellow-ribbon books already seem dated and naive. Their version of victory calls to mind Antony's words in "Julius Caesar," "Are all thy conquests, triumphs, spoils/ Shrunk to this little measure?"
Now a second round of gulf war books is coming out that is more critical of American intelligence, ground and air tactics, "friendly fire" casualties and the precise number of civilian casualties, including Kurdish deaths caused by Saddam Hussein's arms after Iraq was supposedly defeated. One reason for the harsher tone is that the Pentagon's censored battlefield information is slowly dribbling out. A third round of books is expected in a year or so, led by the memoirs of Gen. Norman H. Schwarzkopf. If the Allied commander tells all or nearly all -- especially about the decision to halt the war before conquering Baghdad and toppling the dictator -- it could be a worthwhile book. So could the forthcoming memoirs of Peter Arnett, CNN's daring wartime Baghdad correspondent.
For an understanding of the roots of the gulf war and the enemy's powerful arsenal, "The Death Lobby" is in a class by itself. Mr. Timmerman, a journalist who publishes Mednews, a newsletter about Middle East security and defense issues, is the author of several books, including "Fanning the Flames," about the Iran-Iraq war. He knows the territory.
The book's subtitle, "How the West Armed Iraq," sums up its theme. The Western Governments, including that of the United States, winked at the arms bazaar that directly fortified and indirectly encouraged Iraq's dictator to invade Kuwait and let slip the dogs of war.
It was surely not the author's intention, but by shifting much of the responsibility for the war to the West's arms merchants, Saddam Hussein is partly let off the hook for his military aggression. Still, there's enough blame to go around on all sides in "The Death Lobby."
In non-democratic countries, authors would be imprisoned for writing this bluntly, if what they wrote was printed at all:
"But the fight against Saddam Hussein had little to do with the liberation of Kuwait, no matter how much President Bush and his advisers tried to focus publicly on that goal. It wasn't about jobs, as Secretary of State James Baker once argued, or even about oil. Simply put, the United States went to war to smash the death machine that this country and its Western allies had helped Saddam assemble in the first place. The United States and its allies had no choice but to combat Saddam Hussein on the battlefield because of the greed of Western businesses, misguided analyses by the foreign policy Establishment, and the incompetence of regulatory officials."
Mr. Timmerman mocks the statement made by Assistant Secretary of State John Kelly to Congress, four months before the invasion of Kuwait, that Mr. Hussein was a "force for moderation." He writes that a more accurate statement could be found in the corporate balance sheets because they would show that Iraq was a great market for the most advanced weaponry produced by companies in France, Germany, Italy, Britain, Austria and the United States.
"Saddam Hussein was our creation, our monster. We built him up and then tried to take him down," Mr. Timmerman says. ". . . The arming of Iraq was a 15-year love affair. And it was a world-class enterprise."
Documentation for these devastating accusations can be found in a detailed appendix that lists arms sales to Iraq between 1970 and 1990. The author notes that this information was compiled from public sources and material from his own publication. The appendix includes the Soviet Union as one of the arms merchants, a fact that for the sake of accuracy should have been included in the book's subtitle.
Among the big-ticket military items listed by the author are billions for French Mirage fighter planes and artillery; missile systems from West Germany; communications and surveillance systems from Britain; howitzers and tanks from Austria; Scuds, tanks, artillery and missile systems from the Soviet Union; naval vessels from Italy; howitzers from South Africa; tanks from China; Scuds and missiles from Brazil; artillery from Egypt. Listings from the United States include Hughes and Bell helicopters.
Mr. Timmerman points out that other nonmilitary items -- some disguised as instruments for ordinary business purposes were sold to Iraq for Mr. Hussein's effort to achieve a nuclear capability. Western suppliers provided dual-use technology. For example, "Marubeni of Japan had supplied Saddam with hundreds of buried fuel storage tanks that escaped Allied bombardment," and a "Franco-Italian petrochemicals consortium had built an entire underground diesel fuel plant."
The author writes that a compilation of public sources shows that 445 companies cashed in on this macabre bonanza, one-third of them from Germany alone. Some of the facts in "The Death Lobby" have been brought out before Congressional committees attempting to control export licenses and have been reported in serious newspapers; the great value of the book is that all this information now appears in one place, with no punches pulled. Mr. Timmerman concludes: "Until concrete steps are taken to curb this type of trade, the 'new world order' President Bush is so keen on heralding will turn out to be more death as usual."

HEADLINE: GOLF;
For the Public, Pebble Beach May Become a Missing Links

BYLINE: By JAIME DIAZ, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PEBBLE BEACH, Calif., Jan. 29

 BODY:
To a golfer walking its cliffside fairways this week at the A.T.&T. Pebble Beach National Pro-Am, the famous course on Monterey Bay has never looked better. Indeed, for the United States Open in June, Pebble Beach is being meticulously restored to accent its rugged, linkscharacter.
But to financial analysts charting the bottom line, Pebble Beach has some serious problems. 
 Since being sold in 1990 -- along with sister courses Spyglass Hill, Spanish Bay and Old Del Monte -- to Minoru Isutani of Japan for an astounding price of $840 million, its owner has been scrambling to install a plan that will pay for the purchase.
As a result, the precise future of one of America's greatest courses is uncertain. Although United States Golf Association officials said this week that Pebble Beach's financial situation would have no bearing on it holding the Open, the larger question of who will be able to play the course in the future is very much in the air.
 
Nicklaus's Choice
 Simply, no one knows if Pebble Beach will ultimately remain -- as it has been since being opened in 1919 -- completely open to the public.
The very possibility that Pebble Beach, along with St. Andrews in Scotland the most renowned public course in the world, could become for all practical purposes inaccessible to the everyday golfer strikes an emotional chord in the national sporting psyche.
When Jack Nicklaus said, "If I had one last round to play, I'd choose to play it at Pebble Beach," he was speaking for countless golfers who would have gladly paid the $200 green fees to have the same choice.
The man who has attempted to change the public character of Pebble Beach is Isutani, a 51-year-old businessman whose holdings include Cosmo World, a computer software manufacturer, and Olympia Staff, a sporting goods company.
Isutani, who reportedly owns 12 golf courses outside of the United States, bought the 530-acre Pebble Beach property from Marvin Davis, who had purchased the resort for $12 million in 1977. In paying the highest price in history for a golf course, Isutani recently admitted he probably paid too much.
"I bought the property with 50 percent of my business mind and 50 percent of my golf mind because that was my dream," Isutani told The San Francisco Examiner in December in his only interview with an American publication. "My dream is knowing that everybody thinks that Pebble Beach is the best course in the world."
At the time of the purchase, a Pebble Beach spokesman said Isutani's representatives also discussed plans for building 1,000 condominium units and a fifth golf course on the property.
 
Membership Plan
 Isutani's plan was to repay the loans he needed to make the landmark purchase by selling private memberships. Six months after the sale, Japanese newspapers reported that Isutani had plans to place 760 private memberships on sale for the incredible amount of $740,000 each.
Although extremely expensive by American standards, the expectation was that Japanese businessmen, who buy and sell golf club memberships in the same way that commodities are sold on the stock market, would rush to join what Isutani proposed calling the Pebble Beach National Club.
But the effects of the recession in Japan knocked the bottom out of the golf membership market, and the estimated selling price for the memberships dropped all the way to $150,000 apiece. It meant that Isutani would have to sell many more memberships than he originally planned to repay his loans.
To accommodate the proposed membership structure, Isutani's representatives put forth a plan in which two hours of midmorning starting times at Pebble Beach each day would be reserved for members. Each day's last hour of starting times would be allocated to the public, with the remaining several hours of starting times for resort guests.
Even though non-resort guests at Pebble Beach currently have no guaranteed starting times on the course, opponents of Isutani's plan said it would have cut public, non-resort guest play from 27 percent to 7 percent of total play.
Once the plan was circulated in the press, it soon became clear that Isutani, besides overestimating what his new acquisition was worth, had also underestimated how strong public opposition would be.
Many opponents in the Monterey area feared that if Isutani had his way, the 17 Mile Drive would be eventually become a condominium-filled resort like others under Japanese ownership in Hawaii.
Although the plan was approved by Monterey County supervisors last July, it was rejected by the California Coastal Commission, which ruled that the proposed private memberships would limit public access and possibly set bad precedent for future land uses along the coast.
That decision left Isutani unable to repay his loans through revenues generated by the resort. With more than $400 million in loans due in September, Isutani received a one-year extension from Mitsubishi Trust & Banking, a Japanese bank.
With the heat reduced slightly, Isutani decided to withdraw his original proposal three weeks ago. His representatives said today that no decision has been made on whether there would be a new plan proposing private membership or when it might be submitted.
For the moment, it is anybody's guess what Isutani's next move will be. He may choose to pay his loans for Pebble Beach by selling off some of his other assets. Isutani said in his interview that he has no intention of selling the property.
"I bought the property not knowing how strongly the local community would object," he said in December. "We must leave significant access to the public. It is very important to keep in constant contact with the local community."

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 6; National Desk
