HEADLINE: STATE OF THE UNION;
BUSH VOWS ECONOMIC RELIEF AND PROPOSES MODEST STEPS IN STATE OF THE UNION TALK

BYLINE: By ANDREW ROSENTHAL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 28

 BODY:
Staking his political fortunes on advice that the economy will right itself by summer, President Bush delivered a State of the Union Message tonight that offered modest proposals to ease the immediate pain of middle-class taxpayers and erase any impression that he is insensitive to America's economic burdens.
Mr. Bush used his own powers to give some quick relief to taxpayers and inject about $20 billion into the economy in the next six months. He directed that tax-withholding rules be changed immediately, meaning that some money normally given back later in refunds will be left in paychecks so it can be spent quickly. The change is expected to inject some $2 billion a month into the economy when fully effective. Mr. Bush also ordered Federal spending on some programs accelerated.
 
Rewarding the Taxpayer
 
 Challenging Congress to act within eight weeks and promising a "battle" if it does not, Mr. Bush proposed short-term relief measures like an increase in tax exemptions for children, tax breaks for first-time home buyers and the housing industry and tax credits for the purchase of health insurance by those earning low wages. [A transcript of the President's address is on page A16.]
In a televised address that at nearly an hour was longer than most State of the Union messages, the President declared that it was time to reward the American taxpayer for decades of paying for cold war arms programs. He proposed $50 billion worth of Pentagon cuts over the next five years and outlined new arms-control measures, including a proposal that the United States and the republics of the former Soviet Union eliminate all multiple-warhead nuclear missiles based on land. Mr. Bush did not make clear whether the money saved from the Pentagon would be plowed into domestic programs.
 
Trying to Regain Stature
 But the bulk of Mr. Bush's third State of the Union Message was dedicated to stopping the President's slide in the public opinion polls and strengthening him for the re-election campaign. Mr. Bush's speech was immediately criticized by Democrats, some of whom said it was too little and too late.
Earlier today, to sidestep a bitter election-year issue, Mr. Bush and House leaders agreed to spend $2.7 billion on another 13 weeks of unemployment benefits for about two million of the long-term unemployed. [Page A18.]
With his popularity at the lowest ebb of his three years in office, Mr. Bush sought to recapture the stature he enjoyed just one year ago as Commander in Chief in the war against Iraq and as the leader of the free world during the collapse of Communism. He even borrowed his own Persian Gulf war oratory in an effort to persuade voters that he could bring the leadership he demonstrated in battle to bear on the economy.
"We are going to lift this nation out of hard times inch by inch and day by day, and those who would stop us had better step aside," Mr. Bush declared in a line that drew loud applause in the packed chamber of the House of Representatives. "Because I look at hard times, and I make this vow: This will not stand." The pledge echoed his promise that Iraq's invasion of Kuwait would not stand.
The Speaker of the House, Thomas S. Foley, politely applauded Mr. Bush as the President spoke to the joint session of Congress. But afterward, he criticized the President's proposals as too little, too late. "For too long, we were told to wait, that things would get better on their own," he said in the Democrats' official response. "There was even an effort to talk us out of the recession, or to tell us that it wasn't really happening at all."
After hearing the address, some of the Democratic Presidential candidates said they were disappointed with the President's economic plans.
Senator Tom Harkin of Iowa, who was campaigning at a school in Franklin, N.H., offered this: "All I can say after watching his speech is the State of the Union is coming from someone who is totally out of touch with what is happening in America." Incongruously for a speech that had been billed as his moment to define not only his campaign agenda but the second term he hopes to win in November, Mr. Bush began his remarks in the chamber of the House of Representatives tonight with two jokes.
 
Poking Fun at Himself
 "You know, with the big buildup this speech has had, I wanted to make sure it would be a big hit, but I couldn't convince Barbara to deliver it for me," he said, as the chamber erupted into laughter and applause.
He smiled again and said: "I see the Speaker and the Vice President are laughing. They saw what I did in Japan, and they're just happy they're sitting behind me."
The politicians in the House chamber interrupted Mr. Bush's speech dozens of times for applause. Sometimes the applause came from both sides of the aisle, as it did for politically popular themes like Head Start and the war in the Persian Gulf. At other times, the applause was Republican only, as when Mr. Bush spoke of cutting the capital gains tax on profits from investments.
Mr. Bush tried to paint himself as Presidential and Congress as partisan. Striking a compromise between aides who urged him to be conciliatory and those who wanted him to start battling the Democrats right away, Mr. Bush challenged lawmakers to pass his proposals by March 20.
"From the day after that, if it must be: the battle is joined," Mr. Bush said. "And you know, when principle is at stake, I relish a good fair fight."
 
Says He Shunned 'Gimmicks'
 Mr. Bush portrayed his plan as a fiscally and politically responsible effort to speed the recession's end, although it came 18 months after the recession began and one year to the day after he promised America that it would soon end, and he asserted that he had resisted the temptation for "new and dazzling" proposals or "gimmicks."
But the President spoke against the backdrop of an extraordinary buildup for the annual speech, which the President has promoted in virtually every public utterance for the past six weeks. Concerned that the advance billing was too ambitious, and still caught up in disputes that kept a major proposal on health-care reform from being included tonight, the White House tried to lower expectations at the last minute.
Heeding the advice of moderate advisers who cautioned against too sharp a break with the past, Mr. Bush made no radical departure from the economic policies he has pursued for three years, nor did he remake the political philosophies that he inherited from Ronald Reagan into his own images.
Details of the President's programs will become clearer on Wednesday when he submits his budget to Congress. Then the White House and Congress begin the complicated process of negotiations and tradeoffs in incorporating the dozens of pieces of the package into legislation.
While economists suggested that Mr. Bush's short-term proposals could help the economy, the President offered no detailed vision of the future of the country tonight. Many of the proposals he outlined were programs that he offered in the past and that Congress rejected.
Among these was a call for a reduction in the rate of taxes on capital gains, which Mr. Bush has made the centerpiece of his economic policy, to as low as 15.4 percent on the profits from the sale of assets held more than three years. The current capital gains tax rate is 28 percent.
"This time, at this hour, I cannot take 'No' for an answer," he said.
Outlining his economic programs, many of which had found their way into news reports in recent weeks, Mr. Bush said he was issuing executive orders that would impose a 90-day moratorium on all new Federal regulations that could hinder growth, and he pledged to change banking regulations to stimulate lending.
To pump money into the economy, he said, the Federal Government will accelerate spending on existing programs, for a total of $10 billion in the next six months. He also proposed a budget "freeze" that would keep spending on discretionary programs in the budget for the 1993 fiscal year at the same level as the current budget. That proposal would produce less than $5 billion in savings.
 
Money Back Sooner
 Mr. Bush ordered the Treasury Department to revise the method by which taxes are withheld from paychecks. This would have no effect on Americans' tax burdens, but would simply give them back their own money sooner. Income taxes are now withheld at a rate higher than the actual tax rate in order to provide extra money to the Federal coffers during the year, and many taxpayers receive refunds.
Mr. Bush also proposed an increase of $500 in the personal tax exemption for each child, tax credits and penalty-free withdrawals from individual retirement accounts for first-time home buyers, tax breaks for the real estate industry, tax credits and deductions for medical insurance.
Declaring that "by the grace of God, American won the cold war," Mr. Bush played to the strongly inward-looking trends that are evident in polls of American voters in this election year.
He said: "For half a century now, the American people have shouldered the burden and paid taxes than were higher than they would have been to support a defense that was bigger than it would have been if imperial Communism had never existed. But it did. Doesn't anymore."
Mr. Bush announced that the enormously expensive B-2 Stealth bomber program would be stopped after the 20 planes already being purchased.
 
Towarde Fewer Warheads
 Mr. Bush also said he was canceling production of the Midgetman nuclear missile and would halt manufacture of new warheads for sea-based nuclear missiles. No more multiple-warhead MX missiles will be built, and the military will not buy any more advanced cruise missiles, Mr. Bush said.
He said that on Saturday, when he meets with President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia, he would propose that if Russia eliminates all land-based missiles with more than one warhead, he will eliminate the multiple-warhead MX missile and reduce the number of warheads on the Minuteman missile to one from three.
Often criticized for seeming deadpan in the face of astonishing events, Mr. Bush said, "Even as as President, with the most fascinating possible vantage point, there were times when I was so busy helping to manage progress and lead change that I didn't always show the joy that was in my heart."

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 4; National Desk

LENGTH: 928 words

HEADLINE: STATE OF THE UNION: Looking to Short Term;
Economists Say Bush Offers Gifts for Many But Probably Won't Deliver Lasting Growth

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 28

 BODY:
 President Bush proposed something for everyone in his State of the Union Message tonight except perhaps for economists. They said his proposals might do more to lift his popularity in an election year than to lift the nation out of recession and promote economic growth after that.
The Federal Reserve's recent interest rate cuts will probably do more to spur the economy over the next six to nine months than anything the President or Congress could do, many economists said. They said the President's tax-cut proposals, if they became law, would further fuel the recovery by giving consumers more money to spend and by offering businesses new incentives for investment. 
 But as for strengthening the economy over the longer term -- which economists consider far more fundamental than whether $20 billion or $50 billion is pumped in the short term -- many experts saw the President as proposing little new to help.
Administration officials say the President's proposals to accelerate depreciation and cut the capital gains tax should spur corporate investment in new plants and equipment. They say his proposals to make a tax credit for research and development permanent would help keep America in the forefront of innovation. Other tax measures, they say, would boost consumer confidence and homebuilding.
 
No Bold New Vision
 But many economists, worrying more than ever before about the nation's declining competitiveness, had been hoping that the President would lay out a bold new vision. Instead, they complained, he offered a rehash of warmed-over ideas, several of which have already been rejected by Congress.
These critics say Washington should be doing far more to train unskilled workers and rebuild cities if it is to increase competitiveness over the long haul. They would also like the Government to push companies to think more about long-term growth rather than quarterly profits and to step up efforts to unsnarl airports and roads that cut productivity.
Some economists are also concerned that adopting the President's tax-cut proposals would increase the mushrooming budget deficit, although the Administration insists that this is not the case. In addition, many economists say his proposals offer no startling new solutions to help America where it is faltering, in education and public investment, for example.
"It's a real Christmas tree for everybody," said Bernard L. Weinstein, director of the center for economic development and research at the University of North Texas. "It's the traditional short-term approach to goosing the economy, designed to garner votes. It doesn't address the long-term structural, institutional problems that are key to productivity, trade and unemployment."
For the affluent, the President's proposals would not only lower the capital gains tax but eliminate the luxury tax on yachts and private airplanes, a step the Administration classifies as a stimulus to create jobs in the ship-building and aviation industries.
For the middle class, the President called for raising the personal exemption by $500 a child. He also proposed a $5,000 tax credit for first-time home buyers that would be phased out after a year. And to encourage Americans to go to college, he called for deducting interest on student loans.
 
Some Enthusiasm
 For the less well-off, he offered a tax credit of up to $3,750 to make medical insurance more affordable.
And for everyone, he proposed reducing the amount the Government withholds from paychecks so that taxpayers would receive this year much of the tax refund they are due to receive in 1993.
"The tax credit for first-time home purchases, if it's temporary, is not a bad idea to spur growth, but it doesn't speak to any clear vision of wanting to do anything systematic for the economy," said Robert Solow, an economics professor at Massachusetts Institute of Technology and winner of the Nobel Memorial Prize in Economics. "It sounds like bits and pieces paying off political debts."
Rudolph Penner, a former director of of the Congressional Budget Office who is now a fellow at the Urban Institute, a research organization here, was more enthusiastic about the address. He said America's competitiveness would be helped by making permanent the tax credits that corporations receive for research and development. As for the investment tax allowance that would allow companies to depreciate an extra 15 percent of investments in their first year, Mr. Penner said, "If you're going to try for stimulus, that's probably an effective way to do it.
"The important point to make is it appears to be a fairly restrained package," Mr. Penner continued. "The hope will be you can keep it restrained as it goes through Congress. My worry is that there will be a bidding war that will lead to a substantial long-term budget deficit increase and a stimulus we don't need."
 
Rejected by Congress
 At a background news briefing today, senior Administration officials said recovery seemed near, and added that the President's "growth package" should create hundreds of thousands of jobs this year and increase the nation's economic output by hundreds of billions of dollars over the next five years.
In his list of proposals to strengthen the economy over the long run, the President made several proposals that have stalled or been rejected by Congress in the past: reducing the capital gains rate, allowing nationwide banking and giving parents vouchers to make it easier for them to send children to private schools.
The capital gains proposal, which the business community applauded, is sure to spur another huge battle in Congress, with many Democrats saying it would do more to line the pockets of the rich than to spur investment. In this election year, tax-cut proposals aimed at the middle class are expected to find smoother sailing.
But some economists said the short-term tax cuts were hardly needed to combat the recession. "It seems to me that we ought to leave the majority of the countercyclical burden to the Federal Reserve," said Donald Straszheim, chief economist for Merrill Lynch. "They have done a lot. There is a recovery coming. My own bet is that any countercyclical stimulus now will come too late."

HEADLINE: Budget Pretense in New Jersey

 BODY:
Gov. Jim Florio's new budget for New Jersey offers more spending, no new taxes and no more layoffs. Nice trick if he could pull it off. But there's a new reality in Trenton. The Democratic Governor's budget will be filtered through a Republican Legislature this year, and the Republicans are determined to cut both taxes and spending. At this stage, both he and they are just sparring.
Mr. Florio's $15.7 billion proposal for the year starting July 1 -- $700 million more than the current year -- rests on uncertain assumptions of increased revenues. He hopes that an economic recovery will yield higher state tax collections, that Washington will provide a windfall of Medicaid reimbursements and that the Legislature will approve revised accounting for state pension funds. He also proposes small spending cuts, of less than 1 percent, for most state agencies. 
 The Republicans say he should have included a reduction in the state sales tax, because he knows it's coming; they won their veto-proof majorities by promising tax cuts. But it is not Mr. Florio's job to fulfill opposition promises. If they want to cut taxes, let them find spending cuts to keep the books in balance. That will give them a lesson in the harshness of budgeting these days -- no doubt Mr. Florio's intent.
The face-off in New Jersey is sharpened by the Republicans' absolute control of the Legislature, and by the Governor's apparent belief that even these difficult times demand no tighter control over spending. Let the sparring proceed.

HEADLINE: Out of Uniform, Into Service

BYLINE: By Adam Walinsky; Adam Walinsky is a lawyer.

 BODY:
The Democratic candidates seem agreed on the need for deep cuts in the defense budget, perhaps to fund tax cuts for the middle class. The Congress is sure to go along with them. Cutting the budget will mean severe adjustments. Consider the people affected.
There was a great outcry when General Motors announced plans to lay off 74,000 workers by 1995. Defense budget reductions envisioned by Congressional leaders would require Gen. Colin L. Powell, chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, to lay off about 500,000, many of them combat veterans. They would be thrown almost casually into an uncertain and troubled economy. 
 We must see these men and women not as a problem to be got rid of, but as a new reserve of energy and talent. The Federal Government should keep them on its rolls, at their current pay and allowances, and use them for three- or four-year terms of civilian service.
First, since the country is desperately short of police officers to face a rising tide of disorder and violence, 200,000 should be assigned to police forces across the country. Soldiers and marines would need training and tight supervision from civilian police authorities. But they would arrive more than competent to suppress gunfights, to corral wilding groups and generally to help keep the peace.
Second, 100,000 should teach in elementary and secondary schools and in remedial and vocational programs. Of the 500,000 likely to be discharged, 100,000 are officers, mostly college educated. Many of the senior enlisted men are also highly experienced trainers.
Albert Shanker, president of the American Federation of Teachers, has said for years that the nation should capture these people to teach in elementary and secondary schools. They know how to teach job skills, are used to dealing with young people from impoverished backgrounds and would not be afraid to teach in rough neighborhoods, even in jails.
In the last four months, an Army hotline has drawn more than 11,000 inquiries from officers interested in becoming teachers, many qualified to teach mathematics and science, for which good teachers are in short supply.
Third, 30,000 medical corpsmen should be assigned to hospital emergency rooms and public health programs. These technicians have been trained to deal with traumas, accidental and violent, like those inundating inner cities. The Special Forces, trained to bring basic public health practices to remote and primitive third world areas, can do at least that much at home, especially when tuberculosis and sexual diseases are spreading in new and more virulent forms.
America's continuing racial crisis gives such a program special urgency. Thirty percent of those likely to be discharged are black, while the population is 11.7 percent black. The military has been a great highway of advancement of talented men and women without regard to race or ethnic origin.
They should not be simply sent back to depressed communities as isolated individuals in search of a function and place. This program of reconstruction would bring them home with a special mission and the support of a committed nation. These are patriotic people of discipline and spirit -- potential leaders whom we need. They would be inspiring examples to the legions of lost black and Hispanic children in ghettos across the country -- and the rest of us, too.
This is not a permanent but a transitional program, at a time of special need. We must still establish the Police Corps, which both houses of Congress established last year and which would enlist young people for four years of police service in exchange for college scholarships. And we will still require fundamental reform in education and health care. But it would bring our military people home to highly useful temporary and perhaps permanent jobs.
It would not reduce Federal outlays, but it could enormously increase our sense of security, prosperity and well-being. Personal safety, the quality of a child's education, quick assistance to the sick and injured -- these are hard to quantify and are omitted from economic statistics. Yet they can determine personal and national satisfaction more surely than any number of things measurable in dollars.
America faces awesome problems of race, poverty and urban decay. Suddenly and unexpectedly, we will have at our disposal a great reservoir of strength to confront them. Let's use it.

SECTION: Section A; Page 20; Column 6; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 398 words

HEADLINE: Is Islamic Fundamentalism a New Red Scare?;
The State of the Law

 BODY:

To the Editor:
"Algeria Chooses Islam" (editorial, Jan. 3), unwittingly proffers an answer to a question that has troubled Muslims since the abolition of the caliphate in 1924: Is it conceivable for vibrant Islam to exist without Islamic dominion? Muslim fundamentalists say no, and you seem to agree, provided the imposition of Islamic law in Algeria proves "wise, merciful and just." 
 Recent experience however disputes the fundamentalist claim. Pakistan, where a Muslim population was the main justification for a state separate from the rest of India, has been through three constitutions since its founding in 1948 and remains uncertain about the feasibility of Islamic law governing all social and economic relations.
In Iran, the principle that the ultimate judicial and legislative authority resides in the ayatollah as the legitimate successor of Mohammed did not outlast Ayatollah Ruhollah Khomeini's death. The contradictions between an elected legislative assembly and an unimpeachable clerical establishment sharing power led to the abolition of the supreme religious authority in the revised constitution.
The Arab Muslim states, including Algeria, suffer from duality in their legal orders. Their constitutions have left Islamic law and institutions unencumbered by the adoption of modern codes and organization.
This has led to duplicity by certain regimes toward fundamentalists and one another, reaching ludicrous proportions in the Persian Gulf crisis, when Baghdad, Riyadh and Cairo invoked Islamic law both to assail and support United States intervention in a primarily Muslim quarrel.
Before Muslim fundamentalists in Algeria or elsewhere build a viable Islamic state, they need to take a critical look at the state of Islamic law, whose interrupted evolution as early as the 10th century deprived it of the rationalization and systematization that law must have in modern society. They need to take heed of what their co-religionists trained in Western legal science have to say: that only by resurrecting the principles of Islamic law from under the heap of outmoded interpretations, opinions and rulings, and recasting them more compatibly with today's social and economic needs, will it be possible to revive Islamic legal culture.
 
GEORGE N. SFEIR
Cupertino, Calif., Jan. 8, 1992
 
The writer is an international legal consultant.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 3; National Desk

HEADLINE: The Federal Budget: Budget as Bush Campaign Manifesto;
Bottom-Line: A Hands-Off Government

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
The budget that President Bush sent Congress today is essentially his campaign manifesto, and it delineates how starkly his views differ from those of his Democratic opponents, in Congress and on the campaign trail.
With minor exceptions, the new budget underscores Mr. Bush's intention of maintaining the same course the country has been on for the last dozen years, one in which the Government uses tax and spending policy as little as possible to guide the nation's economic affairs. 
 That philosophy was more or less embraced today by Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, who wields his considerable influence over the economy independently of the Bush Administration.
Mr. Greenspan told Congress, as he had earlier, that he was confident he could manipulate monetary policy in such a way that the economy would begin growing again before this summer. Speaking in his typically cryptic manner, he cautioned the lawmakers, and by extension Mr. Bush, against breaching the hands-off principle and letting the fever for tax cuts get out of hand in a way that would damage the economy over the long haul. [Page D1.]
Mr. Bush's view is directly in conflict with that of the Democrats, who, while disagreeing on details, are unified in wanting bold changes in direction and an active, deeply involved Government. The President's approach is also being challenged by a small but vocal group of conservative Republicans led by the House minority whip, Newt Gingrich of Georgia, who want the Government to be more aggressive in promoting investment and economic growth.
In an earlier time, Mr. Bush might have been able to use Mr. Greenspan's stance as ammunition in the President's political battles. But in Congress and elsewhere, many politicians of both parties hold Mr. Greenspan to be at least partly responsible for the meager growth and the recession that have plagued the economy in recent years. So his political influence has waned considerably.
 
Tar for the Democrats
 Mr. Bush clearly intends to tar the Democrats with the same brush the Republicans have used successfully in the last three Presidential elections: as favoring wasteful spending and a weak military, as supporting intrusions into the American system of private enterprise and opposing tax cuts that the Republicans say would generate more private investment and economic expansion.
With that in mind, the President laid down these markers:
*Over the next five years, he wants to cut spending for domestic programs below what would be needed to keep pace with inflation. Democrats want heavy new investment in public projects like highways, transit systems, environmental cleanup and airports.
*Mr. Bush wants to save $50 billion from the projected military budget over five years and to use the money for either tax cuts or deficit reduction. Democrats want a military cut of at least $100 billion, with the money saved to be transferred into public works projects, education, medical care and other domestic programs.
*The President wants merely to tinker with the nation's system of private health care insurance and essentially keep the Government's hands off. The Democrats, in Congress and in New Hampshire, favor national health insurance or at least a system in which the Government would play an important role.
*Mr. Bush wants no additional taxes on the wealthy, and many of the tax breaks he proposes would benefit the well-to-do more than others. Democrats want to give tax reductions mostly to the poor and the middle class while raising raise taxes on the wealthy.
Although other issues like abortion and trade policy will obviously rise to the fore at times during this year's campaign, questions about the direction of the nation's economy are likely to dominate politics between now and November. Both sides express a willingness to wage battle on that turf.
 
'Cornerstone of Policy'
 This budget, Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady told reporters today, "will be the cornerstone of a policy that will keep America strong and competitive into the next century."
The Democrats, for their part, are relying on the history of Presidents' losing political ground when the economy falters.
A chapter in the budget shows that the Administration's economists expect the economy to grow by only 2.2 percent this year if Mr. Bush's proposals are adopted, a forecast in the same range as those of private economists.
The last two Presidential election years when the growth rate was that low were 1960 and 1980, and both times the incumbent party was ousted. The winning candidates, John F. Kennedy and Ronald Reagan, had emphasized in their campaigns that they wanted to turn the country in a new direction.
Mr. Bush's budget does offer some policy changes he has never proposed before, like the elimination of some weapons systems, an increase in spending for early childhood education and a variety of new tax breaks for individuals and businesses. But those initiatives are the exceptions. Despite the current bad times and the gloomy expectations for the future expressed in public opinion polls, the new budget differs only on the margins from Mr. Bush's previous ones.
 
Call for Standing Pat
 His message seems to be that the Government should more or less stand pat.
In a few areas, like more money for Head Start, Mr. Bush clearly expects Congress to go along with his recommendations. But other budget proposals, like a lower tax rate on capital gains, seem intended mostly to pick a fight with Congress. If past is prologue, Mr. Bush will run this year as much against Congress, which gets rock-bottom approval ratings in public opinion polls, as against the Democratic Presidential nominee.
Democrats, even most of those who favor other forms of a capital gains tax reduction, maintain that Mr. Bush's proposal would be a windfall for the wealthy. They think they can use the President's position to show that he is the patron of fat cats.
The President says that most of the people whom the tax cut would directly benefit are middle class and that investments generated by the cut would speed recovery from the recession and thus benefit all Americans, rich and poor. If Democrats oppose it, Mr. Bush plans to blame them for hard times.
Other budget proposals, like the elimination of 246 Government programs, many of which have their own constituencies and are thus difficult to get rid of, stand little more chance of enactment this year than they did in previous years, when they failed. But by proposing their abolition, even though he knows it will not come about, Mr. Bush can show savings that make his balance sheet come out right.
This tactic, used by the Republicans year after year, traps the Democrats who control Congress. If they do not want to abolish programs that are important to particular constituents, they must either oppose the President's tax reductions or find other programs, perhaps equally popular, to cut.
 
Polls Offers Democrats Hope
 Mr. Bush relies in his budget proposals on fundamental Republican dogma: that spending on many domestic programs is basically wasteful, that military cuts beyond those he proposes cannot be risked, that the medical care system should remain private and that low taxes for the wealthy lead to economic growth and therefore to jobs and prosperity for those less well off.
Those principles have served the party well for years. Mr. Reagan and Mr. Bush won three Presidential elections in part because they persuaded the public that Democrats led by Jimmy Carter had destroyed the national economy with crippling taxes to pay for Government waste.
But Democrats take heart from recent polls that show the public less interested in tax cuts than in more Government spending for schools, medical care, job creation, environmental protection and public works. People seem to be saying they want the Government to be more ambitious and active.
If Mr. Bush's forecast is right, he will have presided over the slowest four-year period of economic activity since the Presidency of Herbert Hoover. His opponents will say that was Mr. Bush's fault, for embracing tax and spending policies that allowed the Government to atrophy.

HEADLINE: Mideast Parley Ends in Moscow Under Shadow

BYLINE: By CLYDE HABERMAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Jan. 29

 BODY:
The two-day Moscow round of Middle East peace talks ended today much as they had begun, with a pall cast over the future by the question of who will speak for the Palestinians.
Yet despite wobbly moments caused by this question of Palestinian representation, it was clear that the talks here focused on a panoply of issues affecting the entire Middle East, and that therefore the peace process did have a future. 
 Delegates from two dozen countries, including the broadest band of Arab countries ever to sit at the same table with Israel, agreed to resume their discussions in the spring. They set up five small groups, each of which is to focus on a specific problem and to convene in April or May in capitals stretching from Tokyo to Ottawa.
 
Success, in a Sense
 Viewed from that vantage point, some of the nations represented in Moscow said, their gathering was significant, even successful.
But the unresolved dispute over Palestinian representation hung over the conference more thickly than snow clouds over the city.
It appeared that, at least for now, the arguments were centered only on this regional phase of negotiations and not the companion direct, two-party talks between Israel and delegations representing the Palestinians, Jordan, Syria and Lebanon. Those negotiations, held thus far in Washington, are supposed to resume in mid-February, although the location has not yet been decided.
For a second day, Palestinians did not attend the talks here because their choice of delegates was unacceptable to the American and Russian sponsors, who had acceded to Israeli demands that Palestinians from East Jerusalem and from outside the occupied West Bank and Gaza Strip be excluded.
 
Guidance From the Hosts
 But there were strong signs that the United States and Russia were looking for a way to get the Palestinians involved when the working groups reconvene this spring to discuss regional concerns.
The next working-group sessions will be spread across the globe in April and May. Talks on economic development are to be held in Belgium, on the environment in Japan, on arms control and regional security in Washington, on refugees in Canada, and on water resources in either Turkey or Austria.
The meetings on refugees and economic development are being called "seminars." The purpose of such language is not clear, but it may be intended to bring about some form of compromise on the question of Palestinian representation by labeling those sessions something other than working groups.
It is quite possible, said Vladimir Petrovsky, the Russian Deputy Foreign Minister, that there will be "adjustments" to present agreements forbidding the involvement of Arabs from Jerusalem or of overseas Palestinians. On Tuesday, Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d said he supported the idea of having Palestinians from outside the territories join the discussions at a later date, especially on an issue like refugees, which affects them directly.
 
A Measure of Hope
 Hanan Ashrawi, the Palestinian spokeswoman, said today that she regarded the Secretary's remarks as a commitment to change the rules, although other members of the Palestinian delegation said they were not yet convinced that the Americans meant business.
If Mrs. Ashrawi is right, Washington could be headed into a collision course with Israel, a road already well traveled as a result of clashes over the building of Jewish settlements in the territories and the Israeli request for $10 billion in American loan guarantees over five years to resettle Russians and other Jewish emigres.
Yosef Hadass, director general of the Israeli Foreign Ministry, repeated his country's opposition to any change of procedure, often referred to as the "Madrid formula" because they governed the opening peace conference in Madrid last October.
"We have accepted participation of Palestinians who live with us in the territories," Mr. Hadass said tonight. "We are not going to change it."
"I don't want to tell you we will even consider it in the future," he added. "We will not."
On Tuesday, Foreign Minister David Levy of Israel said that if Palestinians considered unacceptable by his Government were allowed into the peace process, "they will meet by themselves."
The Israelis argue that the rules establishedmust be followed down the line.
For them, letting in Palestinians from abroad or from Arab East Jerusalem would imply that they are now willing to negotiate both their absorption of East Jerusalem soon after their victory in the 1967 war and to accept the right of hundreds of thousands of Arabs to return one day to pre-1948 Palestine. In addition, by excluding "outside Palestinians," they can prevent direct participation by the Palestine Liberation Organization, labeled by Israel as a terrorist group.
But the Palestinians say that in these talks on regional matters, it is senseless and unfair to limit their involvement to delegates evaluated under the criteria set initially by Israel and then accepted by the sponsors to get the peace process under way. While insisting that their refusal to play now by Madrid rules is a matter of substance and not pride, they have decided to draw the line on conditions that all along they have found humiliating but had to swallow out of political weakness.
"Enough is enough," Saeb Erakat, a Palestinian delegate said.
The United States and Russia told the Palestinians that most of their chosen delegates were unacceptable for the Moscow round. But now they are talking about possibly changing the rules the next time, because they worry, among other things, that broad Arab involvement in this delicate process could be jeopardized if the Palestinians stay away.
Referring specifically to the Mideast refugee issue, a senior Russian official, Oleg Dirkovsky, said, "We do not understand how these negotiations can proceed without participation by the Palestinians."
Israel's concern about this shift by the sponsors was signaled by an official who said, "In the long run, the Palestinians may have scored a major political victory because of this American and Russian departure."
Still, Israelis pronounced themselves satisfied with the Moscow talks, in large part because Saudi Arabia and other Arab gulf nations, along with North African countries, sat down for negotiations involving Israel for the first time. Also attending were delegations from outside the Mideast region -- Japan, the European Community, China and Canada, whowere welcomed for their political and diplomatic expertise, and also for their money.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 6; National Desk

LENGTH: 2031 words

HEADLINE: The Federal Budget: The Overview;
PRESIDENT OFFERS CAUTIOUS CHANGES IN SPENDING PLAN

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
In an election-year budget of $1.52 trillion, President Bush called today for modest increases in a wide range of popular domestic programs, but he avoided any major change in spending priorities to fight the recession.
Most of the President's proposed budget carries out decisions and commitments made in the past. Social Security overtakes military spending for the first time, as the Pentagon budget declines while cash payments to the elderly rise steadily. 
 Hoping to breathe life into a listless economy, Mr. Bush proposed tax breaks for investors, home buyers, businesses and families with children. His proposals turned out to be less drastic than some of the tax cuts considered by his advisers over the last two months.
 
Predictions on Deficit
 Mr. Bush's budget shows a deficit of $399 billion in the current fiscal year and $352 billion in 1993, both far above the record $269 billion reported last year. The actual deficit for any year is often bigger than the President predicts. Mr. Bush assumes an upswing in the economy this year, but if that does not occur soon, the deficit may be larger than his estimate, because tax collections would be lower.
The President's budget would provide almost $40 billion for unemployment insurance benefits in the current fiscal year, including $2.7 billion in extra aid for people who have exhausted their regular benefits. But the budget contains no major jobs program of the type sought by many Democrats and big-city mayors.
Overshadowing the entire budget are the nation's economic problems. President Bush and his budget director, Richard G. Darman, insisted that it was neither necessary nor desirable to break the budget agreement of 1990, which set separate limits on domestic and military spending through the fiscal year 1993. But Mr. Darman said grudgingly today that the Administration would, under certain conditions, consider a change in that agreement to use savings from the military budget to finance a tax cut for families with children.
 
Not Yielding to Democrats
 Mr. Darman's comments were the clearest indication to date that the Administration might be willing to renegotiate the budget agreement. But he expressed no willingness to bow to Congressional Democrats who want to take money from the Pentagon and spend it on social welfare programs.
The budget sent to Congress today covers the fiscal year 1993, which begins Oct. 1, five weeks before Election Day. One section of the budget, describing the Administration's economic assumptions, predicts confidently that "a sustained upturn will soon begin," thanks in part to Mr. Bush's efforts to stimulate economic growth.
But in a separate chapter of the budget, Mr. Darman is more cautious, saying: "Here at home, the euphoria of summer has been displaced by another winter's gloom. The domestic economy has not recovered in the manner that had been widely forecast."
And the Agriculture Department, in its budget, predicts that the number of people on food stamps, 24.2 million in October, will reach a monthly average of 25.6 million this year and 25.8 million next year.
Senator Robert C. Byrd, a West Virginia Democrat who is chairman of the Appropriations Committee, said, "I saw no immediate burst for the economy in any of the tax-cut proposals." He was referring to Mr. Bush's State of the Union Message on Tuesday as well as the budget request today.
Asked why there was no big tax cut to revive the economy, Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady said, "The President's feeling is that the recovery is on its way." Samuel K. Skinner, the White House chief of staff, said the most important boost for the economy came late last year, when the Federal Reserve lowered the discount rate, which it charges for lending to commercial banks.
This year's deficit would be $30 billion higher but for Mr. Darman's decision to make an accounting change that sharply reduces the apparent cost of Federal insurance for banks and savings-and-loan associations. Representative Leon E. Panetta of California and Senator Jim Sasser of Tennessee, Democrats who serve as chairmen of budget committees in the two chambers, said the proposed accounting change was a gimmick that makes the deficit seem more manageable.
Mr. Panetta described Mr. Bush's budget request as "a return to the smoke-and-mirrors, buy-now-pay-later budgets of the 1980's."
 
What Does It All Mean?
 The President's budget is a request to Congress, which may or may not accept his suggestions. What emerges from Congress is invariably different from what the President proposes, in part because Presidents sometimes do not lobby hard for their own proposals.
The President said his budget complies with the 1990 budget agreement. The agreement set limits on the Federal deficit, but they are essentially meaningless because they are adjusted for changes in economic conditions and other factors.
Mr. Bush's budget gives no indication that he will aggressively pursue any proposals likely to alienate large numbers of voters. As expected, he proposes to increase spending for Head Start, child nutrition, preventive health programs, biomedical research and Pell grants, the major source of Federal aid to college students from low-income families.
Those proposals are all intended to reinforce the message Mr. Bush carried this month to New Hampshire, where the people have been battered by the recession and where there is a primary election on Feb. 18. "I care," he said on his trip to New Hampshire, where he acknowledged that "this economy is in a free fall."
 
In the Fine Print
 But for each increase in politically popular programs, the fine print of the President's budget shows proposed cuts in other areas, not highlighted by the Administration: cuts in home heating assistance, housing for the elderly and the disabled, Amtrak, operating subsidies for urban mass transit, the Tennessee Valley Authority and aid to Appalachia.
Increases and decreases in these programs more or less offset each other. Mr. Bush would freeze this type of "domestic discretionary" spending, which is subject to annual appropriations by Congress.
The driving force in the budget, surging beyond any such constraint, is benefit programs like Social Security, Medicare and Medicaid. Taken together, such benefit programs account for more than half of all Federal spending, but they are "largely exempt from budgetary discipline," Mr. Darman said in his introduction. Under these programs, the Government is required to pay benefits to any applicant who meets eligibility criteria.
Disappointed with the results of the Gramm-Rudman-Hollings law and other measures intended to balance the budget, Mr. Bush and Mr. Darman urge Congress to establish a new procedure to curb the growth of benefit programs. They would set an overall ceiling on such spending, allowing increases for inflation and population growth, but not for high unemployment or recession.
 
Laying a Foundation
 Under this proposal, some benefits could be automatically cut if total spending on such programs would otherwise exceed the limit. The proposal, though unlikely to be adopted in the exact form suggested by Mr. Bush, lays a foundation for future efforts to control benefit programs, usually treated as untouchable in election years.
Mr. Darman said Congress should impose "an enforceable cap" on the growth of spending for these programs. Otherwise, he said, "they just keep on going and growing automatically."
Without waiting for Congress to act, President Bush today proposed cutbacks and other changes in benefit programs intended to save $7.7 billion this year and $68 billion over six years. The proposals include a sharp increase in monthly premiums for affluent people enrolled in Medicare, the Federal health insurance program for the elderly and disabled.
The proposal would apply to individuals with annual income of more than $100,000 and couples with income of more than $125,000. It would roughly triple the monthly premium, now $31.80, for insurance covering the cost of doctors' services.
Congress took no action on a similar proposal last year, though the House Budget Committee said the idea had "a great deal of merit and should be considered."
 
Health Care Deferred
 The budget does not include comprehensive proposals to overhaul the nation's health-care system, control soaring medical costs or increase access to care for millions of people with no health insurance. The Administration has been studying that issue for more than two years. The White House says Mr. Bush will announce his health proposals on Feb. 6.
Under one proposal already approved by Mr. Bush, poor people could get tax credits to help them buy health insurance. The maximum credit in any year would be $3,750 for families and $1,250 for individuals.
In his military budget, President Bush says he would stop production of the B-2 Stealth bomber, the Seawolf submarine and the nation's most powerful nuclear warhead, used on missles carried by Trident submarines.
He would also terminate the small intercontinental ballistic missile known as the Midgetman, stop all production of the MX missile and shift many long-range bombers to missions predicated on conventional wars.
 
Protective at the Pentagon
 But the Pentagon said that money for the "Star Wars" antimissile system "remains a top priority." Mr. Bush requested $5.4 billion for the program in 1993, up from $4.1 billion this year.
While the Pentagon is cutting expensive weapons systems originally intended to counter the Soviet threat, it hopes to protect its budget for military salaries and training.
The Administration is resisting Congressional calls for swifter cuts in active-duty military personnel. The armed forces, now just under 2 million, would decline by 138,000 this year and 99,000 next year, eventually reaching 1.64 million in 1995 under the President's plan.
Members of Congress from both parties support many of the military cutbacks proposed by Mr. Bush. Some staunch supporters of the Pentagon like Senator John McCain, Republican of Arizona, had already called for ending production of the B-2 bomber and canceling the Seawolf submarine program.
But Mr. Bush says that money saved as a result of the military cutbacks should not be used for domestic programs, as many Democrats propose. The House Committee on Government Operations plans to vote next week on legislation to permit such shifts. Representative John Conyers Jr., the Michigan Democrat who heads the committee, said today that "unneeded defense funds" should be used to create civilian jobs and to help cities and states in fiscal distress.
 
Darman's Understatement
 With considerable understatement, Mr. Darman says "the outlook for debt and deficits remains unattractive." The Administration envisions Federal budget deficits totaling $1.5 trillion from 1992 through 1997. The Government finances such deficits by borrowing money from the public.
Under Mr. Bush's budget, the Federal debt, which represents the accumulated total of all Government borrowing, would soar from $2.7 trillion in 1991 to $4.2 trillion in 1997. The ratio of the Federal debt to gross national product will reach 52.5 percent this year, a level not seen since the mid-1950's, when the Government still owed huge amounts from World War II.
Such debt will be a burden for years. The Government cannot reduce its debt unless it runs a surplus in the Federal budget. In 1993, the Treasury expects to spend $214 billion, or 14 percent of the entire Federal budget, on interest payments to people who have lent money to the Government.
To trim Government spending, Mr. Bush proposes to end 246 domestic programs that together cost $5 billion a year. He would, for example, end construction of public housing for low-income families, eliminate the Commerce Department's Economic Development Administration and end support for improvements to rail tracks in the Northeast Corridor used by Amtrak trains.
If money is still needed for such purposes, he said, it can be provided by state and local governments or by other Federal programs.

HEADLINE: BUSH AND YELTSIN PROPOSE DEEP CUTS IN ATOMIC WEAPONS

BYLINE: By ANDREW ROSENTHAL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
President Bush and President Boris N. Yeltsin made new arms control proposals this week that envision slashing American and Russian long-range nuclear arms by well over half and could eliminate the class of missiles considered the biggest threat to peace.
But Mr. Bush's proposal, outlined in the State of the Union Address on Tuesday, asks Mr. Yeltsin to take a step that no Soviet leader ever entertained: give up his advantage in one area of nuclear arms competition, land-based missiles, in return for an important concession but not parallel cuts in the area of American advantage, submarine-based missiles. 
 Mr. Bush's plan would reduce the American strategic nuclear arsenal to between 4,500 and 5,000 warheads, about 60 percent of the current level and far less than envisioned in last year's strategic arms reduction treaty.
 
Multiple-Warhead Missiles
 It would eliminate missiles with more than one warhead that are fired from land, the most dangerous kind of nuclear weapon and the mainstay of the Soviet nuclear force. In exchange, the United States would sharply reduce its stock of weapons launched from submarines, which are the ones the Russians worry about the most, but Washington would retain an edge.
In response, Mr. Yeltsin said today that he would propose cutting long-range nuclear warheads to between 2,000 and 2,500. That did not appear to include cruise missiles and bombs dropped from airplanes, but would still mean a total significantly lower than what Mr. Bush suggested. [Page A8.]
Administration officials said prospects were good for some sort of an agreement, which Mr. Bush and Mr. Yeltsin will begin discussing at Camp David on Saturday. Mr. Bush on Tuesday described Mr. Yeltsin's early response as "very positive."
 
Offer Called 'Lopsided'
 But Soviet officials visiting the United States in advance of Mr. Yeltsin's arrival for a United Nations Security Council meeting on Friday are reported to have said in conversations here that Mr. Yeltsin found the offer "lopsided" and told Mr. Bush so in a letter received by the White House on Monday. Mr. Yeltsin had been advised in advance of Mr. Bush's proposal.
Mr. Yeltsin also made clear in a speech today that his Government will continue to oppose Washington's "Star Wars" anti-missile program unless the United States agrees to share the technology, something the Bush Administration has refused to consider.
Both sides have indicated in recent days that they recognize that the dynamics of arms control have been transformed. Just last week, Mr. Yeltsin announced that Russian nuclear missiles would no longer be aimed at American targets, and the Pentagon has long conceded there is no apparent military threat from what used to be the Soviet Union.
Indeed, Administration officials said these sorts of developments led Mr. Bush to propose the concessions that Soviet leaders always refused, with some confidence that his ideas would be at least considered.
 
'Out to Sea'
 "This is what we've been trying to do since the beginning of strategic arms negotiations in 1969 -- 'push the Soviets out to sea,' where we have our missiles," said Dunbar Lockwood, a senior analyst with the Arms Control Association, a private research group.
He added, "It has always been our premise that it's more stabilizing to have the bulk of missiles in subs, because they are invisible under the sea and therefore more useful for retaliation and not an attractive target for someone considering a first strike."
As dramatic as the newly proposed cuts may seem, arms control is not the only item on the agenda for Saturday's meeting, nor perhaps even the most pressing.
The White House views the meeting as a chance for Mr. Bush to impress on Mr. Yeltsin the need to continue political and economic liberalization without Russia imposing its will on other former republics.
 
'Big Brother Fashion'
 "We believe there is a need for all the former Soviet republics to move forward along these lines, mutually and not in Big Brother fashion," an Administration official said. "The Russians have shown a tendency to confuse their national interests with those of the peoples surrounding them."
Mr. Bush also planned to give what one official called his "imprimatur" to Mr. Yeltsin's economic policies, which Washington hopes will give the Russian leader a political boost at home, in much the same way that Mr. Bush once sought to shore up President Mikhail S. Gorbachev.
Equally important, officials said, Mr. Bush hopes to establish with Mr. Yeltsin the kind of personal rapport and working relationship that he had with Mr. Gorbachev. "It's the first time he's met with Yeltsin that Bush hasn't had to worry about the problem of dual power and conflicting loyalties," a senior official said.
Another official said, "The only alternative to Yeltsin that we can see is dictatorship and so we want to, A, make sure that he's politically entrenched and, B, make sure as we did with Gorbachev that he follows the path of democracy and reform."
 
1991 Arms Treaty
 Last July, the United States and the Soviet Union signed the Strategic Arms Reduction Treaty, which calls for reducing the American arsenal of about 11,600 nuclear warheads aboard ballistic missiles, jet-like cruise missiles and bombs dropped by airplanes to about 8,600 before the end of the century. The Soviet arsenal would be cut from about 10,900 warheads to about 6,900.
Nuclear arms treaties deal with warheads, which are the actual nuclear bombs, rather than the missiles that carry them, because modern missiles can carry more than 10 warheads apiece. These missiles are considered dangerous or "destabilizing" because they are tempting targets for the other country's military planners.
On Sept. 27, in announcing unilateral cuts in short-range nuclear weapons that are used on the battlefield, Mr. Bush offered to negotiate an elimination of multiple-warhead missiles with the Soviet Union.
 
Cutting Soviet Arsenal
 On Tuesday, Mr. Bush proposed that Russia, Ukraine and Kazakhstan eradicate all of their huge, multiple-warhead missiles based on land. This would have the additional benefit to the United States of removing all intercontinental missiles from Ukrainian and Kazakh soil.
The United States has in the past proposed eliminating multiple-warhead missiles fired from land and the Kremlin has always refused, because the United States was not willing to consider cuts in its submarine-launched missiles.
This time, Mr. Bush offered to do just that if the other three countries accepted his proposal. He said he would eliminate about one-third of the warheads aboard American Trident submarines, reducing the force from 3,456 warheads to about 2,300.
He went a step further by saying he would also destroy America's newest and most powerful land-based weapon, the 10-warhead MX missile, and lower the number of warheads aboard the Minuteman 3 missile to one from three.
If these ideas are adopted, the United States would be left with about 2,800 warheads on its long-range missiles. The rest of the arsenal is composed of bombs dropped by aircraft and cruise missiles, similar to conventionally armed versions used against Iraq last year. It is not clear how many of each kind of missile Mr. Bush intends to keep.

GRAPHIC: Photo: "Both presidents have made far-reaching suggestions and far-reaching proposals," said Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d, left, about arms-reduction plans. HEADLINE: The Federal Budget: Taxes;
Adjustment Brings In Cash, and Risk

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
Among the more than three dozen tax proposals in the budget request for 1993 that the Bush Administration sent to Congress today, the adjustment of withholding tables for most taxpayers to cut the amount taken out of each paycheck stands alone in needing no Congressional assent.
But while the adjustment will inject more than $2 billion a month in spending into the economy this year, some analysts warned that it would also create a potential trap for the unwary. 
 More than 8.5 million taxpayers who would be getting tax refunds in the spring of 1993 without the adjustment will instead owe the Government money unless they take a simple precaution, Treasury figures indicate. That precaution will involve filing a new form to advise the employer that they want more money withheld.
Although the Internal Revenue Service will waive penalties for underpayments of estimated income tax because of the adjustment, many taxpayers may find themselves scrambling for the money to make up for the underpayment.
"It's play in '92, pay in '93," said David Berenson, national director of tax policy for Ernst & Young, an accounting firm. Mr. Berenson said he expected that despite the change, which was being put in place today, relatively few people would file the form to readjust their withholding.
 
Millions to Be Affected
 According to the I.R.S., 89 million income tax returns fall into the low-to-middle income categories, with 72 million of these producing refunds under the old system. The agency also estimates that 88 percent of all taxpayers now getting refunds will still get them. But 12 percent, or about 8.6 million people, will not. In addition, others who now come out even when they file their tax returns will owe money, while many who already owe on April 15 will owe more.
To avoid the change in withholding, which is automatic, workers must complete a new Form W-4 in which they would claim the same number of allowances as before, but would also tell their employers that they want additional money withheld each payday. Generally, the smaller the refund a person is accustomed to getting, the more likely he is to owe money next spring.
Married couples who file a joint return should deduct an additional $345 divided by the number of paydays per year. Single employees should use the figure $172, so that if they are paid weekly, the extra amount withheld from each paycheck would be $3.30.
People at higher income levels will not have to make any decision on withholding. The new plan does not reduce the amount of tax withheld for married people starting with incomes of $90,200 and for individuals with incomes starting at $53,200.
 
Gains and Losses
 In their overall effect, the Administration says, the changes it is proposing will not significantly increase the Federal deficit. The changes would cost the Treasury $5.2 billion in 1992, it says, but receipts would rise $700 million in 1993, $3.1 billion in 1994 and $900 million in each of 1995 and 1996.
But the projection does not take into account the loss to the Treasury of the interest-free money it had been withholding from taxpayers' paychecks. The Government will now have to borrow that money in the capital markets at going interest rates unless it can be found elsewhere.
Among various "sleeper" proposals in the package are provisions that would force charities to file annual returns that document contributions of more than $500. That could cause taxpayers to be more cautious in claiming deductions, or exaggerating them, and might reduce charitable contributions, some analysts said.
Another proposal would require securities dealers to value their inventories at current market rates instead of giving them the choice of using the lower of market value or cost. Some specialists said this could discourage dealers from selling securities that had risen in value.
As a whole, the Administration said, the provisions are designed to spur the economy in the short term and revitalize it over the long term.
A supplementary Treasury document distributed this afternoon grouped the revenue items into four broad categories. They are jobs and investments; families, health, education and savings; homebuyers, and other proposals, a miscellany of provisions affecting both individuals and businesses. All three of the sleeper provisions, for example, are included under "other."
In the jobs and investments category, six proposals are already on the books but are due to expire soon. They include rules that govern the way companies allocate spending on research and experimentation between their domestic and foreign operations, and rules on credits for businesses that invest in solar or geothermal energy.
Three other tax credits for business that would otherwise expire also provide direct benefits to individuals. The Administration would extend for 18 months the tax credit for investments in low-income housing, a tax credit for businesses that hire disadvantaged veterans and people now on welfare, among others, and the authority of state and local governments to issue bonds to aid new farmers.
The extensions are expected to fare well in Congress. Indeed, the proposal to keep the credits on the books is hardly new. Many have been extended several times.
The freshest idea, but one with limited application, would allow a person who sells a principal residence at a loss to deduct the portion of the loss that exceeds 10 percent of income. This would mainly benefit people who have bought homes in recent years at high prices and must sell at low prices. But analysts cautioned that to compute the loss on a residence a taxpayer must take into account any deferred gain on previous homes.
One of the most expensive proposals also involves homes, costing a total of $4.6 billion in 1993 and 1994. It would allow people who have not owned a home within the last three years a 10 percent credit on the purchase price, up to $5,000, spread evenly over the two years. The purchase would have to made before Jan. 1, 1993. Any unused part of the credit could be carried forward for up to five years.
Real estate developers would regain a long-sought tax break that was removed from the tax code in 1986. It would permit developers to treat all their real estate operations as a single entity, thereby allowing them to apply losses in one area against the profits in another. As it stands now, these losses cannot be used to lower tax liability. The Administration contends that developers were unfairly caught in the general crackdown on tax shelters in the 1986 tax overhaul.
 
Cut in Capital Gains Tax
 Mr. Bush's first item on a list of proposals designed to stimulate jobs and investment is a reduction in the capital gains tax rate. It would exclude from taxation 45 percent of the capital gain on assets held for three years or more, 30 percent of the gain on an asset held two to three years, and 15 percent of the gain when the asset is held at least one year.
A 45 percent exclusion would effectively cut the levy to 15.4 percent for taxpayers now subject to a 28 percent capital gains rate and to 8 1/4 percent for those in the lowest bracket, 15 percent.
Prospects for passage of a capital gains tax cut are unclear. Administration officials emphasize statistics showing that 60 percent of the people who benefit from capital gains each year are of moderate income, but private analysts and members of Congress pointed out that most of the gains, in dollar terms, go to the wealthy.
The Administration's proposal also contains an assortment of relatively small provisions that would provide specific benefits for widely different groups of taxpayers.
 
Break for Commuters
 For New Yorkers and residents of other cities with public transit systems, for example, the Administration proposes raising to $60 a month from $21 the amount that a worker can receive tax free from an employer for commuting costs. Under current law, employees who get even a few cents more than $21 in passes, tokens or reimbursements have to count the total amount received as income. Under the proposal, the $60 would be tax free and the benefit would not be lost if a worker received more than that.
For workers in the shipbuilding and aviation industries and for people and corporations buying yachts and private aircraft, the Administration would repeal the luxury tax imposed as part of the 1990 budget agreement. The tax would remain on expensive automobiles, furs and jewelry.
To offset the revenue loss to the Treasury, the Administration would repeal a current exemption for diesel fuel used for pleasure boats, effective July 1, 1992. The tax is now 20.1 cents per gallon.

HEADLINE: Russian Offers Americans Access to K.G.B. Files

BYLINE: By ALISON MITCHELL

 BODY:
The chairman of the Russian parliamentary commission that oversees the archives of the K.G.B. says he is prepared to invite American historians to search for evidence that could shed light on the fate of Americans taken prisoner during the Vietnam war.
The official, Col. Gen. Dmitri Volkogonov, speaking on the eve of President Boris N. Yeltsin's trip to the United States, said the Russian president wanted to assure the United States Senate that he was responding to a request for information on reports that some Americans might have been taken to the Soviet Union. 
 "We want to be honest," said General Volkogonov, who is also an adviser to Mr. Yeltsin. He said he would support "one, two, three American historians or specialists" working with his commission in Moscow.
 
No Support for Kalugin Yet
 General Volkogonov said the commission had begun examining the K.G.B. archives but could not yet speak definitively about contacts with Americans during the Vietnam war.
But he said that so far, he had found nothing to substantiate the statements of Gen. Oleg D. Kalugin, a former K.G.B. official, that Soviet intelligence had interviewed several Americans in Vietnam as late as 1975-78, after the war had ended and all American prisoners were reported released.
"Maybe there are documents but we haven't yet found them," he said in an interview Tuesday at the Russian mission to the United Nations. "Maybe he spoke all the truth. Maybe to the contrary, he spoke untruth. Kalugin spoke by memory. I think that's not enough."
General Volkogonov is part of an advance party of Russian officials who arrived in New York seeking technical assistance and advice on how to wrench Russia toward a market economy and convert factories in the vast military-industrial complex to civilian uses. Mr. Yeltsin is to arrive tonight, attend Friday's session of the United Nations Security Council and meet President Bush on Saturday.
 
Reviewing the Records
 The author of a critical history of Stalin, General Volkogonov fell into disfavor with the military establishment before the failed August coup for saying the Communist system had been responsible for the Soviet Union's huge losses during the World War II.
General Volkogonov said he knew of a case in 1969 when four American deserters from Vietnam went to Japan and then by boat to the Soviet Far East. He said they were questioned by the K.G.B., held for several months in Moscow and then released. He did not identify the four.
He said his committee was also scouring records from the end of World War II, when as many as 22,000 Americans liberated from German prison camps were brought to the Soviet Union and repatriated, primarily through the Ukrainian port of Odessa.
In some cases, General Volkogonov said, Americans with Russian, Ukrainian or Jewish names were held prisoner by the secret police, and that three of them were released several years later in an East-West prisoner exchange.
In December, the Senate Select Committee on P.O.W.-M.I.A. Affairs appealed to Russia for more information on reports that some Americans might have been taken to the Soviet Union after the Vietnam war.
General Kalugin, the former K.G.B. official, testified for three hours before a United States Senate committee last week, saying the K.G.B. interrogated several Americans held by Vietnam in 1975-78. General Kalugin described himself as the former head of the K.G.B.'s counterintelligence operations.
The government of Vietnam has confirmed that it allowed at least one American prisoner of war to be questioned by the K.G.B. in January 1973. Vietnam identified the Soviet interrogator as Oleg Nechiporenko, who worked under General Kalugin. Mr. Nechiporenko has also said that he interviewed an American in 1973, but he has denied that Americans were questioned any later than that.
General Volkogonov seems intent on cementing a close relationship between the American and Russian governments, as Russia moves through a winter of simmering public discontent with price rises and continued shortages.
"We need not only help with bread and with money," he said. "The most important help we need is help in how we should build our market. We need technical help, we need intellectual and organizing help."
General Volkogonov criticized the West for succumbing to a "victor's complex" as the Soviet Union and the former Communist empire collapsed.
"It could turn into Pyrrhic victory," he said. "The problems in the Soviet Union now are not less dangerous. They also could be a danger to the whole world."

HEADLINE: Clinton's Accuser Dismissed From State Job in Arkansas

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: LITTLE ROCK, Ark., Jan. 29

 BODY:
The woman who has maintained that she had an affair with Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas was dismissed from her state job today because of unexcused absences, her superior said.
The Arkansas woman, Gennifer Flowers, had been an administrative assistant for the appeal tribunal, which hears unemployment cases. Her salary was $17,000 a year. 
 "Gennifer Flowers has abandoned her job with the Arkansas Appeal Tribunal by failing to report for work or call in for three days," Don K. Barnes, chairman of the Arkansas Board of Review, said in a prepared statement. Mr. Barnes said Ms. Flowers had been absent without leave since Monday.
A spokesman for Mr. Clinton, Mike Gauldin, said the Governor had nothing to do with the dismissal. "There's a state policy that says if you don't show up for three days, you've abandoned your job," he said.
In a paid interview with a supermarket tabloid, Star, Ms. Flowers asserted last week that she had a 12-year affair with Governor Clinton, a Democratic Presidential contender. The Governor has repeatedly denied having had a sexual relationship with the woman.
