HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Democrats;
Struggling to Be Heard, Kerrey Pounces on Clinton

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH KOLBERT

 BODY:
In most sports, when the front-runner stumbles, his competitors gain a clear opportunity. In politics, however, it is trickier. Because when the front-runner stumbles, he takes all the television cameras with him.
This is the awkward situation now facing Senator Bob Kerrey of Nebraska. For the last several days, he has been trying to draw Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas into a public debate on issues like health care and the environment. But as Mr. Clinton's campaign suffered under accusations that the Governor had carried on a 12-year extramarital affair, he ignored the gibes, and Mr. Kerrey's campaign suffered even more -- from lack of exposure. 
 Yesterday, Mr. Kerrey apparently decided that if the press would not take up his challenge, he would take up its.
At news conferences first in New Hampshire and later in New York, Mr. Kerrey issued his first public comments critical of Mr. Clinton's relationship with Gennifer Flowers, who says she was the Governor's lover. It was not the accusations of marital infidelity that disturbed Mr. Kerrey, though. It was the insensitive comments Mr. Clinton had made about Governor Mario M. Cuomo.
 
A Change of Tone
 Given the range of invective political opponents have been known to heap on one another, Mr. Kerrey's criticism of Mr. Clinton still left him well on the tame side of his profession. But taken together with other remarks he has made in recent days, it is an unmistakable change in tone for Mr. Kerrey, who has often seemed to be trying to run for President without hurting his rivals' feelings.
Opinion polls show that so far Mr. Kerrey has reaped no discernible benefit from Mr. Clinton's contretemps with reporters. Indeed, some polls show the Senator has lost support in recent days. With less than three weeks to go before the New Hampshire primary, the Kerrey campaign is hoping to reverse this slide through an open "engagement" with Mr. Clinton, who is still leading the Democratic field in most New Hampshire polls.
Such an engagement, aides to the Senator say, would at once help to set the two men apart, and provide Mr. Kerrey with some much-needed attention. A top aide to Mr. Kerrey explained, "We're looking for a tactical engagement that will carry over into some news cycles."
By yesterday evening, Mr. Kerrey could claim at least some modest success in this endeavor.
In tapes Ms. Flowers played for reporters on Monday, Mr. Clinton suggested that Governor Cuomo "acts like" a member of the Mafia. He later apologized for the remark, saying that he meant only to imply that Governor Cuomo "was a tough and worthy competitor."
Yesterday, Mr. Kerrey took Mr. Clinton on, saying he thought his apology "was too generic and inadequate."
It did not, he said, "express an understanding of the offense given to those who were mentioned by the tape, not just Governor Cuomo but the Italian-American population."
At an appearance yesterday afternoon in Hampton, N.H., Mr. Clinton responded to Mr. Kerrey. "Of all the things I might be faulted for in my public life," he said, "being insensitive to any kind of minority or ethnic group is not one of them, ever."
And at a fund-raising event tonight in West Orange, N.J., Governor Clinton apologized to Italian-Americans and anyone else who might have been offended by the remark. "Wounds were caused," he said. "I want to apologize again for any hurt that was caused to any Italian-American in this region, state or country or anyone else offended."
Mr. Kerrey's efforts to "engage" Mr. Clinton directly began last week. At a stop in Maine on Friday, for example, he said: "I've got big disagreements with Governor Clinton's environmental record, and significant disagreements over health care." Later that day, he said: "I'm for national health insurance and he's pretending to be."
His most recent television commercials are intended to underscore the theme of difference. In one that began airing in New Hampshire this week, Mr. Kerrey says: "The other candidates in this race have figured out that national health insurance is popular, so they're talking as if they support it. But they don't."
 
'A Counterfeit Proposal'
 "I hope," he concludes in the commercial, "that the people of New Hampshire in this campaign will not accept a counterfeit proposal." Mr. Kerrey supports a national health insurance plan that would be financed though the Federal Government.
Mr. Clinton, in contrast, often says that he supports national health care, but his proposals explicitly avoid any form of nationalized care.
At the same time that Mr. Kerrey is seeking a clear confrontation with Mr. Clinton, he is also seeking a clearer formulation of his own views. Over the last two weeks, his standard speech has been undergoing continual revisions, until now it barely resembles the original. Gone are the abstruse references to "generic technologies," and in their place are repeated invocations of the "urgency" of the task at hand.
His new speech has won good reviews at recent appearances, and aides to Mr. Kerrey say they are encouraged that after a tentative start he is finding a voice. But the campaign has yet to work out all of its glitches.
On Tuesday night, for example, Mr. Kerrey and an entourage of aides went to watch President Bush's State of the Union Message at the home of an unemployed worker in Manchester, N.H. The occasion, chronicled by several television crews, seemed like the perfect opportunity for the Senator to demonstrate his support among victims of the recession and to score some points against the Republicans.
But when asked after spending the evening with Mr. Kerrey whom he planned to vote for in the primary, the unemployed worker, Frank Adamske, a registered Republican, said: "Pat Buchanan."

HEADLINE: Dow Drops 47.18, to 3,224.96, After Remarks by Greenspan

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO

 BODY:
Stock prices swung in a wide arc yesterday, ending with a sizable drop on fading hopes of further interest-rate cuts. The Dow Jones industrial average, which posted solid gains earlier in the day, plunged after remarks on the economy by Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve Board.
The Dow ended the day at 3,224.96, down 47.18 points, or nearly 1.5 percent. But that was 74 points shy of the day's high, the biggest one-day spread since a 120-point swing on Nov. 15, 1990, when the market unraveled during a period when futures and options were expiring. 
 The morning began in weakness as foreign investors sold and disappeared, but then prices moved up steadily through midday, until Mr. Greenspan, speaking before the Senate Banking Committee, gave the market a large dose of disappointment, traders said.
"Mr. Greenspan's comments around midday that the economy was already in the process of stablizing and could show a reasonable improvement in the near term, created a very unfriendly bond market to provide the equity market with support," said Bernard Spilko, senior vice president of the New York branch of Bank Julius Baer.
 
No Shift by the Fed
 Mr. Greenspan said that while the economy showed no signs of revival, recent interest rate cuts should be sufficient, and that he was not inclined to relax credit again soon. Traders noted that he had said these things before, and they remarked it was more a disappointment than a surprise to hear them. After President Bush's State of the Union Message Tuesday night, many investors were expecting a shift by the Fed.
Volume on the New York Stock Exchange was heavy, at 249 million shares, up from 217.2 million on Tuesday, with losing issues outpacing gainers by 1,054 to 653. The Big Board composite closed at 226.64, down 2.31.
The Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks, another blue-chip indicator, fell to 410.34, down 4.62 points, while the American Stock Exchange index fell 3.07 points, to 409.64.
Traders said no issue was protected in yesterday's market, as trading on the Nasdaq lowered its composite by 4.98 points, to 616.31, and the Russell 2,000 to 203.87, down 1.11 points.
Birinyi Associates, a research firm that tracks program trading, said two large program-trade sales entered the market near the end. The New York Stock Exchange's 50-point rule went into effect at 3:32 P.M., restricting program trading.
The two sell programs combined gave the Dow a loss of 48 points, the research firm said.
Renewed economic concerns prompted investors to buy the stocks of companies with the most consistently strong earnings, traders said.
Most of the early gains in the Dow industrials came from Procter & Gamble, up 1 1/2, to 104 1/8; Merck, down 5/8, to 152 3/8; Philip Morris, down 1/2, to 75 3/4, and Allied-Signal, up 1, to 48 1/2. Allied was helped by a Morgan Stanley recommendation.
Before the market faded, drugs, health care, tobacco and household products were up the most on the S.& P. 500 index.
 
Declines for Military Contractors
 With the expectation that military spending is about to be cut, Boeing fell 3, to 50 5/8; General Dynamics 5/8, to 56 5/8, General Electric 3/4, to 77 3/8, and Northrop 1/2, to 25 1/2.
Elsewhere in the market, Roadway Services, a company engaged in trucking and leasing, rose 4, to 71 1/4, as Dean Witter Reynolds repeated a "buy" rating, traders said. On Tuesday, the company reported a rise in its fourth-quarter earnings.
Sun Microsystems fell 4, to 29 7/8, as the company told analysts that it planned to invest $45 million in the next few quarters to insure its position in the computer market.
Storage Technology rose a further 2 7/8, to 69 3/8, as Alex. Brown & Sons reiterated a strong "buy" recommendation. On Tuesday, the company said it had $150 million in commitments for a new disk storage system.

HEADLINE: BRITISH CHIEF SET FOR A CLOSE VOTE

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LONDON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
Like President Bush, with whom he will have dinner in New York on Thursday night, Prime Minister John Major of Britain has to face an election this year in almost the worst possible economic circumstances: a recession whose end is not yet in sight.
Times have changed since the Conservatives threw over Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher and the opposition Labor Party abandoned the radical left-wing policy positions of the early 1980's. For the first time since then, because of the economic mess, it does not seem like a foregone conclusion that Mr. Major and his party will win the next election. 
 That is why he has not yet set a date for it, a prerogative reserved in the British system to Prime Ministers who try to pick a time when they think they have the best chance of winning. But Mr. Major is running out of time, because the term of the current Parliament will expire in early July.
For months, the public opinion polls have shown Neil Kinnock's Labor Party and the Conservatives trading places in the lead, but recently Mr. Major's party has begun easing ahead, 41 percent or so to Labor's 39.
 
Volleys of Hot Air
 With unemployment at 2.55 million, 9 percent, and stillrising, even a slim lead seems good to the Government's supporters, and many are speculating that Mr. Major will announce, traditionally a month ahead of time, that the election will be held on April 9.
"It's going to be a bloody tough fight," the Conservative Party chairman, Chris Patten, said in an interview, though he said Mr. Major could also wait a little longer. He would have had a decent chance of winning last fall, Mr. Patten thought, but he waited because "the economic forecasters were saying the economy would be much better in the spring."
All three major parties -- the Conservatives, Labor and Paddy Ashdown's Liberal Democrats, who are showing support of about 15 percent in the polls and want a change in the election system to proportional representation, to give them more than the 22 seats they have now -- have long since begun their campaigns.
So far, Mr. Kinnock and Mr. Major have been trading volleys of hot words, since there's little either of them can do about the recession except to hope that, with inflation now under control, things will finally start turning up.
 
Clashes of the Past
 Gone are the thunderous clashes between Thatcherism and union-dominated "socialism." With the victory over Argentina in the Falkland war in 1982 and the cold war now only a distant memory, Conservative championship of a strong defense rings hollow, and Labor gave up its disastrous policy of unilateral British nuclear disarmament nearly three years ago.
Now it's Mr. Bush and President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia who are outdoing each other in unilateral nuclear disarmament, and Mr. Major is saying "Not us, yet."
He welcomed the latest American proposals in a statement issued after Mr. Bush's State of the Union Message on Tuesday night, and his spokesmen welcomed most of Mr. Yeltsin's today, but said Britain would go ahead with plans to replace most of its old nuclear deterrent with four American- designed Trident missile submarines, using British warheads. Labor says three boats will do.
Whenever the formal four-week election campaign begins, it is expected to focus on the personalities of the leading contenders for the Prime Minister's job. The imagemakers will cast Mr. Major, a plainspoken man from London's middle class, against Mr. Kinnock, a more orotund speaker from a Welsh working-class background.
 
Trading Billboards
 But there has not been much real debate about those ideas in the campaign so far, nor about how to manage better British economic growth.
Instead the parties have been throwing slogans at each other, and putting up silly billboards. Labor will drop a tax bomb on you, the Conservatives tell the voters. Labor has said it would raise taxes and social insurance payments gradually on those in the highest income brackets, from the present 40 percent to 59 percent, and lower them at the bottom end of the scale from 25 to 20 percent or below, but hasn't offered the details.
On Tuesday, Labor began putting up posters of Chancellor of the Exchequer Norman Lamont masked as "Vatman," saying the Conservatives would raise the value added tax imposed on nearly every purchase from its present level of 17 and a half percent to 22.
At Question Time in the House of Commons on Tuesday, asking Mr. Major what the Conservatives were going to do, Mr. Kinnock demanded "a straight answer to a straight question," and got one.
"There will be no VAT increase," Mr. Major said.
But Labor still described the Government's position as "fudged." VAT, which is like a sales tax, was only 8 percent on some items when the Conservatives took power in 1979.
Many Government services, from schools to subways, have become frayed at the edges, and Mr. Major's main idea for improving them is a "citizen's charter" giving taxpayers the right to demand better performance. The only real way of giving it to them, Labor counters, is to spend more public money instead of starving them and reducing taxes, as the Conservatives have done.

HEADLINE: The Federal Budget: The Home Front;
For the People of Groton, Peace Pays No Dividend

BYLINE: By GEORGE JUDSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: GROTON, Conn., Jan. 29

 BODY:
A Baptist minister walked into Mayor Catherine J. Kolnaski's office this morning and offered to say a prayer for her. She gratefully accepted.
Across southeastern Connecticut and Rhode Island, tens of thousands of Electric Boat employees and their families had awakened to news worse than all their years working in the boom-and-bust weapons industry had prepared them for. The cold war was won, President Bush had said, and their jobs were done. 
 He didn't simply want to slow down the Seawolf program, the next generation of nuclear attack submarines. He wanted to kill it.
In this corner of New England, where the nation's nuclear submarines have been built ever since the Nautilus program in the 1950's, the Seawolf represented not only the next generation of Electric Boat workers, but the next generation of employment. Electric Boat, its contractors and the businesses that depend on their employees make up 82 percent of New London County's $2 billion payroll.
 
Losing the Future
 Electric Boat has a backlog of 13 other submarines, including the last six Tridents, so there will be work here for at least a few more years. But the Seawolf was the future.
"It's pretty somber in there, pretty somber," Tommy Algiere, a machinist with 10 years at Electric Boat, said this afternoon as he waited in a parking lot for his brother and sister-in-law, who also work at the shipyard, for his ride home to Westerly, R.I.
"If you were smart," he said, "you'd quit now and go out and get a job while they're still there."
The budget released by the White House today proposes to kill two $2 billion Seawolf submarines that have already been approved, ending what only two years ago was to be a 29-submarine construction program. A first Seawolf is under construction at the Electric Boat shipyard on the Thames River here.
Congressional representatives from Connecticut and Rhode Island, where Electric Boat employs 4,000 people at Quonset Point, vowed today to restore those two submarines, and to fight for as many more as possible, to keep Electric Boat's yards open.
 
Victory and Then Defeat
 "The irony is that the first notice these people are getting from the peace dividend is a pink slip," Representative John F. Reed, a Democrat from Rhode Island, said at a news conference after he and Representative Sam Gejdenson, a Democrat who represents eastern Connecticut, met with union officials and local leaders.
Asked when Electric Boat would have to close its yards if the Seawolf were killed, a spokesman for the company, Neil D. Ruenzel, said that prospect was "unacceptable."
"Electric Boat is not going to lie down and die," he said. "We're going to fight."
Rumors of layoffs are a constant in the arms industry. Only last week, United Technologies, Connecticut's largest private employer, announced a reorganization that will eliminate some 6,400 jobs. Electric Boat, a division of General Dynamics, has also had its ups and downs. Employment, which peaked in the 1980's at 18,000 in Groton, is already down to 15,000, and is projected to hit 11,000 by 1996, even if Electric Boat wins more Seawolf contracts.
But the idea that the entire shipyard could be threatened, so soon after the glory years of the Reagan Administration's military buildup, has created enormous anxiety.
"The good news is that if Electric Boat gets one sub contract a year for the next five years and stays in business, we lose only 7,000 jobs," said William D. Moore, president of the Chamber of Commerce of Southeastern Connecticut. "If Electric Boat were not to get any submarine contracts, we're expecting a total employment loss of 27,000 by 1997, out of a county work force of 125,000."
For Groton, the loss of Electric Boat would be devastating. The shipyard is not only its largest taxpayer, but also the largest customer of its municipal utility, paying $9 million for water and electricity. A closed shipyard would saddle residents with both higher property taxes and higher electric rates.
 
Pride and Anger
 New London County is also home to the Navy submarine base, the Coast Guard Academy and the Naval Underwater Systems Center research laboratory, and all the businesses that depend on them. Today many people expressed pride in the region's military contribution and anger at how summarily it seemed to be ended.
But they were also frustrated that more had not been done to diversify the region's economy to make it less dependent on Pentagon spending.
Local chief executives have been meeting to discuss the issue, and groups have been formed to promote diversification, Mayor Kolnaski said.
Electric Boat itself has taken one step, with the help of a $1.5 million grant: starting a career transition center that began last summer by teaching laid-off workers how to look for new jobs. A second center, for non-Electric Boat workers, opened in January.
"We told the people last summer, they were among the lucky ones," said Richard Flath, a personnel consultant who helped train the people let go by Electric Boat. "If a couple hundred people are laid off, that's one thing. A couple of thousand people, that's another. This is a small area."

HEADLINE: Occidental Posts Profit

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Jan. 29

 BODY:
The Occidental Petroleum Corporation today reported a fourth-quarter profit of $6 million, compared with a $2 billion loss a year earlier when it began remaking itself after its former chairman, Armand Hammer, died. The quarterly profit equaled 2 cents a share. The results came on revenues of $2.6 billion, down 25 percent from $3.5 billion in the final period of 1990. The lower revenues reflected the sale of some company assets. Occidental's chairman, Ray R. Irani, said the company had nearly completed its restructuring and had reduced its debt by $2.6 billion of the target $3 billion. He said the weak economy and low energy prices had hurt the Los Angeles-based oil giant.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Voters;
Patch of Mid-America Not Impressed by Bush Talk

BYLINE: By ISABEL WILKERSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: OAK PARK, Ill., Jan. 29

 BODY:
Theoretically, this quaint patch of middle America should be a Republican President's dream. It is 4.5 square miles of well-educated, white-collar, voting literate types who read the Op-Ed pages, who can cite Keynesian theory and who keep up with the minutest changes in the tax law.
When President Bush speaks of lowering the capital gains tax or making it easier to withdraw from individual retirement accounts, he is talking to Oak Park. 
 And so, during perhaps the most important speech of Mr. Bush's term, nearly every television set on the 200 block of South Elmwood Avenue was tuned to the State of the Union Message. And those who were not watching were listening from their car radios on their way home from the office.
Joseph Norton, a partner in a Chicago law firm, was looking for corporate tax incentives for job training in a field where he says skilled support jobs go wanting. Across the street, Karla Nacion, a nurse, was looking for a national health care plan.
What they heard, they said, was vague and predictable and not particularly reassuring. Many said they turned off their television sets feeling as bewildered about the future health of the economy as they had when the evening began.
"Platitudes," muttered William Brace, a college professor, as he sat on his sofa watching President Bush talk of lifting the nation out of hard times "inch by inch."
To look at the 19th-century victorians and neatly shoveled walks on Elmwood Avenue, one would never know there was a recession in the country, and most people's anger about the economy, while pointed, is mostly second-hand.
But even in this well-to-do suburb of 53,000 people where everyone has food on the table and the luxury of worrying about the Amazon rain forest, the troubles of the economy have hit home.
Mrs. Nacion's husband, Robert, a pharmacist, was astonished to find himself out of work for much of last year in a field where he thought employment was assured. After quitting his job because of its poor hours, it took him seven months to find another one.
Rand Gerald moved in with the Braces last May after he lost his job as a systems engineer in a company takeover. He said he had thought he would be staying a couple of weeks. But it has been seven months and he is still there and still jobless.
The day after watching the President's address, people like Mr. Gerald and Mr. Nacion said the economic picture looked no better.
"My expectations are lower today than they were yesterday," Mr. Gerald, 46 years old and a lifelong Republican, said. "It sounds as if it's going to be political business as usual. The Democrats and the Republicans are going to spend the next six months fighting each other. They're all basically the same. Neither side is looking out for what's best for the country."
Those who have managed to escape the tendrils of the recession say they feel its effects through young relatives looking for jobs or clients pulling back. They also say they are concerned that the recession may reach them yet.
Some residents say they worry that their children may not be able to live as well as they do. Many of them say their children cannot afford to buy a house in their own neighborhood, where the average house costs $170,000.
"The economy is dead in the water," Mr. Norton said while watching the President on television. "I want to give the man credit for having a positive approach. But I have the feeling he's another Mike Bilandic. That's the Chicago Mayor who sat in his office and tried to tell people it wasn't snowing."
That is not how his neighbor, Paul Nowicki, saw the speech. Mr. Nowicki, a 22-year-old solicitor at an investment banking company who lives with his parents, said he felt good because at least he has a job. Where his neighbors saw shameless flag-waving Tuesday night, he saw hope and optimism.
"I know the President was trying to bring back the memories of past wars and bring out that red, white and blue spirit," Mr. Nowicki said. "My biggest fear is that Congress is not going to cooperate with him."
 
Support for Military Cuts
 The President generally found support among Democrats and Republicans, those hurting and those thriving, for proposing limited cutbacks on military spending and corporate tax incentives for research and development and equipment purchases.
But most people doubted that quick fixes would be enough. Those with children said a few tax credits or exemptions would not make them change their spending habits and were generally leery of what they consider economic patchwork.
"He's still doing what Reagan was doing, and that's how we got where we are," said Mrs. Nacion.
Mr. Brace could not keep from yelling "balderdash" at his television set as the President congratulated himself and the country on winning the cold war. "We've got five new nations that have atomic bombs," Mr. Brace said. "This is an improvement?"
Through the more than 50 minutes of televised speech and applause, people on Elmwood Avenue kept looking for glimpses of the education President or the kinder, gentler President promised in the last election. They said they could not find him.
"Not one word about the homeless," said Barbara Mullarkey, a home economist and writer.
Many residents said the speech had more sentiment that substance.
"It was a well-crafted speech and had all the emotional buzzwords, but I don't feel he's added anything," said Mr. Brace's wife, Pamela, who runs a secretarial service company. "He just woke up to the fact that we have a crisis. Now I think we're headed in the right direction, but not fast enough. We need a Band-Aid until we can have surgery."

HEADLINE: Fed Chief Won't Back Tax Cuts

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
Saying he expected the economy to recover in the second quarter, Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve, told Congress today that he did not think the White House and Congress needed to cut taxes to lift the economy.
A day after President Bush disclosed a tax-cut package to stimulate the economy, Mr. Greenspan, the Government official with the most influence over the economy, told the Senate Banking Committee that he feared that all the competing stimulus proposals now circulating on Capitol Hill would be added together and end up pushing up the budget deficit and long-term interest rates. 
 He commended the tax-cut proposals made by the President in his State of the Union Message on Tuesday for being restrained but he clearly said he did not think it wise to try to enact a tax-cut package at all.
 
Turmoil in Markets
 Mr. Greenspan insisted, as he has in other recent Congressional testimony, that the Federal Reserve's recent interest rate cuts should suffice to pull the economy out of recession.
His remarks created turmoil in the stock and bond markets as traders' hopes for still lower interest rates appeared to fade. The blue-chip Dow Jones industrial average lost a midday gain and tumbled to close down 47.18 points. Bonds did a similar turnabout, ending the day with a large selloff. [Pages D12 and D18.]
Speaking in his customary circumspect, non-confrontational manner, Mr. Greenspan took pains not to criticize the President's proposals in particular. On Jan. 10, in his last testimony before Congress, Mr. Greenspan said the Federal Reserve's recent interest rates cuts were enough "by any historical standard" to move the economy forward. Today he said "this economy can move out of this extreme lethargy with monetary policy alone."
 
Forecasts an Upturn
 When Senator Richard C. Shelby, a Democrat from Alabama, asked him whether he foresaw an upturn in the second quarter, the Fed chairman said: "Yes, I do. I think my best judgment as to the forces that are now beginning to emerge suggests that."
He cautioned that his forecast was tentative. Indeed, in the middle of last year, he predicted a substantial upturn that failed to materialize. He said today that this recession was somewhat unpredictable because unlike previous postwar downturns, real estate values remained stubbornly low in many states and many banks remained reluctant to lend money. Still, he said, "as far as I'm concerned, I would expect the economy to quicken its pace."
Mr. Greenspan, who is 65 years old, was appearing at a hearing on his nomination to a second four-year term. Confirmation is widely expected.
Although the tone remained respectful, Senator Donald W. Riegle Jr., a Michigan Democrat, led a barrage of criticisms from Democrats and Republican committee members, who said the Federal Reserve's interest rate cuts had been too little and too late to help lift the nation out of recession. The Fed most recently made a significant cut in interest rates on Dec. 20 in an effort to give a real spark to the economy.
 
'Need Something More'
 "We need something more than we've already done in the way of monetary policy," Senator Riegle said. "I am very concerned about waiting any longer to see some bounce."
But Mr. Greenspan said he feared that new interest rate cuts could backfire by raising inflationary fears and long-term interest rates.
Borrowers continue to face a shortage of credit, he said. But he added that the shortage might begin to ease soon because the amount of loans not being repaid at banks has begun to "flatten." He said banks were wary to increase loans until they were confident the loans on their books would stop hemmorrhaging.
In response to repeated questions from the senators on his views about using tax cuts and other fiscal means to stimulate the economy, Mr. Greenspan said his big concern was that such measures would expand the budget deficit. The deficit, he said, was cutting into private savings and investment.
"The concern that I have is that, in the process of trying to implement any of these programs into a general agreement that can pass both houses," he said, the Congress and the President would not average the programs but would "add them up, and that would concern me because I do think that would create a long-term problem for the economy, which would immediately be perceived in the markets, which would move long-term interest rates back up."
Trying to moderate those remarks, he praised President Bush's tax-cut proposals for being restrained and added: 'My main concern about a limited package is it will not remain limited. If it were limited, then I really have no strong objections toward it."
In testimony on other topics, Mr. Greenspan criticized proposals to allow banks to reduce their capital ratios, suggesting that such a move would weaken banks and violate international agreements. Many bankers and legislators have called for weakening capital standards because they believe it will encourage banks to lend more and help the nation out of recession.
But he called on Congress to make it easier for banks to increase their capital. He specifically backed the Bush Administration's proposals -- which Congress rejected last year -- to allow banks to operate across state lines and to engage in commercial and investment banking. He said these measures would help strong banks increase their capital by making it easier for them to increase profits and sell equity.
He said that a tax credit of up to $5,000 for first-time home buyers, which President Bush proposed in his speech Tuesday, should spur homebuilding -- and perhaps raise depressed real estate values.
The only two proposals made by the President that Mr. Greenspan singled out for praise were lowering the capital gains tax and restoring certain tax breaks to help real estate developers. Mr. Greenspan has supported both in the past.
Mr. Greenspan said he did not see how commercial banks and insurance companies could get back to full strength and fulfill their role in helping the economy "until this real estate issue is resolved."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Alan Greenspan, right, chairman of the Federal Reserve, talking yesterday with Senator Donald W. Riegle Jr. before a hearing on Mr. Greenspan's nomination to a second four-year term. (Jose R. Lopez/The New York Times) (pg. D2)

 BODY:

The Economy
Southern California's air-quality regulators said they plan a radical new approach to controlling air pollution that will allow companies to purchase the right to pollute if they cannot or choose not to reduce emissions on their own. [Page A1.] 
 No tax cuts are needed, Greenspan told Congress, saying he expected the economy to recover in the second quarter. He reiterated that recent Fed rate cuts should suffice. [D1.]
The economy all but came to a standstill in the final quarter of 1991, with the G.D.P. edging up 0.3 percent after two quarters of small advances, the Commerce Department reported. The report confirmed neither the fears of Americans of renewed recession nor the Administration's hopes of early recovery. [D1.]
In an election-year budget of $1.52 trillion, Bush called for modest increases in many popular domestic programs, but avoided any big change in spending priorities. [A1.] The budget is essentially Bush's campaign manifesto. News Analysis. [A1.]

HEADLINE: Crises Surmounted, Balloon Ready for Global Trip

BYLINE: By MALCOLM W. BROWNE

 BODY:
For six anxious hours Tuesday night, a platoon of technicians, engineers and master balloonists in Akron, Ohio, wrestled with cables, machinery and tank trucks of helium gas as they inflated Earthwinds, whose crew hopes to be the first in history to circle the earth in a balloon.
After surmounting several heart-stopping technical crises, the team pronounced the $5 million balloon inflated and nearly ready to begin its perilous voyage. 
 The empty lifting balloon, made of gossamer-thin polyethylene film, was first unpacked from its crate and laid out on the ground outside the huge Loral Defense Systems Air Dock, where components of the complex Earthwinds flight system are being assembled. With a team of meteorologists watching for any change in the weather that could produce dangerous gusts, the technicians opened valves that sent 220,000 cubic feet of helium roaring up two flailing fill tubes to the top of the balloon.
Near midnight, the balloon's lift approached 20,000 pounds and it began tugging skyward. But by then the big tractor holding it down, a 30-foot-high front-end loader, was struggling to stay in place. Technicians loaded tons of concrete and scrap steel into the tractor's scoop, said Larry Newman of Scottsdale, Ariz., captain of the balloon flight, but the balloon still threatened to defeat the tractor's hydraulic system and pull the scoop upward. Only after technicians welded a steel beam between the tractor and the scoop, thereby immobilizing it, was the wayward balloon brought to heel.
 
Nine Feet to Spare
 Had the scoop been forced upward, the balloon tethered to it would have risen enough that it might have brushed against the hangar roof while being rolled in, possibly causing irreparable damage. As it is, clearance between the top of the balloon and the roof of the cavernous Air Dock is only about nine feet.
At another point in the inflation, a kink formed in one of the polyethylene fill tubes, and the crew feared that the tube might rip open under the pressure of the gas pouring from the tank truck. Just in time, the kink was straightened out.
As dawn broke yesterday, Earthwind's lifting balloon had been hauled into the Air Dock hangar to await launching, nearly touching the 200-foot high roof.
"It stands absolutely motionless -- elegant and shimmering," Mr. Newman said. "In all my years of ballooning, I've never seen anything so beautiful."
 
Flight May Begin Sunday
 If the weather is favorable, Earthwinds could begin its 22,000-mile flight as early as Sunday. Aboard, besides Mr. Newman, will be Maj. Gen. Vladimir Dzhanibekov, a decorated senior astronaut in the Russian air force, and Don Moses, an American builder, mechanic and pilot. The crew plans to ascend to 35,000 feet and enter the eastward jet stream which, at speeds that may exceed 200 miles an hour, is expected to carry them to the Arctic, across northern Europe, south toward the Persian Gulf, then northward again, across Russia and Siberia.
The innovative Earthwinds system consists of a lifting balloon, in which helium will expand at cruising altitude to 1.1 million cubic feet; a pressurized crew capsule with advanced communication and support systems, and a 100-foot-diameter "anchor balloon" suspended beneath the capsule. Compressed air pumped into the anchor during the flight will add weight to the system at times when the balloon would otherwise rise uncontrollably, forcing the crew to release precious helium.
With the launch imminent, the crew yesterday began stowing food and survival equipment, cleaning the craft's gasoline tanks, tightening seals and readying rigging cables. "It's been a long wait," Mr. Newman said, "but it's almost over."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Technicians, engineers and master balloonists in Akron, Ohio, inflated the lifting balloon of Earthwinds on Tuesday night. The craft's three-man crew hopes to be the first in history to circle the earth in a balloon. At rear is an air-filled ballast balloon, which will hang below the capsule. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: Transit Authority and Union Reach Tentative Accord on Contract

BYLINE: By ALAN FINDER

 BODY:
The Transit Authority and the union representing 32,000 New York City transit workers announced last night that they had reached a tentative agreement on a new contract that calls for a raise of 4.5 percent this year and an additional 2 percent next year.
Subway conductors and motormen, token clerks, bus drivers, maintenance workers and other unionized transit employees have been working without a new contract for nine months. The agreement is complex; it covers a total of 38 months and is retroactive to May 1991. 
 Among other things, the settlement includes a $1,000 payment to each transit worker to cover inflation since last May, said officials of the Metropolitan Transportation Authority, the Transit Authority's parent agency.
M.T.A. officials said the agreement would not affect the $1.25 subway and bus fare or the Transit Authority's $3.6 billion budget for 1992. They said the increased costs of the contract's first two years would be offset by savings from various contract concessions -- including changes in the wage scale and in compensation for workers injured on the job -- and savings in pension contributions.
 
Health Care a Critical Issue
 The tentative contract calls for additional negotiations on productivity savings, with transit workers to receive money in May 1993 if such savings are realized. The first portion of any productivity savings would go to the transit workers, up to an amount equal to 1 percent of the union's total wage package -- which would be about $14 million right now, M.T.A. officials said.
All savings beyond that would be split this way: one-third would be used to offset any deficits in the Transit Authority's budget; one-third would be used to enhance subway and bus service, and one-third would be go to the members of the transit workers' union as bonuses.
Sonny Hall, the president of the Transport Workers Union, Local 100, said in a statement last night that the union's executive board had approved the tentative contract. He said it could take as much as six weeks for the union's membership to vote on the agreement by a mail ballot.
Mr. Hall said that the most critical issue in the negotiations was health care, and that management had agreed to maintain existing levels.
 
Balancing Wage Increases
 "This contract was, by far, the most difficult and complicated labor agreement in my 15 years of contract negotiations," he said in the statement. "The economy as well as the operating deficit of the authorities presented severe pressure on the negotiation process."
Mr. Hall said he would not discuss any additional details about the agreement until his members had had the chance to see the tentative contract and vote on it.
The M.T.A. chairman, Peter E. Stangl, said in a statement that he believed the settlement was "fair to our customers, fair to our employees and fair to the taxpayers who support the M.T.A." The settlement must also go to the M.T.A. board for its decision.
Officials said the contract's net increase in cost to the Transit Authority would be 3.1 percent. Much of the cost of the wage increases would be offset by changes in the wage scale, in the way the Transit Authority pays workers who are hurt on the job and in recalculations by the city of interest rates that will result in savings in the authority's pension contributions.

HEADLINE: Yeltsin Tells Russians of Arms Cutbacks

BYLINE: By SERGE SCHMEMANN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Jan. 29

 BODY:
Within hours of President Bush's announcement on Tuesday of deep cuts in America's military spending, President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia went on television today to outline sharp arms cutbacks under way in the former Soviet armed forces.
Mr. Yeltsin's program was in large part a summary of military reductions either previously pledged by Moscow or necessitated by the economic and political crisis in what is now the Commonwealth of Independent States. 
 It included details of some arms programs that have been curtailed or cancelled by Moscow, such as production of strategic bombers and long-range cruise missiles. Mr. Yeltsin also said proposals were being readied to reduce strategic arsenals on the American and Russian sides to 2,500 or even 2,000 nuclear warheads each, well below levels required by existing treaties or proposed by President Bush.
In his address, Mr. Yeltsin also spoke for strategic weapons deployed in Ukraine, Belarus and Kazakhstan, underscoring the agreement among the former Soviet republics that their strategic weapons would remain under unified command.
 
Open to Further Talks
 Mr. Yeltsin further expressed readiness to discuss a wide variety of other reductions, ranging from a curb on development or elimination of various kinds of cruise missiles to a halt in submarine patrols to a joint global air defense system to replace the strategic defense initiative of the United States.
The United States is not likely at this time to discuss sharing the technology of the strategic defense initiative or curbs on submarine patrols, which have traditionally been central to American deterrence. But mutual interest in reducing arms spending could lead to various further agreements.
In an interview with ABC News on Saturday, Mr. Yeltsin said Russia would no longer target its missiles on the United States. In his televised statement today, the Russian leader said only that weapons remaining in the two countries "should not be aimed at American and Russian targets respectively."
Each president has praised the other's initiative and pledged to pursue the dismantling of cold war arsenals further when they meet Saturday in Camp David.
 
Yeltsin on Tour
 Mr. Yeltsin leaves Thursday on a trip that also includes visits to London, the United Nations and Ottawa. It will be his first major foreign tour since the Soviet Union collapsed and Russia assumed the bulk of the superpower's military legacy, and security issues are certain to be central in his talks.
To Western diplomats in Moscow, Mr. Yeltsin's address was significant less for any specific disarmament proposals than for the demonstration by the Russian president that he was now the heir to all the disarmament functions previously performed by President Mikhail S. Gorbachev.
Much of Mr. Yeltsin's address was in fact a reaffirmation of pledges made by Mr. Gorbachev last October, when President Bush and the Soviet leader both announced sweeping arms cuts, and of other obligations undertaken by Mr. Gorbachev.
 
Emphasizing Who's in Charge
 Earlier this week, Mr. Yeltsin made an unannounced visit to the Black Sea Fleet, which had been a bone of contention between Ukraine and Russia. Like today's speech, that visit was viewed in part as an attempt to affirm Russia's claim to the unity of the former Soviet military under the formal aegis of the Commonwealth of Independent States and under the specific jurisdiction of Russia as the primary heir to the former Soviet state.
The latter role will be underscored at the United Nations when Mr. Yeltsin appears before the Security Council, where Russia now occupies the permanent seat held by the Soviet Union.
Today, Mr. Yeltsin met with Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d, who is in Moscow for the Middle East peace conference. At a news conference later, both Mr. Baker and the Russian Foreign Minister, Andrei V. Kozyrev, confirmed that the statements by the American and Russian presidents had been discussed by them in advance.
"Both presidents have made far-reaching suggestions and far-reaching proposals that I think reflect the new political realities and changed circumstances and conditions with respect to relations between the United States and Russia," Mr. Baker said.
 
Communiques Less Grim
 Among the signs of the new realities was the fact that the coordinated statements no longer resembled the painstaking arms-controls agreements of past years, in which the two superpowers negotiated cautiously balanced arms reductions.
Instead, the speeches in Washington and Moscow were broadly coordinated statements of separate reductions prompted by the collapse of the Communist empire, coupled with some proposals for reciprocal gestures.
Russia in particular has been compelled to reduce arms procurement sharply because of its extreme economic crisis, and Mr. Yeltsin has already announced severe cutbacks in arms procurement to concentrate military spending on the social needs of soldiers.
"The leadership of Russia confirms its commitment to the policy of radical cuts in nuclear arms, insuring maximum safety of nuclear arms and all installations related to their development, production and exploitation," Mr. Yeltsin declared at the start of his televised address.
He said Russia was preparing an initative to create an international agency that would monitor nuclear arms reductions and eventually control the production of nuclear fuels and the dumping of nuclear wastes.
Mr. Yeltsin reported that 1,250 strategic warheads had already been removed from stand-by alert and that 130 intercontinental silos were being dismantled. Six submarines are being stripped of nuclear launchers.
In addition, he said, the Soviet Union was halting the production of two heavy bombers, the Blackjack and the Bear-H, and of long-range air- and sea-borne cruise missiles.
Expanding on Mr. Gorbachev's pledges in October to exceed limits set by the Strategic Arms Reduction Treaty, Mr. Yeltsin said Russia intended to reach agreed levels within three years, instead of the seven envisioned by the treaty.
Mr. Yeltsin also outlined reductions in tactical weapons, which both the United States and the Soviet Union have agreed to largely eliminate. He said Russia had stopped production of most short-range nuclear weapons and was reducing stockpiles of various weapons.
Mr. Yeltsin also reiterated that the armed forces would be reduced by 700,000, to 3 million.
Arms-control experts noted that this and some other steps reflected internal political and economic realities. Most Soviet submarines are currently in port, they said, Ukraine has insisted on removing nuclear weapons from its soil and intercontinental missiles in other republics were probably already off alert.

GRAPHIC: Photo: President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia yesterday announced sharp arms cutbacks in the former Soviet armed forces and said proposals would be made for further reductions in Russian and American nuclear arsenals. Muscovites watched Mr. Yeltsin as he made his televised address. (Reuters)

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS;
Coverage of Smoking Linked to Tobacco Ads

BYLINE: By DEIRDRE CARMODY

 BODY:
A new report by researchers at the University of Michigan says that magazines that carry cigarette advertising have consistently restricted or softened coverage of the dangers of smoking, possibly out of fear of economic reprisals by cigarette manufacturers.
The study, which appears in today's issue of The New England Journal of Medicine, examined 99 American magazines published from 1959 to 1986. Although other reports have offered anecdotal evidence about the relationship between cigarette advertising and the coverage of tobacco as a health issue, the new report is one of the most comprehensive statistical looks at the subject. 
 The researchers tracked annual cigarette advertising revenues for the magazines and correlated dependence on cigarette ad revenues with the presence or absence of articles on smoking and health in each magazine. They found that magazines that carried no cigarette ads were 40 percent more likely to have articles on the risks of smoking.
 
 
'Statistically Significant'
 "We have found that the higher the percentage of cigarette advertising revenues, the lower the likelihood that a magazine will publish an article on the dangers of smoking," said Prof. Kenneth E. Warner, chairman of public health policy and administration at the University of Michigan and an author of the report. "That is a statistically significant finding."
Even so, Mr. Warner said his research did not prove that advertising pressures were actually the cause of such modified coverage.
The journal of medicine article does not identify any of the magazines studied, though it does say that the correlation was highest among women's magazines.
Mr. Warner said a "wealth of anecdotal evidence" had convinced him that they were often influenced by advertisers. "Almost all the journalists who will speak to me off the record will acknowledge that this phenomenon exists," Mr. Warner said. "If they speak on the record, they will deny it."
 Donald Kummerfeld, president of the Magazine Publishers of America, disagreed. "It is not surprising at all that there is this statistical correlation between the editorial content of magazines that accept advertising and magazines that don't," he said. "Magazines that write about health, fitness and nutrition generally do not accept tobacco advertising and never have."
But the report specificially mentions women's magazines, many of which accept cigarette advertising and also cover health issues.
Of the 164 magazines tracked by the magazine publishers' group in 1991, 58 had no cigarette ads, 36 had fewer than 10 pages a year and 70 carried tobacco advertising.
Moreover, Mr. Kummerfeld asserted, the dangers of smoking have been so well publicized that most magazines no longer write about the issue. He also pointed out that tobacco advertising had declined.
In an interview yesterday, Grace Mirabella, founder and publication director of Mirabella magazine, said that when she was editor in chief of Vogue she had been asked to "soft-pedal a little bit" on health articles that mentioned the negative effects of smoking, but that she was never asked to withhold an article or alter it substantially.
Dr. Susan Okie, a physician and a correspondent for The Washington Post who now lives in Nairobi, Kenya, said she had had "a couple of run-ins" at Cosmopolitan when she was writing a question-and-answer column from 1980 to 1991. They came up in connection with her inclusion of smoking in a list of risk factors for heart disease.
"One time they sent me the copy to check because they didn't want to offend their advertisers, and we had a big fight," she said.
In addition to Professor Warner, the authors of the report are Catherine G. McLaughlin, associate professor of health services management and policy at the University of Michigan, and Linda M. Goldenhar, research fellow of the University of Michigan Institute of Gerontology.

HEADLINE: A Tiny Gain For Economy Last Quarter

BYLINE: By SYLVIA NASAR

 BODY:
The nation's economy all but came to a standstill in the final quarter of 1991 after two quarters of small advances, the Commerce Department reported yesterday. Neither fears of renewed recession nor the Administration's hopes of early recovery were confirmed by the report.
"Economic activity was essentially flat in the fourth quarter," said J. Antonio Villamil, the Commerce Department's chief economist. 
 The nation's gross domestic product -- goods and services produced inside United States borders -- edged up a tiny three-tenths of a percent in real terms in the fourth quarter after rising at 1.8 percent rate in the summer and a 1.4 percent rate in the spring.
 
Decline for Year
 For all of 1991, because of a first-quarter plunge at a 2.5 percent rate, G.D.P. was down seven-tenths of a percent. That was the first yearly decline since 1982, when G.D.P. fell three times as much.
"The economy has shifted into neutral, not reverse," said Roger Brinner, chief economist at Data Resources/McGraw-Hill.
The financial markets shrugged off the fall's statistically insignificant rise in G.D.P., which was about what the Blue Chip consensus of 48 economic forecasters had predicted earlier this month.
A spurt in exports and housing, Mr. Villamil said, basically offset a drop in spending by consumers, commercial builders and the Federal Government.
And a much-feared pileup in business inventories, which could have inflated fourth-quarter G.D.P. and touched off a correspondingly sharp production decline in the current quarter, turned out to be smaller than many economists had been expecting.
The report buttressed the view among a majority of economists that the economy hit bottom last spring. But until growth is brisk enough to ease unemployment and lift incomes significantly, most Americans are not likely to care about the technical distinction between recession and recovery.
"We're still in a growth recession, although we're not in a business cycle recession," said Geoffrey H. Moore, director of the International Center for Business Cycle Research.
G.D.P. has recovered a little more than half of the $78 billion -- in 1987 constant dollars -- drop from its pre-recession peak, and industrial production and business sales are above their levels last spring. But employment and income are barely higher.
"Anytime you have growth of less than 2.5 percent, some regions of the country and some industries are declining and unemployment is rising," said Alan C. Lerner, chief economist at the Bankers Trust Company.
 
Revision Likely
 In any case, the initial snapshot of the economy at the end of the year is likely to be revised at the end of February and again in March. And if the revisions fall within the range of past adjustments, the Commerce Department's release said, the final estimate could show G.D.P. rising at a 1.3 percent annual rate or slipping as much as two-tenths of a percent.
The brightest spot in the report -- from the point of view of consumers and investors, anyway -- was a sharp decline in inflation to a 1.7 percent annual rate, a rate reminiscent of the Eisenhower era. Lower inflation, among other things, helped lift Americans' after-tax incomes a tad in real terms. And saving, which had dipped last summer when consumers were still relatively optimistic, picked up too.
But when it came to spending money, consumers, who account for two-thirds of the economy, were in a funk. Total consumer spending, which is divided almost equally between goods and services, dipped at a 1.1 percent annual rate. But a truer measure of the scared mood of consumers -- and retailers' distress -- was the steep cutback of spending on all kinds of goods, from clothes to cars and other big-ticket items. Americans spent more only on life's necessities -- housing, utilities and medical care.
Government, which accounts for a fifth of the United States economy, also turned more tight-fisted last quarter. Outlays by state and local government, where fiscal distress has been acute, were flat. Washington slashed spending on military and other goods and services at a 14.1 percent rate -- the second consecutive large decline.
 
Capital Spending Pared
 Business continued to pare back capital spending, for the fourth quarter in a row. But the modest decline, at a 2.4 percent annual rate, masked another steep drop in business building and a small rise, the second straight, on spending on new equipment.
Construction of new shopping malls and offices and factories has been hit hard by sky-high vacancy rates as well as by bankers who have lost their enthusiasm for real estate lending. At the same time, ferocious competition at home and abroad is spurring business, despite the squeeze on profits, to seek ways to spur productivity.
Inventories climbed in the fourth quarter after a year of deep cuts, rising at a $3.6 billion annual rate (in 1987 dollars). Much of that increase, said Edward E. Yardeni, chief economist at C. J. Lawrence, was unplanned. Detroit, for one, is planning to pare inventories on dealers' lots this winter.
 
Jump in Housing
 Housing, helped by the lowest mortgage rates in nearly two decades, jumped at a 10.6 annual rate, nearly matching its summer gain. Apartments, which are nearly as overbuilt as offices, are still slumping. But starts of single-family homes, now 41 percent over their low last January, have risen in each of the last three months.
The biggest lift for the economy, however, came from an export surge. The volume of exports rose at a 15.4 percent annual rate. "It's just sensational," said H. Erich Heinemann, chief economist at Ladenburg, Thalmann & Company. Overseas sales have been rising at double-digit rates for the last five years and America's trade deficit, not even counting the large and growing surplus in services, has shrunk by a third.
Imports fell at a 2 percent rate after pouring in at a 22 percent annual rate last summer, when retailers were still expecting consumers to keep spending. The decline in imports, which reflects the weakness in final sales, is probably one reason for the small increase in inventories in the fall.
"If imports had climbed the way they had been, they all would have ended up in inventories," Mr. Yardeni said. Foreign producers of consumer goods, in other words, are sharing some of the pain of declining sales.
Looking ahead, the Administration and most private economists are predicting, some say hoping for, a modest recovery this year. The Office of Management and Budget, which released the Administration's 1993 budget yesterday, is counting on 2.2 percent growth in 1992 and 3 percent growth in 1993.
"As far as I am concerned, I would expect the economy to quicken its pace," said the Federal Reserve's chairman, Alan Greenspan, before the Senate Banking Committee yesterday, "but I am spending a great deal of time watching rather than forecasting."
Neal M. Soss, chief economist at the First Boston Corporation, said, "The recovery is still six months away -- as it has been for a while." Mr. Soss and most other forecasters expect the economy to revive slowly, with the current quarter looking much like the last one.
Data included in the gross domestic product for the fourth quarter of 1991 follow, with all dollar amounts in billions at seasonally adjusted annual rates. Percentage changes are from the previous quarter at seasonally adjusted annual rates.

LENGTH: 890 words

HEADLINE: CHESTER JOURNAL;
Remembering an Embodiment of Small-Town Life

BYLINE: By CONSTANCE L. HAYS

DATELINE: CHESTER, Conn.

 BODY:
There is an emptiness lately in this waterside village steeped in its own past, where the trim 18th-century cottages seem plucked from the pages of a storybook and where immortality, at least in the architectural sense, does seem possible.
It is all because of one resident, James L. Grote, who died this month after living here for almost 101 years. A man blessed with a booming voice and a seemingly unquenchable thirst for public life, Mr. Grote became a municipal one-man band, holding an array of official and unofficial positions that ranged from fire chief -- a job he retained for almost half a century -- to front-row worshiper at the local Catholic church. 
 "He represented what we all think of small-town New England being, in a nice way," said Tom Perry, a fifth-generation resident who owns an antiques shop called One of a Kind. "I think everybody's still in shock that he's gone. I keep expecting him to walk through that door."
Mr. Grote was the town's postmaster for 35 years and served as fire marshal for 69 years. For 46 years, he was president of the Agricultural-Mechanical Society, which runs the Chester Fair, one of Connecticut's largest country fairs. And his life, in other ways, was a historical marker for the town: he was the first baby boy to be baptized at his church, was enrolled in Chester's first kindergarten class in 1896 and was a member of Chester High School's first graduating class in 1909.
Day to day, residents were accustomed to seeing Mr. Grote, steering his bright red Ford through the streets or walking briskly down the sidewalks. He was vigilant about inspecting property for fire hazards practically until the day he died, and he kept routines, like raising the American flag on a flagpole in front of his neat white cottage, just about as long.
His death has saddened the town, already huddled against the chill of winter and the disappointments of a battered regional economy. Chester is a place where crime is so occasional that the five local police officers all work part time, and problems like hunger and homelessness are all but nonexistent.
"It kind of makes you realize your own mortality," Mr. Perry said. "When you looked at Jim, you thought, well, maybe I can live forever."
In May, the town held a party for Mr. Grote at the Chester Meeting House, which predated the guest of honor by less than a century. Mr. Grote, the marshal, who for years traveled with a Dalmatian by his side, was greeted with two cakes supporting 100 flaming candles.
"I guess you take people who are around so long for granted," said Dawn Burr, president of the Chester Historical Society, which Mr. Grote also headed at one time but for just two years. "He was there in evidence. You'd meet him at the post office. If you went into Robbie's, he'd be there having a cup of coffee. He loved to talk about himself, and you really didn't mind because he was such an interesting character."
Mr. Grote, who participated in a bucket brigade as a schoolboy, helped form the Chester Hose Company in 1912. The fire company, now in a modern brick building, still maintains a red hose cart, the kind once pulled to a burning building by horses, that was bought by Mr. Grote in 1924.
In Mr. Grote's lifetime, Chester saw many an industry come and go. Now a popular weekend destination for New Yorkers, the town, which is on the western bank of the Connecticut River, clings fiercely to its independence.
Residents are pleased that they are not deluged with tourists like nearby Essex and will tell outsiders proudly that Federal judges and prominent television journalists have houses here, but then they refuse to say exactly where. The town is popular with artists and is home to several well-known groups, including the National Theater of the Deaf.
When the town was incorporated in 1836, Pattaconk Creek, flowing through its center, was deep enough that tall ships could sail straight up to Main Street. That made Chester a manufacturing center for items made from ivory, like knitting needles, hooks-and-eyes and other essentials of 19th-century life.
Although most of those factories have disappeared, Chester until recently remained prominent for knitting-needle production as the home of the Susan Bates Company. But the company, a division of Coats Viyella, has announced plans to move to South Carolina in March, meaning a 118,000-square-foot office and factory complex on the edge of town will be vacant. About 75 jobs will be lost in Chester, said Dennis Tar, the company's general manager.
The complex is not the only building to be emptied this year. The Chester Bank's longtime headquarters, a yellow brick miniature Monticello, is locked up and for sale. The bank was absorbed into another bank that has branches elsewhere around town, said Bettie Perreault, the First Selectman of Chester.
And front yards bristle with "House for Sale" signs, evidence that some of the weekend dwellers, as well as year-round residents, are having trouble making ends meet.
Knowing the history of the town makes some people here optimistic.
"I remember people 20 years ago saying Chester is dead, it's never coming back," Mrs. Perreault said. "But I think this area will probably rejuvenate early, simply because it is a wonderful place to live." Even if one doesn't make it to 100.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: New Hampshire;
For Roomful of Faithful, Bush's Talk Meets Need

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LACONIA, N.H., Jan. 29

 BODY:
The blue Bush placards and brochures were stacked in the kitchen, and the 20-odd people gathered in Newbold and Nancy LeRoy's living room to watch the State of the Union Message Tuesday night were all die-hard Republicans, ready to give the President the benefit of the doubt -- several doubts, in fact.
But as Mr. Bush started to speak, even in this group, not at all enamored of insurgents like Patrick J. Buchanan or Democrats like Bill Clinton, there were a couple of gibes at the image on the television screen. 
 "He looks nervous," someone said.
"Oughta be," another replied.
When the President remarked good-naturedly that he wished he could have his wife, Barbara, deliver his speech, a man sitting on the floor commented: "Now he should sit down. He was joking. I wish he was serious."
But after that, everyone watched attentively. Times are bad in New Hampshire, as the whole country knows by now, and the people who had braved the LeRoys' icy driveway wanted to hear every word. Only whispered asides and a couple of whoops of delight -- one when Mr. Bush said he would make interest on student loans tax-deductible -- broke the hourlong quiet.
 
Party Foot Soldiers
 Nancy LeRoy conceded, "We're not typical voters in this room." But they were precisely the kinds of loyal party foot soldiers whose enthusiastic support Mr. Bush needs to retain if he is to convincingly beat back Mr. Buchanan's stiff challenge in the primary election on Feb. 18.
At the end, all agreed that the President had given a good speech and all expressed hope that he had got through to the state's voters.
"I think he's touched on most of the things that have been wrong," said Peter Karagianis. Mark Thurston, who operates a marina on Lake Winnipesaukee, said Mr. Bush had made "legitimate, usable, tangible shifts" that would appeal to the hard-pressed people of New Hampshire.
But there was a bit of New England stoicism in Mr. Thurston's reaction, too. Unlike some, he clearly did not expect the moon from Mr. Bush.
"Look, you heard the man talking about cutting the military back further, and that's good," he said. "But that obviously hurts our region. So it's an inevitability, this contraction we're going through, and we'll just have to get to the other end. It's not much fun, but then. . . ."
And there was also some New England skepticism on display as well.
 
Puzzled by the Timing
 State Representative Carl R. Johnson of Meredith said he doubted that the President's proposals would have much impact here by November. He said he wondered why Mr. Bush had waited so long, "why he didn't make these decisions six months ago."
The reason, suggested Ed Covino, a hotel manager, was that the President had been preoccupied with the war in the Persian Gulf and the revolution in the Soviet Union. That was fine with him, Mr. Covino said, adding: "It would have been very foolish for any President not to have taken a once-in-a-lifetime shot at making the world a safer place for us and our kids."
Mr. LeRoy, a civil engineer who, like Mr. Bush, went to Andover and Yale, though a good bit later, said, "I'm a little bit dubious of George's capacity to influence Congress."
 
Some Want a Hard Line
 So what should Mr. Bush do? Some, like Gov. Judd Gregg, who said in an interview the other day that he hoped the President "will put his thumb in Congress's eye," advocated a hard line.
Mr. Thurston said, "He'll have to call the Democratic leadership to blame for this stagnation," and Francis LeBranch, applauded Mr. Bush for "drawing a line in the sand here."
But "they won't pass his program," Mr. LeBranch said, because that would help "keep the Republicans in the White House for another 20 years." After March 21, Mr. Bush's deadline for Congressional action, he said, "They'll keep wrangling, and the people will get more disillusioned."
Bob Powers, who runs a scenic railway near the lake, said he hoped the President and Congress would work together, so he welcomed Mr. Bush's appeal for bipartisan cooperation with the Democrats to put the economy right.
 
'You Got to Compromise'
 "In your marriage, your business, with your kids, you got to compromise," Mr. Powers added. "Same thing here. I like his saying, let's pull together the way we did in Desert Storm. George Bush has never been an extremist, not a reactionary, not from the far right or the far left."
Gail Thomas, who teaches at the New Hampshire Technical College in Laconoia, said many of her students, some of whom are unemployed adults seeking new skills, "simply can't afford to go to the doctor or to take their kids." She described herself as "middle class or lower middle class or something -- we keep slipping" -- and said that she and her husband had trouble paying their own medical bills when their insurance did not cover everything.
"I don't want a Federal program," she said, "but I need to hear more from the President. Tax credits are fine, but still, where do poor people find the actual cash, money in hand, to pay for their health care?"
There were other questions as well. But at least in one living room in the shadow of the White Mountains, Mr. Bush seemed to have stilled most of the apprehensions of his backers at a crucial moment in his Presidency.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
McDonald's Profit Up 8% In Quarter

BYLINE: By EBEN SHAPIRO

 BODY:
The McDonald's Corporation said yesterday that profits increased 8 percent in the fourth quarter as it benefited from discount pricing in the United States and healthy sales overseas.
McDonald's, the world's largest restaurant operator, said fourth-quarter profits increased to a quarterly record for the company of $200 million, compared with $186 million for the corresponding quarter last year. Earnings per share were 54 cents for the quarter, compared with 51 cents a share a year earlier. Revenues rose to $1.73 billion from $1.70 billion. 
 Systemwide sales, which include sales of franchises along with company-owned stores, grew 7 percent for the quarter to $5.1 billion.
 
Indiana Jones Promotion
 In the United States, where McDonald's business has been sluggish, consumers responded to the company's lower prices on basic hamburgers and other products, the company said. Also, the company's Indiana Jones videotape promotions attracted customers in December.
The company said operations in a number of countries reported "excellent results" in spite of the impact of changing foreign currencies.
Results were particularly strong in Japan, France, Germany, Hong Kong, Australia and across Latin America, the company said.
Analysts said the latest results indicated that the company might be turning the corner. "This is probably one of the best quarters they've had in a year or more," said Richard Pyle, an analyst with Piper, Jaffray & Hopwood Inc. in Minneapolis.
Edward H. Rensi, president of McDonald's U.S.A., said that several new products, including breakfast burritos, were also beginning to pay off.
 
Possible Bellwether
 Restaurant sales are closely watched because they are viewed as an indicator of the health of the economy. Mr. Rensi said, "Let's hope this is a bellwether of what's going on in the United States economy."
Overseas sales, which now account for about 40 percent of McDonald's sales, may exceed 50 percent by 1995, said Rebecca Barfield, an analyst with the First Boston Corporation. "I compare McDonald's to Coca-Cola 10 years ago," she said. Nearly 80 percent of Coke's profits now come from its business overseas.
In the long run, Ms. Barfield expects the company's overseas business to grow 22 percent a year, compared with 7 percent a year for McDonald's domestic business.
McDonald's stock fell $1.25, to $42.25, in trading on the New York Stock Exchange.

SECTION: Section A; Page 15; Column 3; National Desk

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Republicans;
Buchanan Says Bush Stole His Ideas

BYLINE: By STEVEN A. HOLMES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MANCHESTER, N.H., Jan. 29

 BODY:
The conservative columnist Patrick J. Buchanan, seeking to embarrass President Bush in this state's Feb. 18 primary, today accused Mr. Bush of stealing his agenda in his State of the Union Message and called on the President to debate him.
As he has done often since he announced in December that he would seek the Republican Presidential nomination, Mr. Buchanan said Mr. Bush had betrayed conservative principles while in office. 
 While crediting Mr. Bush with giving a "well-crafted and well-delivered" speech, Mr. Buchanan accused him of "a little bit of intellectual property theft" and said the President had belatedly returned to such conservative themes as cutting taxes, reducing government regulation and freezing Federal spending, themes that Mr. Buchanan has espoused throughout his campaign in New Hampshire.
"For three years, Mr. Bush has governed as a Rockefeller Republican, as a liberal Republican," Mr. Buchanan told reporters here. "Now he's going to run again as a conservative."
 
Any Time, Any Place
 Mr. Buchanan said he would travel anywhere in the country to debate the President, though he acknowledged that such a confrontation is unlikely.
The challenger, who plans to spend the bulk of the remaining days before the primary within New Hampshire, said the issue is Mr. Bush's credibility. He recalled that during the 1988 campaign Mr. Bush promised not to raise taxes, then accepted a tax increase as part of an agreement with Congress on the 1991 budget.
Mr. Buchanan said the President's speech on Tuesday night indicated that he "clearly intends to run in New Hampshire as a conservative for the last three weeks of the campaign, because he knows that is the political disposition of the Republican Party in New Hampshire. I don't think it's good enough, and I don't think the people of New Hampshire are going to buy this bill of goods a second time."
 
'Read My Lips'
 The question of the President's change on taxes has become the centerpiece of Mr. Buchanan's campaign in this state, which saved Mr. Bush's 1988 primary campaign by giving him a victory over the Senate minority leader, Bob Dole of Kansas, largely on the tax issue. In television and radio commercials that began running last week, Mr. Buchanan criticized Mr. Bush's reversal on taxes, using tape of the President making his now-famous pledge of "read my lips, no new taxes."
Mr. Buchanan hopes such a message will hit home in a state that has been battered by a prolonged recession that many voters blame on the President. While recent polls have indicated some movement away from the President and toward Mr. Buchanan, they also indicate difficulties for the challenger.
For example, in a telephone survey of 400 Republicans likely to vote in New Hampshire, 21 percent of those polled said they would vote for Mr. Buchanan, compared to 56 percent who favored Mr. Bush. Moreover, 27 percent viewed Mr. Buchanan unfavorably, a high unfavorable rating for a candidate. The survey, conducted for The Boston Globe and WBZ-TV of Boston, had a margin of error of plus or minus five percentage points.
"People in New Hampshire are very unhappy with Bush," said Gerry Chervinsky, president of KRC Communications Research, which conducted the poll. "But they don't like Buchanan."
 
A Moral Victory?
 Even so, Mr. Buchanan is relishing what he sees as one success of his campaign: pushing the President to the right. "There is no question that we not only have been driving the debate, we have been winning the debate," Mr. Buchanan said. "The proof of that is George Bush has moved crabwise in a conservative direction ever since we announced seven weeks ago."
The Administration's strategy seems to be to express regret over the tax increase and to highlight the President's call for a cut in capital gain taxes, levies imposed on the profits from the sale of stocks, bonds and real estate.
Jack F. Kemp, the Secretary of Housing and Urban Development, who was in the state today campaigning for Mr. Bush, said, "Only speaking for Jack Kemp, I believe the President regrets trying to make a deal with this liberal Democratic Congress."
"But the fact is he has come back to the American people and asked them in a call to arms to bring back the tax cut to help restore value to capital assets held by the American people, both fixed and financial," Mr. Kemp said.
