HEADLINE: Sordid Business Booms in Queens

BYLINE: By DONATELLA LORCH

 BODY:
Roosevelt Avenue by day is a whirl of traffic and shoppers, to the roar and clatter of the I.R.T. passing overhead.
Nightfall brings traffic and trade of another sort. As steerers walk the avenue openly soliciting clients, men emerge from cars, cabs and the subway and ascend to the low-rent, low-price brothel world that flourishes overhead. There, women -- many of them undocumented immigrants -- do business in warrens of sordid cubicles behind second-story windows painted black. 
 Twenty minutes by the Flushing line from Times Square, these brothels have proliferated so fast in the last few years that the police say they comprise the largest center of prostitution in the city outside Manhattan.
In part, their growth is a traditional downside of successful law enforcement. A crackdown on prostitution in mid-Manhattan in the 1980's simply pushed many of the houses to the other boroughs. But their concentration in the largely Hispanic and South Asian immigrant neighborhoods along Roosevelt Avenue in Queens is also a matter of entrepreneurship, economics and exploitation: brothel operators, most of them South Americans, taking advantage of low rents, proximity to Manhattan and a large population of poor and often lonely immigrant men.
"They did an excellent job in Manhattan," said Capt. Henry C. Roge, commander of the 115th Precinct in Jackson Heights, of the Mayor's Midtown Enforcement task force that was at the forefront of the decade-long crackdown on Times Square. "However, the problem has now moved to Queens. Obviously all the ingredients are here. We are fertile ground for the houses."
As the houses have flourished -- 40 of them along two miles of the avenue in Elmhurst, Jackson Heights and Corona, with perhaps 30 more on surrounding streets -- so have street drug sales, traffic congestion, robberies and shootings, law-enforcement officials said.
And in the last six months, amid complaints from community boards and local residents, the authorities have begun a crackdown against the houses. Last week, in what the police called one of the largest anti-prostitution operations outside Manhattan, a special task force conducted an all-night operation that involved more than 100 police officers, the Queens District Attorney's office and members of the Mayor's Midtown Enforcement Task Force.
Prostitution in Queens, in particular street-walking, is not a new phenomenon. Since the midtown crackdown, the more expensive Asian massage parlors moved almost entirely to Queens. But many have closed recently because of the recession, AIDS and law enforcement, said Norman Dion, chief of operations for the Mayor's Office of Midtown Enforcement. It is the Hispanic houses of prostitution -- far greater in numbers, more visible, cheaper and extremely mobile -- that have angered the local communities and proved much harder to close down, the police and residents said. Since December there has been a murder and at least one shooting in the houses.
"This has developed into an environmental issue," said Helen Sears, a Jackson Heights district leader and community activist. "It sends out a real message that there is a breakdown in law and order. It tells the residents: 'I do not have a safe corner.' "
Many of the clients live in the area and are lonely, single immigrants who have either left their families in their native country or are not yet married. Although American prostitutes work the houses, the police say, the vast majority are undocumented immigrants from South America.
The police say they believe the houses are backed by drug money and have an established network that supplies them with South American women, many of whom are supporting their children, who are kept in virtual bondage until they pay back their smugglers.
Ten to 15 women work in each apartment and prices are low -- $20 for 15 minutes, $1 for a condom, with a cold beer thrown in for customers who have to wait. Vacant apartments are easily available and cheaper than in Manhattan. When a house is closed, the police say, it reopens nearby within days. The women, who are paid about $10 an hour, rarely cooperate with police investigations.
 
'Business Is Booming'
 "Business is booming here," said Capt. Paul Quinn, commanding officer of the Queens public morals squad, adding that prostitution in Queens had increased by 300 percent in the last five years. "Here they cater to as many men as they can. We seem to have no problem arresting them. The problem is that there are just so many of them."
At the end of last week's all-night raid, 10 Queens houses had been raided by the police, 63 people had been arrested on charges related to prostitution and three houses had been legally closed. But many of the women were more exasperated than scared; the men were embarrassed. The second-floor apartment at 93-15 Roosevelt Avenue was like many others. Fifteen men, some naked below the waist, and 10 scantily clad women crouched on their knees shoulder to shoulder, their hands behind their heads as the police made the arrests.
The three-bedroom apartment had been illegally partitioned with plywood into seven narrow cubicles where there was barely room for a bed and a night table. Each table had Babywipes and lubricating jelly. Condom wrappers and soiled paper overflowed from the waste baskets. Exposed wires looped across walls, and each room had a space heater.
In the kitchen, beneath statuettes of the Virgin Mary and other saints, calendars were scribbled with women's names and the refrigerator was full of Budweiser. For every client, the madam punches a hole on the woman's card, at times setting a timer for 15 minutes. Some cards had as many as 15 holes.
Since the Queens crackdown began six months ago, nine houses have been legally closed and lawsuits filed against the landlords and tenants for health code, building or fire-code violations. But the police say enforcement is difficult. Too-few available officers has resulted in a 60 percent decrease since 1990 in prostitution arrests in Queens bringing it down to 400 people in 1991, Captain Quinn said.
One of the prostitutes at a house closed last week, 22-year-old Dana Fischetti, said she would probably work elsewhere for a while until the neighborhood calmed down. Ultimately, law-enforcement officials believe that the crackdown will only lead to further displacement.
"The procurers tend to move the women," said Chief Frank Biehler, commanding officer of the public morals unit. "They are business people. They follow the dollar."

HEADLINE: State Street Boston in Deal

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: BOSTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
The State Street Boston Corporation has apparently been selected custodian for $64.7 billion in assets from a California pension fund, the second largest in the country.
A State Street spokesman, George Russell, confirmed Tuesday that the bank had been selected as custodian for the California Public Employees Retirement System fund. 
 DeWitt Bowman, chief investment officer at the California fund, said the decision was made last week.
A pension custodian is responsible for holding the securities owned by the fund, a function that is kept separate from trading and investing the assets, to protect the pension beneficiaries.
"The contract has not yet been negotiated and signed with State Street, but we are in the process of doing it at this time," Mr. Bowman told The Boston Herald.
The pension fund, roughly equivalent to the gross national product of Hungary, is second only to the Teachers Insurance and Annuity Association College and Retirement Equity Fund. That fund has roughly $99.6 billion in assets.
Mr. Bowman said State Street, which currently holds custody of more than $1 trillion in institutional assets, won the bidding because it had the lowest price.
State Street edged out several other competitors, including Bankers Trust, Mellon Bank and the Boston Company, which has held the fund since 1987.
State Street also beat out two bids by the Chase Manhattan Bank and Morgan Stanley to take custody of the $8.5 billion in international assets owned by the California retirement system.
Mr. Bowman said the account review was based on a California law requiring the fund to open up the bidding process to financial services firms every five years.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
One-Time Charges Cause Du Pont to Lose $240 Million

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WILMINGTON, Del., Jan. 29

 BODY:
Du Pont lost $240 million in the final three months of 1991 because of one-time charges for cost-cutting and a recall of its fungicide, the chemical company said today.
The charges also contributed to a 39 percent drop in net income for the year. 
 The fourth-quarter loss came on sales of $10.05 billion. In the 1990 period, Du Pont had a profit of $468 million, or 69 cents a share, on sales of $11.18 billion.
Without the charges, the company's fourth-quarter earnings would still have been 43 percent lower than the period a year earlier. The company said today that the charges amounted to 70 cents a share.
For the year, Du Pont earned $1.4 billion, or $2.08 a share, compared with net income of $2.31 billion, or $3.40 a share, the year before. Sales were $39.52 billion, compared with $40.56 billion in 1990.
"We continued to see operating results affected by sluggish economies worldwide, resulting in weak demand and price pressures" for several Du Pont businesses, including chemicals, said Edgar S. Woolard Jr., chairman and chief executive of E. I. du Pont de Nemours & Company.
The one-time charges stem from the company's $1 billion cost-cutting program announced in July and from insurance payments related to the recall of Benlate, a fungicide.
Benlate was pulled from the market last March after an unusually large number of growers in Florida and other Southeastern states reported stunted plants and leaf damage after application of the chemical to control fungal diseases. Research showed that the problem could be the result of Benlate breaking down into other chemicals that damage plants when exposed to sunlight and heat.
On the New York Stock Exchange today, Dupont's shares slipped 50 cents, to $48.25.

HEADLINE: Some Cheers, Some Jeers for Bush Agenda

BYLINE: By JOHN HOLUSHA

 BODY:
Leaders of American business offered a range of reactions to the economic proposals in President Bush's State of the Union Message, and disagreed sharply over the pressure the President put on Congress to enact legislation in an election year.
"The investment tax credit and the research and development credits are good ideas," said James L. Koontz, president of the Kingsbury Corporation, a producer of machine tools in Keene, N.H., adding that he hoped the research and development credit would be applied to collaborative research. 
 "But there is a shadow over everything because most of it has to pass Congress," he continued. "The general reaction around here was that it was a great speech, but nothing is going to happen until next year."
But Jerry Della Femina, chairman of Della Femina McNamee, an agency in New York, disagreed. "I was more than a little bit disappointed," he said. "The speech failed to show us the way out of the economic mess. He would have made more progress in restoring confidence among the public if he hadn't passed the buck by blaming Congress."
John R. Molloy, a senior vice president of the Du Pont Company, echoed Mr. Koontz's sentiments. "We hope that in an election year that the President and Congress can find a way for bipartisan closure on these issues, but we are not confident," Mr. Molloy said. "A long debate and inaction will stall a turnaround. Uncertainty feeds on itself."
The President said he was acting to get more purchasing power in the hands of the public by reducing the amount of Federal tax withheld from the paychecks of workers. This plan, he said, could pump as much as $25 billion into the economy in the next year so that people can "pay for clothing, college, or to get a new car."
Executives at the auto makers, which have been in a deep sales slump and losing billions of dollars, said they liked the idea. "People have been scared," said Lee A. Iacocca, the chairman of the Chrysler Corporation. "They've put off buying high-ticket items like cars and homes that mean jobs. With interest rates as low as they are, the President's proposals may be just the shot in the arm this economy needs."
Harold A. Poling, the chairman of the Ford Motor Company, said, "The President's message points this country clearly in the direction it has to go, if we are to successfully rebuild and nurture a strong industrial base."
The chairman of the General Motors Corporation, Robert C. Stempel, joined his Big Three counterparts in urging Congress to respond to Mr. Bush's agenda. "He has pointed the way and Congress should give his program prompt and serious attention," Mr. Stempel said.
 
Bankers' Reaction
 Although Mr. Bush said he was directing bank regulators to end "overkill" in their evaluation of loans, which he said is causing a "credit crunch," some bankers reiterated yesterday that the low level of loans was due to slack demand.
"Consumers are not beating down the doors to borrow, except for refinancing mortgages," said Edward B. Brandon, chairman of the National City Corporation in Cleveland. "I'm not sure you can call it a credit crunch when people are not willing to borrow."
The President again raised the prospect of bank regulatory changes to lift old rules that bar banks from entering new businesses. Robert Dugger, a spokesman for the American Bankers Association, noted that Mr. Bush was unsuccessful last year in persuading Congress to allow banks to expand across state lines and to enter the securities and insurance businesses.
"After last year's battle, we are very wary of attempting to obtain meaningful reform from Congress in the near term," he said. He agreed with the President that fewer restrictions would promote economic growth, but he added that even if laws were changed, it would take years for the nation to benefit.
"I don't see bank reform providing any quick fix for the economy," Mr. Dugger said.
 
Markets' Reaction Noted
 Robert White, a vice president of First Interstate Bank in Los Angeles, noted the decline yesterday in both the stock and bond markets.
He attributed much of the reaction to testimony by the chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, Alan Greenspan, who said the economy would improve by midyear and that there was no immediate need to cut interest rates further. But Mr. White said the markets were listless anyway.
"There was certainly no euphoria in the financial markets today," Mr., White said. "You could say there was a lack of upturn because of the State of the Union. It was one of the better-delivered speeches in a long time, but the meat of it was not what people are looking for."
 
Attention to Real Estate Noted
 The President paid special attention to the real estate market, which has been slumping badly in many parts of the country. He proposed a $5,000 tax credit for first-time home buyers, a lifting of penalties on withdrawals from individual retirement accounts and an easing of rules that make real estate attractive to investors who are not developers.
George Puskar, the chairman of Equitable Real Estate Investment Management Inc., said he was pleased that real estate had been given prominent attention. "The fact that the President chose to mention real estate in the speech is recognition of its significant impact on the economy," he said. "I don't think that was clear until recently."
He said the proposed change in the tax treatment of real estate investors who suffer losses would help bring in new capital to ease the slump. "Where there is real estate that is good but troubled, it will add an element of people staying with their properties and working them harder if their losses can have some value."
 
Disappointment at Morgan
 Jackson Tai, managing director of J. P. Morgan & Company's real estate investment banking group said he was disappointed that Mr. Bush proposed few measures that would strengthen the value of the dollar. "I was looking for steps that would attract foreign capital, because you need foreign capital to get real estate going again," he said. "Foreign capital needs a strong dollar."
The credit for first-time buyers of homes could add as many as 100,000 housing starts this year, said David Shulman, head of Salomon Brothers' real estate research unit. Some housing starts planned for next year will be pulled into this year because of the special stimulus, he added.
But not everyone agreed. President Bush's plan "had some useful elements, but the problems go far beyond quick fixes," said John O'Toole, president of the American Association of Advertising Agencies. "Things like a tax credit for first-time homeowners is peanuts. It isn't going to get consumers spending again."
Much of the impact of the President's proposed changes aimed at home buying would affect inexpensive homes and apartments across the country, said Edward S. Gordon, a commercial real estate broker in New York. But he said it would take time to get new housing started, "so I am not sure it will have an immediate impact," he concluded.
 
Views on Health Care
 Executives of both health-care providers and insurance companies said they were happy that the President rejected both a nationalized health-care system and one that coerced employers into increasing medical benefits. But health-care providers said the President had not sufficiently addressed the needs of poor people.
"We are very pleased that the President came out strongly opposed to play or pay, under which an employer would have to offer a minimum level of benefits or pay a tax," said F. Peter Libassi, a senior vice president of the Travelers Corporation. "We support the President's plan to use public and private resources to expand health care."
But David A. Jones, chairman of Humana Inc., a chain of health maintenance organizations and hospitals, said the presentation glossed over the cost of providing health care to the poor. "If we are going to cover the poor people who aren't covered -- and I think we must -- we are going to have to tax ourselves as a nation in some way," he said. "All the politicians ought to admit that, but none of them seem willing."
The President's plan did not go far enough, agreed C. Thomas Smith, President of Voluntary Hospitals of America, a cooperative group with 860 hospitals as members. "His model stopped short of achieving the goal of universal access," Mr. Smith said. "We have to have tort reform, which he suggested, and reform of the small-group insurance market, but we also have to change the delivery system. Mr. Bush's remarks did not address that."
Leaders of high-technology companies focused on the proposed tax credits for investment and research and development, which they said were important to fostering technological development.
"The part I liked is that, at last, there is support for spending that is very research-and-development intensive," said Craig Barrett, executive vice president of the Intel Corporation, the big maker of integrated circuits. "It will promote the strengthening of our industry."
Bert C. Roberts, president of the MCI Communication Corporation, said, "I think it is important to lower the capital-gains tax to encourage equity markets to stop focusing on short-term investments and focus more on the long term."
The credit for research and development was also popular with David J. Rynne, chief financial officer of the Tandem Computer Company in Cupertino, Calif.
"We spend about 14 percent of our sales, about $300 million, on research and development, so it is an important economic issue for us and a competitiveness issue," he said.
The President's proposals disappointed companies that explore for oil and natural gas.
"President Bush talked about cutting the alternate minimum tax on accelerated depreciation, which affects steel, chemicals and motor vehicles," said Denise Bode, president of the Independent Petroleum Association of America. "But nothing he proposed will help drillers," she added, claiming that the oil industry had lost more jobs than any of those three industries.

HEADLINE: CONSUMER RATES;
Money Fund Yields Drop but Slide Slows

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH M. FOWLER

 BODY:
Yields on both tax-exempt and taxable money funds continued to decline, but at a slower rate, for the seven-day periods ended Monday and Tuesday, respectively, IBC/Donoghue's Money Fund Report said yesterday. .
"Both tax-exempt and taxable yields are in the process of bottoming out, having both dropped this week, but not sharply," said Walter S. Frank, an economist for IBC/Donoghue's. 
 The seven-day compound average yield for the tax-exempt funds dropped by a basis point, to 2.75 percent, and the seven-day simple yield also dropped a basis point, to 2.71 percent. A basis point is a hundredth of a percentage point.
In the same period, the 30-day compound yield fell sharply, to 3.21 percent from 3.57 percent, and the 30-day simple yield dropped to 3.16 percent from 3.51 percent.
The Connecticut fund with the top yield continued to be Fidelity Connecticut Municipal Money Market Portfolio, which reported a seven-day compound yield of 3.22 percent, down from 3.28 percent.
In New Jersey, the top fund once again was Fidelity Spartan New Jersey Municipal Money Market Portfolio with a yield of 3.26 percent, up one basis point from last week.
In New York, the top fund again was Salomon Brothers New York Municipal Money Market fund. It had a seven-day compound yield of 3.60 percent, the same as the previous week.
Assets of the tax-exempt funds, which had risen to a record of $98.1 billion last week, declined to $97.2 billion.
Donoghue's said the average number of days to maturity of securities held in the tax-free portfolios dropped by one day, to 51 days.
Of the funds monitored by IBC/Donoghue's with assets larger than $100 million, the Strong Municipal Money Market Fund, Milwaukee, continued to lead with a seven-day compound yield of 3.74 percent, down from 3.84 percent.
 
Taxable Assets Rise
 Meanwhile, assets of the taxable funds rose by $2.6 billion, to a record of $505.1 billion. Mr. Frank said $2.5 billion of the $2.6 billion increase came from institutional buying. The average days to maturity of holdings in the taxable funds dropped to 56 days from 57 days.
Mr. Frank said the taxable money fund yields were sensitive to Federal Reserve policy. "It is apparent we are coming to the end of the decline of short-term money fund yields," he said. "We would expect that institutions will tend to move out of the taxable funds and into Treasury securities and other open market investments."
The average seven-day compound taxable yield was 4.07 percent, down from 4.14 percent, while the seven-day simple yield fell to 3.99 percent from 4.05 percent.
At the same time the 30-day compound yield declined to 4.27 percent from 4.39 percent, and the simple yield dropped to 4.18 percent, from 4.30 percent last week.
The taxable fund with the best seven-day compound return for the week was Fidelity Spartan Money Market fund with a yield of 4.95 percent
By comparison, the average bank money market account, a prime competitor of money funds, was at 3.92 percent, according to The Bank Rate Monitor's national index.

HEADLINE: Florio's Whirlwind Makes Paramus 'Capital for a Day'

BYLINE: By JOSEPH F. SULLIVAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PARAMUS, N.J., Jan. 29

 BODY:
Gov. Jim Florio moved the state capital here for a few hours today in an exercise that was part civics lesson and part political road show.
After declaring the borough New Jersey's first "Capital for a Day," the Governor and members of his cabinet fanned out around to meet with officials and community leaders; visit hospitals, a veterans home, a nutrition center for the elderly and the town jail, watch police officers rappel down a five-story building at the Garden State Plaza and drop off a $110,000 check for local road improvements before meeting with residents at a town meeting. 
 Mayor Clifford Gennarelli, a Republican, was not impressed as much by the Democratic Governor's visit as he was by Mr. Florio's entourage. "It's more important that the Governor's staff is here, the movers and shakers," he said. "Instead of writing a letter to the Governor's office that is just one of 10,000, the residents have an opportunity to talk to real people about real problems and real solutions."
 
'Running for Re-election'
 The "Capital for a Day" event, which state officials said will be repeated almost monthly around the state, was billed as an effort to bring government closer to the people. Mr. Florio said he wanted to listen to people's concerns, but he also wanted to talk about his proposed budget and focus attention on his priorities before the new Republican majorities in the Legislature begin reviewing his spending plan.
"He's clearly running for re-election," said Paul J. Contillo, a Democrat who lost his Senate seat in November, when the Republicans took overwhelming majorities in both the Senate and Assembly. "What he is going to do is focus attention on his priorities and force the Republicans to respond, and that will take time."
The two general elections since Mr. Florio pushed through a $2.8 billion tax package in 1990 have been difficult for his party, and he appears to have taken seriously the criticism that he failed to seek a consensus on the need for the tax increases before he acted.
In a meeting with more than a dozen community leaders at the Paramus borough hall today, Mr. Florio said, "There are choices that have to be made, and my purpose is to hear from you and the citizens about how we sort out what is important.
"We must engage people to begin evaluating trade-offs," he said. "There are multiple answers to any problem, and we must find those that, on balance, make the most sense."
One such conflict was evident in the room, and Mr. Florio was quick to point it out. Frank Cikutovich, a retired insulation installer, has seen his property taxes double, to $5,600, in the last three years; he wanted the Governor to do more to hold down property taxes. Jacqueline Bishar Pieroni, the president of the Paramus Education Association, wanted him to ease the mandated spending limits on the school budget, but the Governor said that would raise Mr. Cikutovich's taxes.
Rabbi Neal Borovitz, president of the Bergen County Board of Rabbis, told the Governor that he is part of a group that provides meals for about 150 homeless people at a shelter in Hackensack that is in danger of closing. He asked the Governor what could be done to help, adding he was not looking for a promise of funds but for "moral leadership."
 
The People Speak
 Mr. Florio replied with a discussion of the recession, which, he said, had resulted in a "sea change" in the way people live and view the future. What is needed are programs to get the economy moving, the Governor said, adding that President Bush had failed to address the problem in his State of the Union Message on Tuesday. Rabbi Borovitz thanked Mr. Florio but said, "That doesn't put food on the plates of hungry people."
The Governor heard complaints about red tape that delays school construction and said the problem was being addressed. When he was asked to stop the New Jersey Supreme Court from making and enforcing laws from the bench, he agreed that a problem exists and said he would see what he could do about it.
Mr. Florio and his wife, Lucinda, had lunch at the Forum Diner on Route 4 with seven local residents who had written letters to the Governor's office over the last two years. With reporters and cameramen hovering over their table, the meeting produced no sharp exchanges or insights.
Susan Altenkirch of Hackensack, who was in the diner but not at the Governor's table, said she thought it was a good idea for Mr. Florio to get around the state. "He should have done it sooner, and should continue to do it," she said. "I still see the 'Dump Florio' signs, and I don't know if that's a fair image."

HEADLINE: California Proposal Would Let Industry Sell Pollution Rights

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Jan. 29

 BODY:
Southern California's air-quality regulators said today that they planned a radical new approach to controlling air pollution, allowing companies to purchase the right to pollute if they could not or chose not to reduce emissions on their own.
The new approach, which is still subject to formal approval, would supplant the current system of tight company-by-company regulation and would be the largest and most ambitious attempt yet to clean up the air by replacing regulatory fiat with free-market forces. 
 Specific elements of the plan have brought opposition from business, labor and environmental groups as the proposal has been drafted in recent months, but regulators said they were confident that the basic concept would be approved by the board of the regulatory authority, allowing it to go into effect by 1994.
Based on a long-debated concept known as emissions trading, the plan would dictate to polluters the amount by which they would have to reduce their emissions each year, but would not tell them how to do it. Businesses that developed techniques or technologies to reduce their emissions below the required levels would generate credits they could sell on the open market to other companies that were unable to meet the requirements or found it uneconomical to do so.
The idea is to reduce the costs to companies while providing them with an economic incentive to cut emissions as much as possible and to develop antipollution processes. The concept, long advocated by economists, was endorsed in the Clean Air Act passed by Congress last year, and has been used to limited degrees within particular industries. Utilities are moving toward adopting it in their efforts to reduce acid rain, but it has yet to be applied across an entire region or on a scale envisioned by the California plan.
Because the Southern California regulatory body, the South Coast Air Quality Management District, is extremely influential in policy circles throughout the country, many people involved in the issue said they expected the approach to spread to other regions with air-quality problems linked to factories, oil refineries, auto body shops and other stationary sources of pollution. The plan does not affect the regulatory approach to reducing emissions from automobiles.
 
'A Model for the Nation'
 "It's a tremendous departure in the sense that in the past the district has mandated regulations that specify a technology or a process or a housekeeping rule for how things should be done," said Joel Schwartz, a staff scientist for the Coalition for Clean Air, an environmental group. "Now the district is going to take its hands off and say that as long as you can prove you're getting real reductions you can do anything you want to reduce emissions, or you can buy permits from other companies that have proven they have reduced their emissions. This will be a model for the nation."
The air-quality district has been drafting plans for the shift for nearly a year against a backdrop of growing pressure by the business community and elected officials to reduce the industrial costs of complying with Southern California's strict air pollution regulations. The air-quality district for many years was resistant to what is called the "marketable permits system," but its executive officer, James Lents, said in an interview today that achieving further improvements in the region's air required a new approach.
"We have some of the world's strictest air-quality regulations in California, but it has not been enough to solve the air-quality problem here," Mr. Lents said. "We feel this will be a better system than the traditional command-and-control approach," he said, by giving businesses incentives to make additional improvements.
 
Further Debate Likely
 While there is general consensus that the board of the air-quality district will approve the proposal, there will almost certainly be changes to specific elements of it as public debate on the issue intensifies between now and March 6, when the board is scheduled to vote. There also appears to be a minority on the board that is not yet convinced the program will work.
"I have a real problem gambling on economic theory," said Larry Berg, a board member and the director of the Institute for Politics and Government at the University of Southern California.
Representatives of environmental groups have been pushing the regulators to adopt a go-slow approach. They advocate limiting the program in the beginning to sources of nitrogen oxides, such as oil refineries and utilities, which can be monitored and measured relatively easily and accurately. The groups want the regulators initially to retain their traditional tight regulation of sources of reactive organic gases, such as those contained in paints, coatings and solvents, which are far more difficult to track.
"We're concerned that the district not go forward with such a big plan and give up the whole regulatory structure until they've learned to make it work," said Mr. Schwartz of the Coalition for Clean Air. "They haven't proven to us that they'll be able to enforce the program."
 
Why Labor Groups Worry
 Labor groups are also expressing some reservations about the concept. They say they fear that by allowing companies to sell their pollution rights the system will reward companies that choose to close plants in the region and move elsewhere, taking jobs with them. So labor groups are pushing for limits on the ability of companies that are leaving the area to sell their credits.
"We don't want to deny companies the right to leave, but we don't want them packing off to Mexico with a sackful of money from selling their credits, either," said Kelly Candaele, a political representative for the Los Angeles Federation of Labor.
Business groups have generally supported the concept as a way of making it more economical to stay and expand their operations in California. Although some smaller companies have expressed fears that larger competitors might benefit inordinately from looser regulation, most industries see emissions trading as giving them greater flexibility in dealing with the basic regulatory requirement of reducing pollution by a mandated percentage each year.
 
Citing an Example
 A study conducted last month by the Regulatory Flexibility Group, a coalition of corporations in the Los Angeles area, cited the example of a small electronics firm that faced huge costs in reducing emissions of reactive organic gases from its semiconductor operations. To make the reductions on its own, the company would have to pay $200,000 per ton of annual emissions.
But a company that paints or coats ships spends only $2,000 a ton to reduce its emissions of the same gases. Under an emissions trading program, the electronics company might be able to buy pollution credits from the ship-painting company for a price much closer to $2,000 a ton than $200,000, sharply reducing its expenses.
As proposed by the air quality district, the plan would initially apply to the 2,000 companies in the region that account for 85 percent of the smog-forming hydrocarbons emitted by the businesses that the district monitors, and to the 700 businesses that account for 95 percent of nitrogen oxide emissions. The plan calls for smaller companies to be added to the program later, with only a handful of businesses, including restaurants and dry cleaners, likely to be exempted, Mr. Lents said.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Big Selloff in Treasury Securities

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Remarks by the Federal Reserve Board's chairman, Alan Greenspan, took precedence over other important developments yesterday, and a violent selloff in the secondary market for Treasury securities ensued after Mr. Greenspan said an economic recovery appeared likely in the spring.
Mr. Greenspan made his forecast in confirmation testimony before the Senate Banking Committee. His remarks wiped out what had been a positive early response in the credit markets to President Bush's State of the Union address and a preliminary report from the Commerce Department showing that the economy expanded only three-tenths of a percent during the final quarter of 1991. 
 Ever the cautious economist, Mr. Greenspan went out of his way to note the economy's unusual condition made predictions difficult. At the moment, he said, the Fed is doing more watching than forecasting.
 
'A Silly Reason to Sell'
 Nevertheless, "there is a general sense that the Fed is done easing policy for now," a government bond trader said. "That is a silly reason to sell securities, because that notion was already in the market. At this point, I think that prices are cheap."
Mr. Greenspan has made many of the same comments before, and some analysts acknowledged that they were a bit mystified at the market's severe reaction.
"Market reaction to Greenspan's testimony has been enormously overblown," economists at MMS International noted in their daily market commentary.
"We would argue that his remarks are merely supportive of the view that the Fed is on hold near term," they said, "but that the potential for further easing down the road still exists if economic data continue to depict a stagnant economy."
 
Losses Exceed a Full Point
 The selloff in the bond market, which amounted to more than a full point, or more than $10 for each $1,000 face amount of securities, was even more surprising in light of the Commerce Department's report on the weak economy.
The three-tenths of a percent rise in gross domestic product was about in line with Wall Street's expectations. But the report also noted that inflation in the fourth quarter rose by just 1.7 percent, a full percentage point below expectations. This was the lowest quarterly increase in 25 years.
Most economists said the modest economic package proposed by President Bush on Tuesday night should have placated credit market participants, assuming Congress does not try to increase its size significantly. And the early reaction to the speech was positive, as prices moved higher during overnight trading in Asia.
But some analysts speculated that good feeling about the proposals may have evaporated later, after the Administration's 1993 budget was released.
 
'Pretty Ugly Stuff'
 "The budget is pretty ugly stuff," said Ward McCarthy, a managing director at Stone & McCarthy Research Associates, a Princeton, N.J., firm that provides research on developments in the foreign-exchange and fixed-income markets.
After doing the math, Mr. McCarthy said the Administration's projected deficit of just under $400 billion made it likely that Treasury borrowings in the July-September quarter, the last of the Government's fiscal year, could be as high as $184 billion.
"We are talking about such enormous numbers that it is a bit mind-boggling," he said. "And since the President has been outmaneuvered in previous budget negotiations, it seems safe to assume the deficit will be even larger than projected."
 
Notes and Bonds Plunge
 The bulk of the day's selloff was concentrated among longer-term notes and bonds.
By late in the day, the Treasury's 8 percent 30-year bonds of 2021 were offered at a price of 10230/32 , down just over a point, to yield 7.74 percent, compared with 7.66 percent late Tuesday.
Among Treasury note issues, the 7.50 percent 10-year notes were offered at 10126/32 , down 19/32 to yield 7.23 percent. And the 4 7/8 percent two-year notes were offered at 9917/32 , down 3/32 , to yield 5.12 percent.
Despite Mr. Greenspan's comments, short-term Treasury bill rates were little changed. Three-month bills were offered at a discount rate of 3.81 percent late yesterday, unchanged from late Tuesday. And six-month bill rates rose by just a basis point, or one-hundredth of a percentage point, to 3.90 percent.
The big selloff in the Treasury securities market did not deter a number of large investment-grade corporate issuers from pricing enormous amounts of new issues.
 
$1.5 Billion Offering
 By far the largest deal was a three-part, $1.5 billion offering from Coca-Cola Enterprises Inc., the main bottler for the Coca-Cola Company. The issue consists of $750 million worth of 30-year bonds, $250 million worth of 20-year securities and $500 million of 10-year notes.
The 8.50 percent 30-year bonds were priced at $98.83 to yield 8.61 percent, 89 basis points over the prevailing yield on the Treasury's 8 percent 30-year bonds at the time of pricing.
The 8.50 percent 20-year securities were priced at $100, or par, 78 basis points over the prevailing yield on the Treasury's 8 percent 30-year bond at the time of pricing.
And the 7 7/8 percent 10-year notes were also priced at $100, 68 basis points over the prevailing yield on the Treasury's 7.50 percent 10-year notes at the time of pricing.
 
Retiring Debt
 Proceeds from the sale will be used to retire higher-cost outstanding debt, including $550 million worth of high-yield securities issued by Johnston Coca-Cola Bottling Group Inc., which Coca-Cola Enterprises acquired in December.
Salomon Brothers acted as lead manager for the offering. All three parts of the issue are rated A-3 by Moody's Investors Service and AA-by the Standard & Poor's Corporation.
Elsewhere, the Ford Motor Credit Corporation offered $1 billion worth of securities backed by dealer receivables. The 6 7/8 percent five-year securities were priced at $99.281, to yield 7.05 percent, 80 basis points over the prevailing yield on the Treasury's 6 1/4 percent five-year notes at the time of pricing.
Merrill Lynch & Company acted as lead manager for the offering. The securities are rated AAA by both Moody's and Standard & Poor's.
 
Tax-Exempt Issues
 A number of offerings were also priced in the tax-exempt municipal bond market. The largest offering was a sale of more than $550 million worth of securities for the State of Washington.
The negotiated sale consists of serial bonds with yields from 3.30 percent on securities maturing in 1993 to 6.25 percent in 2006.
Yields on term bonds range from 6.35 percent in 2011 to a maximum yield of 6.50 percent in 2014.
Lehman Brothers acted as lead manager for the offering. The securities are rated AA by both Moody's and Standard & Poor's.

HEADLINE: Arms Makers Brace for Peace

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Jan. 29

 BODY:
Even after six years of gradual Pentagon cuts, the Bush Administration's plan to slash military spending by canceling or scaling back a number of high-profile arms programs came as a jolt to weapons builders and to thousands of workers from Connecticut to California who are likely to lose their jobs in coming years.
Virtually every military contractor faces the loss of some business. The ensuing job losses would affect communities across the country, especially as business cutbacks spread to subcontractors and suppliers. 
 The Administration plan, laid out by President Bush in the State of the Union Message on Tuesday night and in budget documents submitted to Congress today, calls for an end to the Northrop Corporation's B-2 Stealth bomber, built in Palmdale and Pico Rivera, Calif., and to the General Dynamics Corporation's Seawolf submarine, assembled at the Electric Boat shipyard in Groton, Conn.
 
 
Programs Scaled Back
 The plan also calls for other programs to be scaled back substantially, including the Comanche helicopter being designed for the Army, largely in Connecticut, by the United Technologies Corporation and the Boeing Company; the advanced cruise missile, built by the McDonnell Douglas Corporation and General Dynamics, and a program for General Dynamics to upgrade the M-1 tank.
The Administration's proposal calls for the Pentagon's budget for the next fiscal year to be cut by $10 billion from the current $291 billion. Many members of Congress had called for similar cuts in the wake of the Soviet Union's dissolution, but the President's proposal represents a sharp shift for the Administration, which until recently had resisted canceling or delaying advanced weapons programs. The plan, if enacted by Congress, would greatly accelerate the industry's shrinkage and hasten its reorientation toward technological research rather than mass production of weapons.
"If you make helicopters, aircraft, missiles, ships, look out, because you're going to get a lot smaller a lot faster than you think," said Gordon Adams, the director of the Defense Budget Project, a nonpartisan research group in Washington.
 "It will be a substantially smaller industry, one that emphasizes the inputs to the weapons platform rather than production of the platform itself," Mr. Adams said. "It's an industry without a lot of production workers and with a heavy emphasis on research and design teams."
Analysts said that by the time Congress finished its work on the budget, the cuts could go even deeper. But they said Congress faced a painful choice between reaping the peace dividend and preserving military contracting jobs in members' home districts during an election year and a recession.
"Bush just established a new ceiling that was lower than the prior ceiling, and it doesn't leave a lot of headroom for the industry," said Howard A. Rubel, an analyst at C. J. Lawrence. "There's going to be a lot of arm twisting and favor paying in Congress before this thing is played out."
 
F-22 Plans Go Forward
 There were a few winners. The Pentagon wants to press ahead with the Air Force's new F-22 jet fighter, to be built by a team headed by the Lockheed Corporation. Final assembly will take place in Georgia, the home state of Senator Sam Nunn, the influential Democrat who is chairman of the Senate Armed Services Committee. The Navy will get a new aircraft carrier it wanted, to be built at Tenneco Inc.'s Newport News Shipyard in Virginia.
But many more companies and communities are likely to be losers. In Groton -- the home of Electric Boat, long the nation's leading submarine builder -- the cancellation of the Seawolf program, after just one boat under construction is completed, leaves the company with a rapidly diminishing backlog of orders.
While the company would not specify possible job losses today, it has said in the past that most of Electric Boat's 19,000 jobs in Connecticut and Rhode Island would disappear and the company's long-run survival would be in doubt without further Seawolf orders.
"It is very clear right now that it will have major implications for each of our facilities," Electric Boat said in a statement today about the program's cancellation.
 
 
Sikorsky May Suffer
 The cuts could also hurt another big contractor in Connecticut, the Sikorsky division of United Technologies, which had been scheduled to build the Army's Comanche helicopter in Stratford as part of a team that also includes Boeing. Defense Secretary Dick Cheney said production of the Comanche, which had been scheduled to begin in 1996, would be deferred indefinitely.
Foster Morgan, a Sikorsky spokesman, said that more than 2,000 people work on the program, 650 of them for Sikorsky in Connecticut. But he said it was impossible to know how the indefinite delay would affect employment until the Army negotiated a new contract with the company.
In California, Northrop said today that it expected to lay off 1,500 employees from the B-2 program by the end of the year. President Bush's proposal calls for production of the radar-evading, bat-winged bomber to be halted after 20 planes, down from the Air Force's most recent request of 75 and an original order of 132.
There are 16 planes on order, and orders for 4 additional planes would probably extend production through the middle of the decade. But as the program winds down, most of the 13,400 Northrop employees working on the program will lose their jobs.
Also at risk are the more than 40,000 others who work on the program for Northrop's subcontractors and suppliers. The LTV Corporation said that about 1,500 jobs pertaining to its work on the B-2 would be eliminated by the end of the year if the program was halted. Boeing, which had planned to cut 800 B-2 jobs this year, announced that it will cut 2,000 immediately.
Employment at the Government plutonium plant at Rocky Flats, Colo., will also be cut sharply. The plant, idle for years because of environmental and safety concerns, will cease production and concentrate on a cleanup, Energy Secretary James D. Watkins announced today. The department anticipates that about 1,000 of the 8,875 jobs at Rocky Flats will be eliminated in the 1993 fiscal year, with the work force gradually reduced to about 4,500 by the 1995 fiscal year.
Military contractors continued to put the best face on the sharp declines in their business, as they have for years. General Dynamics, the nation's second-largest Pentagon supplier, said in a statement that the Administration's budget contained "relatively few surprises," even though few analysts had expected the Seawolf program to be canceled after a single submarine. General Dynamics said it remained "well positioned" to get what business will be available from the military.
Northrop, too, put on a brave face, despite the loss of the B-2, which currently accounts for half of its revenues. A top Northrop executive said at a meeting with reporters last week that even without the B-2, the company would be able to continue reducing its debt and that it would have the financial strength to make acquisitions or invest in those aspects of its business that are growing.
"Even if the B-2 ends at only 16 aircraft, the present number approved by Congress, we'll be a profitable aerospace company with an enviable business base," the Northrop executive said.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Philip Morris Income Falls Because of Special Charges

BYLINE: By EBEN SHAPIRO

 BODY:
The Philip Morris Companies said yesterday that its fourth-quarter net income fell 12.8 percent, to $767 million, because of charges related to an accounting change and the costs of revamping its worldwide food operations.
Excluding the charges, the tobacco and food company's quarterly profits jumped 21 percent from the period a year ago, to $1.06 billion. 
 Sales dropped 3.5 percent, to $13.7 billion. The company attributed the drop to unfavorable currency rates and lower revenues from its North American food operations.
 
 
Kraft Sales Are Sluggish
 The gains were driven in large part by the strong performance of the company's tobacco business, both overseas and in the United States. The company's Kraft General Foods business in the United States fared less well, reflecting what analysts say is an intensely competitive and somewhat sluggish time for the packaged foods industry.
Philip Morris's earnings per share fell 12.6 percent, to 83 cents from 95 cents, for the three months that ended on Dec. 31. Excluding the charges, earnings per share rose 22.1 percent, to $1.16.
"It was a good performance," said Lawrence Adelman, an analyst with Dean Witter Reynolds. "The numbers were very strong."
Philip Morris stock lost 50 cents a share yesterday, closing at $75.75 on the New York Stock Exchange.
 
 
Shift to Cheaper Cheeses
 Operating income was down for the company's North American food business in the quarter. For the year, the company's volume in cheese, salad dressing and mayonnaise was down 5 percent. In the recession, Kraft's brand-name cheeses have lost market share to cheaper private-label brands. To stem the losses, the company recently cut prices 8 percent. "They got a little tired of losing market share," Mr. Adelman said.
In a statement, Michael A. Miles, chairman and chief executive of the company, said, "Although we expect consumer packaged goods companies to face extremely competitive conditions in a weak economic environment during most of 1992, we are well positioned for continued earnings and cash flow growth." He added, "We expect a good year in 1992."
Analysts expect overall profits, buoyed by cigarette sales, which account for about two-thirds of the company's profits, to grow by 20 percent this year.
"The tobacco business continues to be the crown jewel," said John C. Maxwell, an analyst with Wheat, First Securities.
 
 
Small Gain for Miller Beer
 Philip Morris's Marlboro brand is the world's best-selling cigarette. The company, which controls 43.3 percent of the American market, has also been aggressively building its lower-priced brands, Cambridge and Bucks.
At the company's Miller Beer brewing division, volume rose slightly last year, compared with a decline of more than 2 percent for the United States beer industry. Miller Lite volume, however, declined for the year.
During the fourth quarter, the company voluntarily adopted a new accounting method for retiree benefits. The change resulted in a charge against earnings for Philip Morris of nearly $1 billion.
In the fourth quarter, the company also spent $455 million for costs related to consolidating factories and other cost-cutting measures in its worldwide food divisions.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: Advertising;
Anti-Japan Auto Ads May Backfire

BYLINE: By STUART ELLIOTT

 BODY:
A RENEWED wave of harsh attacks on Japanese cars and trucks is inundating television and newspapers, as sort of the evil twin of the automotive industry's "Buy American" advertising.
Many of these new passionate, possibly xenophobic pitches seem the automotive equivalent of negative political advertising. Instead of Willie Horton's mug shot, however, the villain is depicted by rising suns, backed by sour-sounding string music meant to mimic the tones of the Japanese koto. And lest these be too subtle, there are remonstrations to remember Pearl Harbor. 
 The new round of ads stems from the perceived failure of President Bush's trade mission to Japan and from the recent criticism of American workers by Japanese politicians and business executives.
The ads, which not only praise American vehicles but also bash the Toyotas and Hondas of the world, are typified by a television commercial by the Deutsch Inc. agency for the metropolitan New York Pontiac dealers. A stern-voiced announcer reminds viewers of the remarks by Yoshio Sakurauchi, a Japanese politician, calling Americans lazy and illiterate. The remarks are printed on screen, against a rising sun; the word "Japanese" is all uppercase.
The announcer says, sarcastically, "Well, excuse us." After touting the attributes of the Pontiac Grand Am over the Honda Accord and the Toyota Camry, he adds, sneeringly, "Maybe they need a reading lesson."
Among other such campaigns are print advertisements for a Chrysler-Plymouth dealer in Anchorage that present World War II images from Pearl Harbor Day to V-J Day, and one for a Yonkers Lincoln-Mercury dealer that mocks Danny, the fictional salesman in a long-running campaign for the metropolitan New York Honda dealers. "Sorry Danny, not all Americans are stupid!" reads the ad, which lists "American jobs" as a selling point for the Mercury Sable.
The overheated rhetoric is far more prevalent in ads by local dealers than in national campaigns, in the same way that local politicians are more likely to "go negative" than, say, the President. While these ads attract attention -- crucial at a time when recession-weary consumers are loath to buy cars or trucks -- automotive industry consultants, as well as many advertising executives and academics, condemn them. Their opposition stems not so much from whether the ads are mean spirited or unpleasant, but from an opinion that they simply do not work, and may even be counterproductive.
Such advertising is "tawdry political grandstanding and disgusting," said Robert Lauterborn, a professor of advertising at the University of North Carolina's journalism school at Chapel Hill.
"Demagoguery has always been the successful refuge of the scoundrel," he added, "but it's still demagoguery."
A car maker or dealer using this strategy "is digging a hole for himself," said Christopher Cedergren, senior vice president of the Auto Pacific Group, a Santa Ana, Calif., automotive marketing consulting company.
"I can understand why they're doing it," he added. "Business worse than stinks. They'll think of anything they can to try to move product.
"But negative advertising like that," he said, "where you are basically scolding the consumer, doesn't do your business any good."
One big reason these ads backfire, said David Stewart, a professor of marketing at the University of Southern California, is that "they may only frustrate the consumer, because it's difficult to determine what's a Japanese product and what's an American product."
For instance, the cars cited in Deutsch's Pontiac spot, the Accord and Camry, are made in American factories. Indeed, a current television and print campaign by Saatchi & Saatchi DFS Pacific, salutes the American workers at Toyota's Georgetown, Ky., Camry plant.
And "you might have a Dodge dealer waving the flag," Mr. Cedergren said, "while half the mini-vans he's selling have V-6 engines from Mitsubishi." That is in addition, he added, to the anomaly that "a lot of the dealers who are shouting 'Buy American!' are the same dealers who own the Nissan dealership down the street."
Another reason these ads do not succeed, Mr. Lauterborn said, is that they appeal mostly to Americans "who are still fighting the Second World War" -- that is, older consumers who are already more inclined to buy American-built vehicles rather than those made overseas.
Donny Deutsch, the executive vice president and creative director at Deutsch, acknowledged that "there is a danger" in campaigns like his. Indeed, previous Deutsch spots for the Pontiac dealers were castigated for their jingoism; one warned consumers that if they "keep buying Japanese cars," they might someday visit "the big Christmas tree at Hirohito Center."
Yet, he said: "It's a message a lot of people want to hear. And you need emotion to get people thinking.
"Will it sell cars?" he asked. "We'll see."

GRAPHIC: Photo: In a frame from a television commercial by Deutsch Inc. for metropolitan New York Pontiac dealers, remarks made by a Japanese politician are shown against a rising sun and read by a stern-voiced announcer. (Deutsch Inc.)

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Off on Bush's Plans But Is Buoyed by Greenspan

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar ended mixed in United States trading yesterday, weakened at first by disappointment over President Bush's economic proposals but buoyed later on comments from the Federal Reserve chairman, Alan Greenspan.
Gold prices were narrowly mixed, weakening slightly overseas but strengthening marginally in the United States. On the New York Commodity Exchange, gold for current delivery fetched $356.10 an ounce, up 30 cents. At the Republic National Bank in New York, gold was quoted at $356.50 a troy ounce as of 4 P.M., up 85 cents from Tuesday. The dollar's value fell early in the global trading day, largely in reaction to President Bush's State of the Union address Tuesday night. 
 Traders said they saw few meaningful stimulants in his economic plan, which suggested that interest rates in the United States would likely have to decline again. That would make dollar-denominated investments worth less.
 
Dollar Begins to Rally
 But the dollar began to rally about midway through the trading day in the United States, when Mr. Greenspan, testifying before Congress, repeated earlier assertions that the significant interest rate cuts of recent months were enough to get the economy growing again. He also said he saw subtle signs of economic improvement after a prolonged slump.
"His comments all were directed to the idea that the economy doesn't look so bad," said Andrew Hodge, vice president of corporate treasury services at the New York branch of Bank Brussels Lambert. "So the Fed might not have to ease at all."
Some foreign exchange strategists cautioned, however, that persistent weakness in the United States economy could still result in another round of interest rate cuts, which would hurt the dollar.
"Greenspan said he didn't see any great need to lower the rates and was looking for an improvement in the economy, but I'm not really sure that's the case," said Hillel Waxman, a dealer at the New York branch of Bank Leumi Trust. Mr. Waxman said he foresaw at least one more cut in interest rates.
In Tokyo, the dollar fell 0.20 yen to close at 125.17 yen. Later, in London, it was quoted at 125.35 yen. By the time trading concluded in New York the dollar fetched 125.65 yen, down from 125.70 yen late Tuesday.
In London, the British pound rose to $1.8095, from $1.7950 late Tuesday. In New York, the pound fetched $1.7975, more expensive than its $1.7925 exchange rate late Tuesday.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS PEOPLE;
New Man Taking Over At Pacific Power Helm

 BODY:
Paul Lorenzini, a nuclear engineer and lawyer, will take the helm on Saturday as president of the Pacific Power and Light Company, one of the largest investor-owned utilities on the West Coast. The company is still in the throes of wrenching operational changes since its merger in 1990 with the Utah Power and Light Company.
Mr. Lorenzini, 49, who is now executive vice president for operations for the Portland, Ore., utility, replaces Robert Moench, who is retiring. His promotion is part of a management restructuring by the parent company, Pacificorp, to pave a smooth transition for the utility, which is trying to reduce costs and keep power prices low. 
 "It has been very difficult to merge two company cultures," Mr. Lorenzini said. "People get used to doing business in different ways and have spent their entire careers working for either Pacific Power or Utah Power. We've needed to devise processes to merge operations such as accounting, economic development and even generating and transmission resources."
Mr. Lorenzini grew up in Portland and as a youth worked at Whizburger, a fast-food chain that sold 19-cent hamburgers when it was founded in the 1960's by his father, Gilbert.
Once while hitchhiking to school, he was offered a ride by a naval officer who suggested he get a degree from one of the nation's military academies. As a result of that chance meeting, in 1964, he graduated from the United States Merchant Marine Academy in Kings Point, L.I. An instructor who taught a course in nuclear physics "made nuclear engineering sound fascinating," and in 1969 he received a doctorate in the field from Oregon State University in Corvallis. He received a law degree in 1975 from Loyola Law School in Los Angeles.
From 1969 to 1976, he was employed as a nuclear engineer at Rockwell International in Los Angeles. While working at Rockwell, he was co-chairman of a committee formed to defeat an antinuclear initiative, Proposition 15. In 1976, he joined the law firm of Tooze, Kerr, Peterson, Marshall & Shenker in Portland. In 1979 he joined the Rockwell Hanford Operation, Richland, Wash., and in 1984 was named vice president and general manager, responsible for a staff of 5,000 and radioactive waste management from nuclear operations there. He joined Pacific Power in 1987.
"I'm pretty happy where I am now and welcome challenges," he said. "But one thing I've learned is that I don't plan my life too far in advance." He and his wife, Jan, live in Lake Oswego, Ore., and have two children.

HEADLINE: DECORATING CONFESSIONS;
The Secret Shame of Ostentation

BYLINE: By SUZANNE SLESIN

 BODY:
"I'LL take it," a tall, elegant man whispered before slipping away into the crowd at the opening of the Winter Antiques Show in Manhattan.
"He'll take it," Blanche Greenstein echoed to her partner, Thomas K. Woodard. 
 Ms. Greenstein and Mr. Woodard, who are folk-art dealers, then placed a red "sold" sticker on the $3,500 architectural model. Many of their prime pieces already bore red stickers, including an early-19th-century pumpkin-pine and tin tollhouse window tagged $12,000.
"We sold it the first night and could have sold it 100 times since," Ms. Greenstein said. "The woman who bought it kept saying: 'I don't need it. I just love it.' "
Despite the recession, despite sales that antiques dealers describe as sluggish, despite waves of layoffs in architectural and interior design offices, there are some people who are still renovating, still decorating and always buying.
But even those who once justified any expenditure to achieve decorating splendor are now affecting a very low profile, a definite slink. In the decorating world, mum's the word right now. Call it the new guilt.
Decorating seems to have progressed from the "I did it all myself" school of the 1970's to the "Mario [or Mark or John] is doing it" line of the 1980's to the more socially acceptable, "Of course, we're not doing much of anything."
Lucy Greenberg works in a New York design firm and has just moved to a large Park Avenue apartment that she says needs "everything."
"But opulence is on the wane," she said. "In the past, everything was faux. Now we're back to the real thing.
"If I did decide to do 100 percent silk draperies on the windows," Mrs. Greenberg added, "I certainly wouldn't say anything to anybody."
Mario Buatta, the decorator, said: "The bug doesn't leave you People like me who have a buying sickness just don't stop. They might go for smaller-ticket items and keep quieter about it."
Mr. Buatta, who calls decorating an addiction, continued: "It's like a diet. You might say, I'm never going to be fat again, but sooner or later the pounds will be back. That's how it is with things that give you pleasure."
Of course, denial and abstinence have never been closely associated with interior decorating.
"We're the charlotte Russe, the extra cream," said Robert Metzger, a New York decorator who has made excess his signature. Like others in his profession, Mr. Metzger has recently had to react to a sea change and has toned down his interiors.
Of course, everything is relative.
"Now people want to look low-key in their spending approach," Mr. Metzger said. "It's just not appropriate to flaunt. That might embarrass someone or make someone feel bad. And that's just not nice."
As Mr. Metzger sees it, this new attitude includes "not serving caviar at your dinner party."
The guests at such a dinner party might have heeded Heather Cohane's publisher's letter in the November issue of Quest, a monthly magazine filled with features on the well-to-do and listings for luxurious real estate. To her friends who say "it's not chic to spend money," Ms. Cohane, citing concern for the economy, answered: "Please continue to buy. Since there is no need to tell anyone you're doing it, you can remain as chic as ever in your friends' eyes."
And Peter Marino asks, "What recession?" One of the designers who seemed to define the extravagant decorating of the 1980's, Mr. Marino is busier than ever, though half his business these days is overseas.
"What I hear most frequently from my American clients is, 'It's not the 80's,' or 'This is the 90's, you know,' " he said.
Most clients are not only keeping their decorating projects very quiet but demanding that their designers do the same. "Some people are dying to get their names in print," said Carolyn Sollis, the decorating director of HG magazine. "But others are worried about security, taxes, kidnappings and being too ostentatious."
Anthony Ingrao, an antiques dealer and interior decorator in New York, said, "I just can't reveal any names at all these days," adding that he had just sold "a very important chandelier by Charpentier for $75,000."
"That was probably too cheap, because I have five back-up offers," Mr. Ingrao said. Just don't ask him to tell you who they are from.
Mariette Himes Gomez, a New York designer who has championed a somewhat understated though not inexpensive look, said, "These days, the emphasis is more on creature comfort than on the art, antiques and views of the showy apartment."
Renovating when things get worn and accommodating growing children and new babies are part of what Mrs. Gomez called "the new utilitarian age."
"In uncertain times," said Glenn Gissler, a New York designer, " the home environment is one place you can have a sense of control, whether it's real or not."
Mr. Gissler, adding that he was "insanely busy," said that decorating these days is not about indulging every possible whim, but neither is it about doing without. "These days people may have to work harder to stay in the same place," he said, "but I hear them saying, 'Damn it, I'm going to have a place that's nice.' "
Decorating that doesn't look like decorating seems to be the trend. For example, when Barbara Toll, an art dealer, recently renovated her SoHo gallery, she wanted people to see the improvement. But she added: "I didn't want them to notice that I did anything to it at all. Like dyeing your hair: it looks different and better, but people can't put their finger on why."
Fixing up what one has rather than moving and starting over assuages a lot of people's decorating guilt.
"For seven years, we've wanted to have our own bathroom, and a gorgeous one at that," said Libby Bruck, who works for Reality Productions, a film and video company in Fairfield, N.J. and lives with her family in Montclair, N.J.
A whirlpool bath and a walk-in closet were on Mrs. Bruck's wish list. Now she has them, as well as a recently completed den and study over the garage.
"I'm thrilled," she said. "Fixing up the house you're in is not as pretentious as moving to a big mansion."
That attitude can even extend to items that were traditionally considered as basic as the kitchen stove or the living room sofa.
At George Smith, a SoHo store that specializes in expensive, emphatically dowdy upholstered English furniture, kilim-covered sofas are still being grabbed up like popcorn, at $8,800 to $12,000 apiece. Steven Shanstrom, the showroom manager, said about 200 have made their way out the door in the last two years.
"Even people who really didn't need another sofa couldn't resist the ones we had in our sale," Mr. Shanstrom said. "They think of them as a piece of art, and they make a space for them."
The same might be true for the current Rolls-Royce of stoves: La Cornue, which costs about $18,000. (Add $2,000 for copper finish.)
"My customers know they are a wild extravagance, but they justify it as a lifelong investment," said Michael Kaye, who manages a store in New York for Williams-Sonoma, which distributes the stoves. The store has sold 18 in the last two years.
"Think of it as a family heirloom that will enhance the value of your home and property," Mr. Kaye said. The company engraves the stoves with the family names of the owners.
Of course, for some people disclosure was never a consideration. "Most of our clients never talked about it anyway," said Betty Sherrill of McMillan & Company in Manhattan. "We can't say we're not in a recession, but we're an old firm and we didn't have the yuppies, and still don't have them. "
Mrs. Sherrill said she was currently renovating a triplex on Fifth Avenue and "working more in Texas again."
Howard Klein, a collector of tin toys and Western furniture, seems truly oblivious to the vagaries of the design trade. Mr. Klein, the creative director at the Clarkson N. Potter publishing company, lives in a spacious apartment on the Upper West Side that is being designed in a style he called "cowboy Deco."
"I'm such a careful shopper that I'm recession proof," he said. "It takes me so long to decide about anything that by the time I buy it, the economic climate can have changed completely."

HEADLINE: In Hard Times, Boarders in the Suburbs

 BODY:
AS her bills piled up last year, Grace G. became so worried about her finances that she felt she had only one option: to give up part of her home in a Westchester village to a stranger.
The stranger sleeps in an upstairs bedroom in the split-level brick house, prepares meals in the kitchen and keeps a toothbrush and a jumble of toiletries in the family bathroom. And each month, she pays Grace G. $325 in rent. 
 As hard times persist, more and more middle-class people around the country who never thought of taking in boarders are now quietly doing so, hoping to use the extra money to help pay mortgages or other debts that counted while the economy sagged.
Though there are no statistics on how many people are renting out rooms for the first time, services that place boarders say that in the last year, they have received a surge of inquiries from the kinds of homeowners they had hardly ever heard from: middle-class professionals living in expensive suburbs.
"There has been a great increase," said Mark W. Smith, president of Roommate Connection, which places boarders in the Boston area. "People are now registering with us who in years past would not have. People are very insecure about their futures, so they are taking in boarders to pad their savings accounts."
Many of these homeowners do not want their relatives, friends and neighbors to know outsiders are living with them, or that they have financial problems.
And some of these homeowners also do not report the rent as income on their Federal and state tax returns; some are violating zoning laws.
Perhaps most important, many people say that in their communities, taking in boarders is taboo. It is a custom of the poor and working classes, not of people living in expensive houses in quiet suburbs.
"We feel, the more private, the better," said an executive at a small computer company who is renting a room in his colonial-style four-bedroom house in Montvale, N.J., a suburb of New York City. "It's not something you jump up on a soapbox and talk about."
Like most of the homeowners interviewed, the man insisted that he not be identified. He and his wife took in a boarder, he said, because he feared being laid off and wanted another source of income.
"My job is only as safe as a job can be these days," he said, adding that the careers of a number of his friends in his field had been devastated by the recession.
He said his family had agonized over losing privacy and possibly renting to a noisy, messy or irresponsible boarder. He and his wife drew up a questionnaire for prospective tenants and agreed on some rules: no smoking, no guests, no loud music.
Their second-floor guest room was first taken by a 29-year-old electronics engineer, who paid $110 a week. He left in early December, after staying for a year.
A few weeks later, a 43-year-old executive of Mercedes-Benz moved in. That boarder, who also owns a house in southern New Jersey, usually spends four nights a week in Montvale, which is near his office.
He has his own bathroom and enters the house through a side entrance, so the family members see him only a few times a week, mostly in the kitchen.
"In the first few weeks, it wasn't so much apprehension as it was trying to discover some kind of feeling," the computer executive said.
Last month, Catherine Hanley began looking for a boarder for the upstairs bedroom in her 4,500-square-foot home in Carollton, Tex., a suburb of Dallas. "We think the money would be nice," she said, "but the loss of privacy and security is a little threatening."
Mrs. Hanley, who is a French teacher, said her husband, Timothy, recently lost his job as a claims manager for an insurance company. Mr. Hanley, 54 years old, had worked for the company for 25 years.
They bought their three-bedroom brick house in 1990, expecting that his salary and hers would pay the bills. Now they need the $500 a month a boarder would pay.
Grace G., who is divorced and works for a cosmetics company, had similar reservations the first time she advertised for a boarder. She lives with her 90-year-old mother and her 29-year-old daughter, who is a singer.
"I was very frightened because I didn't feel trusting," said Grace G., who is 54 years old. "But I figured that if I didn't do this, I would have to sell my house and move."
Now she has grown accustomed to the arrangement and enjoys the company of a boarder as well as the chance to meet new people, like the college student who lived in her house for six months. She now rents to a 32-year-old artist.
Besides adjusting to losing domain over part of their home, some of the new landlords said they felt forced to change their habits.
In December, Maj. David P. Dauer of the Army rented the basement of his town house in Reston, Va., to Rob Casto, an architect. Mr. Casto, who was referred by Roommates Preferred, a service in Washington that places boarders, pays $450 a month and has his own entrance.
Major Dauer, who works for the Defense Department in Washington, took in the boarder because he was worried about supporting his three children and his wife, Heather, who left her job with a telecommunications company after the children were born.
"Our expenses are going up rather than going down," he said. "Initially, I brought it up as just an off-the-wall type idea. But when I thought about it, the pieces fell into shape. It was my idea, but I had to convince my wife."
Major Dauer said he had become more accustomed to Mr. Casto than his children had. Now, they have to be quiet in the evening so they do not disturb the boarder.
"Our 4-year-old is still trying to figure out what this strange person is doing in our house," Major Dauer said.
He also said he had grown fond of Mr. Casto. They have similar interests, like construction.
Still, even after many months, some homeowners who have taken in boarders remain uncomfortable with the arrangement. They say they are always on guard.
"I do a lot of praying so that I'm protected and so that nothing happens to me," said a women renting out a room in her four-bedroom ranch house in Hialeah, Fla. "You just don't know."
