HEADLINE: Books of The Times;
Investigating a Murder Japan Wants Unsolved

BYLINE: By CHRISTOPHER LEHMANN-HAUPT

 BODY:

Rising Sun
By Michael Crichton
355 pages. Alfred A. Knopf. $22.
In his novel "Jurassic Park," Michael Crichton invented an island theme park where dinosaurs came to life and wreaked bloody havoc on visitors from the North American mainland. In his new novel, "Rising Sun," he has dreamed up an even greater threat to Americans: the Japanese. 
 In "Rising Sun," Mr. Crichton steps into a classroom with a pointer, dims the lights and enacts a violent murder mystery. At its opening, Peter J. Smith, a detective with the Los Angeles Police Department who specializes in liaison work with foreign nationals, describes being summoned to investigate a murder that occurred at the gala opening of the Nakamoto Tower, the American headquarters of Nakamoto Industries. On the way, he picks up a senior officer in his department, John Connor, who immediately starts lecturing him on the difficulties of dealing with the Japanese.
Upon arriving at the party, Smith and Connor learn that the murder victim is a beautiful young woman named Cheryl Lynn Austin, known to have been involved with kinky sex, and that her killing was recorded on Nakamoto's highly sophisticated video surveillance system, which had been installed to "kaizen" the company's office workers; that is, "to observe workers during the day, and help them improve their efficiency."
But they also run into strong resistance to their investigation from one of Nakamoto's senior executives. And they soon discover that the tapes have been tampered with and cannot easily be analyzed because, as various characters explain, Japan is far advanced over the United States in the technology of video recording, and is able, through its insidious penetration of American society, to prevent Smith and Connor from doing their work.
Despite such obstacles, the plot of "Rising Sun" does thicken at a satisfying pace, and Smith and Connor are soon fighting an extensive cover-up whose motives reach to the heart of the Japanese industrial system. In the progress of the mystery's unwinding, we are treated to a cleverly contrived series of surprises, which occasionally even surpass plot expedience and take on the deep colorings of human irrationality.
Yet we never go for long without hearing the whack of Professor Crichton's classroom pointer against the slate of the blackboard. In his lectures, he attempts to illustrate how the Japanese compete with us unfairly, how they have taken away our manufacturing base, how they are rapidly buying up our remaining resources, how they are planning to run our economy and make us play inferior roles in it, how they have hired a vast army of lobbyists to blind us to what is happening and how when people try to point all this out they are accused of being racist when in fact Japan itself is a profoundly racist culture.
As a typical snatch of the novel's dialogue reads: " 'The Japanese can be tough,' Connor said. 'They say "business is war," and they mean it. You know how Japan is always telling us that their markets are open. Well, in the old days, if a Japanese bought an American car, he got audited by the Government. So pretty soon, nobody bought an American car. The officials shrug: what can they do? Their market is open: they can't help it if nobody wants an American car. The obstructions are endless. Every imported car has to be individually tested on the dock to make sure it complies with exhaust-emission laws. Foreign drugs can only be tested in Japanese laboratories on Japanese nationals. Foreign skis were once banned because Japanese snow was said to be wetter than European and American snow. That's the way they treat other countries, so it's not surprising they worry about getting a taste of their own medicine.' "
In short, by the time he springs his last considerable plot surprise, Mr. Crichton has managed to sum up the entire case made by books critical of Japan like Clyde V. Prestowitz Jr.'s "Trading Places: How We Are Giving Our Future to Japan and How to Reclaim It" (Basic Books), Karel van Wolferen's "Enigma of Japanese Power" (Knopf) and Pat Choates's "Agents of Influence" (Knopf), which are listed at the end in a bibliography along with more than three dozen other books pertinent in various degrees to Japanese-American relations. This back matter is introduced with a remark by Akio Morita, the founder of the Sony Corporation, "If you don't want Japan to buy it, don't sell it."
The trouble with "Rising Sun" is obviously that as a serious discourse on why we should begin waging economic war against Japan, the book is far too entertaining. And as an entertainment, it is far too didactic. Whenever you begin to worry over how far down the road the country may have gone toward selling its birthright, you recall that this is a highly contrived fiction. And whenever you begin to sweat over the trouble Peter Smith is getting into, you are snapped out of it by the sound of that pointer hitting on the blackboard.
Call this dystopian fiction then, the opposite of Edward Bellamy's utopian "Looking Backward" and more like Orson Welles's famous Mercury Theater of the Air adaptation of H. G. Wells's "War of the Worlds." Yet pray that it doesn't send people running hysterically into the streets, crying for another bombing of Japan, but instead moves them to thoughtful debate. "Rising Sun" is remarkably timely, given this country's increasing frustration over its Far Eastern rival. It even makes one wonder if contending with Japan isn't going to be the United States' next great mission now that the cold war is over. But let us also hope that Mr. Crichton's assiduously researched fantasy does not turn out to be too extremely timely.

HEADLINE: OLYMPICS;
Deal Maker a Torchbearer? Trump Wants Olympics Here

BYLINE: By GERALD ESKENAZI

 BODY:
Donald Trump once rebuilt the skating rink in Central Park in four months. His latest dream is to bring the Summer Olympics to New York in the year 2004.
But it may be easier to get a casino permit in New Jersey than to convince the United States Olympic Committee that the Big Apple could manage the feat. The city was turned down for the 1984 Games (awarded to Los Angeles) despite the efforts of a committee headed by a former city official and businessman, Richard Ravitch. 
 And just this week, the city lost an Indy-car race it was to hold in September when talks broke down with the sponsor. The city also lost the Jets to Giants Stadium in New Jersey in 1984 when it was unable to come up with a timetable to refurbish Shea Stadium and its leaky bathrooms.
Still, Trump said in an interview that he spoke to Mayor David N. Dinkins yesterday about his idea. "The Mayor responded favorably," said Trump. Ruby Ryles, a spokeswoman for Dinkins, said she could not reach him to confirm the conversation.
In any event, the U.S.O.C. must deal with an elected city official, and not an individual, even one as well known as Trump.
 
'Extraordinary Opportunity'
 Despite Trump's embattled financial empire, the builder sounded upbeat.
"This is an extraordinary opportunity and we will be bringing the Olympics to New York City, where it belongs," he said. "Hopefully, it will give a great boost to the economy."
Trump would not comment further. But it is known that he is a good friend of George Steinbrenner, the embattled majority owner of the Yankees, who also serves on the U.S.O.C.
But where would these Games take place? Trump is known to favor the construction of a stadium adjacent to Shea Stadium in Flushing, Queens. If not, he says that Giants Stadium could accommodate the Games. But that venue would have to be reconfigured, with stands removed, to house a 400-meter racing oval.
Trump would prefer the attendant drama of an Olympics in the year 2000. But that, apparently, cannot happen. The Games, staged every four years, have never been held consecutively in the same country. And Atlanta is already the host for the 1996 Games. Furthermore, the deadline for submitting bids to the U.S.O.C. for 2000 has passed.
As yet, only one United States city has approached the committee to be the host for 2004: Boston.
Trump's plans are so preliminary that a consortium of business leaders he plans to include -- such as the Tisch Brothers, Lew Rudin, and the heads of the three major television networks -- have not yet received letters telling them of his idea.
 
The Mayor Is the Man
 "Under no circumstances would we deal with an individual," said Mike Moran, director of information for the U.S.O.C. "The only person we'd deal with is the mayor of a city."
Trump was praised for finishing the skating rink after the city spent seven years and made little progress. But he also received a public black eye for his first venture in sports.
That was with the now-defunct United States Football League. Trump bought the New Jersey Generals, who played in Giants Stadium, and began hiring high-priced players, notably Herschel Walker and Doug Flutie. Trump then spearheaded a move to shift the league out of its springtime schedule and into the fall, where, presumably, it could compete against the National Football League.
Trump's profligacy was blamed for killing the league.

SECTION: Section A; Page 20; Column 1; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 411 words

HEADLINE: Haiti: Tighten the Embargo

 BODY:
Rampaging military hard-liners have torpedoed a promising Haitian political compromise mediated by the Organization of American States. As a result more Haitians are dying, more are fleeing for their lives, and the eventual task of reconstruction has been made more difficult. The hard-liners seem unlikely to permit any settlement unless forced to by a more stringent regional economic embargo.
The embargo was imposed by the O.A.S. in the aftermath of the Sept. 30, 1991, military coup against Haiti's first freely elected President, Jean-Bertrand Aristide. Though it applied only to Western Hemisphere countries, the embargo was effective at first. By late November, critical supplies of gasoline were nearly exhausted. 
 But since then, three oil tankers have unloaded their cargoes. Haitian authorities say they have two months' worth of oil reserves. The leverage of regional diplomats over military and civilian leaders has been noticeably weakened.
Earlier this month, diplomats persuaded President Aristide to accept Rene Theodore as prime minister, a concession to critics of the mob violence that marred the President's seven months in power. Though a Communist, Mr. Theodore is a moderate who enjoys U.S. support.
But when it came time to ratify the Theodore appointment, Parliament backed off. Mr. Theodore has now become the target of a campaign of military intimidation. Last Saturday, police under army command attacked a meeting at which he was host, gunning down his bodyguard and brutalizing other national political figures.
With passions running high, compromises won't come easily. But without more pressure on the men controlling the guns, compromises won't come at all. A new torrent of refugees has made Washington eager for a quick solution.
The Bush Administration is now trying to decide how to step up pressure on Haiti's hard-liners. Invasion and occupation, Grenada or Panama style, is a bad idea. A 20-year U.S. occupation earlier this century did little to solve Haiti's problems. Freezing assets of the Haitian elite in this country might send a useful message.
But the most straightforward approach would be to add U.S. naval muscle to the existing O.A.S. blockade. Humanitarian deliveries could be increased, on a carefully monitored basis, even as the overall net is tightened.
Only by standing up to the hard-liners can Haiti be returned to democratic rule and its people delivered from their cruel ordeal.

HEADLINE: The Tinkerer;
Mr. Bush's New War, and Paper Sword

 BODY:
"This will not stand." George Bush's steely challenge to Saddam Hussein remains one of his most memorable expressions. Tuesday night, mired in recession and fighting for his political life, Mr. Bush invoked those words again in his State of the Union address. "I look at hard times and make this vow: This will not stand." This will not fly would be more like it.
When the President resurrects the metaphor of Desert Storm to demonstrate his commitment to economic recovery, he might reasonably be expected to deliver a program to match the threat. But nowhere did he come close to the economic equivalent of 550,000 troops. What his speech and new budget offer instead is a laundry list with no main text. What they disclose is not an anti-recession warrior but a tinkerer. 
 Give the President this much: He did not sugar-coat his problems. The state of the Union, he confessed, is not good; the time had come for him to "look homeward." Give him this, too: It's neither unexpected nor wrong for a President who's been under fire for ignoring domestic needs to look for an oratorical fix. Unfortunately, the President's rhetoric also illuminates how marginal are his remedies.
Time and again, Churchillian flourishes introduced blinding banalities. "This time, at this hour," he thundered, "I cannot take No for an answer." No to what? A capital gains tax cut! "The power of America rests in a stirring but simple idea: that people will do great things if only you set them free." How do that? Among other things, "modify the passive loss rule"!
If Mr. Bush gets lucky, and the economy comes around on its own steam, he may win applause for not having reached for more dramatic initiatives. Moreover, the first third of the speech, celebrating the triumph of democracy and the fortitude of its American defenders, was sharply written and compellingly delivered.
Mr. Bush hasn't come close to honoring his pledge to be the "environment President" and the "education President." But he finds himself in the enviable position of truly becoming the "disarmament President." This weekend, he pointedly reminded his audience, it is he -- not Clinton, Kerrey, Harkin, Tsongas or anybody else -- who'll meet with Boris Yeltsin to discuss $50 billion in arms cuts. A reduction in nuclear stockpiles will do nothing to hurt his chances in November.
What will hurt is a stingy response to real economic problems. And that's all, after weeks of buildup, that Mr. Bush now offers. Where his budget proposals are timid they should have been bold, and where they are bold they are often wrong. Hundreds of ideas spread over 600 pages, many individually meritorious, add up to a program that solves no problem well.
The serious response to recession he promised in New Hampshire amounts to little more than an accelerated schedule of tax refunds -- a rearrangement of timing, but not the amount, of tax bills. The President is justifiably wary of signing onto plans favored by some Democrats that include huge tax cuts and spending hikes. But there are safe ways to deliver adrenaline. A temporary across-the-board cut in payroll and income taxes of, say, $80 billion wouldn't raise the long-term deficit yet could stop a slide into deeper recession.
In his new budget, the President likewise ducks for cover on health care. To control skyrocketing costs, he firmly rejects Government price controls in favor of the discipline of competitive markets. But markets can't work unless consumers have incentive to seek cost-effective insurance. Budget Director Richard Darman originally urged therefore that wealthy families not get a full tax break for deluxe insurance. The budget language was killed; if the proposal is too, Mr. Bush will have undermined his program.
Mr. Bush proposes lavish tax breaks on real estate investors, capital gains and retirement savings. Yet the tax code already subsidizes real estate. And his insistence on cutting the capital gains tax rate remains extravagant and illogical. How would it promote future investment to cut taxes on past investment?
The new budget does contain one admirable clarion call. Mr. Darman focuses on mandatory spending -- programs driven by formulas rather than yearly appropriations. Means-tested entitlements like Medicare and unemployment insurance now account for an astonishing $800 billion. If such programs, excluding Social Security, could be held to a modest increase, the five-year saving would be enough to balance the budget. So Mr. Darman boldly proposes cutting subsidies for, among others, affluent farmers and rich Medicare recipients.
The trouble with this display of courage is that Mr. Darman knows its certain fate on Capitol Hill: This will not stand. However sensible, it's just another idea in a welter of proposals. If Mr. Bush wants to lead America in a wholehearted attack on recession, it will take more than a paper sword. If, conversely, he thinks the right thing to do is stay the course, let him say so.

HEADLINE: Clinton's Middle Class Hang-Up

BYLINE: By Ted Van Dyk; Ted Van Dyk is a longtime Democratic strategist.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
With style and shrewdness, Gov. Bill Clinton appears to have beaten the rap on his philandering and emerged stronger than he was before the Gennifer Flowers episode came up. Unless a second shoe (or bedroom slipper) drops to indicate he's a liar, his national exposure during his troubles has helped establish him more clearly as the Democrats' front-runner and likely nominee.
Now he faces a real challenge: to move beyond poll-driven boiler plate to presentation of a strong, relevant agenda that will genuinely address the country's problems and give voters a real reason to elect him. 
 Governor Clinton enters the New Hampshire primary homestretch with far more money and a stronger organization than his competitors. The media, perhaps overcompensating for window-peeping, are giving him more generous and ample coverage than the other Democrats. If he wins, as expected, his competitors may well be down and broke. This would provide an opportunity to forget parts of his platform that falsely promise voters pleasure without pain, and to draw real differences between President Bush and himself.
First, the Governor should abandon his shameless pandering to the middle class. Hardly a sentence passes his mouth without use of the words "middle class." The first two economic planks of his Plan for America's Future are naked tax cut appeals to voters that would do justice to Ronald Reagan. The first, titled Tax Relief for the Forgotten Middle Class, says "we should cut middle class taxes immediately by 10 percent." The second, an expanded children's tax credit, would provide an immediate $480 annual tax saving for each child in an "average income" family.
To provide billions of dollars to pay for these cuts, the Governor proposes vaguely to close unspecified tax loopholes for the rich, soak high income individuals and cut the Federal bureaucracy. In other words, he has no idea where he'd find the money but is making promises anyway and socking easy targets in the bargain.
More disturbing than the tax cut grandstanding is the shorthand at work in the repetitive use of "middle class." In 1980's and early 1990's political parlance, the term has been a code phrase used by moderate and conservative Democratic candidates to mean "taxpayers, not tax-eaters." Repeated and emphasized often, it signals 1980's Reagan Democrats that it is safe to come home to their party because poor, black, Hispanic, urban, homeless, hungry, and other people and problems out of favor in Middle America will no longer get the favored treatment they got from mushy 1960's and 1970's Democratic liberals.
The Governor also needs to clean up his act on trade. Leading Arkansas, dependent on international trade, he began his campaign by resisting the protectionism preached by Tom Harkin and Bob Kerrey. But he recently has used "getting tough on trade" and other code phrases, sprinkled with references to Japan, all meant to imply that foreign devils rather than our own failings are to blame for our economic weakness.
America needs a modern infrastructure; better educated, housed and nourished people; training and retraining for its workforce; an all-out attack on the homelessness, crime and drugs crushing urban areas, and a plan for economic investment and growth that will keep us competitive in changing world markets while reducing the red ink of the Reagan-Bush years. It will get none of these things through tax cut grandstanding, pandering to the middle class and easy protectionist jargon.
I've known Bill Clinton since 1972, when he joined the McGovern Presidential campaign as a young organizer. That Bill Clinton was filled with idealism, wanted to end the Vietnam War and was committed to justice for people without power. He has adjusted those views to succeed in populist but conservative border-state politics. Now he needs to think and act in a broader setting.
With the hard part nearly behind him, he needs to get serious -- beginning with tomorrow night's nationally televised prime-time debate among the Democratic Presidential candidates on the Public Broadcasting Service.

HEADLINE: Our Place in the Western Community

 BODY:
To the Editor:
I don't believe you go far enough in "American Abdication in Europe" (editorial, Jan. 11). It's not merely domination of Europe by Germany we have to worry about, but also our own position in the world.
By the end of the century, Europe will be united. Even a reluctant Britain will have joined the European Community, which will expand to include the balance of the Western European countries and Eastern Europe as well. Boris N. Yeltsin, the Russian President, has already asked for admission to the North Atlantic Treaty Organization and the World Bank. In a few years, Russia and the other commonwealth nations will be in both, probably also in the E.C., creating a European market stretching from the Atlantic to the Pacific, with close to a billion bright, industrious people, as inventive as we and richer in natural resources, especially with a developed Russian oil industry. 
 At the same time, Japan will lead the East, including the young tigers, Korea, Singapore, Taiwan, Indonesia and Thailand, and a reinvigorated China once its old-line leaders are no longer with us. India and Pakistan will lag, but they'll lean toward the East, while Africa will be in need for generations, eventually joining the West. This will leave us with Canada and the poorer nations of Central and South America. In such a competition, we would finish a distant third.
While it may be a hard pill for us proud Americans to swallow, our only chance to survive in this coming worldwide contest is by no longer thinking of ourselves as No. 1 but as a senior partner in a new confederation. If we don't want to be fighting constant wars to prove our weapons superiority or serve as hired policemen for the wealthier countries, we must stay in NATO and be as close as possible to the E.C., made up of countries from which most of our citizens emigrated.
If we're invited to join, we should. Canada would surely be allowed in immediately. Our other hemisphere neighbors, like the Eastern European countries, would follow as they qualified. African nations will be assisted, then welcomed into the community as their economies expanded.
I recognize that this is even more difficult to sell to the United States public than Franklin D. Roosevelt's attempt to get us into World War II before Pearl Harbor. He set the stage by giving aid to Britain, including warplanes. We'll have to use NATO as our steppingstone, but we must not allow ourselves to fall behind. We saved Europe. Let's unite with those countries before they leave us in the dust. It's human to forget past favors, like our having saved them from Hitler. It's going to be a two-power world, East and West. We must be an integral part of the West of the future, and that future is tomorrow, not the day after.
 
RICHARD LEWIS
Somers, N.Y., Jan. 13, 1992

HEADLINE: Bethlehem Halts Dividends And Plans Cuts After Loss

BYLINE: By THOMAS C. HAYES

 BODY:
The Bethlehem Steel Corporation, after reporting a loss of $638.1 million for the fourth quarter, said yesterday that it would cut its work force by 6,500 and stop paying its annual stock dividend.
The job cuts at the nation's second-largest steelmaker will amount to about 25 percent of the work force. The reductions will be made over the next two years through layoffs, early retirement programs and sales of parts of the company. 
 Like other big steelmakers whose main customers include the depressed auto, construction and machinery industries, Bethlehem has been buffeted anew by recession, low prices and competition from smaller, more efficient steel mills. The USX Corporation, which owns the nation's largest steelmaker, said on Tuesday that its steel group lost $165 million in the fourth quarter, compared with a profit of $51 million a year ago.
Bethlehem's jarring cutbacks will sever the company's historic link to workers and steel products made under the Bethlehem banner in Johnstown, Pa., since 1908.
The 1991 quarterly loss exceeded the loss of $516.8 million for the comparable 1990 quarter. Sales fell 14.2 percent, to $1.03 billion, from $1.2 billion in the corresponding quarter a year earlier. The latest loss included a one-time charge of $575 million for anticipated costs in the restructuring. Analysts said most of the costs were associated with severance, pension and health-care plans for the large number of workers who will leave the company by the end of 1993.
For the year, Bethlehem lost $767 million, compared with a loss of $463.5 million in 1990. Sales fell nearly 12 percent, to $4.32 billion from $4.9 billion.
The company said it would stop paying the annual dividend of 40 cents a share on its common stock to conserve cash. Bethlehem shares fell $2.25 yesterday, closing at $13.75 on the Big Board.
The company said it would search for buyers for the bar, rod and wire division based in Johnstown, but would close any plants in the unit that it did not sell. The unit includes 2,600 workers, with 1,900 in Johnstown and another 700 at plants in Lackawanna, N.Y., and near Baltimore in Sparrows Point, Md.
It also plans to sell a plant in Steelton, Pa., with 400 jobs, and several coal mines in West Virginia, which employ 800. Another 400 jobs will be lost from the shutdown of coke oven batteries at Sparrows Point. The rest of the job reductions will be throughout the company.
"We have been experiencing losses at these facilities for some time, and there is no reasonable prospect for their return to profitability," said Walter F. Williams, chairman and chief executive. He blamed environmental regulations, high labor costs, competition and imports for the decision.
But analysts said the steelmaker, based in Bethlehem, Pa., had dragged its feet, hoping to survive in a niche of the domestic steel business now dominated by smaller, lower-cost mini-mills. The smaller mills typically have lower cost advantages from newer equipment and nonunion work forces.
 
Once Employed 289,000
 The cutbacks will reduce Bethlehem's work force to 20,000, a staggering decline from 157,000 workers in 1953, when the steel industry was one of the most powerful forces on the nation's industrial landscape. Bethlehem's peak employment was 289,000 in 1943, during a wartime campaign to build 1,110 warships. Bethlehem acquired the Johnstown works some 56 years after it opened in 1852 as the Cambria Iron Company to make steel rails for the Pennsylvania Railroad.
Analysts said workers at the plants Bethlehem wants to sell probably would lose their jobs, at least for a while. That steel is sold mainly to auto makers and machinery manufacturers, who have increasingly turned to mini-mills like Nucor and Chaparral Steel for shipments since the early 1980's.
"The problem is that people working there are not interested in accepting concessions, having jobs or wages cut," said John E. Jacobson, an analyst at AUS Consultants in Philadelphia. "Once plants are closed for a few years, workers often lower their expectations" and buyers move in, he said.
But leaders of the United Steelworkers of America said Bethlehem rejected the union's cost-cutting proposals for the Johnstown division last year. "The union did everything it could to keep the division alive," said Andrew V. Palm, head of the union's District 15, which includes the Johnstown plant.
"We believed we had a handshake deal last summer" to change the labor agreement, he said. And Bethlehem intended to invest $140 million to $180 million for a new continuous caster and other equipment. But Bethlehem's directors rejected the agreement, he said.
Analysts said losses in the steel industry for 1991 would exceed $1 billion, partly because of slumping demand and prices that fell last summer to the lowest level in 10 years.

HEADLINE: CHINA SAID TO SELL PARTS FOR MISSILES

BYLINE: By ELAINE SCIOLINO with ERIC SCHMITT, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
American intelligence reports indicate that China is continuing to sell missile technology to Syria and Pakistan despite statements by Chinese leaders that they are willing to curb missile exports, according to senior Administration officials.
Beijing has recently delivered to Syria about 30 tons of chemicals needed to make a solid-fuel missile and plans to deliver an additional 60 tons in March or April, said the officials, who added that the amount was enough to make a "significant" number of intermediate-range missiles. 
 It has also delivered to Pakistan guidance units that could be used to control the flight of M-11 ballistic missiles, they added.
The issue is important because the United States is ready to lift the sanctions on the sale of American satellite parts and high-speed computers that were imposed last spring after the United States learned that China had secretly delivered launchers for M-11 missiles to Pakistan.
President Bush, who favors removal of the sanctions, will raise the issue of China's proliferation practices when he meets at the United Nations on Friday with China's Prime Minister, Li Peng. Leading lawmakers and human rights organizations have sharply criticized the meeting, the first between the leaders since Chinese troops crushed the democracy movement in China in June 1989.
During the visit of Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d to Beijing in November, Chinese officials said they would abide by the provisions of a 1987 international agreement restricting the export of missiles and missile technology, but only if the Administration lifted the sanctions.
The Administration has made the issue of nonproliferation a linchpin of its post-cold-war foreign policy, and is especially eager to halt the transfer of nuclear, chemical and biological weapons and ballistic missiles to the developing world.
The President's plan to meet with Mr. Li reflects Mr. Bush's firm belief that the best way to moderate China's behavior is through dialogue, a strategy that has been criticized even inside the Adminstration.
"There was great controversy about whether the President should even meet Li Peng," a senior Administration official said. "It raised the whole debate about how best to bring the Chinese along -- by stiff-arming them or by coaxing them when they make a few moves in the right direction."
Administration officials have played down the significance of Friday's meeting, describing it as a short encounter and as a "courtesy" to the Chinese, who requested it.
When asked at a briefing today whether an announcement to lift sanctions was imminent, a State Department spokesman, Joe Snyder, said that once the two Governments completed the agreement reached during Mr. Baker's meeting in November, "the Administration plans to take the steps necessary to lift the June 1991 sanctions."
"We have no set timetable for lifting the sanctions," he said.
 
Divisions in Administration
 There are deep divisions in the Administration over the wisdom of lifting the sanctions. Many intelligence officials and some senior Pentagon officials oppose any easing of restrictions, while the White House is clearly in favor of such a move.
The State Department notified Congress in a letter in mid-December that it intends to lift the sanctions. But resistance by Chinese leaders to an American demand that they put their oral promise to Mr. Baker in writing has contributed to a delay, Administration officials said.
Some non-proliferation experts in the Administration are convinced that the recent sales to Syria and Pakistan would constitute clear violations of the agreement, known as the Missile Technology Control Regime, which was devised by the United States and other powers to limit the supply of ballistic missiles to the developing world.
But other officials use a broader interpretation, saying it is nearly impossible to determine whether the transfer of certain technology that might have dual uses is a violation.
The suspicion felt toward China in some quarters of the Administration was reflected in remarks by Defense Secretary Dick Cheney after a speech in Wisconsin this week. Mr. Cheney said that his "prime concern" with regard to China was proliferation.
"They have in the past, on occasion, been less than scrupulous in their concern for maintaining control over that technology," he said.
 
China's Policy 'Worrisome'
 Similarly, in testimony before the Senate Armed Services Committee last week, Lieut. Gen. James R. Clapper Jr., Director of the Defense Intelligence Agency, called Chinese arms sales and proliferation policies "worrisome," adding, "China is currently assisting many of the nations that we estimate will acquire a ballistic missile capability by the end of the decade."
China has vigorously denied selling entire missiles or warheads to Syria, Pakistan or any other country in the Middle East or Southwest Asia. Indeed, American officials say there is no proof Beijing has shipped such weapons.
Many American officials and independent non-proliferation experts have expressed growing concern, however, that China is circumventing the spirit of missile-control agreements by selling missile components. Such transfers are significant because developing countries can assemble the parts and, in some cases, market the technology themselves.
"These transfers would help both Syria and Pakistan develop a manufacturing capability for their own missiles and would even allow them to proliferate missiles to other countries," said Gary Milhollin, director of the Wisconsin Project on Nuclear Arms Control, a private group. "It happens at the very time that China is promising to change its behavior."
 
Limitations to Guidelines
 A senior Pentagon official acknowledged the limitations of the missile control guidelines, saying they were "a way to keep a lid on things, but not strictly control" proliferation.
American intelligence reports over the last few years have also shown that China has discussed with Iranian officials the possible sale of M-9 missiles.
Launchers and M-11 training missiles were delivered to Pakistan last year, despite Beijing's pledges not to sell medium-range missiles in the Middle East and Southwest Asia. The Chinese view the M-11, which is said to have a range of about 180 miles and can carry a nuclear warhead, as a short-range missile, although the United States considers it a medium-range missile covered by the missile technology agreement.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Democrats;
Democrats Anxiously Monitor Clinton's Strength

BYLINE: By ROBIN TONER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
These are jittery days for the Democrats, as they monitor the vital signs of Gov. Bill Clinton's campaign and watch the horizon for signs of a new candidate.
One Democrat on Capitol Hill described it as "CNN emotions," the unending ripples of political anxiety in the party since a supermarket tabloid leveled accusations that Mr. Clinton conducted a 12-year affair with a cabaret singer. 
 Mr. Clinton's allies say he is through the worst of the "feeding frenzy," and his campaign asserts that the candidate is back on track after denying the singer's accusations.
But Democrats -- those sympathetic to Mr. Clinton and those who hope he stumbles -- are still trying to assess the damage, and almost inevitably some Democrats are wondering whether other candidates will enter the race.
Howard Gelt, the Democratic chairman of Colorado, said, "There's been a little bit of talk out here, just as there's been around the country: Where's Al Gore, where's Sam Nunn, where's Mario -- again. That's just a nervousness that exists."
Mr. Gelt says the Arkansas Governor seems to be bearing up under the furor, but many Democrats argue that the real verdict on his political strength will come in the New Hampshire primary on Feb. 18.
The Clinton campaign is trying to declare the crisis over. George Stephanopolous, deputy campaign manager for Mr. Clinton, said today: "Look at the facts. The fact is, at the height of the scandal, Clinton raised almost half a million dollars in three days and received public endorsements from a host of public officials, while the polls either held steady or rose in New Hampshire."
He added, "The public has indicated it really didn't pay much attention to trash-for-cash journalism, and wanted to get back to the real issues."
Spokesmen for the potential candidates most often speculated about, including Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, Senator Lloyd Bentsen of Texas, Senator Bill Bradley of New Jersey and Senator Al Gore of Tennessee, dismiss the notion that they are reconsidering a race.
In a typical response, Jack DeVore, a spokesman for Senator Bentsen, said, "He has declined, when asked, to join in any of the speculation." Mr. DeVore added, "He has consistently been getting a lot of calls, and yes, the number of calls has increased."
Gov. Mario M. Cuomo reiterated this week that New York State's budget difficulties continued to preclude him from joining the race.
Mr. Gephardt is a favorite of many party insiders, because of his experience in the 1988 campaign and his message of economic nationalism, considered particularly acute during a recession. But spokesmen say he is planning to run for re-election to the Congress this year, although in an interview on NBC's "Today" program this week he did not seem to close the door completely on a Presidential bid.
The other campaigns are moving to quash the notion that a late-entry strategy could work. The campaign of Senator Bob Kerrey of Nebraska recently prepared a memorandum that outlined how difficult it would be for a candidate to enter the race at this point.
"In light of the Clinton situation, a lot of people would probably want to reconsider whether they'd like to be candidates this year," said Tad Devine, campaign director for Mr. Kerrey. "But I think if they look at it seriously, they will find they're effectively foreclosed because they will not be able to satisfy the filing requirements in a large number of states."
 
Missing the Deadlines
 Any new candidate would face formidable hurdles: By the end of January, filing deadlines will have passed in 18 states, which produce 40 percent of the pledged delegates to the convention, according to the Democratic National Committee. By the time the New Hampshire primary is over, filing deadlines will have passed in 27 states, covering nearly 60 percent of the pledged delegates, those who are chosen on the basis of primary and caucus results and are committed to particular candidates.
Still, Thomas E. Donilon, who was a delegate hunter for both Walter F. Mondale in 1984 and President Jimmy Carter in 1980, said a late entry, while unlikely, was "certainly not impossible." And Ronald H. Brown, the chairman of the Democratic National Committee, said, "It's never too late, but I think that speculation is being grossly overstated."
In fact, all the talk may be only a sign of an unsettled race. Mr. Clinton has presented himself as the most "electable" candidate in the field, and many in his party, conservative and liberal, seemed to believe him. "What I hear from Democrats is down deep they hope he can survive this thing because they don't see anybody else who could be the nominee," said Natalie Davis, a member of the Democratic National Committee from Alabama, who described herself as uncommitted.
Just a few weeks ago, many of his supporters saw him on a painless glide path to an early nomination. Now, his rivals sense an opening, although they are careful not to seem to be capitalizing on his misfortune.
"My sense of Clinton's support was that it was powered very much by a perception here that he was the candidate best able to beat Bush," said Mr. Devine, interviewed by telephone in New Hampshire this week. "We'll have to see if that perception is damaged.
Tim Raftis, who heads the campaign of Senator Tom Harkin of Iowa, argues, "I sense people are rethinking his electability argument, and not just on that, but also on his record in Arkansas."
All of this heightens the importance of New Hampshire, which will render the first primary verdict on Mr. Clinton's handling of the furor, including his appearance on the CBS News program "60 Minutes" and his acknowledgement of past difficulties in his marriage.
 
'I Want to Win'
 "This is all inane, stupid and insulting, and I hope the American people jam it down your-all's throat," Bob Slagle, the chairman of the Democratic Party in Texas, told reporters. "The national press corps is acting like a common street gossip in a small town."
Still, other Democrats are holding back. Representative Bob Wise of West Virginia, who said he was uncommitted but "very interested" in Mr. Clinton, said he wanted to wait a bit to see how well the Governor weathered the storm. "I want to win," Mr. Wise said. "I just don't want to have my heart broken one more time by somebody falling down."
Indeed, an important part of the party's psychology is the increasing conviction that Mr. Bush can be beaten -- if the Democrats rise to the moment. "The general attitude is, we can win it," said Mr. Brown, the party chairman. "I see Democrats all around the country, and the first thing they say to me is, we gotta win this thing. And if I say, who are you for, they say, 'I'm for the guy who can beat George Bush.' "

HEADLINE: Dinkins Orders Deep Cuts In an Austere Budget Plan

BYLINE: By JAMES C. McKINLEY Jr.

 BODY:
Mayor David N. Dinkins put forward an austere $29.4 billion spending plan yesterday that called for heavy cuts for all city agencies except the Police Department in the next fiscal year while holding the line on property taxes through 1996.
"I won't be glib," he said. "This plan does not contain much good news for New Yorkers in the short run. Someday I hope to be a good-news Mayor, presiding over good economic times." 
 The preliminary budget for the fiscal year beginning on July 1, which comes in the third year of the Mayor's four-year term, reflects the problem that has plagued him throughout his administration: how to satisfy increasingly demanding state fiscal watchdogs without abandoning his liberal agenda.
His approach is to be fiscally conservative while protecting his ambitious plan to expand the Police Department and shielding some social programs serving the poor, like drop-in centers for the homeless and infant mortality programs.
The cuts range widely among city agencies, but not evenly. The plan, which is subject to City Council approval, would put more police officers on the streets but fewer teachers in the schools and would settle for dirtier parks and more potholes.
The spending plan outlines a budget for the next fiscal year and shows how the Mayor proposes to keep the budget balanced in the three fiscal years after that. It avoids layoffs but freezes wages over the next two years. Leaders of the city's municipal labor unions said yesterday that they were pleased that the plan contained no layoffs but were concerned that it did not do enough to save jobs.
 
A Long Political Struggle
 The preliminary budget, only the first step in a long political struggle before a final budget is adopted in June, quickly drew cautious praise from the Council leaders and state fiscal monitors who had criticized earlier versions of the Mayor's long-term fiscal vision as unrealistic and timid.
The Council Speaker, Peter F. Vallone, a Democrat who controls enough votes in the Council to pass the final budget, said the plan was a good starting point for negotiations. "This is the first budget we've received from the Mayor that is a major step in the right direction," he said.
The budget now goes to the Council for preliminary hearings.
To fill a projected $1.5 billion gap in the next fiscal year and larger shortfalls in the next three years after that, the Mayor's spending plan proposes trimming more than 11,000 jobs through attrition by 1996; raising $115 million through new taxes on gasoline, automobiles and commuters' income next year; slashing spending on capital construction projects by 18 percent over four years, and freezing municipal wages at current levels for the first two years.
The plan also counts on "productivity" measures to pare $147 million from administrative and overhead costs of the sprawling municipal bureaucracy next year. For some agencies like the Social Services Department, the Mayor announced plans to take workers off the city payroll by, for instance, turning over the management of adult homeless shelters and some family preservation programs to private nonprofit groups.
Mr. Dinkins said his plan "doesn't depend on miracles or fiscal sleights of hand, doesn't put off till tomorrow the tough decisions that must be made today."
Still, the city's spending will actually rise by about $440 million next year, and the plan calls for cutting a mere 1,400 of the 211,000-strong municipal work force in the fiscal year beginning in June, though it promises to eliminate another 9,000 jobs by 1996. And while Mr. Dinkins proposed canceling construction projects ranging from new schools to a new jail on Staten Island, the bulk of the cuts will come in the last two years of the plan, in keeping with Mr. Dinkins's promise to fuel the local economy.
Among the plan's myriad effects, classes in schools would increase in size, about 50 percent fewer workers would be hired to fill potholes, parks would be visited by maintenance workers 8 times a month instead of 10 and the Fire Department would lose an engine company and two battalion command centers.
In a clear challenge to labor, the Mayor said he would freeze wages for the next two years unless municipal unions could pay for raises by saving through work-rule changes. He also said he would seek $150 million in labor concessions if some part of his plan fell apart.
Leaders of municipal labor unions warned that because the plan contained no wage increases in the next two years except through what is saved through concessions and work-rule changes, it would devastate city services and demoralize workers who may not be able to participate.
"We are not opposed to gain-sharing, but we are concerned that some union workers may not qualify for it because of the nature of the jobs," said Stanley Hill, president of District Council 37 of the American Federation of State, County and Municipal Employees.
Some of the larger cuts include $100 million in the 1993 fiscal year for the Board of Education, which will be asked to slash money for classroom supplies, after-school programs, summer school instruction and building maintenance. In the Sanitation Department, a cut of more than $36 million would mean the recycling program would not be expanded, and in the Transportation Department, a $31 million cut would curtail projects including street-sign repair and traffic engineering studies.
The plan calls for delaying a class of new police officers at the end of the 1993 fiscal year by a month to save money on paper by pushing the hiring into the next year's budget. But the Police Department will continue to grow over the next four years by 3,900 officers.
 
Crucial Element
 A crucial element in the Mayor's long-term fiscal plan is $700 million that would be raised by floating new city bonds and buying back older bonds now held by the state's Municipal Assistance Corporation. That would free sales tax revenues the city normally would pay to the corporation to back its bonds, the city's Budget Director, Philip R. Michael, said.
He said that the money would be used to "ease us over the hump" and that the plan would permanently shrink the government by enough to bring its long-term spending in line with revenues decimated by the recession. But the money would not be targeted toward specific programs that streamline the government, as some critics of the Mayor have urged, Mr. Michael said.
"That money is fungible," he said, adding that "there is no real earmarking of it."
Felix G. Rohatyn, chairman of the Municipal Assistance Corporation, which would play a large role in providing the aid, said the budget plan was "an improvement over the plan that was submitted last fall." But he expressed alarm about cuts in the capital budget that would curb construction of new schools and said he would support using the M.A.C. to raise money for that purpose.
The Mayor's plan also avoided using $200 million in surplus M.A.C. money that was available to it. City officials said that money might be used as a bargaining chip in negotiations with unions. It could be held out as an incentive get municipal labor leaders to provide savings, they said.
A good part of Mr. Dinkins's plan depends on actions by the State Legislature that are far from a forgone conclusion. The plan calls for a 5-cent gasoline tax that would cost the average driver about $25 a year, a property tax on automobiles and a tax on commuters that would provide the city $60 million a year.
Spokesmen for legislative leaders in Albany voiced skepticism about the property tax on automobiles and the gasoline tax, saying the two measures have repeatedly failed to win approval in the past, mostly because suburban legislators oppose them. The commuter tax will also face an uphill battle, they said.
"All three we've rejected in the past," said John E. McArdle, a spokesman for the State Senate majority leader, Ralph J. Marino, a Long Island Republican. "We have no reason to believe that if submitted again they would meet a different fate."
Mr. Dinkins's plan also hinges on increases in state aid for schools and an accelerated state takeover of Medicaid costs that would require approval by the Legislature. Both are unlikely, given the state's dire financial situation, legislative leaders said.
"It's going to be tough," Steven Greenberg, a spokesman for Assembly Speaker Saul Weprin, a Queens Democrat, said.
Raymond D. Horton, president of the Citizens Budget Commission, a private group financed by businesses, said he was disappointed that the Mayor's plan does not prune entire services out of the government but appears to borrow money and then reduce services over all. He said the use of the $700 million in refinanced bonds amounted to "deficit financing."

HEADLINE: Yeltsin's Ambiguity Persists: Democrat Who Rules by Fiat

BYLINE: By SERGE SCHMEMANN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Jan. 30

 BODY:
Seven months since he became President of Russia and seven weeks since he led the dismantling of the Soviet Union, Boris N. Yeltsin remains an ambiguous and complex figure: a democrat who rules by decree, a populist who risks draconian reform, a public politician who defies easy classification.
Making his first visit to America as head of an independent Russia and heir to the atomic weapons of the Kremlin, he still carries traces of the clumsiness and inexperience that marked his first forays abroad. 
 But he has confounded foreign skeptics who thought he could never shoulder the mantle of independent power, claiming for Russia the legacy and responsibilities of Soviet power and demonstrating his intention to persevere in liquidating all vestiges of the cold war.
At home, Russians chafe and grumble under the steep price increases he ordered as the first phase of a bold economic gamble. But despite relentless sniping from foes and onetime allies, he has refused to retreat and has plunged into angry crowds around Russia to urge perseverance on his nation.
His critics concede his courage and his singular hold on the Russian masses, while his supporters chafe at his neglect of democratic processes as he issues sheaves of decrees and dispatches personal envoys to carry his will around the nation.
He is accused of accumulating the trappings of imperial power, moving in quick succession from his "White House" offices to the old Communist headquarters and finally into the Kremlin. But his team remains quaintly inexperienced and his actions often seem improvised, and he has made no effort to curb the steady flow of criticism from politicians and the press.
 
Views of a Leader
 He is arguably the most public leader in Russian or Soviet history, with a published autobiography and regular public appearances. But his thoughts and ambitions remain a mystery, and Muscovites seem unable to decide whether he is the cunning peasant of popular lore, a Communist boss in new clothing or a budding Russian potentate -- or something of them all.
A month ago, surrendering the levers of power to his victorious adversary, Mikhail S. Gorbachev declared in an interview: "I think the phenomenon of Yeltsin is still to be revealed. I wish he could always be consistent. I wish he were more democratic. It wouldn't hurt him."
The sentiment was widely shared then and still is now. But then it was Mr. Gorbachev's repeated error in his long struggle with Mr. Yeltsin that he failed to fully appreciate the consistency of Mr. Yeltsin's fundamental faith in democracy and reform, however vague his perception of either.
Somewhat like former President Ronald Reagan or Chancellor Helmut Kohl in Germany, Mr. Yeltsin has advanced in part by exceeding expectations. Americans dismissed him after his first visit as a boozing provincial politician, Russian intellectuals treated him patronizingly in opposition as a convenient foil against Mr. Gorbachev, and Mr. Gorbachev initially thought he could shoo him away like an annoying gnat.
Yet Mr. Yeltsin now has the central stage to himself, and if the reviews are modest, they are probably better than many expected.
"Yeltsin's first months in office form a complex, contradictory but generally encouraging picture," said Dimitri K. Simes, a senior associate with the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace in Washington and a frequent visitor to Moscow.
"What is encouraging is his commitment to reform. He was not frightened by the first resistance to higher prices. He demonstrated flexibility, yet toughness with other commonwealth members. The buck stops with him. And in foreign policy his heart is certainly in the right place.
 
'Old Party Apparatus'
 "But he is still Boris Yeltsin. He is from the old party apparatus, and when the going gets tough he still has a tendency to rely on people of his type, people he knew, people he is comfortable with. The sense is that he is determined to proceed with reforms, but that very often he's bigger on intentions than on appreciation of nuance and complexity."
There is a growing perception, for example, that Mr. Yeltsin was had by President Leonid M. Kravchuk of Ukraine in launching the Commonwealth of Independent States. In Kiev they talk of the "Ukrainian fox" getting the better of the "Russian bear."
Mr. Yeltsin evidently thought that the commonwealth agreement would serve to preserve a measure of economic and military unity among the old Soviet republics. But no sooner was the ink dry and Mr. Gorbachev ousted, than Ukraine plunged ahead with preparations for its own currency and army, and finally with a claim to the Black Sea Fleet.
 
Dispute Over Fleet
 Mr. Yeltsin is now stuck with a nasty dispute, and his adversaries among Russian nationalists -- Vice President Aleksandr Rutskoi first among them -- have been handed a dandy political weapon in the form of Russian indignation at Ukrainian control of the Crimea.
At home, nobody questions Mr. Yeltsin's determination and courage in launching the economic reforms. But his Government, largely composed of people of marginal experience, does not yet inspire great faith.
Gennadi E. Burbulis, the Deputy Prime Minister, is a former ideology instructor from Sverdlovsk who ruthlessly dispersed his adversaries, but has demonstrated little skill in forging political alliances. Yegor T. Gaidar, the architect of the economic program, has had little experience in government, and Moscow businessmen say his foreign advisers, including Jeffrey Sachs from the United States, have little appreciation of Russian realities.
Mr. Yeltsin's decision to merge the K.G.B. and the Interior Ministry sent shivers through a nation only recently delivered of a police state, and his own newly formed Constitutional Court declared the super-security apparatus unlawful.
The Russian President's efforts at halting ethnic strife in the Caucasus, whether by trying to mediate between Armenians and Azerbaijanis in Nagorno-Karabakh or attempting to impose martial law in the rebellious region of Chechen-Ingushetia, proved humiliatingly futile.
Mr. Yeltsin has also repeatedly demonstrated that however strong his devotion to democracy or private enterprise, his understanding of them is shaky. Touring Russian cities recently, he alternated between attempts to explain the purpose of higher prices with peremptory and dubiously legal orders that store managers who raised prices too high be dismissed.
These and other shaky actions have fed sustained criticism not only from former Communists, but also from Mr. Yeltsin's former allies -- Mr. Rutskoi again first among them, followed closely by Ruslan Khasbulatov, the man Mr. Yeltsin backed as Speaker of the Russian Parliament.
And while Mr. Yeltsin has taken efforts to sharpen his image, tamping down his unruly shock of gray hair and acquiring a wardrobe of double-breasted suits, he still has his share of social bloopers -- like the time in Bonn last fall when he downed a glass of champagne at a gulp, Russian-style, during a series of formal toasts.
 
Steadiness on Market Policy
 Yet Mr. Yeltsin has earned credit for staying the course. His response to the Ukrainian challenges has been measured and calm, preventing the dispute from turning unduly emotional. Officials in his Government say he has not wavered in his support for the economic reforms, however strong the political and public outcries.
Despite rumors of heavy drinking, he demonstrates robust energy for a man of 60, maintaining a grueling schedule of travel and public appearances. He never loses control in public, sitting steadily through long speeches and delivering his own in a booming, authoritative voice.
If he was outmaneuvered by Mr. Kravchuk in the creation of the commonwealth, he has never wavered in his assertion of Russian primacy among the heirs to the Soviet Union, and has reassured the West by calmly assuming responsibility for the nuclear arsenal and the bulk of the military and by reaffirming Russia's adherence to extensive arms reductions.
If Mr. Yeltsin often acts as an "elected autocrat," it seems less a grasp for power than a search for what is expedient. That, as Mr. Gorbachev suggested, is worrying, but then it is no less than Mr. Gorbachev did when he assumed extraordinary powers. Unlike Mr. Yeltsin, Mr. Gorbachev was never elected by popular vote.
 
A Strong Hand?
 Perhaps more to the point, the combination of democratic conviction and autocratic authority appears to be what Russia wants, and may well be the only force that can guide it through this critical juncture in its history.
Most Russians accept that the crisis in their land can only be countered through decisive action, and no leader comes anywhere near Mr. Yeltsin in popular acceptance.
A public-opinion poll by the Institute of Sociology conducted in Moscow this month found that 32.8 percent of those questioned had full confidence in Mr. Yeltsin, and another 19.6 percent answered "I rather trust him than not." His most active supporters were the young and businessmen.
"He is indispensable for the time being," Mr. Simes said. "There is no one else with his commitment to change who can carry the Russian people with him. That is what Americans have to understand, that with all his shortcomings, there is no constructive alternative."

HEADLINE: THE FEDERAL BUDGET: The Overview;
Mood of Compromise Prevails on Election-Year Budget

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
President Bush's budget director, Richard G. Darman, went to Capitol Hill today to begin the yearly ritual of negotiating with Congress over the President's tax and spending proposals, amid indications that both sides might be willing to strike some compromises in this election year.
Several Democrats welcomed Mr. Bush's proposal to impose some type of overall limit on the growth of Federal benefit programs, including Medicare and Medicaid. Mr. Darman indicated that the Administration would consider some changes in the budget agreement of 1990, which severely restricts the ability of Congress to shift money from one category of spending to another. 
 Nonetheless, Mr. Darman said the Administration would firmly resist Congressional efforts to use savings from the military budget to pay for domestic programs, as many Democrats want to do. Some Democrats say the money cut from the Pentagon budget should be used for social programs and to help workers, cities and counties adversely affected by the closing of military bases and by cutbacks in production of warplanes, missiles, submarines and other weapons.
 
Tax Cut Possibility
 The budget director said Mr. Bush was prepared, under certain conditions, to use military savings to pay for a cut in taxes for families with children.
Mr. Darman's testimony today before the House Budget Committee tended to confirm the impression of many lawmakers that the Administration would be drawn into renegotiating the 1990 agreement. Once such negotiations begin, neither Mr. Bush nor Mr. Darman could control their scope or outcome, lawmakers say.
The agreement, which set a statutory framework for national decisions on taxes and spending, was supposed to last five years. But many members of Congress and economists have concluded that it needs to be revised as a result of the reduced military threat to the United States since the collapse of Communism.
At the hearing today, Mr. Darman made a spirited defense of the President's budget request for the fiscal year 1993, which begins Oct. 1, and seemed to enjoy sparring with members of Congress. He found bipartisan interest in Mr. Bush's proposal to impose, for the first time, an overall limit on Federal spending for rapidly growing benefit programs like Medicare, Medicaid and food stamps.
Representative Leon E. Panetta, a California Democrat who is chairman of the committee, welcomed the proposal as a means of "achieving better control" over such benefit programs. Congress sets eligibility criteria and benefit levels for those programs, but does not directly control the total amounts spent each year; anyone who meets the eligibility criteria can obtain benefits.
Benefit payments accounted for 28 percent of all Federal spending in 1965, when Medicare and Medicaid were created. They now account for slightly more than 50 percent.
Referring to such outlays, Mr. Darman said, "The big gaping hole in the current budget enforcement system is the inherited structure of mandatory spending programs that just continues to grow without control or annual review, and without having to come before Congress or the President."
The ranking Republican on the Senate Budget Committee, Pete V. Domenici of New Mexico, also welcomed the proposal for "an enforceable cap" on Federal spending for benefit programs. "We never have been able to get a handle on them," he said.
Lawmakers of both parties expressed concern that Congress and the Administration would try to outdo each other in cutting taxes in this election year, with the result that future budget deficits would be even higher than now projected.
Mr. Panetta said Mr. Bush's budget included a long list of proposed tax cuts and would tempt Congress to enter "a bidding war of the type we saw in 1981." In that year, Congress approved President Ronald Reagan's proposals to cut individual income tax rates and business taxes in an effort to stimulate economic growth.
The House majority leader, Representative Richard A. Gephardt, Democrat of Missouri, said, "There should not be a bidding war over the tax and recovery package" this year.
Mr. Darman agreed that there was a political temptation to woo voters in an election year, and he said, "We ought to do everything we can to prevent that bidding war from breaking out."
 
Partisan Struggle Foreseen
 Despite the amicable atmosphere of today's hearing, many lawmakers say they expect partisan political warfare over the budget, as Democratic and Republican members of Congress struggle for advantage.
"We as Democrats are committed to prompt passage of an economic recovery program," Mr. Gephardt said. "Democrats will not obstruct, but we will try to improve the President's package. Democrats will cooperate, but we will not fold. We will provide a clear distinction between the allegiances and ambitions of the two political parties."
In a separate move, the White House today disclosed details of its plan for "a 90-day moratorium on any new Federal regulations that could hinder growth" of the nation's economy. Mr. Bush has asked the head of each Federal agency to identify rules that can be suspended, revised or repealed. Vice President Dan Quayle, as head of Mr. Bush's Council on Competitiveness, will have a central role in reviewing such regulations to determine if the costs outweigh the benefits.
The Vice President today invited businesses to suggest rules that should be changed or repealed. "Now's your chance," he told a group of lobbyists for companies regulated by the Government. "Come and tell us what regulations and rules are burdening the private sector. To the overzealous regulators: you've met the enemy and it's called the Competitiveness Council."
Mr. Bush established the council in 1989 to find ways of making the United States more competitive in international markets. The panel has concluded that Federal officials, including some appointed by Mr. Bush, impose too many regulations on American businesses.
Gary D. Bass, executive director of O.M.B. Watch, a consumer group that monitors the Federal Office of Management and Budget, said, "The 90-day moratorium is essentially a stop-work order" to regulatory agencies. He said it could endanger public health and safety by blocking rules that were to be issued by the Environmental Protection Agency, the Food and Drug Administration and the Occupational Saftey and Health Administration.

Darman, right, the budget director, conferring with Barry Anderson, assistant budget director, holding a briefing book on the budget, before Mr. Darman negotiated with the House Budget Committee over the President's tax and spending proposals yesterday in Washington. (Michael Geissinger for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Yeltsin, in Britain, Warns Of Peril of Russian Unrest

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LONDON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
At a stopover on his way to the United Nations, President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia warned today that his country could collapse into "general unrest" and authoritarian rule if its economic reforms failed, and appealed for continued help from Western countries.
He left with broad promises, but not with much that he could take to the bank, just like the Soviet President, Mikhail S. Gorbachev, last July, after his meeting with leaders of the world's seven most powerful industrial democracies. 
 Mr. Yeltsin arrived at 10 Downing Street this morning on his first international trip since the Christmas collapse of the Soviet Union, with his financial and military advisers in a fleet of polished black Russian Zil limousines. After four hours of talks with the British Prime Minister, John Major, the Russian leader said he "respected" Britain's refusal to include its nuclear forces in any arms reduction talks.
Before leaving Moscow, Mr. Yeltsin had welcomed President Bush's announcement Tuesday night of major cuts in American ground-based and seaborne nuclear warheads, matched them with offers of even bigger cuts in Russia's sea-based systems, and called on Britain, France and China to "follow our example."
 
British Nuclear Force
 Mr. Major explained this morning that Britain would go ahead with plans to replace its Polaris submarine force, and much of its air force nuclear capability, with a deterrent force of four Trident submarines that could carry up to 512 warheads by the end of the 1990's. He told Mr. Yeltsin that it was "only a minimum deterrent force, threatening no one."
Before he left this afternoon, Mr. Yeltsin said Russia would no longer aim its missiles at Britain; he made a similar pledge concerning the United States last week. "The number of nuclear weapons at the disposal of the U.K. is not comparable with ours and therefore the matter is not really worth discussing," he said.
Most of their discussions concerned economic affairs, according to the two leaders and their aides. Mr. Major said Britain would press for full Russian membership in the International Monetary Fund by April of this year, and would urge the other industrial democracies to find ways of giving further assistance to the republic, "possibly in the form of a ruble stabilization fund."
In a joint declaration, Russia and Britain pledged to insure that nuclear weapons and other weapons of mass destruction were held under secure control at all times. Mr. Major said that Britain would send a technical mission to Moscow to see what could be done to help the Russians store, dispose of, and safeguard nuclear materials from the weapons they dismantle as they cut back their military forces.
 
Advice for Russian Military
 He also said he had offered to send "a small number" of British Defense Ministry officials to the Moscow headquarters of Defense Minister Yevgeny I. Shaposhnikov to help with restructuring of the Russian armed forces and insuring effective democratic control. Mr. Yeltsin gave no indication whether he would welcome them.
Saying that Britain was determined to help democracy in Russia, Mr. Major enumerated the aid programs already underway, bilaterally and within the European Community, amounting to $670 million so far, and pointed to the Group of Seven wealthy industrial nations and the International Monetary Fund as the best sources of further assistance. The only new aid he mentioned today was $504 million Britain was making available for an International Monetary Fund program insuring export credits and investments in Russia and other former Soviet republics
"The only thing that can impede our progress will be general unrest," Mr. Yeltsin said, "and general unrest will happen if our reforms fail. Should the reforms fail, we shall face a new leadership and Russia will fall into the habits which tortured us for 74 years." Before leaving for the United States, he said the amount of aid the West was providing was "by no means" enough. "Deeds we need, not words," he said.
In a joint declaration, the two countries agreed to be "friendly states and partners within the international community," and promised to formalize their relationship in a British-Russian treaty.
Mr. Yeltsin then went off to visit Mr. Major's predecessor, Margaret Thatcher, and reboard his plane for New York.

HEADLINE: Russian Vice President Wants to Redraw Borders

BYLINE: By CELESTINE BOHLEN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MOSCOW, Jan. 30

 BODY:
In a nationalist broadside published today in the newspaper Pravda, Vice President Aleksandr V. Rutskoi of Russia said he would take up the cause of Russia's "historical conscience" and seek a redrawing of borders that would reflect a "glorious page" in the nation's past.
Although Mr. Rutskoi did not mention the Crimean Peninsula directly, his latest statement -- another in a series that have put distance between him and his old running mate, President Boris N. Yeltsin -- threatens to sharpen an emotional issue that lies at the heart of a dispute between Russia and Ukraine. 
 The Crimea, including Sevastopol, home port for the Black Sea Fleet, was made part of Ukraine in 1954 by an order issued by Nikita S. Khrushchev, then the Soviet leader. But for many Russians, the Crimea is an inseparable part of their history, a region rich in romance, summer home to czars and writers, and scene of bloody battles in the Crimean War.
"For us, the decree of former Communist administrators, hasty decisions by the Politburo do not amount to a decree," wrote Mr. Rutskoi. "For us, the principal decree is Russia's thousand-year-old history. The demarcation of borders, and only the demarcation of borders, will dot all the "i"s and define Russia as a power, and not as a beggar within the Commonwealth of Independent States.
"Russians' historic conscience does not allow the mechanical association of the borders of Russia and of the Russian federation, thus denying what was a glorious page in Russia history."
Negotiations are under way over the disposition of the Black Sea Fleet, with the commonwealth expected to take command of its strategic components and Ukraine to assume control over the rest.
But the issue was clouded again this week by Mr. Yeltsin's abrupt and unannounced meeting with the fleet's commanders off Russia's Black Sea coast on the eve of his trip to Europe and the United States.
Mr. Yeltsin's trip and Mr. Rutskoi's article indicate that tensions between Russian and Ukraine are far from resolved. A week ago, the Russian Parliament called for a re-examination of the Crimean question, scheduling hearings for early February. At the time, Mr. Yeltsin voiced his regret that the issue was being raised at all, but popular sentiment might force him to change course.
Last month, Mr. Rutskoi, an air force general twice wounded in Afghanistan, angered members of Mr. Yeltsin's Government with a public and taunting critique of its plans to liberalize prices. Soon afterward, shunned by Mr. Yeltsin, he was stripped of the chairmanship of five committees he had been named to head.
Today, nearly a month after the price reforms began, Mr. Rutskoi declared them to be a political and economic disaster.
"The impoverishment of the population is taking on catastrophic proportions," he said. "Now it seems to me, we can already discuss our survival, as a Russian nation."
Other political figures, including the chairman of the Russian Parliament, Ruslan Khasbulatov, have joined Mr. Rutskoi in attacking the Government's economic policies, a position that is likely to gain favor as popular discontent over high prices and sluggish supplies rises.
Today's article in Pravda marked the second time in a just over a month that front-page display in the old Communist paper had been given to Mr. Rutskoi, who heads a party that is an offshoot of the defunct Communist Party.
