HEADLINE: Influx From Ex-Soviet Lands Falling, Israel Says

BYLINE: By ALAN COWELL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: JERUSALEM, Jan. 30

 BODY:
As Israel presses the United States for loan guarantees to cope with a projected huge influx of immigrants from the former Soviet Union, officials here said today that the immigrant flow this month had sunk to its lowest in almost two years and could dwindle even further.
Jewish Agency officials attributed the reduced flow of immigrants to the acute difficulties facing those who have already arrived, cannot find jobs or decent homes and write back to friends and relatives in the former Soviet Union chronicling their tribulations. 
 "Sadly, we have to admit the facts," said Uri Gordon, chairman of the quasi-governmental agency's immigration department. "We are on the threshold of losing a great opportunity for immigration because of the dire unemployment situation and because of housing problems."
"If the existing situation continues, this may lead to a slow dying out of immigration," he said. "The Government has no answer to the question of employment. Until now it hasn't formulated a national plan for absorption. Every ministry is doing things for absorption on its own, but the plans have remained on paper."
The Jewish Agency, formed before Israel's founding in 1948, takes initial responsibility for immigrants, but their long-term fortunes here are the responsibility of Government ministries.
"Absorption is in crisis," Mr. Gordon said. "After three years of immigration from the Soviet Union, I see a situation of impotence. There is no long-term planning. I believe there'll be continued immigration, but the situation is worrisome."
According to the agency's provisional figures, 5,800 immigrants from the former Soviet Union have arrived so far this month, with 600 more expected before the start of February, a lower figure than those recorded during the Persian Gulf war, when Iraqi Scud missiles fell on Tel Aviv and other places. The last time the figure fell below 7,000 was in February 1990.
"No doubt the main reason for lower immigration figures is indeed hardships of absorption principally in the field of employment," said Gad Ben Ari, a Jewish Agency spokesman who took issue with Mr. Gordon's comments, saying there had been fluctuations in immigration patterns in the past. "I do not see this month's low figure as a significant change."
Even after Iraq's Scud attacks began last January, however, more than 7,000 immigrants arrived the month after and the flow rebounded to 13,000 in March 1991.
Since late 1989, when the wave of Jewish emigration from the Soviet Union began, some 350,000 immigrants have arrived in Israel. The wave peaked in December 1990, when, according to the Jewish Agency, more than 35,000 arrived, making 1990 a record year.
Since then, the figures have generally been lower.
Right now, Israel is heading for early elections in which the immigrants will play a double role both as an issue and as first-time voters.
The Labor Party's parliamentary chairman, Haim Ramon, said the party would agree to a proposal by Prime Minister Yitzhak Shamir's Likud Party that the vote be held on June 23. Up until today, Labor had been pressing for a June 16 election. But by tonight there had been no formal announcement of the election date.
The immigration issue has assumed a further dimension, moreover, as Israel seeks $10 billion in loan guarantees from the United States to cope with the influx. The American guarantees would make it easier and cheaper to raise commercial loans.
 
Contradictory Arguments
 The reportedly dwindling immigration figures, however, could promote two contradictory arguments: either that the sought-after amount is exaggerated or that the guarantees are more necessary than ever to persuade would-be immigrants that they will find a better life than those who have already arrived.
Israeli conversation is peppered with stories of Jews from the former Soviet Union finding only menial work despite high qualifications -- concert musicians playing on street corners, a computer systems engineer delivering milk, a doctor working as a hospital orderly.
The United States is seeking to link the loan guarantees to a halt to further settlements in occupied territories, but Ariel Sharon, the hard-line Housing Minister, reiterated today that Israel would continue building settlements throughout territory under its control.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Personal Finances;
Brown Avoids Doing the Expected, Even With His Own Money

BYLINE: By B. DRUMMOND AYRES Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SACRAMENTO, Calif., Jan. 24

 BODY:
Former Gov. Edmund G. Brown Jr. of California has always been a politician willing to take the big risk and the contrarian course, and he manages his personal finances in exactly the same way.
Over the last 20 years he has accumulated about $1 million in land, buildings and stocks, which in many instances, though not all, are holdings well out of the economic mainstream. 
 A few years ago, according to information drawn from Federal and state financial disclosure statements, Mr. Brown was probably worth more than he is now, perhaps several hundred thousand dollars more. But some of his riskier investments in real estate and newly formed advanced-technology companies have been hit hard by the recession. Some are now worth less than he paid, while producing no dividends or other income.
For example, an investment of $20,000 in the saturated apartment market here produced less than $200 in income last year. Similarly, investments in a mail-order prescription drug firm and a new Texas company that specializes in private satellite communications paid few if any dividends last year.
 
Fluctuating Assessments
 Mr. Brown estimated his financial worth today as closer to $750,000 than the more than $1 million shown on his financial statements and other public documents. But he acknowledged that a precise figure was hard to reach because of widely fluctuating assessments in the present economic climate. More than half his investments are in real estate, including an old San Francisco fire station that he converted into a house. The rest are divided fairly evenly between bank deposits and stocks and bonds.
Much of his financial worth, he said, resulted from investments he made after receiving cash gifts from his father, Edmund G. Brown Sr., the former California Governor who made his fortune through practicing law and investing, particularly in petroleum interests. The younger Mr. Brown would not disclose the size of the gifts from his father, but he said a good part of his wealth had also been accumulated through a law practice and by living as frugally as the priest he once wanted to be.
"My family has been very, very generous to me," he said. "But I've also been good at picking emerging stocks, such as newly started medical and communication ventures, the kind of things that appeal to my mind and imagination. The rest of what I've got comes from the law and from being one of the world's great savers -- living off less than $1,000 a month most of my life."
He recalled that when he was Governor here from 1974 to 1982 he lived in a bare-walled apartment rather than the Executive Mansion and moved about the state in an old sedan rather than a limousine.
 
'I'm Not Money Oriented'
 "I saved my money, and I saved the state's money," he said. "I'm not money oriented. But I've got enough for me, for a bachelor."
Mr. Brown had one of his best years in 1990, the last year for which figures are available. His stocks and real estate investments suffered from the recession, but his law practice brought in more than $50,000, and a longtime friend who is a Los Angeles real estate agent paid him a finder's fee of more than $50,000 after he found a buyer for a piece of property in a neighborhood favored by film stars.
Neither the agent, Joyce Rey, nor Mr. Brown would disclose details of the transaction.
The former Governor said much of his law practice consisted of "bringing in clients" for Fulbright & Jaworski, a major firm with offices around the United States and abroad.
In past elections Mr. Brown has accepted the maximum amounts of campaign money allowed from political action committees and individuals, some of them close acquaintances. But in the 1992 campaign he has made his major issue a vow not to accept money from political action committees or to take gifts larger than $100 from individuals -- one-tenth of the allowable amount.
Thus far, according to the latest figures available from Mr. Brown's campaign headquarters, he has raised only $700,000, ranking him last in collections among the five major Democratic candidates. He has not contributed any of his own money to the campaign.
"I've concluded that too many politicians get too close to the people who give them big money," he said. "I can make it through this campaign on the small gifts that are coming in and I won't be beholden to any big interests."
Mr. Brown's personal financial investments, in some instances, are in companies and ventures controlled by friends and political acquaintances. But in the current campaign, as in his previous ones, he has cut many of his business ties, resigning from the board of ICN Biomedicals, a California medical supply company that is part of ICN Pharmaceuticals. He also gave up his association with Fulbright & Jaworski.
Official documents released by Mr. Brown over the years show a slow but steady accumulation of wealth. Two decades ago he owned only a home in Los Angeles, valued at less than $100,000, and had an annual income of less than $40,000, all of it salary except for a few hundred dollars in interest and dividends.
Today, according to various government documents, he is worth fromroughly $1.2 million to $1.4 million. It is not possible to pin down the worth any more precisely because in disclosing his holdings to run for office he is required to give only a range of value for a piece of real estate, a stock or other financial entity.
Thus, he listed his interest in Glenborough Ltd., a San Francisco-based commercial real estate partnership, as worth from $100,000 to $250,000, and he reported his earnings in it in 1990, the last year available, as more than $2,500 but less than $5,000.
"Actually," he said in an interview, "it's worth about zero in the current real estate market."
At times, Mr. Brown's investment decisions have paid off handsomely, as they did in the mid-1980's, when he was able to sell a home in Los Angeles for more than $700,000, roughly 10 times what he bought it for 15 years earlier.
But real estate agents estimate that the converted fire station, which he and his father bought in 1989, is now worth about $200,000 less than the $1.2 million purchase price because of the recession.
On the other hand, Mr. Brown's other major real estate investment, 160 acres of undeveloped mountain property northeast of Sacramento, has increased in value since he purchased it in 1978. It is now assessed at $232,000, about $50,000 more than its assessed value in 1978.
Like many investors, Mr. Brown suffered heavily in the stock market in 1990. One major holding, Medi Mail, a new California company specializing in mail-order drugs, paid him less than $200 in dividends on an investment of over $50,000.
"It's one of those start-up, cutting-edge companies that I like to deal in," Mr. Brown said. "They'll take off eventually."

HEADLINE: Drive U.S. Cars (and Sensibly), Judge Says

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: PONTIAC, Mich., Jan. 30

 BODY:
A judge has given a chronic speeder his driver's license back on the condition that he drive only American-built cars.
Alexander Zelikov, a 25-year-old professional test driver, lost his license to suspension in July after getting too many speeding tickets in his own car. 
 When he appeared on Tuesday in Oakland County Circuit Court in Pontiac, 25 miles north of Detroit, Judge Hilda Gage called Mr. Zelikov a "menace to the county." But she restored his license on condition that he drive to and from work on a single route, carry increased liability insurance and test only American-built cars for his employer, the Daikin Clutch Corporation, which supplies manual-transmission clutches to the Big Three and foreign automobile makers.
The order was prompted "by a sense of patriotism and a concern for the economy," said Judge Gage, who drives an Oldsmobile.
"What can I say," said Mr. Zelikov, "except that I have to drive and I'm not going to contest it. I have to go to work."

HEADLINE: Dow Rebounds by 19.90 And Closes at 3,244.86

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO

 BODY:
Stock prices displayed some resilience yesterday, rebounding from the sharp selloff the previous day, but some analysts said the market was showing signs of weakness. After a 47-point drop on Wednesday, the Dow Jones industrial average gained 19.90 to finish at 3,244.86.
Analysts said the bounce was typical after a big selloff, and some said they expected a pullback, adding that when the Dow set a record on Tuesday other indexes lagged behind. 
 Robert C. Walberg, equity analyst for MMS International, said investors regained their composure after overreacting to comments about the economy on Wednesday by Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman. "People regained some confidence that if the employment numbers, due out next week, are weak, the Fed is likely to act," he said.
Another factor, Mr. Walberg said, is that there are still many investors waiting to enter the market, and Wednesday's selloff gave them that opportunity. But he found it disturbing that recent market gains had come with narrow leadership, with Coca-Cola and Boeing the strong Dow stocks yesterday. "If the rest of the troops do not follow these generals, then the battle will be lost," he said.
Volume on the New York Stock Exchange was down, but still heavy at 195.1 million shares, compared with 249 million shares on Wednesday, as the Big Board composite gained nearly three-quarters of a point, to 227.37. Gaining issues were ahead of losing ones, 888 to 789.
The recent pattern of high volume and volatility but little gains is another sign that the market may be near its top and ripe for a correction, Mr. Walberg added.
The Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks rose 1.28 points, to 411.62, with the American Stock Exchange up a similar amount to 410.92. Trading on the Nasdaq was heavy as the composite gained 5.06, to 621.37, while the Russell 2,000 gained a point to close at 204.87.
 
Blue-Chip Strength
 In early trading, the market failed twice to rally. But investors noticed little damage, with the support level of 3,215 on the Dow holding, and that seemed to encourage more buying. By midday, the blue chips were steadily higher, helped by strong gains in Coca-Cola, up 5 5/8, to 77 1/8; Merck, 2 7/8, to 155 1/4; Boeing, 2 1/8, to 52 3/4, and McDonald's, 2, to 44 1/4.
Among the economic data released yesterday, personal spending was up only three-tenths of 1 percent in December, and while personal incomes climbed 1 percent, that gain was partly attributed to increasing unemployment insurance payments.
eral Reserve will cut interest rates again, traders said. It was the first decline in three months, and the largest in more than a year.
In Big Board activity, strong fourth-quarter earnings and plans for a 2-for-1 stock split were behind Coke's performance yesterday.
Bristol-Myers Squibb and Immunex posted gains after they announced an agreement to exchange the marketing rights involving several cancer drugs. Bristol-Myers was up 7/8, to 80 5/8, and Immunex 5 3/8, to 62.
Many of the aerospace stocks that fell on Wednesday over concern about military budget cuts recouped yesterday. Boeing came back after it reported better-than-expected earnings for the fourth quarter, prompting a number of analysts to raise their 1992 earnings forecasts and repeat their "buy" recommendations.

HEADLINE: India Drops a Ban on Foreign Trademarks

BYLINE: By SANJOY HAZARIKA, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: NEW DELHI, Jan. 30

 BODY:
In steps aimed at opening the economy further, India's central bank announced today that it was dismantling an 18-year-old law that banned big foreign companies from selling products under their internationally known trademarks. It also ended an earlier rule that blocked the companies from owning property in India.
The developments came as Prime Minister P.V. Narasimha Rao, who heads a minority Government and is pushing ambitious economic changes, traveled to New York to attend a special United Nations Security Council meeting called by Prime Minister John Major of Britain. 
 This is Mr. Rao's first visit to the United States since he became head of Government last summer and started India's most wide-ranging economic changes since independence in 1947, slashing Government controls, rules and subsidies and undercutting the dominance of the public sector. He is expected to meet with President Bush, President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia and Prime Minister Li Peng of China.
 
Trademarks Are In
 The decision on trademarks means that multinational corporations can sell products ranging from soft drinks to computers with their original names.
For example, Pepsico, the soft drink giant, markets its drinks here under the brand name Lehar Pepsi. It has two Indian collaborators.
The easing of these rules are aimed at making foreign products more competitive and attractive to consumers in India. It also means that foreign companies can, theoretically, strongly challenge the country's powerful but largely protected private corporations in almost every sector.
United States Trade Representative Carla A. Hills has filed a legal case against India calling for the strengthening of its trademark, patent and copyright laws and enforcement, and has set a deadline of Feb. 26 for either an agreement or the imposition of trade sanctions. Today's action by India appeared to address only one of these three areas of concerns.
 
Delegations Are Visiting
 India's economic announcements, published today, were made during the visits of Mario Soares, the President of Portugal and the European Economic Community, and a powerful Japanese business delegation that has insisted on major changes in the country's investment policies. Although the changes began last year, the size of new investment has not been significant, and Western diplomats and business executives as well as politicians and officials here attribute this to a wait-and-see policy.
"Many foreign corporations want to see whether the Government will last, whether it will be able to implement its policies and whether there will be opposition from within," a senior executive with Digital Equipment, the computer company, said.
Cabinet ministers in Mr. Rao's Government have publicly spoken of adverse political repercussions of the liberalization, especially among peasants, who pay no income tax and benefit from Government subsidies for seeds, fertilizers and electricity, and from organized labor, worried by large-scale layoffs that could follow plant modernization.
In other moves, the Reserve Bank of India said that foreign companies, which were regulated by the Foreign Exchange Regulation Act aimed at increasing the inflow of foreign funds into India and curbing the outflow, will be allowed to buy property, set up direct trading offices, instead of depending on subsidiaries, and borrow money and accept deposits from the public.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Weighing the Budget
The Administration will firmly resist Congressional efforts to use savings from the military budget to pay for domestic programs, as many Democrats want, Bush's budget director said. [A14.]
Builders reacted with enthusiasm to Bush's proposal to stimulate housing sales. [A14.] Throughout the real estate industry, hopes rose that Congress would adopt a tax change. [A14.] 
 The flexible I.R.A. proposed by Bush could better be described as a savings program for middle-income people. [A15.]
Though it includes several notable incentives to spur research and development, the budget stops short of being a full-fledged effort that many executives say is needed. [A15.]
 
The Economy
Orders to U.S. factories for durable goods plunged an unexpectedly steep 5 percent, the first decline in three months. [D1.] Personal spending slowed to a gain of 0.3 percent in December. [D6.] Jobless claims rose again. [D6.]
Bush's plans to cut military spending by deferring production of newly designed weapons may result in serious delays and higher costs when they are actually built. [D1.]
The National Energy Strategy proposed by the President during the gulf crisis seems likely to pass this year. [D1.]
When Bush castigated regulators for treating banks too harshly, he sounded a theme also heard from Democratic Presidential candidates but rejected by many others. News Analysis. [D4.]
 
Companies
UAL reported a loss twice the size of the year-earlier loss, and said it would cut capital spending by $2 billion. [D1.]
Coca-Cola said its profits rose 22 percent, driven by strong sales overseas and a pickup in the U.S., and its stock jumped. [D1.]
Household International said it was shutting down a big portion of its commercial finance operation. [D2.]
Lotus Development said it was accelerating its cost-cutting plans, resulting in a bigger-than-expected charge. [D4.]

Bristol-Myers Squibb and Immunex said they had agreed to exchange marketing rights for several drugs. [D3.]

International
India's central bank said it was dismantling an 18-year-old law that banned big foreign companies from selling products under their internationally known trademarks. [D6.]
 
Markets

The dollar seesawed in hectic trading, falling on weak economic statistics, rebounding on a rumor of a Russian coup. [D13.] Precious metals futures plunged. [D13.]

HEADLINE: THE FEDERAL BUDGET: The Tax Battle
Divining the Winners;
Again, Parties Squabble Over Who Would Benefit From President's Plan

BYLINE: By JASON DePARLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
President Bush took pains this week to present his budget as one that would benefit Americans across the income spectrum, but it has set off a war of interpretation over which income classes would gain and by how much.
The budget ink had yet to dry before Democrats, pouncing on an issue that has helped them in the past, were criticizing the plan as another in a long line of Republican proposals that offered unfair windfalls to the rich. 
 Congressional Democrats asserted that the Bush proposals would cut taxes by $19,000 a year for the richest Americans while saving a middle-income family only $150.
"Big tax cuts for the rich, sacrifices for everybody else," said Leon E. Panetta, the California Democrat who heads the House Budget Committee, in attacking the Administration's budget.
Republicans, as sensitive to the issue as their adversaries, were ready with their counter-analysis, which came in two strains.
Many Republican analysts agreed with the Democrats that wealthiest families would reap the largest immediate gains, primarily through a cut in the capital gains tax. But they argued that the Democrats' numbers overlook some important elements of the Bush tax-cut plan. And they added, as they have in the past, that even if the affluent win in the short run, this is not unfair because the tax cuts would stimulate the economy and provide jobs to Americans at all income levels.
This was the kind of argument that President Bush seemed to be making in his State of the Union Message. "Never has an issue been more demagogued," he said in defense of a capital gains tax cut. "A cut in the capital gains tax increases jobs and helps just about everybody in the country."
Other Republicans went even farther, saying that the Democrats were concocting phony statistics and that it was unfair to argue that the wealthy would gain at all. Because the appreciation on assets like stocks, paintings or real estate is not taxed until the asset is sold, the Republicans said, a cut in the tax would actually increase the taxes rich people pay by inducing them to sell things they would otherwise hold.
In the past, Congressional committees have produced detailed tables showing the effect of budget proposals on Americans at various income levels and they are expected to do it again in a week or two.
But in this election year, the debate has jumped ahead of the detailed reports.
 
Plan for Middle Class
 In his address, Mr. Bush mentioned a long list of proposals to help the broad American middle class, including those that would allow people to deduct interest payments on student loans, use Individual Retirement Accounts to help buy their first homes and increase the personal exemption for families by $500 a child.
But critics argue that the amount each family would save from those proposals is dwarfed by the savings the richest Americans would get from a cut in the capital gains tax.
Robert Greenstein, director of the Center on Budget and Policy Priorities, whose analyses are drawn on frequently by Democrats, prepared a study comparing the amounts. Mr. Greenstein totaled the tax savings in nine areas the President said would help the middle class, including new rules on I.R.A.'s, a credit of up to $5,000 for first-time homebuyers, and the increase in the personal exemption. Such proposals total $39.2 billion over six years and would be spread out among a very broad range of taxpayers. By contrast, Mr. Greenstein estimated that the capital gains cut alone would amount to about $19 billion a year, and would be concentrated among the richest.
In arguing that the wealthiest Americans would save $19,000 a year, Mr. Panetta was drawing on an analysis last fall by the Joint Committee on Taxation. That analysis showed that a proposed 30 percent cut in the capital gains tax would bring an annual savings of $12,761 to families with capital gains who earned more than $200,000 a year. The President has proposed a larger cut, of 45 percent, and Mr. Panetta extrapolated.
President Bush, in asserting that the Democratic argument amounted to demagoguery, said 60 percent of the Americans who had capital gains earned less than $50,000 a year. But the amounts middle-income Americans earn in capital gains are far exceeded by the claims at the top. The study by the Joint Committee on Taxation showed that 83 percent of the money earned in capital gains goes to families earning more than $100,000.
In arguing that a typical middle-income family would save $150 from the President's proposals, Mr. Panetta was focusing only on the increase in the personal exemption for children. He arrived at that figure by taking the proposed increase of $500 a child, multiplying it by two, the average number of children in a family, and applying a 15 percent tax rate, the most common.
Some Republicans agreed that the initial winners would be the wealthy. "The beneficiaries are certainly not low-income people and are more upper-income people than middle-income people," said a Republican Congressional aide, who spoke on the condition of anonymity.
But many added that the figures used by the tax committee were flawed because they did not take into account the ways in which a capital gains cut would stimulate the economy, an argument repeated in Congressional testimony today by Richard G. Darman, the budget director. "It is helpful to the middle insofar as it ultimately creates more jobs," he said.
In addition, the figures tossed around by the Democrats do not take into account one of the President's major initiatives for working families, a tax credit of up to $3,750 to help families buy health insurance. While the bulk of that money is expected to help lower-income families, Democrats say they cannot calculate it because the Administration has provided no details.
And other proposals in the President's budget are intended to help those with modest incomes, but their effects are difficult to measure. Chief among them is an 18-month extension of a tax credit for those who build low-income housing.
Some Republicans, including Representative Bill Gradison of Ohio, the ranking minority member of the House budget committee, take a more defiant view in favor of cutting the tax on capital gains.
Lowering the rate does not really benefit the wealthy, he said, because it coaxes them into paying taxes they might otherwise not pay at all. Most wealthy people, he said, have the option of simply letting their assets accrue.
"How do you gain from paying more tax?" he said. "That's nonsense."

HEADLINE: Bush Budget Would Hurt Area, Officials Say

BYLINE: By LINDSEY GRUSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
By continuing the decadelong trend of shifting the cost of social programs to the cities and states, President Bush's budget proposal would aggravate the increasingly desperate fiscal plight of the New York region, officials from the region said today.
In a rising chorus, the officials complained that the budget would give short shrift to a variety of programs they consider vital to urban areas, from mass transit to health-care costs. 
 Ultimately, some officials said, the budget would force more state and municipal tax increases, which would lead to new layoffs in both the public and private sectors and more than cancel any economic stimulus from the tax changes proposed by the President in his $1.52 trillion budget.
"The proposal continues the trend of abdication of Federal responsibility," the New Jersey State office here concluded in an analysis released today.
Brad C. Johnson, Washington counsel to Gov. Mario M. Cuomo, an outspoken critic of the administration's domestic priorities, said, "It's a classic example of borrowing from Mario to pay George."
The worst news in the budget is for employees of defense industries, because of the President's plan to cut defense spending by $50 billion over the next six years. And few states are likely to suffer as much as Connecticut, because of the proposed cancellation of the Seawolf submarine, built by Electric Boat in Groton, and the scaling back of the Comanche helicopter, built at United Technologies' division in Stratford.
"President Bush, while proclaiming the need for economic growth, is sowing the seeds for economic decline in Connecticut," Senator Joseph I. Lieberman, Democrat of Connecticut, said in a statement released today.
 
Costs Shifting or Cut?
 City and state officials across the region said the Bush budget would aggravate the region's problems by, among other things, capping the growth in the amount the Federal Government reimburses for Medicare and Medicaid.
"A threat of such severe losses puts the viability of the public hospitals, which serve primarily the indigent, at risk," said Judy L. Chesser, the New York City lobbyist, wrote in an analysis of the budget sent to Mayor David N. Dinkins.
The proposed budget for the 1993 fiscal year, which begins Oct. 1, includes a $600 million increase in funding for Head Start, a pre-school program for children from low income families. It would allow the program to add 5,700 more children to the 35,000 currently enrolled, New York officials said. But officials said there still would be only enough financing to enroll one in four eligible pre-schoolers.
The budget would increase Federal spending for some social programs, like educating the handicapped; some types of student loans, and AIDS research. And it includes $70 million to build a secondary sewage treatment plant in New York City, $2.5 million to dredge New York Harbor, $15.4 million for a courthouse on Long Island and $23 million to repair and renovate the Jacob K. Javits Federal Office Building.
But any benefits from those increases would be more than offset by cuts in other key social services, officials from the region said. The administration's budget would cut or eliminate financing to more than 30 other waste-water treatment projects, they said. And they said it would lead to the closing of about 300 community-based social-service organizations in New York City alone and the loss of about 5,000 jobs statewide.
"It's basically a wash," said Mr. Johnson.
The analysis by New Jersey officials found that while the state would gain money for some programs, it would lose $31 million for guaranteed student loans, $10 million for removing asbestos and $15.28 million for the construction of waste-water treatment centers.

HEADLINE: Are Champagne Prices in Store for Beer?

BYLINE: By ALLEN R. MYERSON

 BODY:
The local economy may be a mess, crime a constant threat and living costs still ruinous, but one of New York City's consolations has been some of the nation's kinder prices for beer. Now, the nation's largest beer producer is pitted against the city's independent beer distributors in a battle whose outcome, the distributors and city officials say, could create a wholesaling monopoly and raise prices by a dollar or more a case.
Anheuser-Busch Companies Inc., which supplies more than half of the city's beer, added a dollar a case on Monday to wholesale prices statewide on its most popular brands. The company will issue rebates only to those retailers who buy all their Budweiser and other company brands directly from the company's four franchised wholesalers. 
 The city's 70 independent distributors call this a scheme to drive them out of business. Anheuser-Busch would then raise prices gradually but substantially, city officials and the distributors say, costing New Yorkers at least $100 million a year.
 
Seeing a Sinister Plan
 Anheuser-Busch, based in St. Louis, responded that, far from raising prices, it plans to offer some discounts through the end of the year. "This is not a price increase," the company said in a statement, adding that its competitors have similar strategies. Independent distributors will still have a market, said Joseph V. Corcoran, vice president of the eastern sales region for the company. The goals, the company said, are to control quality and to persuade New Yorkers, who guzzle Bud like no other brand but pass up Bud Light, Busch and Michelob, to try these other company brews.
Carlos D. Nazario, owner of Metro Beer and Soda Distributors in the South Bronx, sees a more sinister plan, to kill off entrepreneurs who built Budweiser's popularity. Mr. Nazario, president of the 325-member Empire State Beer Distributors Association, said businesses like his ventured into neighborhoods where major brewers had scant distribution.
When his father, a Puerto Rican immigrant, founded Metro Beer 22 years ago, Budweiser was a minor presence, Mr. Nazario said. Now, it is so popular in the city's Hispanic neighborhoods that it accounts for 58 percent of his business, he said.
From the warehouse and store he bought just last year, his view now includes the Major Deegan Expressway and a dark future. "I don't think I can survive," he said.
Mr. Nazario stocked up before Monday, when his cost went up a dollar to $11.55 a case. But by the end of the week, he said, he will have to restock, passing that extra dollar along to the bars, restaurants, groceries and bodegas he serves. Inevitably, he said, they will switch their business to the franchise distributors to capture rebates of $1.15, including an extra 15-cent incentive.
Mr. Nazario's customers said they would have no choice but to buy elsewhere. "Let me tell you something," said Auero Soto, who owns the Metro Superette in Parkchester in the Bronx. "If the beer company raises a dollar from Carlos, he has to raise a dollar from us, and I have to raise a dollar from the customer. The customer is going to go somewhere else." Companies like Mr. Nazario's are also called home distributors, reflecting a time when each neighborhood and ethnic enclave had its own breweries, and the brew was delivered to homes. The tradition has persisted to the extent that about half the city's distributors are Puerto Rican, Dominican, Asian or black, often reflecting the makeup of their neighborhoods.
The independent distributors do what anyone trying to keep down the costs of a beer blast would do: they comparison shop. Often, they buy beer from upstate wholesalers willing to undercut the wholesalers closer to the city.
Leslie Gersing, a spokeswoman for Robert Abrams, the state Attorney General, said Mr. Abrams has long fought Anheuser-Busch's practices in court. "It's just another example of their anti-competitive behavior," she said.
Mark Green, the city's Consumer Affairs Commissioner, said he would urge the Justice Department to start an antitrust investigation of Anheuser-Busch. "It's a case of monopoly muscle," he said.
Once the company drives independent distributors out of business, he said, it will gradually raise prices $1 to $2 a case, with other brewers most likely to follow. Retail prices in the city, ranging from about $11 to $16 a case, now tend to be 10 to 20 percent less than the national average, he said. On sales of 100 million cases of beer a year, Mr. Green said, the added cost to New Yorkers would be at least $100 million.

HEADLINE: Coca-Cola's Earnings and Stock Climb

BYLINE: By EBEN SHAPIRO

 BODY:
The Coca-Cola Company said yesterday that its fourth-quarter profit had risen 22 percent, driven by strong sales overseas and a pickup of sales in the United States, and its stock jumped nearly 8 percent.
The company also said it planned to recommend a 2-for-1 stock split to its board at its February meeting. The split, if approved, is expected to be effective in April or May. 
 Coca-Cola stock climbed $5.625 a share yesterday, closing at $77.125 in heavy trading on the New York Stock Exchange. It was the fifth-most-active Big Board stock.
Fourth-quarter net income for the world's biggest soft-drink company rose to $358.4 million, or 54 cents a share, compared with $294.3 million, or 44 cents a share, for the period last year. The fourth-quarter results included a one-time after-tax gain of $18 million, or 3 cents a share, for the sale of the company's shares in the Johnston Coca-Cola Bottling Group to Coca-Cola Enterprises.
Revenues rose 12.6 percent, to $2.88 billion from $2.56 billion.
Roberto C. Goizueta, Coca-Cola's chairman and chief executive, signaled that the company's strong growth would continue this year. "In spite of harsh economic conditions in many world markets, we have moved into 1992 with undiminished momentum," he said. "This augurs well for the current year's results."
In the competitive domestic market, Coca-Cola increased its lead during the year over its main rival, Pepsico Inc. Coke now commands 41 percent of soft-drink sales in the United States, compared with Pepsi's 33 percent.
After a difficult year, Coca-Cola's fourth-quarter case sales picked up in the United States by 3 percent.
International business, which accounts for 80 percent of Coca-Cola's profit, grew at a healthy clip in the quarter, despite weakness in Britain.
The company's case sales grew by 6 percent in the European Community and Latin America. Case sales grew by 3 percent in the Pacific and Africa.
At Coca-Cola Foods, which markets the Minute Maid juice brand, orange juice volume increased 17 percent.
During 1991, Coca-Cola expanded into several new markets, including joint ventures in Hungary and Romania. The company also made investments in eastern Germany and Poland. Coca-Cola also formed a joint venture with Nestle S.A. to sell coffee and tea drinks.
Emanuel Goldman, a securities analyst with Paine Webber in San Francisco, said Coca-Cola's strong performance in a weak economy was attracting interest in the stock market. "Investors are still looking for companies that have demonstrated an ability to achieve strong growth though thick or thin," he said.
Mr. Goldman expects Coca-Cola to complete a stock-repurchase program this year, buying up to 15 million shares, a move that could push up the company's stock price.
For all of 1991, Coca-Cola earned $1.62 billion, or $2.43 a share, a 17 percent increase over the previous year's earnings of $1.38 billion, or $2.04 a share. Full-year revenues totaled $11.57 billion, up 13 percent from $10.24 billion in 1990.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Republicans;
Executives Give Bush a Tepid Greeting

BYLINE: By ANDREW ROSENTHAL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PHILADELPHIA, Jan. 30

 BODY:
President Bush told jokes about Boris Yeltsin and the Super Bowl. He denounced "sweet-sounding quick fixes," "empty symbols and slogans" and "feel-good gimmicks." He tried out a new slogan for his economic growth programs: "It will work."
He even appeared before just about the friendliest audience the White House could find, the Philadelphia Chamber of Commerce, as he took to the road today to begin the campaign to sell his economic proposals outside of Washington.
 
Polite but Unenthusiastic
 But having announced that he would hold his political fire until after the March 20 deadline he set for Congress to pass his programs, Mr. Bush found himself restricted largely to a textbookish reprise of his State of the Union Message, without the oratorical flourishes of Tuesday night's speech or any of what he likes to call his "red meat" political lines. 
 The 500 or so business executives applauded when Mr. Bush scorned lawyers who sue business people and called for a cut in the tax on investment profits. But his listeners, many of whom said they were eager to begin helping the President with his political problems and the nation's economic troubles, were for the most part polite but distinctly unenthusiastic.
As Mr. Bush moved on to New York to meet with other world leaders, including Mr. Yeltsin, at the United Nations, an activity more to his liking than grappling with the economic and political consequences of the recession, some of those in the audience said they agreed with many of the points Mr. Bush made in his speech on Tuesday and here today.
Mac Thompson, an insurance broker, was more inclined to give Mr. Bush the benefit of the doubt. "He's addressing the right issues, even if it was just a repitition of the State of the Union without a lot new," he said.
 
'Preaching to the Choir'
 But some said he had waited too long to acknowledge the country's economic difficulty and then to take action to solve it. "This long-term denial has put us in a difficult position," said Charles Greene, executive director of public affairs for Bell of Pennyslvania. "Here he is preaching to the choir and many of the points he's making are valid, but no one seems too enthused."
Mr. Greene's response is precisely what some of Mr. Bush's political advisers had feared would occur because the President insisted on waiting for weeks to unveil his economic proposals in his State of the Union Message.
Before Mr. Bush decided to set the March 20 deadline, some campaign planners and conservative officials had urged him to ignore the advice of Richard G. Darman, the budget director, and Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady, who had counseled Mr. Bush not to take on Congress immediately after his State of the Union Message.
 
No Direct Criticism
 The officials pressing for action have argued that the Democrats will not spring to Mr. Bush's aide simply because he refrains from criticizing them directly and have counseled the President to start his offensive. But today Mr. Bush only used Republican code words for "Democrat."
"This is no time for gloom and doom," Mr. Bush said. "It is time for action in Washington to restore confidence and get this economy moving again."
To help Mr. Bush regain his momentum and present his economic plan to the nation, the White House and the Bush-Quayle campaign are sending a stream of public officials, friendly business executives and other dignitaries around the country on his behalf.
For instance, Vice President Dan Quayle, Senator John McCain of Arizona, Housing Secretary Jack F. Kemp and Carla A. Hills, the Trade Representative, are all going to New Hampshire in coming days, where Mr. Bush faces a challenge from the conservative columnist Patrick J. Buchanan in the Republican primary on Feb. 18.
 
A Joke Misfires
 But Mr. Bush has to play the central role, as he sought to do today in Philadelphia.
"Please be seated," he told his audience, which rose for him as he entered the room. "I don't want to keep Boris Yeltsin waiting later on."
Mr. Bush said the last few weeks "have been pretty high pressure -- high pressure time for me -- what with all the experts and the instant analysis and the columnists giving unsolicited advice. Thank goodness the Super Bowl is over."
The audience seemed puzzled, although there were a few scattered chuckles.
Trying to paint Democratic budget proposals as irresponsible, Mr. Bush said, "The air back in Washington has been thick with feel-good gimmicks that have nothing to do with true prosperity and everything to do with politics."

HEADLINE: ALBANY MEMO;
Mental Hospital Closings Draw Few Cheers

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ALBANY, Jan. 30

 BODY:
There is a certain purity to the logic of the case being made by state mental health officials that half of the state's 22 psychiatric hospitals should either be mothballed or reduced in size by the end of the decade.
The state has a dire need to expand community services for the mentally ill. But about 70 percent of its $2.4 billion mental health budget must be spent to maintain anachronistic hospitals that house only 12,500 of the estimated 185,000 New Yorkers with serious mental illnesses. 
 Therefore, the state officials reason, why not reduce the size of the hospital system, which gets more expensive each day because of badly needed renovations, and pump the savings into outpatient and community residential programs?
But the test of logic is only one hurdle that must be vaulted in Albany in order for the Legislature to adopt such a shift in policy. The larger obstacles are political, and it is unclear whether the political imperatives for closing the hospitals will overcome those for keeping them open.
 
Holding On to Jobs
 The report on closing and consolidating psychiatric hospitals that was released yesterday by the State Office of Mental Health outlined a preliminary plan. But opposition has mounted quickly in the communities that are home to the hospitals, largely because of the potential loss of thousands of jobs.
Three of the four hospitals scheduled for closure -- Gowanda Psychiatric Center in southern Erie County, Harlem Valley Psychiatric Center in Dutchess County, and Willard Psychiatric Center in Seneca County -- are the largest employers in their immediate areas. The fourth hospital is Kings Park Psychiatric Center on Long Island. Seven other hospitals would be substantially scaled back.
"Politically it's always difficult to close an institution because of the political pressures of the existing state workforce and the economic impact on the local communities," said Steven Greenfield, executive director of the Association of Community Living Agencies in Mental Health. "But we think that the state has to recognize that the purpose of these institutions is to serve people with mental illnesses."
The State Commissioner of Mental Health, Richard C. Surles, has long argued that the state's vast system of psychiatric institutions has drained badly needed financing from community-based mental health programs, where the vast majority of patients are served.
In good times, the state was able to afford the inefficiency. But the state's chronic budget problems have made it impossible to pay for the renovations needed to keep the hospitals up to Federal standards and to meet the growing demand for community mental health programs.
By closing and consolidating hospitals, the state could save an estimated $360 million annually by the end of the decade, according to Bruce Feig, the Executive Deputy Commissioner of the Office of Mental Health. That would nearly double the state's current budget of $440 million for community services.
As it is, Mr. Feig said, the state needs to add 12,000 community residential beds for the mentally ill to its existing pool of 28,000 and it also needs to expand outpatient mental health clinics and psychiatric emergency rooms.
But the recession also has left New York with an increasingly severe unemployment problem, and Mr. Surles's plan could mean the loss of more than 9,000 jobs. Many of the institutions scheduled to be either closed or cut back are in small communities in upstate New York.
The communities' complaints carry weight with the Republicans who control the State Senate, most of whom come from either upstate New York or the suburbs of New York City.
They may find allies in the Democrats who control the Assembly, most of whom hail from New York City, and who can be expected to question whether the state will expand community services quickly enough.
 
'Dumping' Charged
 Leaders of the unions that represent state workers are encouraging these questions.
"Without adequate funding of state-operated community-based services and the continuity of care into the community, this plan can only be called dumping," said Howard Shafer, president of the Public Employees Federation.
Mr. Feig said that more than half of the patients currently in state hospitals would require continued institutionalization because of geriatric conditions or severe mental illness.
He predicted that few of the patients who are discharged would become homeless as long as savings from hospital closings are earmarked for community programs. Some mental health activists have proposed legislation to insure that happens.
Senator Nicholas A. Spano, the Yonkers Republican who is chairman of the Mental Hygiene Committee, had his doubts.
"I don't see the commitment to a community-based system of care that would be necessary and I haven't seen the commitment to quality of care at the psychiatric centers that will be forced to carry an increased caseload," he said. "There are going to be a number of obstacles."

HEADLINE: THE FEDERAL BUDGET: Personal Savings;
An I.R.A. Plan for Middle-Class Needs

BYLINE: By LESLIE WAYNE

 BODY:
It goes by the name flexible individual retirement account, but what President Bush proposed this week might better be described as a savings program for middle-income people.
"It's a misnomer to call it a retirement plan," said Stephen Corrick, a tax partner at the Washington office of Arthur Andersen. "But it certainly is flexible and it provides tax-advantaged savings, and there's nothing wrong with that." 
 As part of President Bush's attempt to revive a sagging economy, the Administration proposed a series of tax breaks in the election-year budget for investors and middle-income families. Among them is the proposal to create a new type of I.R.A., to be called a flexible I.R.A.
Contributions to a flexible I.R.A. would not be tax deductible; by contrast some existing I.R.A.'s are deductible, primarily those for taxpayers without a company pension plan or whose incomes fall below $25,000 for individuals and $40,000 for couples. But earnings in a flexible I.R.A. could be withdrawn tax-free after seven years; by contrast, for existing I.R.A.'s the withdrawals are taxed.
 
Like a Municipal Bond
 "It's a great proposal for people who can take advantage of it," said Stephen Pennachio, a tax partner at KPMG Peat Marwick. "It's very similar to a municipal bond. And for those who cannot deduct the existing I.R.A.'s and who qualify for this, it's a wonderful opportunity."
Under the Bush Administration proposal, an individual with an adjusted gross income of under $60,000, a head of household with an adjusted income under $100,000 and a married couple whose adjusted income is under $120,000 can qualify to invest up to $2,500 a year in a flexible I.R.A.
A person withdrawing money from a flexible I.R.A. within three years would face a tax bill, plus a 10 percent early-withdrawal penalty. Withdrawals made between years three and seven would be taxable, but would not be penalized.
Tax planning experts say a flexible I.R.A. is designed for people who want to accumulate savings over a medium length of time, most likely to buy a house or finance a child's college education.
"The seven-year time period encourages people to keep it there and not grab it right away," said George E. L. Barbee, executive director of client services at Price Waterhouse. "Personally, it's a trade-off. I'd like to see people saving for retirement. But there's a real movement for help for a first home or for an education."
 
Answering the Critics
 For the economy, the program is designed to increase the domestic savings rate, which is considered crucial to long-term international economic competitiveness. At the same time, it is designed to answer critics of President Bush who say that far too many of his tax proposals are designed for the wealthy and ignore the middle-income earners.
The Treasury Department estimates that the plan will cost the Treasury $2.8 billion between now and 1997. It must be approved by a Democratic-controlled Congress and how Congress will react is unclear. But Robert Willens, a tax and accounting analyst at Lehman Brothers, said it was "very much a mainstream proposal" and in line with what many in Congress had been proposing as recently as last year.
Last year, for instance, Senator Lloyd Bentsen of Texas, the Democratic chairman of the Senate Finance Committee, submitted a bill to broaden the existing I.R.A. program in a variety of ways: to allow employees with corporate pensions to deduct them, to permit penalty-free withdrawals for expenses including college, costly health care and first-time home payments, and to set up a flexible I.R.A. savings plan similar to the one proposed by President Bush but for five years.
 
Tax-Free Investment
 That Bentsen plan was supported by 70 senators, more than a two-thirds majority, but it was immediately opposed by the Bush Administration, which said at the time, "We are not in favor."
Andrew Zuckerman, a benefits specialist at the Washington office of the accounting firm Grant Thornton, said the advantage of a flexible I.R.A. was that it could turn a taxable investment into a tax-exempt one. For instance, a person could invest the $2,500 a year permitted in the program in high-yielding corporate taxable bonds. The interest on these bonds, normally taxable, would escape taxation completely if left for seven years in a flexible I.R.A.
Mr. Willens of Lehman Brothers said that anyone considering investing in a flexible I.R.A. should compare the after-tax returns of an investment in a flexible I.R.A. with those of traditional tax-exempt investments, such as municipal bonds. Mr. Willens predicted that if the flexible I.R.A.'s were approved by Congress, "brokers are going to be marketing them for a lot of situations."
Investors with existing I.R.A.'s are cautioned by tax experts not to roll over their funds immediately into flexible I.R.A.'s since they would face a large penalty and tax bill for early withdrawal. This sum might be far greater than the returns that could accumulate tax free in a flexible I.R.A. and would not offset the costs of making a change.

GRAPHIC: Chart: "Comparing the Savings Plans"
 
EXISTING I.R.A.'S
 Contributions: Up to $2,000 annually for all individuals with employment income.
 Deductibility: Fully deductible for taxpayers not covered by employer-sponsored pension or retirement plans and for individuals with income less than $25,000 and couples with income less than $40,000.
 Tax Implications: Taxes are deferred until money is withdrawn. Withdrawals before the age of 59 1/2 incur immediate tax liability and a 10 percent penalty.
 
PROPOSED FLEXIBLE I.R.A.'s
 Contributions: Up to $2,500 annually. Adjusted gross income cannot exceed $60,000 for singles, $100,000 for heads of households or $120,000 for married couples.
 Deductibility: Contributions are not tax-deductible.
 Tax Implications: Earnings grow tax-free and are not taxed if held at least seven years. Withdrawals before three years face taxes and a 10 percent penalty; withdrawals in years three throughy seven are taxed but face no penalty.
 
Graph: "I.R.A. Assets" shows the dollar amount at year-end, in billions, from '85-'90*.
 
*Latest figure available.
(Source: Investment Company Institute)

HEADLINE: THE FEDERAL BUDGET: Incentives;
Businesses Want Broader Measures for Research

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, Jan. 30

 BODY:
President Bush's proposed budget contains several notable incentives to spur research and development and improve the nation's competitiveness, many executives said today. But it stops short of the full-fledged effort that many business leaders say the nation needs.
The budget would increase Federal research and development expenditures, particularly for nonmilitary research in such areas as computers and biotechnology. There are also some tax incentives for industrial spending on research and new equipment, but experts say these measures would have only a modest impact. 
 Some executives said, however, that by increasing Federal research and development for civilian technology the Bush Administration seems to be softening its ideological resistance to helping particular industries.
"We see signs of making sure the Federal R & D budget supports industrial competitiveness," said Daniel F. Burton Jr., executive vice president of the Council on Competitiveness, a Washington lobbying group.
Under the President's proposal, which requires Congressional approval, overall Federal expenditures for research and development would total $76.6 billion, an increase of 3 percent from the year before.
Civilian research and development would increase by 7 percent, making up 41 percent of overall research spending in the fiscal year 1993, up from 39 percent in 1992 and well above the level during the years of the Reagan military buildup. Industry executives have long complained that too much Federal research spending goes to arcane military uses with potential few commercial spinoffs.
The budget also includes increased support for the Commerce Department's Advanced Technology Program and a new "critical technology" program, both of which the Administration had not supported in the past, Mr. Burton said.
"They are moving a little bit in the direction of technology policy," said John Mancini, senior vice president of the American Electronics Association. But he added, "It's a good first step but not enough in the long run."
Indeed, some executives said they were disappointed that President Bush, in his State of the Union address Tuesday, made little mention of competitiveness. Instead, he seemed to dwell on spurring home building, which would create new jobs but not help the nation in foreign markets.
Among the specific tax incentives the President proposed, executives said that by far the biggest boost could come from a cut in the capital gains tax to 15.4 percent from 28 percent for investments held at least three years. That could particularly help start-up companies that are backed by venture capital.
But such a cut in the capital gains tax has been repeatedly proposed by President Bush only to be derailed by Democrats in Congress who say that the benefits would fall mainly to the wealthy. This time, though, the President sounds more determined. And with the economy reeling, the Democrats might be more willing to go along.
Another proposed budget measure would make the research and development tax credit permanent. Companies would get a tax credit of 20 percent on increases in their research-and-development spending according to a complex formula.
Although the credit has been extended annually for years, making it permanent would end any uncertainty and provide an incentive for long-term increases in research and development, executives said.
But the tax credit will not benefit many companies in the development stage, particularly in biotechnology, because these companies do not yet make profits and therefore have no use for a tax credit.
"Is the R & D tax credit going to make me do anything differently?" said Roger Salquist, chief executive of Calgene, an agricultural biotechnology company in Davis, Calif. "The answer is no. It's just irrelevant."

HEADLINE: Hidden Costs in a Military Cutback

BYLINE: By JOHN HOLUSHA

 BODY:
President Bush's plans to cut military spending by deferring production of newly designed weapons may result in serious delays and higher costs when they are actually built, manufacturing specialists say.
The "design now, build later" approach that is being proposed by the Defense Department aims to save 35 to 45 percent of the cost of new weapons by designing them and building test models, but delaying production until they are actually needed. The idea is to keep American weapons technology at the cutting edge without spending billions for unnecessary hardware, Defense Department officials have said. 
 But separating design from manufacturing is just the opposite of what the most advanced industrial companies are trying to do today. In fact, most American manufacturers have been imitating Japanese manufacturers of everything from cars and cameras by using "concurrent engineering" methods that closely link designers and production specialists. The objective is to design a product that can be easily and efficiently manufactured and to get engineers and designers to quickly correct any problems that might crop up in production.
"You have to consider manufacturing if you want to convert a design into something that is real," said Arun Maira, a production specialist at the Arthur D. Little consulting firm in Cambridge, Mass. If it turns out that a designer has called for a part too big or with tolerances too tight for any machine in the factory to make, for instance, the whole project could be delayed until the part is redesigned or new machinery is installed. Working out such kinks is all part of the process, Mr. Maira says, and waiting months or years until a military conflict threatens might not be the best time to start working out the bugs in a system.
What is more, he says, if a design is approved and put on a shelf, the designers may not be available to make the necessary alterations years later in response to changing needs.
Such issues, long simmering, have become more urgent for the military contracting community in light of President Bush's State of the Union speech Tuesday and the subsequent release of Mr. Bush's proposed budget. The Administration has proposed shutting down production of the B-2 bomber, which is built by the Northrop Corporation in Southern California, after 20 of the Stealth airplanes are built.
The President has proposed to halt building Seawolf attack submarines, made by the General Dynamics Corporation's Electric Boat shipyard in Groton, Conn., after the first one is built. He is also seeking to delay production of the Commanche light helicopter, made by United Technologies' Sikorsky Helicopter division in Stratford, Conn.
Several missile systems will be stopped as well, if Congress accepts the President's plan.
 
Dual-Use Factories Discussed
 Some industry analysts say that continuing improvements in the quality of commercial products and the increasing flexibility of computer-controlled machinery offers at least the theoretical possibilty of dual-use factories, which could switch as needed from civilian to military production.
"It can work if certain conditions exist," said Edward A. Miller, president of the National Center for Manufacturing Sciences, a cooperative research organization in Ann Arbor, Mich. "It works if you have a strong commercial and industrial base."
For example, he said, aerospace companies capable of building airliners could quickly convert to military planes, particularly if the tooling to make military aircraft had been mothballed, rather than scrapped. Indeed, production of the U-2 airplane for high-altitude surveillance was resumed in 1980 after being shut down more than 10 years earlier.
 
Institutional Obstacles
 Still, even if elaborate computer-controlled factories can be made flexible enough to produce both civilian and military products, the nation's military procurement system will have to be thoroughly overhauled to make such an approach practical, military analysts say.
"It is not going to happen the way things are now," said Debra Van Opstal, an analyst at the Center for Strategic and International Studies in Washington, who has studied the procurement process. "There are four different problems and each one is a show stopper."
A Senate committee staff member familiar with the issue said: "Converting military contractors to civilian products is one of the hardest things anyone can imagine doing. They have been operating as two parallel systems for decades, with very little contact."
But other experts say that civilian plants can be adapted to military products, and weapons plants to civilian products, if the same basic capabilities are present. "The Soviets built tanks and railroad cars in the same plant, so it is an example of using a similar process to make different products,' said Jacques Gansler, a former Pentagon official who is now a consultant. He added that in electronics, civilian products had improved so much that they now exceed military specifications.
"The control chip in your car is far tougher than milspecs, so you can design using civilian products," he said. "That was not true 20 years ago."
But Mr. Gansler agreed that Defense Department procurement procedures were an effective bar to producing military and civilian products in the same factories.
Ms. Van Opstal, the Washington analyst, said military auditors would not accept civilian accounting systems, forcing military contractors to install expensive separate reporting requirements to prevent fraud. To limit the reach of Government auditors now, she said, companies like Motorola and I.B.M. make similar products for civilian and military markets but build them in different plants.
The Government also requires contractors to turn over trade secrets, which are often divulged to competitors in future rounds of procurement bidding. Military procurement also includes a welter of special preferences, such as "Buy America" provisions, and narrowly drawn specifications that often differ from civilian products that perform better.
Mr. Gansler said he estimated that changing the Government's military procurement culture would take from 5 to 10 years, even with strong leadership from top Defense Department officials.
 
New Thinking on Prototypes
 Several manufacturing specialists said a key to making the "design now, build later" strategy work was to produce more prototypes than usual to prove that the weapons actually worked and could be made at an acceptable cost. Nowadays, test models are typically hand-built from specially made parts, and they differ from later production models.
"We have to redefine what a prototype means," said Ramchandran Jaikumar, a professor at Harvard Business School. "There is a difference between one that is an artifact in a laboratory and an effective prototype that is the result of a manufacturing process."
He said that by planning to produce enough prototypes to demonstrate the effectiveness and economics of production, contractors would have to perform concurrent engineering and arrange for an adequate network of suppliers. This would increase costs, he said, but help insure that the weapons could be made when needed. "Once you have an effective prototype," he added, "you can ramp up to high-volume production."
 
Parts Suppliers Are Crucial
 Parts suppliers are often the weak point in the production chain. If there is a shortage of landing gear because of bottlenecks at a subcontractor, aircraft production cannot be increased, even if all the other components are ready.
"Most things tend to be systems, so if you have a problem in the critical path, everything gets held up," said John Ettlie, a professor at the University of Michigan Business School.
Professor Jaikumar agreed. "You have to keep the infrastructure fit," he said, but added that if the technology and the production process were developed, switching from civilian to military production was mostly an organizational change. "As long as we know where we are going, that does not concern me as much," he said. "In World War II we did it in nine months."
But some experts question how long some components will last while they sit on the shelf. "There is an issue of how far you can go with mothballing," Mr. Maira said. Over time, critical subcontractors may change their businesses so much that they will no long be prepared to quickly convert to weapons work.
"After 10 or 20 years," he said, "you may have changes in critical parts or processes."

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Loss by Dow Chemical of $92 Million

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: MIDLAND, Mich., Jan. 30

 BODY:
The Dow Chemical Company said today that it lost $92 million in the fourth quarter because of a big charge against earnings to pay for plant closings and a lower estimate of the value of some oil and gas holdings.
The loss for the three months compared with earnings of $273 million, or $1.01 a share, in the fourth quarter of 1990, Dow said. 
 Fourth-quarter sales were $4.56 billion, 11 percent below the $5.14 billion in the period a year ago.
The special charge amounted to $370 million before taxes. Without it, fourth-quarter earnings would have been $146 million, or 54 cents a share.
 
Plant Closings Set
 The charge reflects Dow's Jan. 21 announcement that it would shut down Dow Canada's chlor-alkali and derivatives operation at Sarnia, Ontario, and at some plants in Pittsburg, Calif., Germany and Brazil.
Frank P. Popoff, president and chief executive, said all of Dow's basic products were affected by a slowdown in the global economy and weak pricing for Dow's basic chemicals. He said consumer products brought in 57 percent of operating income.
"Years like 1991 demonstrate the value of diversifying our product mix into value-added specialties," Mr. Popoff said. He predicted a return to profitability by the second half of the year.
For the year, Dow earned $935 million, or $3.46 a share, including a $213 million gain for the initial stock sale for its Destec Energy subsidiary. But the results were 32 percent below the $1.37 billion, or $5.10 a share, earned in 1990. Sales were $18.8 billion, 5 percent below a record $19.8 billion in 1990.
On the New York Stock Exchange today, Dow's shares rose $1.75, to $54.375.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasuries Lower in Light Trading

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities moved lower as interest rates rose further in light, nervous trading yesterday.
Credit market participants said the selling was a continuation of Wednesday's steep price decline, a slide apparently touched off by the suggestion by the Federal Reserve chairman, Alan Greenspan, that the central bank was not likely to ease policy again soon. 
 "Greenspan made it clear he won't ease until after the refunding," a government bond trader said. "That means we have to figure out the levels at which we can underwrite all this new paper. And nobody in the market has the stomach to buy anything."
Next week, the Treasury will announce the size and configuration of its quarterly refunding auctions, to be held the second week of February. Four times a year, the Treasury sells 3-year and 10-year notes and 30-year bonds.
 
Reduced Auction Seen
 Comments by officials from the Fed and the Treasury over the last few weeks have heightened expectations among traders that the size of next month's bond auction will be reduced.
But in light of the recent surge in negative market sentiment, traders said it would take a significant reduction in the auction's size to have a positive effect on long-term interest rates.
By reducing the size of the bond auctions, the Government would not only be seeking to reduce its interest costs, but would be hoping to encourage a drop in long-term interest rates.
"If the size of the bond is only cut back by a small amount, say $2 billion, I don't see any reason in the world why the new bonds won't be auctioned at around 8 percent," the trader said.
Long-term bond yields are now just above 7.75 percent. Earlier this month, they were below 7.40 percent.
Concern about coping with a heavy supply of new Treasury issues is a constant concern in the credit markets.
 
'Where Is the Recovery?'
 But the recent selloff remains a mystery to economists, who note that recent reports on the economy and inflation should be sending positive signals to potential buyers, not negative ones.
"Something is going on that is totally out of sync with reality," said Steven Slifer, an executive vice president and financial market economist at Lehman Brothers Inc. "I look at the economy and the numbers we have had, and I ask, 'Where is the recovery?' And yet, everybody is claiming that the economy is on the verge of recovery."
A troubling sign for the future was imbedded in yesterday's durable-goods report, which showed that orders fell by 5 percent in December.
While the overall figure is important, many economists prefer to look at what happens to order backlogs to get a reading on future production trends. And in December, the backlog for new orders fell for the fourth straight month.
"We know retailers got stuck with a lot of inventory they didn't want," in December, Mr. Slifer said. "My guess would be they will be cutting back still further. That implies additional cutbacks in production."
 
Little Effect on Trading
 The durable-goods report, on items expected to last three years or more, and a rise in weekly unemployment claims, another sign of economic weakness, had no effect on traders yesterday.
By late in the session, the Treasury's 8 percent 30-year bonds of 2021 were offered at a price of 10215/32 , down by 13/32 , to yield 7.78 percent.
Among Treasury note issues, the 7.50 percent 10-year notes were offered late yesterday at 101 3/8, down by 14/32 , to yield 7.29 percent. Prices of the 6 3/8 percent seven-year notes fell by 9/32 , to a late offered rate of 6.91 percent. And the 4 7/8 percent two-year notes were offered at 9914/32 , down by 3/32 , to yield 5.17 percent.
Short-term Treasury bill rates rose slightly.
Three-month bills were offered at a discount rate of 3.84 percent late yesterday, up by 3 basis points, or three one-hundredths of a percentage point. Six-month bill rates rose by a similar amount, to a late offered rate of 3.93 percent.
 
Gains in Money Supply
 In other developments, the Fed reported yesterday that all three measures of the nation's money supply posted strong gains during the week that ended on Jan. 20.
It was the second straight week of robust money growth, and placed growth in M-2 and M-3, the broadest aggregates, at 3.8 percent and 3.1 percent, respectively, over their fourth-quarter bases.
The Fed, which has the opportunity to revise its money growth targets in February, now has a target growth range of 2.5 percent to 6.5 percent for M-2, the aggregate it follows most closely.
The money report had no effect on prices in the credit markets yesterday, and analysts said M-2 needed to expand a bit more rapidly.
"In this economic environment, I think a 4 percent rate would be better," said Stuart Hoffman, a senior vice president at the PNC Financial Corporation in Pittsburgh.
 
Bankers Trust Sale
 New-issue activity in the investment-grade corporate bond market slowed markedly yesterday.
The largest issue was a $200 million offering of two-year notes by the Bankers Trust Company.
The 5 3/8 percent securities were priced at $99.933 to yield 5.41 percent, or 30 basis points over the prevailing yield on the Treasury's 4 7/8 percent two-year notes at the time of pricing.
Goldman, Sachs & Company was lead manager for the offering. The securities, which are noncallable for life, are rated AA-2 by Moody's Investors Service Inc. and AA by the Standard & Poor's Corporation.
In the tax-exempt municipal bond market, a bid from an investor group led by Lehman Brothers won an offering of $100 million in college student loan bonds issued by Texas.
The securities, which are subject to alternate minimum tax provisions, were reoffered at yields ranging from 4.70 percent in 1995 to a maximum yield of 6.65 percent on bonds maturing in 2011.
The bonds are rated AA by Moody's and Standard & Poor's.
In the secondary market for investment-grade corporate bonds, traders said prices of most issues dipped by about an eighth of a point to a quarter of a point in quiet trading.
"When rates in the Treasury market back up like this, people just don't focus on corporates," one trader said.
Participants in the secondary market for tax-exempt municipal securities said prices of most widely quoted issues fell by an eighth of a point to a quarter of a point.
