HEADLINE: Banking Regulation;
All of the Oratory in New Hampshire Isn't Likely to Make Borrowing Easier

BYLINE: By MICHAEL QUINT

 BODY:
When President Bush castigated regulators in his State of the Union Message on Tuesday for treating banks too harshly and creating a shortage of credit, he sounded a theme also heard from Democratic Presidential candidates but rejected by the regulators and many bankers.
These latest election-year outbursts are unlikely to loosen the strict standards of the nation's more than 10,000 bank examiners. Regulators still contend that failing to force banks to admit their mistakes would mislead investors and allow greater problems to fester untreated. 
 In New Hampshire, where Presidential hopefuls are congregating before the primary on Feb. 18, bank regulators and examiners are an especially easy target. Regulators late last year had to find new owners for seven insolvent New Hampshire banks that accounted for a quarter of the state's banking assets. The weak economy, a high level of loan defaults and the shift of many loans from insolvent banks to the Government's hands have caused a 21.5 percent decline in lending from New Hampshire banks since the end of 1990 -- the sharpest drop of any state.
 
Upset Over S.&L. Woes
 "There is real schizophrenia about bank regulation," said Robert L. Clarke, the Comptroller of the Currency and the top regulator for most of the nation's largest banks. "Taxpayers and Congress are upset at the cost of the savings and loans problems, which were brought on by lax regulation."
But businesses and legislators in states where the economy is weakest blame regulators for treating banks so harshly that the banks have cut back on lending. "Everybody wants the garbage to be picked up, but they don't want it to inconvenience anybody when it is dropped somewhere else," Mr. Clarke said.
Mr. Clarke, who leaves office at the end of February, failed last year to win confirmation to a new term amid conflicting accusations that he was both too harsh and too lenient on banks. "A lot of bankers thought the charges of leniency were hilarious," he said. As for charges of being too tough, he noted that this complaint has been common during recessions going back at least to the 1930's, and that regulators have worked hard in the last year to rein in overzealous examiners.
 
Administration Seeks Leniency
 Last year, President Bush and Treasury Department officials prodded bank regulators to clarify standards for evaluating loans and ask examiners to avoid using the bleakest possible assumptions. Regulators also reminded examiners to encourage arrangements for delinquent borrowers to repay their banks, rather than having the banks foreclose on the first sign of trouble.
Recent announcements by several California banks of heavy loan losses indicate that regulators and examiners have shown little response to all this prodding. The regulators' definition of a bad loan has not changed much, and examiners have not backed away from insisting that bankers recognize losses on loans that will not be repaid in full -- even if those borrowers happen to be paying interest on time.
Some of the Democratic candidates for President are also saying that regulators should do more to alleviate the plight of banks and borrowers.
 
Important for the Economy
 Paul E. Tsongas, a former Senator from Massachusetts, which has more than its share of banks hurt by delinquent real estate loans, says changes in bank regulation are among the most important ways of improving the economy. Mr. Tsongas has said he will "call in the banks and direct them to halt all foreclosures of viable businesses and homeowners whose loans are current."
Bill Clinton, the Governor of Arkansas is criticizing bankers and their regulators. On a recent visit to a paint brush factory in New Hampshire, Mr. Clinton was quoted by the American Banker as saying, "I would have regulators avoid foreclosing on banks whenever possible" and banks in turn should "stop foreclosing on mortgages whenever possible."
Many bankers who were shocked two years ago by the downturn in real estate prices and examiners' insistence that they admit their losses are now saying that examiners' standards are not much of a problem.
"On balance the bank examiners have been more right than wrong," said John B. Robinson Jr., an executive vice president at Fleet/Norstar Financial Group, Providence, R.I.

HEADLINE: Nassau's Bond Rating Is Lowered Over Fiscal Questions

BYLINE: By JOSH BARBANEL

 BODY:
With Nassau County's government locked in a stalemate and its celebrated Rebublican political machine in disarray, Moody's Investor Service yesterday lowered the county's bond rating, citing "substantial uncertainty" about its fiscal stewardship.
The rating, lowered from A1 to A, the fifth of seven investment grade ratings, remained one notch above ratings in neighboring New York City and Suffolk County, areas also hard hit by the recession. But rating officials warned that the county's rating could be lowered still further. 
 "Moody's will continue to monitor the implementation of the budget and take further rating action as needed," the agency said.
The rating was a reaction to the political and budgetary convulsions in the county, after the Republican Party -- which has ruled an expansive county government for most of the century -- abruptly reversed directions and moved to impose drastic spending cuts.
 
Cuts and Demonstrations
 Since then, the county has seen ever-worsening governmental gridlock, partisan infighting, legislative screaming matches and public demonstrations, as the Republican County Executive, Thomas S. Gulotta, struggled to carry out budget cuts that Republican legislators had adopted over his objections.
The budget calls for eliminating as many as 5,000 of 16,000 county jobs, according to some estimates, making deep cuts in health and social programs, and cutting in half spending on parks, the elderly and cultural events.
But typing in numbers on a budget document is a lot easier than carrying out the painful dismissals and program cuts that alienate thousands of residents -- and voters -- that the numbers require.
And in its rating decision yesterday, Moody's questioned whether the county would be able to carry out the drastic revisions -- or drift into a deep deficit. It also questioned some optimistic spending assumptions.
"This budget is extremely aggressive," said Michael Johnston, a vice president for public finance at Moody's. "Laying off a large chunk of the county's work force is not an easy task."
 
'Still a Good Rating'
 When the Republican legislators, led by the Republican county leader, Joseph N. Mondello, adopted the budget, Mr. Gulotta stood in front of the County Executive Building and told reporters that the budget was unbalanced and that it could lead to a downgrading of the county's $1.3 billion in outstanding bonds.
Yesterday, Mr. Gulotta said that he was disappointed that the rating was lowered, but attributed the reduction to the "recessionary economic downturn."
"The revised rating is similar to an A-minus grade in school instead of an A-plus," he said. "It is still a good rating."
But the lower rating could increase the county's borrowing costs when it sells $100 million in bonds this spring, said John V. Scaduto, the county treasurer.
County officials insisted that the county was moving as quickly as it could under complicated civil service rules to lay off thousands of employees and had already removed 1,000 from the payroll. But they said the spending cuts were so deep that they could require far more layoffs than the Board of Supervisors ever imagined.
The Republicans acted almost immediately after last November's elections, after Democrats made signficant gains on the County Board of Supervisors -- winning enough proportional votes to block Republican initiatives, though not enough to pass their own alternatives.
Mr. Gulotta's budget -- which called for a 25 percent increase in county property taxes and another $60 million in home-heating taxes -- was rejected by his own party, and replaced with another one that eliminated most of the tax increase.
 
Calling for More Trims
 Yesterday, Mr. Mondello, who has been provided with police protection in response to threats, county officials said, called the downgrading by Moody's regrettable, but said that it only "reaffirms my belief that the county must hold true to a course designed to reduce expenditures and provide county residents with property-tax relief."
"There can be only one answer to the county's fiscal situation and that is to cut costs," he said. "I will oppose any call for additional taxation with all of the resources at my disposal."
For weeks, the meetings of the Board of Supervisors have been faced with demonstrations and complaints by county workers, parents of children with birth defects, black and Hispanic groups, and women who support the right to an abortion, all of them angry about cuts in one clinic or service or another.
Adding to the confusion in the county is a continuing impass between Mr. Mondello and Democrats on the Board of Supervisors, that has prevented the passage of all but the most routine spending measures at board meetings.
Democrats, who with their new voting strengh have the authority to call emergency meetings, have already summoned their Republican colleagues to two meetings, including one scheduled for Monday, to explore the impact of the cuts.
So far this impasse has even blocked passage of an ordinance routinely passed during the first meeting of the year authorizing the county to issue checks to its employees. It is known as Ordinance One, because it is always the first ordinance on the calendar.
If not for a ruling by the County Attorney -- one contested by the Democrats -- concluding that last year's ordinance remains in effect this year, the county would have been barred from issuing checks to its employees.
The Democrats have challenged patronage practices by the ruling Republicans -- calling for layoffs of some of the 31 of 69 ranking Republican leaders in the county -- before other county workers are laid off.
But they have also linked the approval of Ordinance One to the allocation of more money for staff to the Democrats, a move the Republicans say smacks of patronage, too.
Asked whether the Democrats bore any responsibility for the downgrading, Benjamin Zwirn, the Democratic Supervisor from the town of North Hempstead, said: "How could we? It's not our budget."

HEADLINE: Weekly Jobless Claims Post Another Rise

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
Jobless claims rose again in mid-January as 465,000 Americans made first-time visits to unemployment offices, the Government reported today.
The number of Americans filing initial claims rose by 24,000 during the week ended Jan. 18, up from the previous week's revised figure of 440,000, the Labor Department said. Originally, the previous week's level was reported at 447,000. 
 The claims number should add to momentum in Congress to speed a second extension of unemployment benefits for the long-term jobless.
 
Efforts to Extend Benefits
 The bill, which has President Bush's support, won unanimous approval today from the Senate Finance Committee. It is a $2.7 billion measure giving the recession's victims 13 additional weeks of jobless coverage.
Analysts said today's claims report could be a discouraging signpost for January's unemployment rate, due out next week from the Labor Department.
The rate could increase to 7.2 percent from December's rate of 7.1 percent, some analysts predicted. That would be a new high for the recession.
The jobless total has swelled by 2.1 million since the recession started. In December, there were 8.9 million people without work.
The latest advance in jobless claims was the second straight increase and more than erased the improvements seen around the first of January, when holiday-shortened workweeks spurred a decline in the number of people reporting layoffs.
Mid-January's rise in jobless claims is probably a delayed effect of big layoffs that occurred in November and December, said David Wyss of DRI-McGraw Hill.
 
Delays May Be Factor
 Because laid-off workers generally receive severance pay or vacation checks after they lose their jobs, it takes weeks for layoffs to show up in the claims number, Mr. Wyss said.
Nevertheless, he said, "labor markets are deteriorating; we're off to a bad start in 1992."
Because the claims number can be extremely volatile from week to week, analysts generally look at a four-week average for a better barometer of hiring practices. Today's report left that average essentially unchanged, at about 450,000.

HEADLINE: Congress Expected to Pass Scaled-Down Energy Plan

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 29

 BODY:
Stripped of its most controversial provisions, the National Energy Strategy proposed by President Bush during the Persian Gulf crisis seems likely to pass this year, according to lawmakers, energy company executives and others.
The Senate version of the bill has been introduced again, minus the provisions on drilling in the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge and on stricter gasoline mileage standards for new cars, and may reach the floor as early as Monday. 
 The trimmed-down version still includes significant proposals for electricity regulation, electric energy efficiency, licensing nuclear power plants and interstate natural gas pipeline construction, among others.
The White House had initially insisted on the Arctic drilling provision, but on Capitol Hill many experts think that the President prefers a bill with no Arctic drilling to no bill at all. Mr. Bush gave the National Energy Strategy brief mention in the State of the Union Message.
The package was initially pushed by the Administration as a balanced plan that would cut energy imports and save the economy money, but it died last year when some senators pledged a filibuster over the Arctic drilling, and the Senate failed to muster enough votes to cut off debate. The strategy's real flaw, many in Washington say, was that it seemed to be less than the sum of its parts, tending to unite its fractious opponents, each opposed to a different part, rather than building support.
The head of the oil lobby, for example, lamented that access to the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge, known as A.N.W.R., could not even be debated in the Senate in part because of votes against cloture by a half-dozen senators who might have favored the drilling. But the six were dead set against proposed changes in regulations governing interstate operations by electric utilities, he said.
But the Senate is expected to try again in February, on the same package without the Arctic drilling and a stricter "Corporate Average Fuel Economy," or CAFE, standard, which would require Detroit to make more fuel-efficient cars. The two have no connection except a political one; environmentalists opposed to drilling in the wildlife reserve have put the mileage standards forward as an alternative, making one a kind of hostage to the other.
According to people who have lobbied for one part or another of the strategy, the consensus is that drilling in the Arctic refuge is a dead issue, at least for now, and so, therefore, is the fuel economy proposal. "Now Congress can consider the strategy without the bookends of A.N.W.R. and CAFE," Richard Dick Farman, the chairman of the Southern California Gas Company and the chairman of the American Gas Association, said in an interview. What is left might be better characterized as an approach rather than a strategy, but there are many other important proposals on the table, say the experts. Most of them have been floating around Washington long before Saddam Hussein briefly focused the nation's attention on oil.
They include freeing utilities from 50-year-old regulations that make it hard for them to operate across state lines; streamlining the licensing of new nuclear plants; ordering higher efficiency in new electric lights, air conditioners and motors; requiring state public utility commissions to consider changing their regulations to encourage conservation instead of new construction; simplifying the regulatory process for new natural gas pipelines; filling the Strategic Petroleum Reserve with oil, and opening up the electric power transmission grid to something approaching common carrier status, like an airline, rather than a private channel among big utilities.
There is also a strong reason to take up the matter this year, before the election. Otherwise, said Representative Phil Sharp, the Indiana Democrat who is chairman of the House Energy Committee's Subcommittee on Energy and Power, elected officials may face a tough campaign issue. "How do you answer the question, 'For Pete's sake, what have you done about energy?' " said Mr. Sharp. At the moment Congress is more worried about the economy and other matters, he said, but "You don't want to have to answer, 'Other things got to be more important.' "
 
Opposing Interests in Congress
 He added that in a Congress representing the interests of both energy producers and energy consumers, "there never has been a time when we did a be-all and end-all energy strategy, but there are a lot of good things here."
Mr. Sharp and others who worked hard on a bill last year say they were encouraged by President Bush's mention of energy in the State of the Union address, because it was only a mention, not a table-thumping demand. "The energy strategy has entered a nonpartisan, neutral zone," said one Senate aide who was involved last year. "The Democrats know George Bush lost on A.N.W.R., which is what he really wanted, and George Bush knows there is still a lot of stuff he likes, and he is almost sure he will not get CAFE, which he doesn't like."
 
Talk of Caribou and Cars
 With the two issues of strong popular interest, the caribou of the Arctic and the cars of Detroit, now sidelined, said the aide, "what's left, we can discuss on its merits."
And the Administration seems willing. When the Secretary of Energy, James D. Watkins, gave details of his department's new budget, he again called for passage of the National Energy Strategy, but put a different cast on the question from what the White House did last year, when it was given as an all-or-nothing proposition. "Nothing's locked in concrete so hard that it can't be worked on the Hill," Mr. Watkins said.
The surviving issues are still contentious, of course, and the House, Senate and White House versions differ in many respects.

HEADLINE: Spending Weak, but Incomes Jump

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
Personal spending slowed to a gain of three-tenths of 1 percent in December, the Government said today, as consumers continued to worry about their jobs and incomes.
At the same time, personal incomes jumped 1 percent, the largest increase in three years, but the gain was affected by several factors including unemployment insurance. 
 For the year, the Commerce Department said, incomes rose 3.3 percent, less than half the 6.8 percent increase in 1990 and barely beating a 3.1 percent increase in inflation.
 
Spending Is Off
 Consumer spending rose 3.9 percent during 1991, down from 6.4 percent a year earlier.
Larry Meyer of Laurence H. Meyer & Associates, an economic forecasting service in St. Louis, said: "Confidence is still down; the unemployment rate will probably continue to grow. We're not likely to see much more than 1 percent growth in consumption in the first quarter."
That would bode ill for the economy, since consumer spending accounts for two-thirds of economic activity.
The differences between incomes and spending contributed to a 5.8 percent savings rate in December, up from 5 percent a month earlier. Americans' savings rate for the year was 5.3 percent, up from 5.1 percent in 1990.
In addition to an increase in unemployment insurance payments, the December gain in incomes was affected by increases in farm subsidy payments and a December bonus for motor vehicle industry employees.
Personal incomes totaled $4.92 trillion at a seasonally adjusted annual rate, up from $4.87 trillion in November and the biggest increase since a 1.3 percent gain in January 1989.
Personal consumption totaled a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $3.95 trillion, up from $3.94 trillion a month earlier.
 
The Tax Effect
 Disposable incomes -- incomes after taxes -- rose 1.1 percent, the largest increase since a 3.3 percent advance, also in January 1989. Disposable incomes had slipped two-tenths of 1 percent a month earlier.
A key component of the income category -- wages and salaries -- increased $18 billion after gaining $2.7 billion in November.
The income and spending figures were not adjusted for inflation. When adjusted, disposable incomes rose just eight-tenths of 1 percent. Consumer spending, on the other hand, was unchanged from November.
Spending on durable goods -- generally items expected to last more than three years -- fell $400 million on top of a $3 billion loss the previous month.
Spending on nondurable goods dropped $1 billion, adding to a $400 million loss a month earlier.
Spending on services posted a $13.5 billion gain after rising $18.8 billion in November.

HEADLINE: Orders for Durables Off Sharply

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Jan. 30

 BODY:
Orders to United States factories for durable goods plunged an unexpectedly steep 5 percent in December, the first decline in three months and the largest in more than a year, the Government said today.
The Commerce Department said the December decline, led by a huge decrease in the transportation sector, helped drive durable-goods orders down 4.6 percent, to $1.44 trillion, for all of 1991. That was the largest loss since the recession year of 1982, when orders fell 6.7 percent. 
 The durable-goods report was just part of a rash of discouraging economic news today.
A separate report said consumers' spending was up only slightly last month, three-tenths of 1 percent. Americans' personal income was up 1 percent, but that was partly attributed to rising jobless payments because of the economic slump. [Page D6.]
And a third report showed that more people sought jobless benefits through the middle of this month. It was the second straight rise in such claims and more than erased the improvements seen around the first of January, when holiday-shortened workweeks spurred a decline in the number of people reporting being laid off. [Page D6.]
Businesses -- and the Bush Administration -- are anxiously seeking any sign of revival. But Gilbert Benz, an economist with the Swiss Bank Corporation in New York, said the reports suggested "there basically will be no growth in the first quarter of this year."
 
'Maintaining Equilibrium'
 Robert G. Dederick, an economist with the Northern Trust Company in Chicago, concurred: "The economy is kind of standing on rubbery legs. It isn't falling down. It's maintaining its equilibrium, but it can do no more."
President Bush did not comment on the new economic reports during a speech to business executives in Philadelphia, but he saw reason for hope in other economic signs. "Inflation is down," he said. "Inventories are down. The market has been expressing optimism in the future. Interest rates are down, and this is no time for gloom and doom."
The Commerce Department reported that orders for long-lasting, usually expensive, durable goods, like cars and computers, fell to a seasonally adjusted $117.7 billion in December. The 5 percent decline was the largest since orders plunged 11.2 percent in November 1990.
Transportation orders were down 13.9 percent, accounting for most of the overall decline. If not for the transportation sector, total orders would have been down just 1.6 percent.
On the other hand, military orders soared 86 percent; without that gain the overall orders decline would have been a staggering 8.9 percent. Orders for military hardware are expected to decline in coming months because of cutbacks in the Pentagon budget.
In today's report, the Commerce Department revised November's increase in new orders to just four-tenths of 1 percent from the estimate of 1 percent made a month ago.
Unfilled orders also declined in December for the fourth straight month. Unfilled orders are a measure of whether current production capacity and manpower are able to keep up with demand. A drop in unfilled orders could signal fewer production lines and jobs.
 
Variation by Category
 Orders for nonmilitary capital goods excluding aircraft were off seven-tenths of 1 percent after edging up one-tenth of 1 percent a month earlier. These orders are often a barometer of industry plans to expand and modernize.
Orders for industrial machinery and equipment also fell, down 3.2 percent after a 1.2 percent gain in November. Orders for primary metals fell 8.9 percent, after rising 2.1 percent in November.
Electronic and other electrical equipment posted the only advance in orders among the major categories, and computer and office equipment registered gains. They were up 2.8 percent on top of a 1.2 percent increase in November.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Profits Post 18.1% Rise At Johnson

BYLINE: By MILT FREUDENHEIM

 BODY:
Buoyed by strong sales gains in pharmaceutical, professional and consumer products, Johnson & Johnson said yesterday that profits in the final quarter of 1991 rose 18.1 percent, to $274 million, or 83 cents a share, on $3.15 billion in worldwide sales. Net income in the final 1990 quarter was $232 million, or 70 cents a share, on $2.79 billion in sales.
For all of 1991, Johnson & Johnson, the world's largest health-care company, said net earnings were a record $1.46 billion, or $4.39 a share, up from $1.14 billion in 1990, when the company wrote off $125 million, after taxes, on Latin American operations, primarily in Argentina. Excluding the one-time charge, 1990 profits were $1.27 billion, or $3.81 a share, and the 1991 gain was 15.2 percent. 
 Sales last year were a record $12.45 billion, a 10.9 percent increase from 1990 sales of $11.23 billion.
Currency exchange fluctuations reduced sales in dollars by 2.6 percent, or $72 million, in the quarter and nine-tenths of 1 percent, or $106 million, for the year. The company, based in New Brunswick, N.J., does half its business abroad, in more than 150 countries.
Roy E. Larsen, the company's chairman, said profits rose last year even though the company increased spending on research and development by 17.5 percent, to $980 million; expanded marketing and promotion for a number of new products, and faced continuing problems with inflation in Brazil.
Neil B. Sweig, an analyst with Brenner Securities, reacted favorably to the company's report, saying, "They are operating on all eight cylinders in many dynamic markets." He noted that pharmaceutical sales rose 16.1 percent in the quarter, to $973 million, and 14.9 percent, to $3.795 billion, for the year.
Although it does not have a billion-dollar blockbuster prescription drug, Johnson had a dozen with sales of at least $100 million each in 1991.
On the New York Stock Exchange yesterday, Johnson & Johnson's stock rose $2.75 to close at $106 a share.

HEADLINE: FUTURES/OPTIONS;
Precious Metals in Plunge On Doubt About Recovery

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
Precious metals futures plunged yesterday on the Commodity Exchange in New York as investors showed doubts about the economy's recovery and about demand from industry for the metals in the near future.
Gold for February delivery settled $2.70 cents lower at $353.40 an ounce; March silver was 10 cents lower at $4.131 an ounce. 
 "The silver market broke down pretty nicely today," said Randolph Donney, a metals analyst with the Pegasus Econometric Group in Hoboken, N.J. "The theory has been that a pickup in economic activity will spur demand for industrial metals. With each passing week that the longs are in the market waiting for some evidence of that, the patience wears thin."
 
Volatility in Chicago
 Grains and soybeans futures had a volatile day on the Chicago Board of Trade and ended mixed after wild speculation that President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia had been ousted.
Wheat for delivery in March settled 5 1/2 cents lower at $4.36 a bushel. March corn was 1/4 cent higher at $2.63 1/2 a bushel. March oats were 4 3/4 cents higher at $1.48 3/4 a bushel, and March soybeans were 3 1/2 cent lower at $5.72 3/4 a bushel.
Prices began slipping on follow-through selling after Wednesday's close, but the slide steepened on the Yeltsin rumor. A State Department spokesman said later that all appeared relatively quiet in the Russian Federation.
Soybeans continued to slide on continually improving conditions in South America, where the Brazilian soybean crop is getting plenty of moisture during a crucial growth period.
Losses were slashed toward the end of the session on word from Agriculture Secretary Edward R. Madigan, who said more grain credits could be extended to the former Soviet republics if they continued to make timely payments on past loans.
Oil futures prices were mixed on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Light sweet crude for March delivery settled 5 cents higher at $18.94 a barrel. February home heating oil slipped 1.85 cents, to 52.14 cents a gallon. Unleaded gasoline rose 0.90 cent, to 55.51 cents a gallon, while March natural gas retreated 1.4 cents, to $1.166 for 1,000 cubic feet.
Coffee futures on the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange in New Yorkpicked up after nearly a week of straight declines.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Ends Mostly Higher In a Hectic Trading Session

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar seesawed yesterday in hectic trading, falling first on weak United States economic statistics, rebounding strongly on a false rumor about a Russian coup and finishing mostly higher.
"It was a crazy day," said John McCarthy, chief foreign exchange dealer at the New York branch of Algemene Banc Nederland, a Dutch bank. "As a whole, the dollar remains quite resilient in the face of less than promising news." 
 Dealers said dollar selling prevailed through the early part of the global trading day, partly on the weakness reported Wednesday in fourth-quarter United States economic growth, the slowest since the 1982 recession.
The selling accelerated when the Government reported yesterday that factories received sharply fewer orders for durable goods in December and more people sought jobless benefits in the middle of January.
Then a false rumor raced through the foreign exchange market that the president of the Russian Federation, Boris Yeltsin, en route to the United States, had been toppled in a coup.
The Russian Government officially denied it, but not before a surge in demand for dollars reversed the currency's slide. The dollar has historically proved to be the most sought-after currency in times of crisis. "There's no question that uncertainty in the former Soviet Union quickly moved people into dollars," Mr. McCarthy said.
Earl I. Johnson, vice president and corporate adviser at the Harris Trust and Savings Bank in Chicago, said the dollar strengthened further on fears of a weakening economy in Germany, where labor unrest is growing.
He also said the Yeltsin rumor, though unfounded, was likely to keep the market nervous and predisposed to move into dollars. Nonetheless, Mr. Johnson said, the economic weakness in the United States was likely to restrain the dollar's value from appreciating very much.
"The economic fundamentals aren't there for the dollar to move up yet," he said. "The numbers don't point to an early recovery in the economy."
In Tokyo, the dollar rose 0.43 yen to close at 125.60 yen. Later in London the dollar fetched 125.95 yen. At the close in New York the dollar fetched 125.70 yen, up from 125.65 yen late Wednesday.
The British pound fell in London to $1.7740, from $1.8095 late Wednesday. In New York the pound fetched $1.7820, cheaper than its $1.7975 rate late Wednesday.

HEADLINE: Scott Paper Reports Loss

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: PHILADELPHIA, Jan. 30

 BODY:
The Scott Paper Company said today that it lost $157.7 million in its fourth quarter because of large restructuring charges and tough economic conditions.
The quarterly loss for the three months ended Dec. 28 compared with a loss of $51.5 million in the quarter a year earlier. Sales for the quarter fell to $1.32 billion from $1.43 billion a year ago. 
 For the year, Scott's loss compared with earnings of $148 million, or 80 cents a share, in 1990. Sales fell to $4.98 billion for the year from $5.36 billion a year earlier.
Scott is in the midst of reducing its work force to concentrate on its core businesses of personal care and cleaning products and coated printing and publishing papers. Special one-time charges related to restructuring and divestments cost the company $183 million in 1991, Scott said. Special charges in 1990 had cost $100 million.
A depressed economy, excess capacity on the part of Scott and the industry, and weak markets hurt earnings of the United States tissue business and its printing and publishing papers business, Philip E. Lippincott, chairman and chief executive, said.
The company's stock gained 62.5 cents, to $41, in trading today on the New York Stock Exchange.

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
The Basic Conflict In Budget Policy

BYLINE: By Leonard Silk

 BODY:
IN his State of the Union Message, President Bush highlighted the key problem facing budget and fiscal policy: that the American economy's short-term needs and long-term requirements are in conflict.
"We must have a short-term plan to address our immediate needs and heat up the economy," he said. "And then we need a long-term plan to keep the combustion going and to guarantee our place in the world economy." 
 But, by using the heat-combustion metaphor, the President obscured the difference between policies intended to lift the economy out of recession faster by stimulative measures that would swell the budget deficit, and policies to insure long-term growth, which would require reducing the budget deficit. Continued deficits will weaken the national savings rate, which is already at 3 percent of national income -- the lowest of any industrial country. The low savings rate is a major cause of America's lagging investment, sluggish productivity growth and loss of ground in world markets.
As the details of his budget show, however, Mr. Bush and his economic advisers were well aware of the policy conflict and did try to reconcile measures to meet short-term economic (and political) aims with those required to attain long-term growth. The question is how well the President's budget plan achieves its double objectives.
For short-term stimulus, the President proposes a grab bag of measures, some old, some new, some borrowed from the Democrats, some blue sky. These include cutting the capital gains tax to 15.4 percent from 28 percent; enacting the Administration's banking reform proposals; easing the credit crunch by telling banking regulators to take it easy, and declaring a three-month moratorium on all new regulations that interfere with growth.
They also call for putting an extra $25 billion in some taxpayers' pockets sooner by reducing their income-tax withholding; providing a $5,000 tax credit for first-time home buyers and restoring "passive" loss rules allowing a netting of gains and losses for tax purposes, but only for "active" real estate developers, thereby partly reopening a loophole closed in the 1986 Tax Reform Act.
By this array of tax breaks and mostly temporary shifts, the President avoided proposing a big tax cut for short-term stimulus that might also aggravate budget deficits in the longer run.
Indeed, Mr. Bush is trying to offset part of the revenue loss from his short-term proposals by unadvertised spending cuts in areas ranging from aid to Appalachia to cuts in home heating and housing for the elderly and disabled. On the day after the President's speech, Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, was not fighting the Administration so much as supporting it with his warning against stimulative tax cuts.
What the Administration is really counting on to turn the economy around by no later than midyear is the lagged effect of monetary stimulus the Fed has already put into the system, especially by its December cut of a full percentage point in the discount rate, to a very low 3 1/2 percent. The Administration's assumption is that the economy will accelerate as the year wears on, with gross domestic product growing at an average rate of 2.2 percent for the year and the unemployment rate falling slightly, to 6.8 percent by year-end.
Even if that wish comes true, the consolidated budget deficit for the fiscal year 1992, which the Administration had until recently projected at $360 billion, will rise to $401 billion, by its new estimate.
The real shocker in the President's long-range plan, however, is not the short- but the long-range budget projections, which show a deficit of $353 billion in 1993 and deficits averaging almost $200 billion each year from 1994 through 1997. The grand total from now till then would be $1.5 trillion.
Even after deducting the cyclical component of those deficits and outlays for savings and loan and bank insurance, the so-called adjusted structural budget deficits will total $1.3 trillion from 1992 through 1997. The structural deficit at the end of that six-year period will be $212 billion, compared with $268 billion at the beginning. That's a lot of negative saving. And it will do much to reduce business investment in new plant and equipment or research and development, which tax breaks cannot be expected to offset.
The Administration's failure to bring deficits down further and sooner stems significantly from the President's decision to make relatively small cuts in military spending. "By 1997, we will have cut defense by 30 percent since I took office," he said. He added, "These cuts are deep, and you must know my resolve: this deep, and no deeper."
Before the Soviet collapse, an estimated 60 percent of United States military spending was allocated to the deterrence or defeat of Soviet and Warsaw Pact aggression. A battle is now shaping up over whether, with the end of the cold war, deeper military cuts would be warranted in the nation's economic, social and security interests.

HEADLINE: UJB Fight By Chilmark

 BODY:
Chilmark Capital Partners, a New York investment group, said yesterday that it planned a proxy fight for one or more board seats at the UJB Financial Corporation, a Princeton, N.J., bank-holding company.
Chilmark, which acqured 9.9 percent of UJB late last year, said it had retained Dewe Rogerson Inc., a proxy soliciting firm, to aid in the contest for the April 20 shareholder meeting. 
 Chilmark said it wanted board seats to push UJB, operator of United Jersey Banks, to consider a merger, which it said could raise the company's stock price.
UJB has been hurt by the downturn in the New Jersey economy and real estate markets but has remained profitable.
On the New York Stock Exchange yesterday, UJB shares gained 75 cents each, closing at $17.

HEADLINE: No Arts Cuts in Dinkins Fiscal Plan

BYLINE: By WILLIAM H. HONAN

 BODY:
Tucked away inside the four-year preliminary financial plan for the city released yesterday by Mayor David N. Dinkins is the assertion that despite drastic cuts for most city agencies the culture budget will remain unchanged.
The bulky document, which proposes allocating more than $100 billion over the next four years for everything from police and firefighters to libraries and schools, includes one brief paragraph headlined "Department of Cultural Affairs": 
 "In recognition of the importance of the city's cultural institutions and their education programs, the beneficial impact they have on the local economy, and the effects of the reductions taken in previous years, the plan contains no further expense reductions during the four-year period."
 
Spreading Joyful Word
 "There's a measure of good news in there -- no further cuts in the culture budget!" Barbara J. Fife, Deputy Mayor for Planning and Development, said yesterday. "Just about everything else -- 80 or 90 percent of all the other agencies -- got cut."
The Mayor's preliminary financial plan is only a first step. He is expected to introduce his so-called executive budget for fiscal 1993 in April, and the final budget for the year is not due to be approved by the City Council until June.
The maintenance of the culture budget is highly unusual, said Deputy Mayor Fife, in that the Mayor's plan anticipates reductions across the board adding up to $190 million in fiscal 1993, and rising to $525 million by fiscal 1996. "Some city agency reductions will go as high as 40 percent on top of large reductions that were taken the last few years," she said.
Arts administrators felt especially hard hit last year when the city's recession-straitened budget lopped 28 percent, or $24 million, off the original $87 million arts appropriation. Yesterday, they expressed relief.
"We welcome the announcement," said Harvey Lichtenstein, the president of the Brooklyn Academy of Music and chairman of the Cultural Institutions Group, a coalition of arts organizations that occupy city-owned real estate.
"The Mayor's assurance is essential," he continued, "because the current year's 30 percent cut in city funding for cultural institutions resulted in severe service cutbacks."
Speaking in his capacity as head of the Brooklyn Academy of Music, Mr. Lichtenstein said that last year's cut obliged him to reduce both staff and programs by about 20 percent.
"Some museums were even harder hit," he continued. "They had to shut galleries and remain closed an additional day or two. Educational programs were especially damaged because bringing a lot of children into a museum requires a lot of maintenance, and so those programs got cut."
 
'We're Still Reeling'
 Jason Steven Cohen, the producing director of the New York Shakespeare Festival, said: "We're still reeling from last year's cut, which came to $301,000. We're gratified by the Mayor's decision, but I hope that in the budget process we can even have some of the earlier cuts restored."
Mr. Lichtenstein was quick to point out the importance of cultural organizations to the economy of the city. "Every dollar the city appropriates for cultural institutions," he said, "leverages $3 in private donations, and enhances the prestige and economic vitality of New York City."
Luis R. Cancel, the Cultural Affairs Commissioner, said, "The Mayor's financial plan is flying in the face of the recession, and against the sort of cuts that are being made in Albany, and the cultural community cannot help but deeply appreciate that."

HEADLINE: Review/Film;
A Variation on the Oedipus Theme

BYLINE: By VINCENT CANBY

 BODY:
European film makers are restless these days. They are roaming the world in search of something, though exactly what remains unclear. Possibly financial backing, maybe subject matter, perhaps something to believe in.
Wim Wenders took his cast and crew through seven countries before finally settling down in Australia to conclude his enjoyably peripatetic new-age comedy, "Until the End of the World."
Volker Schlondorff displays the same itch in his "Voyager," which was shot in Mexico, France, Italy, the United States and Greece. The new film, opening today at the Lincoln Plaza, is no less new age than Mr. Wenders's, though it considers the world in terms that evoke classic Greek tragedy rather than Jean-Luc Godard.
The multi-national auspices behind these films, and the variety of locations in which they were shot, appear to reflect an increasing rootlessness within the European film industry. As recent political and economic upheavals are evident in the manner in which the movies are made, the conditions are also manifest in the concerns expressed in the films themselves.
Politics and party lines are to be avoided. They may be out of date next week. In some ways, Mr. Wenders, Mr. Schlondorff and other European film makers today appear to be as uncertain as the characters within their films.
"Voyager," directed by Mr. Schlondorff and adapted by Rudy Wurlitzer from the 1957 Max Frisch novel, "Homo Faber," is about nothing less than fate and predestination as they operate in the curious story of Walter Faber (Sam Shepard).
The film is a modern variation on the Oedipus myth, recalled in flashbacks within flashbacks that start in June 1957, at the airport in Athens. Walter, an engineer who works with the United Nations, is a man who travels light or, as he tells someone: "Three or four days with a woman is the absolute maximum. After that I dissimulate."
He recalls his student days in Switzerland, just before World War II, when he abandoned Hannah (Barbara Sukowa), his pregnant lover, to return to the United States. He had told himself that he wasn't being a cad. Hannah had promised to get an abortion and his best friend, a German student named Joachim (August Zirner), had promised to look out for her.
Some years later, in another flashback, he remembers hearing that Joachim and Hannah had married, had a child and divorced. Still later he remembers taking a ship from New York to France and meeting Sabeth (Julie Delpy), a spectacularly pretty and sweet young student with whom he falls in love, at least in part because she reminds him of Hannah.
What with all of Walter's musing on the soundtrack about probability and coincidence, the audience is alerted to the truth about Sabeth's parentage long before it dawns on Walter. He only catches on after he and Sabeth have had an idyllic motor tour across the continent, and Walter is about to deliver the young woman to her mother in Greece.
In the film's abrupt and rather brutal conclusion, the gods intervene to punish Walter for his earlier transgression. The man of technology and science is brought to heel by laws that have nothing to do with his universe.
All of this plays with a lot more intelligence than you might expect. The English dialogue often sounds as if it had been translated from something else, but there is an engaging leisureliness, almost a lassitude, about the pace that invites contemplation.
Walter, as played by Mr. Shepard, is seriously eccentric and self-aware. He's a character of some stature. So is Sabeth. Because Miss Delpy is a most attractive actress, the love affair takes on such weight and meaning that the inevitable revelation seems an arbitrary intrusion by a doomily schematic plot.
It also seems to be worlds away from the world in which the film was made.
"Voyager" has been handsomely photographed in all sorts of exotic locations, though it might have been just as effective if shot in two or three fewer countries. It is well acted by Mr. Shepard and Miss Delpy, and is full of moments so particular and odd that they invite belief. Yet its tale of fate and predestination seems, at last, to be not timeless but absurd.
"Voyager," which has been rated PG-13 (Parents Strongly Cautioned), deals with incest.
 
Voyager
 Directed by Volker Schlondorff; screenplay by Rudy Wurlitzer, based on the novel "Homo Faber," by Max Frisch; directors of photography, Yorgos Arvanitis and Pierre L'Homme; edited by Dagmar Hirtz; music by Stanley Myers; production designer, Nicos Perakis; produced by Eberhard Junkersdorf; released by Castle Hill Productions. At Lincoln Plaza, Broadway at 63d Street, Manhattan. Running time: 110 minutes. This film is rated PG-13.

LENGTH: 1658 words

HEADLINE: The Art Market

BYLINE: By Carol Vogel

 BODY:

Barnes in Court
The controversial administration of the Barnes Foundation is now being legally challenged. The assault comes in the form of a suit alleging that the trustees of the foundation, a world-renowned institution in Merion, Pa., that has a priceless collection of Impressionist and post-Impressionist art, have taken advantage of the foundation's assets. The suit was filed by the De Mazia Trust, which was established by Violette de Mazia, a protegee and close companion of the founder, Dr. Albert C. Barnes. Miss de Mazia, who died four years ago, was a driving force in the institution, which she served as a vice president and the education director.
The Barnes Foundation, established in 1922 by Barnes, a patent-medicine manufacturer, to perpetuate his art collection and his ideas about art education, has always been considered an eccentric, exclusive institution. Barnes, for example, was well known for refusing to allow important collectors like Walter P. Chrysler Jr. and distinguished scholars like Meyer Schapiro and Kenneth Clark to see any of his estimated 800 paintings and 200 sculptures. Before his death in 1951, the founder made another unusual decision: he gave Lincoln University -- a small, predominantly black college 45 miles west of Merion with no track record in art education -- the power to nominate the trustees of the Barnes Foundation.
Both the foundation and its trustees made news last May when a lucrative contract was signed with Alfred A. Knopf to publish for the first time color photographs of the foundation's collection. The holdings include 170 Renoirs, 55 Cezannes and 20 Picassos. The deal was signed a month after the Newhouse Foundation made a $2 million donation to Lincoln University. The head of the Newhouse Foundation, and the son of its founder, is S. I. Newhouse Jr., the chairman of the corporation that owns Knopf.
In its petition filed on Jan. 21 in Montgomery County, Pa., the De Mazia Trust charges that the Barnes trustees used the catalogue contract as a lure for the donation to Lincoln University. (Three out of the five trustees of the Barnes Foundation are also on the board of Lincoln University, and the fourth, Richard H. Glanton, the president, is the university's general counsel.) The donation, they say, should have gone directly to the Barnes Foundation, which owns the artwork to be catalogued. The De Mazia Trust contends that because of the contribution to Lincoln, the Barnes trustees dismissed bids made by other publishers. They say other publishers would have given the foundation a contribution of several million dollars but that their efforts were ignored.
Mr. Glanton's response was: "That's not true. We had four serious proposals. We asked each publisher for a minimum of a $500,000 advance and only one publisher met our request." He added that Harry N. Abrams, the New York-based publisher, submitted the "second best" proposal.
But Paul Gottlieb, the president and publisher of Abrams, denies this. "The Barnes Foundation had no idea what they would have gotten out of us," said Mr. Gottlieb.
Mr. Glanton said on Wednesday that Knopf had awarded the Barnes Foundation a $750,000 advance for publishing the collection, a sum Mr. Gottlieb called "staggering" for an art book. He added, "I don't really believe that's what the advance is."
According to Susan D. Ralston, the editor of the catalogue at Knopf, "$750,000 is an erroneous figure."
"The real amount is a private matter between the Barnes Foundation and Knopf," Mrs. Ralston said, adding that the contract was for two books. The first book, a selection of the Barnes Foundation's great works, is to be in bookstores by the fall of 1993. While Mrs. Ralston said that no writer had been decided on, a name that keeps surfacing is that of J. Carter Brown, the director of the National Gallery of Art in Washington. Mr. Brown announced his resignation last week but is to remain in the post through this year. The second part of the contract is a catalogue raisonne that may be published in more than one volume. Consulting with the Barnes Foundation on the selection of art are several institutions, including the National Gallery, the French National Museums and the Philadelphia Museum of Art.
After the suit concerning the book contract was filed in Montgomery County Orphans' Court by the De Mazia Trust, the Barnes Foundation asked the court to withdraw a petition it made in March to ease visiting-hour restrictions, change investment policies, raise admission fees and allow the building to be used for social functions. But last week Louis D. Stefan, a Montgomery County judge, refused to let the foundation withdraw its suit. Instead, the judge asked the trustees to produce the foundation's financial documents as well as minutes of its board meetings.
"The Attorney General also opposed the request to withdraw the petition," said Judge Stefan in a telephone interview. "He wants to enable the court to look into the allegations raised by the De Mazia Trust and to resolve the serious issues currently being raised." Judge Stefan said that a hearing was tentatively scheduled for March.
Mr. Glanton called the allegations in the suit "very regrettable," and said the foundation's books and records would be open to the court.
The de Mazia suit is the most explosive challenge to the Glanton-led board of the Barnes Foundation. It clearly draws the line between the trustees, who want change, and Barnes loyalists, who would like to preserve the status quo. The De Mazia Trust, along with a group of past and present students, is committed to preserving the 70-year-old foundation's emphasis on art education. The trustees say they are trying to modernize the foundation and make its outstanding but little-known art collection available to a wider audience.
The trustees made their first move in this direction in April, when they petitioned the court for permission to sell up to 15 paintings from their collection to raise what they said was a badly needed $15 million to modernize the deteriorating galleries and to supplement the $10 million endowment that now generates only $1 million annually for operating costs. (It is normal museum practice to sell works of art only to acquire other art, not to use the proceeds for operating costs.) But three months later, the foundation changed its mind, citing adverse publicity as the reason for deciding not to sell works from its collection.
Meanwhile, the board is considering other options that would go against the very strict indenture set up by the foundation's creator. Among the prohibitions laid down by the irascible Dr. Barnes was a stipulation that the collection may never be moved from the present 1926 building that houses the artworks. But the trustees would like to close the museum for renovations and send the collection on tour to other museums like the National Gallery of Art. "It is one of the options under consideration," Mr. Glanton said. "But I cannot really comment any further."
 
A Shrinking Whitney
 The Whitney Museum of American Art is losing two of its three Manhattan branches. It has been asked to vacate by August its first-floor gallery in the Equitable Center, at Seventh Avenue and 52d Street, where the museum has been holding exhibitions for the last six years. By the end of September, the space at 33 Maiden Lane, which has housed the Whitney's downtown gallery since 1988, will be transformed into an I.B.M. marketing and education center.
"It's another sign of the times," said David A. Ross, the director of the Whitney. "These branches were a way of keeping more of our collection on view." Mr. Ross also pointed out that the timing for the closings was particularly bad since the museum's expansion plans have been postponed until the economy picks up.
Since their inception, the branches have always been well attended. During fiscal year 1991, for example, 40,000 people visited the Whitney's downtown branch; about 100,000 passed through the Equitable Center branch. "The branches fulfilled an important purpose," said Mr. Ross. "The Whitney has about 10,000 works of art in its collection and even when the entire third and fourth floors are used, there may only be about 200 artworks in an installation. Without these branches, fewer people will have a chance to see the depth of of our collection."
In financial terms, Mr. Ross said, these branches were neither a drain nor an income producer. Admission to all of the Whitney's branches are free and corporations like Equitable Life Assurance, International Business Machines and Park Tower Realty financed their operating costs.
Equitable, as well as I.B.M. and Park Tower Realty, which jointly own the building on Maiden Lane, said each company would continue to give the museum financial support, as they have in the past. But each corporation had different reasons for closing its branch of the Whitney: at Equitable, Mary Mannarino, the manager of public relations, said the company had decided to "support a wider number of institutions who would benefit from the good location." Ms. Mannarino also explained that Equitable would not be making any public announcement about future exhibitions until the spring.
At the Whitney's Maiden Lane location, an award-winning space designed by the New York architects Tod Williams and Billie Tsien, Paul Neuman, a media spokesman for I.B.M., said the decision to close the gallery wasn't an easy one since they were losing a mini-landmark. "We are downsizing and shrinking our real-estate holdings," he said. "We simply needed the space." Meanwhile, Park Tower Realty, in an effort to continue its association with the museum, is planning to help the Whitney find a new location. "We haven't actually begun the search," said Kate Coburn, Park Tower's director of marketing. "But we will be finding the Whitney a new downtown home."

HEADLINE: The Word's Turn: Urban Poets Re-emerge

BYLINE: By EVE M. KAHN

 BODY:
A CREWCUT young woman, wearing only black, intones with a sultry, hypnotizing voice: "You moan with/the insanity of such/raw miraculous movements." The poem she is reading this night -- her own -- proceeds to describe an overwhelming sexual encounter. The crowd at the nightclub cheers when she finishes, and then 5 audience judges rate her effort: she averages an 8, on a scale of 0 to 10.
A gangly young man prone to arm flails, her competitor in a surreal contest called a Poetry Slam, counters with a string of non sequiturs loosely tied to American malaise: "Zombies do well in an addictive society/. . . The Pope will let you kiss his ring but he won't let you wear his hat." His scores run from a 7.9 to a 10. As the winner, he receives the $10 grand prize; but not before the contest host makes sure to remind the listeners that "the best poet always loses." 
 Such frenetic scenes occur every Friday at the Nuyorican Poets Cafe, a drafty, bare brick loft in the East Village. And while the Nuyorican seems to be the only New York City club that has started regular Poetry Slams, dozens of other night spots and cafes in the five boroughs and beyond have regular poetry events on weekends. The self-indulgent verse sessions of the 1950's Beats have returned, this time with 90's political outrage and hip-hop energy.
"It's an incredibly active scene," says Eve Packer, who calls herself a performance poet and often reads to the musical accompaniment of a live band called the Future. "People hang out, dance, drink, yell, and the work is very urban, very rhythmic, full of sex, drugs, violence, politics, anger, humor. And it's not a depressed scene, it's not introverted or tortured; that's a 50's image."
The monthly New York City Poetry Calendar, founded as a skimpy broadsheet in 1976, has grown unwieldy, with up to a dozen listings per day at more than 60 venues. About two-thirds are old reliables like libraries, churches, art galleries and bookstores; a few private homes also appear. The rest are night spots and cafes that typically discovered the muse less than three years ago.
Bob Holman, the fast-talking host of the Nuyorican's Slams, attributes the scene's new vitality to a cold, high-tech world, where people long for human contact; to rap's "driving the wax out of the ears of teen-agers"; to academic poetry's prevailing lifelessness, and to frustration with empty verbiage spewed by politicians and advertisers. Money is also a factor: poetry helps fill seats at slow hours.
"It's one of our better nights here," says Kerry Smith, owner of Right Bank, a three-year-old bar in Williamsburg, Brooklyn. His biweekly Sunday readings rank as one of the hipper spots in town to bare a soul, says Kathe Burkhart, a poet who lives nearby and won a Nuyorican Slam this month. (Mr. Holman crowned her "Slum Goddess of the 90's"; her most colorful selection raged against the ways women adapt to men and men torment women and concluded, "he will give up nothing for you./Not yet.")
 
Not Quite So Current
 The Moroccan Star, on the other hand, a Middle Eastern restaurant near the Brooklyn Borough Hall, presents Sunday open readings that feel like circa 1975 group therapy. Sponsored by the New Press, which publishes a poetry quarterly, the sessions recently prompted John Todras to berate his ex-wife in verse ("Some marriages resemble tossing dice") and Carol Blair to agonize over memories of the Joel Steinberg trial ("Joel, Hedda, Lisa/. . . ecstasy's violent eruption").
The Ear Inn's 14-year-old program is slicker, presenting two established, although experimental writers on Saturday afternoons. Jerome Sala recently mused on commercialism at the cozy tavern, in SoHo, surrounded by women in earth tone blouses and men in faded lumberjack shirts. "O new coke,/it's not your taste we hate so much/as your lack of integrity," one poem complains, and another warns, "no purchase is necessary to disintegrate."
Sin-e, a two-year-old East Village coffee house with soothing peach walls, attracts sleek young men in black leather jingling with chains, as well as 70-ish widows with spiky hair and bemused smiles. Its programs are as varied as its listeners; a few weeks ago, Dorothy Shubow Nelson contemplated global change ("I see icons everywhere/yet no longer see myself/in relation to anything greater/than the places that I go") and Veronica Vera, a self-described porn queen, read a chapter of her racy autobiography.
A few blocks north, the Pyramid Club presents a year-old poetry series heavy on progressive topics like "treatments of the issues of gayness," says Larry Jones, the program's organizer. The Public Theater's cafe started poetry sessions this year, and Max Fish, a Lower East Side bar so cool it keeps its phone number unlisted, provokes "great reading; people really let their hair down," says Ms. Packer, the poet. Like everywhere else she performs, she adds, "there's that arc of contact and energy with the audience; you create a spark and it builds and builds."
 
It Began in Chicago
 The Nuyorican remains her favorite stage. Founded in 1974, the space closed for renovations in 1982 and didn't reopen again until 1989. To spice up its second incarnation, the directors borrowed an idea from a Chicago bar, the Green Mill Lounge, where Slams have occurred since 1986. "I didn't trademark the name, because I wanted it to spread and spread easily," says Marc Smith, a Chicago poet who is the Slam's inventor and who has watched it expand to 20 other cities so far.
At the Nuyorican, low-cost, highly drinkable wines and beer flow at a bar near the entrance; ethnic groups mingle comfortably, and the staff encourages dancing in the aisles during breaks. But despite the cafe's charms and the spoken-word boom, it is affected by the recession, as are some of its colleagues.
The New Press must shift its programs from restaurant to restaurant, as managers grow tired of late-drinking literati. The Knitting Factory, a renowned setting for avant-garde work in the East Village, no longer pays its poetry readers because New York State has dramatically cut grants for literary efforts. And Webo, a popular performance and poetry club in the East Village, opened in 1990 and died of debt in 1991.
A lot of these places have closed because they can't afford the rent, says Miguel Algarin, a co-director at the Nuyorican, which fortunately owns its own quarters.
"We're not making money," says Mr. Holman. "We're not a smash success. That would require a wholesale rethinking of what culture and civilization are about."
 
WHERE VOICES SPEAK THE VERSES
 Here is a sampling of New York City night spots and cafes that present poetry:
CB's 313 GALLERY, 313 Bowery, Manhattan. Readings are on Sundays at 5:30 P.M. Emerging poets are typical fare, some with musical backup. Admission: $3; beer, wine, snacks and desserts available. Information: (212) 677-0455.
CEDAR TAVERN, 82 University Place, Manhattan. Readings are held erratically on Sunday nights, with full bar available in a glass-walled roof garden. Programs range from established academic poets to younger writers. Free. Information: (212) 929-9089.
CORNELIA STREET CAFE, 29 Cornelia Street, Manhattan. Sponsored by the New Press, readings casually combine prearranged presentations and open mikes from 6 to 8 P.M. on Mondays in a wood-paneled back room. Admission: $2 plus one drink. Information: (212) 473-1897.
COZY CORNER BAR AND RESTAURANT, 60-01 70th Avenue, at 60th Street, Ridgewood, Queens. On Tuesdays, low-key open and scheduled readings run from 7 to 9 P.M. in a slightly kitschy but warm family-run restaurant. Sponsored by the New Press. Admission: $2, plus one food purchase. Information: (718) 386-2737.
DIXON PLACE, 258 Bowery, Manhattan. Colorful poetry and fiction readings occur on one Thursday each month, with occasional poetry performances on other nights, in a 60-person performance arena carved out of a tenement's second floor. Admission: $5 to $7, nonalcoholic beverages available. Information: (212) 219-3088.
EAR INN, 326 Spring Street, Manhattan. A quaint 19th-century tavern presents two readers each Saturday afternoon at 3:30, usually armed with poems that take risks but do not howl. Admission: $3, full menu of homey cooking available. Information: (212) 226-9060.
JOYCE'S, 5057 Broadway, at 216th Street, Manhattan. Scruffy local bar that reserves its side room for open and scheduled readings on Sundays at 3 P.M. Poets tend to be unknown and to write of everyday life; performance theatrics are rare. Free. Information: (212) 567-3839.
KNITTING FACTORY, 47 East Houston Street, Manhattan. The performance space's Knot Room holds readings every Tuesday and Wednesday at 7:30 P.M., and auteurs often comment on the neighborhood's troubles. Admission: $4; full bar available. Information: (212) 219-3055.
MAN RAY, 169 Eighth Avenue, between 18th and 19th Streets, Manhattan. On most Monday nights, poets ranging from minimalists to Rabelaisians speak after 11 P.M. in a black-and-white Art Deco Revival bar. Free. Information: (212) 627-4220.
MAX FISH, 178 Ludlow Street, Manhattan. An intentionally ratty outlet for high-spirited and unpredictably scheduled performances. Free; full bar available. Information: (212) 529-3959.
MOROCCAN STAR, 205 Atlantic Avenue, near Court Street, Brooklyn. A pleasant Middle Eastern restaurant that presents informal Sunday readings at 1 P.M. in its back room. Two poets are scheduled to speak, others are allowed to read before and after. Sponsored by the New Press. Admission: $2 plus one food purchase. Information: (718) 852-2669.
NUYORICAN POETS CAFE, 236 East Third Street, Manhattan. Readings occur regularly on Wednesdays at 7:30 P.M. and sometimes on Thursdays and Saturdays; Fridays around 10 P.M., the cafe presents Poetry Slams, competitions for most popular poet, plus a briefly open mike. Admission: $5; beer and generous helpings of decent wine available. Information: (212) 505-8183.
PUBLIC THEATER CAFE, 425 Lafayette Street, Manhattan. Starting on Feb. 9 with Bob Holman, a co-director at the Nuyorican, as curator, the Public Poetry series will occur on every second Sunday of the month in the theater's lobby cafe and present mainly high-energy performers. Free; snacks, beer and wine available. Information: (212) 598-7150.
PYRAMID CLUB, 101 Avenue A, between Sixth and Seventh Streets, Manhattan. This sometimes outrageous, gay-oriented bar holds a "live literary magazine" called the Nile on Sundays at 7:30 P.M. Admission: $5; full bar available. Information: (212) 529-2336.
RIGHT BANK, 409 Kent Avenue, Williamsburg, Brooklyn. Sunday readings every other week at 8 P.M. in a funky neighborhood watering hole with a bar down one wall and bare brick down the other; next reading is on Feb. 9. Free; beer and wine available. Information: (718) 387-8137.
SIDEKICKS CAFE, 6031 Broadway, near 242d Street, the Bronx. Readings every Wednesday at 10 P.M. present mainly established poets, with an open mike afterward and a musical interlude in between. Restaurant overlooks Van Cortlandt Park; free. Information: (212) 549-5192.
SIN-E, 122 St. Marks Place, Manhattan. Cozy coffee house where poets read alongside an upright piano. Occasional Sundays at 6 P.M. Free. Information: (212) 982-0370.
WINDOWS OF THE VILLAGE, 11th Street and Avenue of the Americas, Manhattan. Open and scheduled readings occur Tuesdays from 7 to 9 P.M. in a former Blimpie's, offering unglamorous but serviceable beer, snacks and sandwiches. Sponsored by the New Press. Admission: $2 plus one food purchase. Information: (212) 860-7860.
WINE GALLERY RESTAURANT AND BAR, 70-20 Austin Street, Forest Hills, Queens. Open and scheduled readings happen on Sundays from 6 to 8 P.M. in an upstairs enclave that seats 20 and features eerie lighting that makes pages seem to glow in the dark. Sponsored by the New Press. Admission: $2 plus one drink. Information: (718) 544-0894. -- EVE M. KAHN

HEADLINE: TV SPORTS;
Syndicator Gives ABC Easy Fast Break on Profit

BYLINE: By Richard Sandomir

 BODY:
The future of network sports comes into a slightly sharper focus Sunday when college basketball returns to ABC at 1 P.M. with a doubleheader package of games featuring U.C.L.A.-Louisville and Kentucky-L.S.U.
It will dribble and quack like network college basketball. But it won't be. The games are produced by Raycom Inc., the Charlotte, N.C., sports syndicator, in what is called a time buy. 
 Raycom is paying ABC $1.8 million for six weeks of network air time to put on 26 games that will be shown regionally, like ABC's college football schedule. Raycom controls the games and sells the advertising. ABC sheds virtually all of its overhead and makes an easy profit, a reversal from recent losses on basketball.
Last spring, ABC was telling colleges how much it would reduce its rights fees to them when Rick and Dee Ray, the husband-and-wife owners of Raycom, approached the network with their time-buy concept.
"We were looking to reduce rights fees by 40 to 60 percent," said Tony Petitti, programming director of ABC Sports. "We lost millions last year. This was a good way to turn a loser into a financial contributor to ABC."
ABC's disappointing 3.0 Nielsen rating for college basketball last year was attributed to the glut of games on network, local and cable dials. "Ratings were down and rights fees were growing," said Petitti.
Enter Raycom, which this season will produce 462 college basketball games that will be seen on outlets like ESPN and the Prime Network or syndicated to local stations.
"We've always wanted a national sell on basketball," said Ken Haines, executive vice president of Raycom. "Our history has been regional sales and combining regional sales for an almost national buy for advertisers."
Crucial to the Raycom-ABC deal is the difference in corporate economics. Because Raycom is not a union shop, it can produce the games for at least 25 percent of what they would cost ABC. Raycom also had the collegiate and conference connections to secure the rights to games that ABC wouldn't normally get on its own.
Said Petitti: "We've taken all the risk out of college basketball."
That's the idea. Networks are struggling to get out from under the burden of big sports losses. NBC is losing money on pro football. CBS is losing about $500 million from the value of its $1.06 billion major league baseball deal.
In every nook and cranny of the sports divisons, small and large cuts are being made to nudge events toward or into profitability.
But in the world of the time buy, the networks see possibilities spawned by outsider producers like Raycom to shed the risk of paying for rights while gaining a guaranteed profit by renting their time for a few hours at a time.
The litany of time-buy events on the networks is growing. Last December, for example, Raycom bought time on CBS to show the Blockbuster Bowl.
Others showcase a mix of legitimate made-for-TV programming like "The Skins Game," and "The Tournament of Champions," a seniors ski event. Still others are trash sports, like the "Reebok Arnold Schwarzenegger Classic," a bodybuilding competition that aired last October .
Bob Horowitz, vice president and general manager of GGP, one of the most active outside producers buying time on the networks, said, "A network feels it can get a network quality show from our outfit or IMG or Don Ohlmeyer, and delivered in a way that everybody wins in the end."
Of course, what makes the outside-produced programming seem more like it emanates from the networks is that the productions often employ network talent. Among the annoucers on ABC's Raycom games are Brent Musburger, Dick Vitale, Jim Valvano, Gary Bender, Cheryl Miller and Mark Jones. The networks' hope is that the presence of their own broadcasters won't simply be masking a chintzy nonnetwork production.
Haines wants the ABC package to be a harbinger of Raycom's future. "This gives us another means of distribution that has long been the sacred domain of the three major networks," he said. "There's certainly every indication that other sports will be packaged like this. But the TV marketplace has been so unpredictable that it's foolish to think it will happen for sure."
The future of sports may see more arrangements like the ABC-Raycom deal, or joint ventures between networks and outside producers, or instances in which networks create and market their own event without paying fees to a league or sanctioning body (as NBC has done with its beach volleyball).
What is certain is absolutely nothing. Even the presence of the networks themselves.

SECTION: Section A; Page 26; Column 1; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 384 words

HEADLINE: A Better City Spending Plan

 BODY:
Mayor David Dinkins's new financial plan for New York City is a welcome step in the right direction -- toward the kind of lasting reforms that might finally bring the budget into long-term balance. Even so, it has flaws that need to be addressed if the city is to have a credible fiscal policy to help it through this recession.
What's very right about the plan is its heart. Despite painful cuts, it doesn't sacrifice day care, infant mortality programs, full-day kindergarten or other efforts aimed at helping those most vulnerable. 
 Mr. Dinkins and his budget experts have resisted the temptation to understate the severity of the city's fiscal problems; they are realistic in their economic predictions. And the Mayor repeatedly asserts that he is dedicated to structural reforms in city government.
He has made some lasting reforms, but could do more -- especially since his plan calls for refinancing city bonds, a fancy term for increased borrowing to cover operating expenses.
Most important, Mr. Dinkins needs to demand more from the municipal labor force. To his credit, the Mayor is showing more independence in dealing with the unions that helped elect him. He's calling for a reduction of about 20,000 city-financed jobs by July 1996, and says there will be no wage increases unless the unions agree to save money by being more productive.
That still falls short. The Mayor is relying on attrition, a haphazard system. Moreover, linking wage increases to productivity is bound to be less effective than using the stick of layoffs. The plan leaves too much to the good faith of self-interested unions.
Another worrisome feature is the deep cut in capital spending on such projects as school construction, housing and highways. Mr. Dinkins is appropriately concerned about the high cost of debt service. But capital spending can stimulate the economy, and delaying rehabilitation of aging infrastructure is risky. The city should explore financing through the Municipal Assistance Corporation, to minimize the impact of capital cuts.
Despite its weaknesses, the plan demonstrates that the Mayor and his administration are learning to adjust to tough times. Mr. Dinkins has time to refine his strategy. Meanwhile, he has given New Yorkers fresh evidence of his resolve.
