HEADLINE: On My Mind;
The Best in Him

BYLINE: By A. M. ROSENTHAL

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES

 BODY:
The President gave the State of the Union Message the very best he had in him, and that is the saddest part of all.
Nobody had to tell him how important it would be to the nation and his own future. He had said so for weeks. 
 Not often has a State of the Union Message been made before Americans more eager to give a President a real chance, for his sake and theirs.
Mr. Bush's millions of still-loyal admirers were rooting for him before he said a word. But also pulling for him were millions of other Americans who had voted for him once but were not sure they would do it twice.
As general recession deepened into specific depression in so many lives -- or because his decision to walk away from victory in the Persian Gulf war became so obviously a historic error -- they were no longer convinced he had the stuff to be President. Still, many of them were open -- waiting for him to show they were right the first time after all.
And among Americans who had not voted for him there were those who looked at the array of Democratic candidates and found none made them get up and jig. Surely there were a great many ready for him to prove that the experienced, known President was better than an untested senator or governor.
If these were good times, Mr. Bush could have rested his case for trust and re-election after reciting his lists of foreign affairs achievements. He could have done that because he does have some achievements -- but also because none of the Democrats running nor the Democratic Party itself, whatever that is, has shown the knowledge or courage to challenge him with a list of his foreign policy errors, reversals and backpedaling.
But in these times he had to get on fast to the recession. And only one thing would really have been good enough -- the full truth. The closest he could come was a couple of sentences about how we are in "hard times" and that unemployment is "too high," some industries in trouble, growth not what it should be.
That's it? Is that supposed to be the whole reality about factories shutting down across the country, retailers crowding bankruptcy courts, the livid scandal of banks failing or on the edge, thousands of Americans lining up in the cold for a handful of rotten jobs, the President himself going on a Fuller Brush trip to the scornful Japanese and the realization of so many Americans that they are not only worse off than they were when Mr. Bush was elected, but that for their children, life is likely to be worse yet?
Speaking the truth is not only important morally and emotionally, but to find out how we got where we are and how in Heaven's name to get out.
To accomplish truth, Mr. Bush would have had to tell Americans that some of the policies of his own Administration and his predecessors' were in some great part responsible and exactly what those policies were and how he proposes to correct whatever mistakes he made himself.
That would be difficult for him to say. But then, it is difficult to stand in line for an unemployment check or send in hundreds of resumes that are never answered while keeping up a brave face before the children.
Since he was not capable of telling the full truth he could not build on it to create a coherent plan unified by purpose and philosophy, strengthened by candor and clear to the public. He gave us instead a grab bag of tax proposals.
Some were useful, like housing credit. One is a bad joke -- lending us for a few months a part of the withholding tax the Government so kindly keeps for us without paying us interest. Mr. Bush wants us to spend that money quick, not even thinking of trying to save some before we have to pay it back.
If he had presented the capital gains tax as part of a real plan, instead of in a hodgepodge, the propriety of reducing taxes on "gains" eaten up by inflation might have impressed the public.
He called it all a "plan for America." A week from now ask yourself precisely what the plan is, how it is supposed to work, how it will shorten these hard times and prevent others from hitting the country. As a matter of fact ask yourself right now.
But it was the best he had in him. So maybe we should not be too hard on him -- as long as he promises not to make any more public jokes about vomit.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

SECTION: Section A; Page 27; Column 1; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 699 words

HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs;
Tomorrowland Glimpsed

BYLINE: By LESLIE H.GELB

 BODY:
Five years from now, historians will scan this week's events and say they snapped into focus, briefly yet sharply, the future of world politics. That future was barely glimpsed in President Bush's mostly backward-looking State of the Union address. But it was splashed all over the newspapers.
The news revealed a world where money is talking as perhaps never before. Money power is dictating not just economic choices but political futures, from Western Europe to China. Money is on the verge of being used with astonishing creativity to deal with conflicts in the Middle East and nuclear safety in the old Soviet Union. Money is reshaping battle lines between nations and international corporations in maddeningly unpredictable ways. 
 You could see this week the unusual sway of money on foreign policy and basic political decisions.
Germany's powerful central bank threw its weight around on fundamental political issues. The Bundesbank let out this word: If West European leaders want a single currency, as they pledged at the Maastricht summit, they had better speed steps toward political union. European leaders will have to take heed.
So will Chinese Communist Party bureaucrats, to comments reportedly just made by Deng Xiaoping. The ancient titan told central planners that they had lost their battle. "Reforms and greater openness [to foreign trade and investment] are China's only way out," he said. His message was that the party would have to alter course and loosen economic controls in order to keep political power.
You could appreciate this week how money will be deployed as a creative tool to resolve lingering military problems.
A centerpiece of the Mideast peace parley in Moscow was the creation of a working group on regional economic development. In time, that may prove to be the critical lubricant in making compromises possible between economically stagnant Arab states such as Syria and Jordan and cash-starved Israel.
It has also become clear that Western leaders are coming together on using aid to stem the expected flow of nuclear know-how from the ex-Soviet states. The idea is to pay former Soviet nuclear scientists and technicians to stay put and help dismantle Soviet nuclear arms and fix decaying nuclear power plants.
Your eyes would cross this week in following how money is driving governments toward protectionism even as corporations reach across borders to each other and to governments to circumvent expected barriers.
Almost unnoticed, leaders of six Southeast Asian nations announced on Tuesday a plan for a regional free-trade zone. Their economies are dynamic. And their Governments are girding to protect themselves if the GATT free-trade talks break down and if Mr. Bush's hoped-for North American Free Trade Agreement hurts them.
At about the same time, the French Government acknowledged that its state-owned electronics industry could not survive alone. Paris approved American-controlled I.B.M. buying into Groupe Bull. The deal could mean new life for both against Japanese rivals.
On the same day, the Russian Federation awarded a contract to explore huge offshore oil and gas deposits to a Japanese-American consortium. If the study pans out, investments could total $16 billion. That is far higher than any presently envisaged Western aid plans. The arrangement could also help Japan's chances of regaining its northern islands from Moscow.
You could faintly hear more familiar menacing sounds this week, also connected with money. Germany's leaders warned Russian and Ukrainian leaders about their military plans. It seems these ex-Soviet states intend to maintain armed forces far larger than any European state. No one can be comfortable with that prospect, especially as U.S. troops are reduced. And South Korea made public a plan to increase its air and naval forces and build up its defense industries as U.S. forces withdraw. South Korea, China and Japan will not be at ease with one another without America either.
Money is hurrying the U.S. toward the security sidelines just when peace would feel more comfortable if America were still strong. And money is making peace an anxious state of affairs.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

SECTION: Section A; Page 27; Column 1; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 467 words

HEADLINE: Boris, Lend Me a Hand

BYLINE: By Jack Kemp; Jack Kemp is Secretary of Housing and Urban Development. This article is adapted from an essay in the Heritage Foundation's Policy Review.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
When the "reactionary forces" in Congress recently voted to slash financing for President Bush's "perestroika" plan to privatize public housing and empower low-income residents to become homeowners, I couldn't help but notice the irony.
Today, Russia may be on the verge of the most exciting experiment in entrepreneurial capitalism since 1776. The Russian Government is chopping away at the status quo with enthusiasm. Meanwhile, the most stultified legislative bureaucracy since the fall of the Supreme Soviet -- the U.S. Congress -- is resisting our efforts to launch a historic new war on poverty. 
 A decade ago, when some colleagues and I introduced the first urban enterprise zone bill, I often inveighed against Soviet totalitarianism. Now, 69 enterprise zone bills later, St. Petersburg looks as if it's going to get enterprise zones before American cities.
Nevertheless, as one who wants to reform America's socialist-style public housing, I have a few ideas that might help Russia.
Since World War II, the U.S. has produced more than 70 million new housing units, enabling more than 60 million families to own homes today. In the former Soviet republics, 75 percent of the housing is government-owned. Privatizing the housing stock poses a great challenge.
All apartment residents should be given the right to buy their apartments at a nominal price to establish the principle that you can't get something for nothing. The new Russian housing law, which endorses homeownership, is on the right track. But the law's complicated pricing system is a recipe for bureaucracy.
There are two central questions that come up regarding any plan to sell off Russia's high-rise housing: who will own the land and who will maintain the buildings? For me, these are familiar questions, as the Bush Administration is engaged in a revolutionary effort to let our own public housing residents manage and buy their own homes.
Like the new resident management corporations in American public housing, the residents of Moscow's high-rises could form cooperative or condominium associations as a prelude to ownership. The association would arrange for maintenance through a combination of resident-management employees and private contractors furnished by the new entrepreneurial economy.
Finally, the Russian Government should foster new housing construction, not by investing public resources but by eliminating all significant barriers to private sector homebuilding.
President Yeltsin, you are fortunate because the new Russian legislative bodies are eager for change. And to think it all began when you stood on top of a tank outside the Russian White House. When you're in Washington tomorrow, you could try the same trick on Capitol Hill. If you bring the bullhorn, I'll bring the tank.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Clinton's Private Life Serves as Distraction

 BODY:
To the Editor:
The surge in interest in the personal life of Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas, who is campaigning for the Democratic Party's Presidential nomination, shows a disturbing trend in American politics. All too often, the American public seems to identify a politician's competence or suitability for elected office with his or her personal life. This unfortunate penchant for sensationalism, which much of the news media feed on, denigrates the political process and eclipses the pressing issues.
There is little evidence in history to support the view that with moral piety comes political effectiveness. Winston Churchill, one of the great statesmen of the 20th century, was certainly no icon of piety. His taste for liquor was well known. John F. Kennedy's personal exploits did not prevent his rise to the highest elected office and place in history. 
 Regardless of the validity of the accusations leveled against Governor Clinton, I believe that this American preoccupation with personal "affairs" tends to obscure the real problems of our time. Perhaps, if our society focused less on sensationalism and the private lives of candidates, and more on the economy, health care, drugs, the homeless, AIDS (the list goes on and on), we might be able to elect a President who can lead and help America in the challenging and frightening times ahead.
 
DANIEL EISENBERG
New York, Jan. 20, 1992

HEADLINE: A Way to Get the Trade Deficit in Balance

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Now that President Bush's trade visit to the Orient has ended in disappointment, it may be time to take stronger measures to eliminate our trade deficit. Here is a simple mechanism that could balance trade:
Each time we exported something, the buyer would receive a chit that allows the bearer to export goods of the same value to us. These chits could be freely traded. All imports to us would have to be accompanied by a chit for equivalent value. The requirement of equal value would balance exports and imports. 
 Since our exports would be accompanied by a valuable chit, their cost would effectively be lowered. That exporters to us would have to obtain a chit would raise the cost of their exports. Since exports must equal imports, the cost of the chits must rise until the balance is achieved.
Foreign producers would still compete with one another for the most cost-beneficial exports. Domestic producers would compete with one another much as before, but would now have a price advantage against foreigners -- the price of the chits.
The transition could be smoothed. Our exports are now equal in volume to about 80 percent of what we import. At first, chits covering only 80 percent could be required to accompany imported items. This should cause no change in trade patterns. The percentage could be made to rise to 100 percent over, say, five years.
Flow of money for services should be included in the chit system, but not flow of money for capital investment and purchase of United States debt. Management of these flows would then be a separable problem.
The exchange rate of the dollar is supposed to have much the same effect as such a chit system would, but the exchange rate is perturbed by capital flow and by Government intervention. Devaluing the dollar would make it cheaper for foreigners to purchase industries and assets in this country, something that we do not want to have happen.
A fundamental economic problem may be that we now have only one unit of account, the dollar. Policy makers try to use the dollar to insure all kinds of balances: trade balance, inflation-deflation balance, Government deficits, family budgets. But one unit of account may be insufficient for all these functions.
Gold used to be a second unit of account with different functions from currency. The suggested import-export chits would likewise be a second unit of account. Pollution chits have been widely discussed. It is possible that even more units of account might be useful for economic problems other than the trade deficit.
Forcing our trade into balance in this way would not solve our serious domestic problems: low productivity, low savings rate, high Federal deficit. It might give us some breathing time to put our house in order before we have to sell the whole store to the Japanese.
 
ROBERT J. WYMAN
New Haven, Jan. 10, 1992
 
The writer is a professor of biology at Yale University.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Campaign Journal;
Now, Journalists Renege On Election Promises

BYLINE: By JOHN TIERNEY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MANCHESTER, N.H., Jan. 30

 BODY:
It is still early in the Presidential campaign, as political analysts have repeatedly determined, but the press coverage is beginning to take a familiar shape. In fact, this week there were two events on national television that could ultimately qualify as official defining moments.
The first, of course, was when Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas went on "60 Minutes" to make what was probably the most public confession in the history of marriage problems. The next came Tuesday night when Mr. Clinton's confession was analyzed -- along with questions on the economy and President Bush's understanding of the middle class -- by what was probably the largest biased sample in the history of instant polling. 
 This sort of thing was not supposed to happen in 1992. Academics and politicians and journalists have spent much of the last four years preparing reports on what went wrong in 1988: obsessive coverage of supposed character issues, endless analysis of strategies and polls instead of who stood for what. Studies showed that a majority of the press coverage concerned the horse race, not the issues.
Journalists vowed to reform this year, but it didn't take them long to be distracted by questions of sex, polls and campaign strategy. Is it all right to report a supermarket tabloid's allegation of adultery? And if not, why did we just do it, and why can't we stop talking about it? Would the State of the Union Message be a defining moment or a victim of the expectations game? How was Bill Clinton's television strategy affecting the polls? How were the polls affecting the President's television strategy?
 
Didn't Seem Worthwhile
 This coverage seems to have made the public even more unhappy than usual with the press. Polls this week showed that most people considered accusations of marital infidelity an unimportant issue, but the extensive publicity guaranteed that these accusations were the only thing that many voters knew about Mr. Clinton. Here in New Hampshire it is unusual to find anyone who approves of the way the story has been covered.
"I'm trying not to pay any attention to all the stuff about Clinton supposedly having an affair," said Mary Ann Foley, 34 years old, who is training to be a truck driver in Londonderry. "I know the press is in the business of selling newspapers and getting ratings, but I don't think it's right. I want to hear about the issues. I'd like to find out more about Paul Tsongas's positions."
This campaign started with good intentions and a spate of articles and television pieces on the issues and the candidates' views. The economy was discussed at length, and some newspapers and newscasts went beyond the candidates' positions to present what may be the most important bit of economic information for voters: Most economists think that politicians' current proposals would do little to end the recession and could be damaging in the long term.
But such issues got pushed aside this week by the Clinton tabloid story, which soon made it to front pages and the top of newscasts. It got confusing. On Tuesday, The Boston Globe's front page proclaimed, "Poll Shows Clinton's Lead Undiminished," while The Boston Herald's banner headline said, "Clinton on the Run." Meanwhile, analysts on television programs like "Nightline" berated themselves for considering such a sordid topic. The passionate discussions usually began with a disclaimer that carried all the moral authority of "We can't go on meeting like this."
 
Quick and Skewed
 And then there were the polls, the campaign strategists' reactions to the polls, and the voters' reactions to the strategists' reactions. By the time President Bush delivered the State of the Union Message on Tuesday night, the chief question raised by analysts on television was not whether his proposals made any sense but how the Democrats and voters would respond to his staff's strategy.
The most ambitious effort to gauge reaction was conducted by CBS immediately after the speech in a program called "America on the Line." It invited viewers to make free phone calls to computers in Omaha and participate in the biggest instant telephone poll ever held. Within a few minutes the computers had heard from 74,200 callers across the country, and Connie Chung reported a result: 53 percent of the callers said they were worse off than four years ago.
"It's important to note here that this is quite dramatic," she said to Dan Rather, and explained that a CBS News poll a month earlier had found only 30 percent of people in the worse-off category. "Dan, it's gone up to 53 percent. Perhaps the group that's calling us now, these across the country, are quite upset, quite angry. This does not bode well for President Bush on the night of his sState of the Union."
Well, it would not have boded well for President Bush if he had had to talk to those angry thousands -- or pay for the calls -- but otherwise it meant very little.
 
Science by the Wayside
 The instant poll involved an unrepresentative sample of Americans, those who were still tuned in after the State of the Union address, from which were selected only those sufficiently motivated to reach the computer in Omaha -- no mean feat, since only about 1 out of every 100 calls got through. And it was further biased by the stories of desperate unemployed and sick people that the viewers were shown during the polling.
"It's a good measure of the number of people who are watching the network and of the intensity of the feelings that lead them to respond," said Philip E. Meyer, an expert in polling who teaches at the University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill. "But it's of no value at all as a measurement of the distribution of public opinion on an issue."
To CBS's credit, it also conducted a nationwide survey of a randomly selected sample, and Dan Rather briefly reported the result: 32 percent of 1,241 people surveyed considered themselves worse off than four years ago, or about the same as in the poll a month previously. So much for the dramatic change.
But by the end of the program Ms. Chung was again talking about the people's pessimistic mood as the tally of callers grew to 314,726. The final result showed that 54 percent declared themsleves worse off, 70 percent thought President Bush did not understand the middle class, and 45 percent were not satisfied with Mr. Clinton's televised confession.
Mr. Rather closed with a solemn word about the importance of voting, although it was not exactly clear what voters were supposed to have learned. Perhaps they could conclude that people who have time to call a computer in Omaha also have time to worry about Mr. Clinton's personal life -- although probably not as much time as the nation's journalists.

CORRECTION-DATE: February 1, 1992, Saturday

 CORRECTION:
Because of an editing error, an article yesterday about news coverage of the Presidential campaign referred incorrectly in some copies to the telephone response to the CBS program "America on the Line," televised on Tuesday night after the President's State of the Union Message. Only about 1 out of every 100 callers was able to get through.
